# THE FACE-KEEPER

*a novel*

**The Pelican Lost-Property Mysteries — Book One**

> She's never forgotten a face — and she just saw a dead girl's eyes on a stranger at the pool.

---

# CHAPTER 1 — One (1) Child's Flip-Flop

The man coming through the turnstile is a 2009 train platform.

Goldie doesn't decide this. It happens to her the way weather happens to a field. He swings his kit bag up over the barrier, ducks under, and before he has cleared it she has him: Cardiff Central, platform four, a delayed 18:42 to Bridgend, him eating a Ginsters with the wrapper tucked under his thumb, reading something on a phone that was three phones ago. He's lost the hair. He has gained a wife, going by the ring, and a stone and a half, going by the jaw, and the small permanent stoop of a man who carries one thing too heavy on one side — a toddler, she'd guess now, always the same hip. The left earlobe still hangs free where the right one fuses flat to the cheek. Sixteen years and a different country of a body, and the earlobe has not moved a millimetre, because earlobes don't, which is the whole rotten miracle of it.

She has never spoken to him in her life.

He doesn't look at her. Nobody looks at the lost-property desk. That is the desk's single greatest virtue, the reason she chose it off the council vacancy list the way other women choose a quiet table at the back of the room. *Receptionist/Lost Property Assistant, the Pelican Leisure Centre, Aberglas — 16hrs.* She'd have taken eight.

"All right," he says to the air in front of him, to the building, to Wales, and goes through to the changing rooms, and that is the end of him. She files him without meaning to, the way you can't help swallowing. Somewhere behind her eyes a drawer she did not open and cannot close slides shut on a man whose name she will never know and whose breakfast in 2009 she now apparently keeps forever.

Twenty past eight on her first morning, and she has already remembered a stranger harder than he has ever been remembered by anyone, including, probably, his mother.

This is the arrangement. She is here precisely because nothing here matters.

* * *

The Pelican smells of chlorine and chips and the particular sorrow of a building that used to be loved.

It is a 1930s lido, or was — a great curved Art Deco frontage gone the colour of a bad tooth, white render flaking off the bones of it like a sunburn that never healed. The outdoor pool has been shut since before the millennium and is full now of leaves and rainwater and ambition. The warm part, the part that still works, is the indoor pool behind her, where the echo and the slap of feet on wet tile come through the swing doors in waves whenever someone opens them. A run of postcards is pinned behind her desk: THE PELICAN IN ITS DAY, 1951, a thousand people in the open-air water like seals on a rock, all of them dead now or nearly, every face frozen at whatever age the photographer caught them, none of them ageing a day in her head if she let them in, which she will not.

She has a counter. She has a hatch. She has a ledger, an actual paper one, A4, ruled, that came with the job and that she has decided to keep because the screen Sai keeps trying to teach her freezes if you so much as breathe near the keyboard.

She has the shelf.

The shelf is, she is coming to understand, the entire content of the human condition, sorted by date and left to go quietly green in a metal cage.

She inventories it because she cannot not. It is half past eight, the boiler is making a noise like a man being slowly disappointed, and Goldie reads the shelf the way she once read riot footage, which is to say completely, against her will, and for no money.

One (1) child's flip-flop, size 9, left foot only, the sole worn through on the inside edge — a pronator, this one, a roller, a small person already pounding the wrong part of the foot into the ground; a future of insoles, a physiotherapist twenty years off who hasn't qualified yet. One flip-flop. The other is somewhere having a better life.

One (1) pair of swimming goggles, adult, the left lens fogged grey and crazed, the right gone entirely — popped clean out — so what she is holding is half a thing pretending to be a whole one, an eyeglass for a man with one eye. Returned, says the tag in Dil's enormous loops, *Tues — left a goggle, says he'll be back for it.* He will not be back for it. Nobody comes back for half.

One (1) set of dentures. Upper. In, of all the containers God provided, a margarine tub. She does not open the margarine tub. She has worked fifteen years in a job that taught her exactly which boxes not to open, and the rest has been the trouble.

One (1) wedding ring, men's, nine carat, the inside worn to a knife-thin band by thirty years of a finger that has now, evidently, stopped wanting it on. No inscription. There's always no inscription on the ones that matter.

A child's armband, size 2–4 years, one of a pair, the valve perished. The other is already somewhere doing better.

She sets the armband down and moves on, fast, because that is what you do, you keep the line moving, you do not let the shelf become a face and the face become a small wet head turning to check you are still—

She writes the armband into the ledger. *Armband, inflatable, 2–4yrs, single. STATUS: unclaimed.* The pen does the swerving for her. The pen is very good at its job.

* * *

"You'll be Marigold."

The woman has arrived at the hatch the way certain weather systems arrive over Cardigan Bay, which is to say without warning and intending to stay for the duration. Sixty-seven, Goldie has her at once, though she'd have guessed older from the hair and younger from the eyes; a perm gone to architecture; reading glasses on a beaded chain doing the work of a second, sterner face; a lanyard so freighted with keys and fobs and a small laminated photograph of a Yorkshire terrier that it has visibly altered the woman's posture over the years, dragging the left shoulder down a clear half-inch. Forty years of carrying the same building on one side. Goldie knows that stoop. She has it herself, only hers is invisible and she carries it behind her eyes.

"Goldie," says Goldie.

"Goldie. Lovely. I'm Dilys, everyone says Dil, I've been on this reception since nineteen eighty-three and I'll go out of it feet first, so you'll want to be nice to me." She delivers this without menace, the way you'd mention the tide times. "I've put you a cup of the café coffee, don't thank me, I'm only sorry."

The coffee is the colour of the building. Goldie tastes it. It is genuinely, almost impressively bad — bitter and thin at once, a coffee that has been *let down*, like a child of immense promise who took up something unfortunate.

"There's a knack to drinking it," says Dil, watching her face with the frank interest of a woman who has nothing to learn from anyone but enjoys the lesson anyway. "You don't taste it, you just decide it's happened to you. Carwyn does it on purpose, mind. The bacon's holy, but the coffee's a sermon about sin. Now." She plants both forearms on the hatch. "I'll tell you who everyone is, save you the bother."

Goldie, who already knows who everyone is, in the only way she knows anything, and would pay folding money never to know one more, says, "There's no need—"

There is, it turns out, every need.

Dil narrates. It is not conversation, it is irrigation; the town comes in through the hatch in a steady warm flow and Goldie has no choice but to let it run over her. The man at the vending machine fighting it for a Twix is *Dai Post, retired, his wife left him for a man from Aberaeron and he's never forgiven the postcode.* The two women shouldering through to aqua are *the Bevan sisters, don't get them started on the bins.* A girl drifts past with a swim bag and a face Goldie reads off in half a second — eighteen, a smoker hiding it, a left-handed cowlick she fights with a clip — and Dil supplies, unasked, *Pryce, that one, well, they're all Pryce, you'll learn, half the bay's Pryce and the other half's Pugh and the trick is which.*

"And which are you?" says Dil, rounding on her. "Okonkwo-Webb, the badge says. That's a mouthful and a half. Where's home, then?"

"Cardiff," says Goldie. "Mam was Aberglas, before. Dad was Lagos." She offers it the way she offers everything about herself, in coins, exact change, no note. "I'm half from here, more or less. The accent skipped me."

"It hasn't skipped you," says Dil, with sudden surprising shrewdness, "you've just sat on it." And then, off Goldie's face, kindly: "We'll have it out of you by Christmas." She straightens, the lanyard swinging its little dead dog. "Met, weren't you? Before. Someone said Met."

And there it is, the soft tug on the line. Someone always says Met.

"Filing," says Goldie. "Mostly filing."

It is, she has found, the most reliable lie she owns, because it is technically true and entirely false in the way that matters, and because nobody has ever once been curious about filing. Dil isn't either. Dil's interest slides off the word like water off the bad render and finds a better surface — the boiler, the funeral on Thursday, a feud about a parking space that has apparently outlived two of its original combatants — and Goldie is released, free, anonymous, a woman who does filing, behind a counter where nothing will ever need her to be sure.

* * *

The boy comes to the counter at ten to nine.

He is nineteen, and Goldie has the rest of him before he opens his mouth, the way she has everyone, the involuntary inventory running its hands over him: tall, still filling out the shoulders, a lifeguard's red shorts with the whistle lanyard and the laminated trainee badge — RHODRI, it says, in the centre's font — a swimmer's permanent faint redness round the sockets, a graze on one shin gone yellow at the edges, three days old. Left-handed; she clocks it from the way he's already reaching across his own body. A good face. An open, unfinished, kind face, the kind that hasn't yet decided what it's going to be and is enjoying not deciding.

"'Scuse me," he says, "sorry — has anyone handed in a goggle? Just the one. Left one. Black strap, the lens is—" he holds up finger and thumb, a small distance, sheepish "—gone, actually. So it's. It's basically not a goggle. I keep doing this."

"Third time," she says, because the tag said so, and finds the half-thing on the shelf and slides it across, half a goggle for a boy who has, she'd put money on it, two good eyes and a careless heart, and he laughs, an easy ordinary laugh, *ah, you're a legend, cheers,* and tips his head up to thank her properly—

and looks at her.

And Goldie's whole life arrives at the counter.

She has, in fifty-one years, never once been wrong about whether she has seen a face before. Not the *who* of it, not the name, not the where — those are ordinary, those go missing like everyone's go missing, like flip-flops. But the *seen-before*, the bare yes-or-no of it, the recognition itself: that has never failed her, not in a riot, not on the worst night-bus footage the Met ever fed her, not once.

And she has never seen this boy before in her life.

And she knows these eyes.

Not the colour, though the colour is part of it — a grey that isn't grey, a wet-slate green with a darker ring at the rim, the kind of eye that changes with the weather and the shirt. It is the *build* of them: the precise width of the set, the slight downward cant at the outer corner that should read sad and instead reads patient, the heaviness of the upper lid that makes a person look, always, like they are about to forgive you. She knows the geometry of them the way she knows the earlobe of a man on a 2009 platform. She knows them to the millimetre. She has held these eyes in her attention before, given them the full forensic weight, and filed them, and the drawer they came from is open now and what is in the drawer is *wrong*, because the drawer is old and the eyes across the counter are nineteen and warm and alive, and somewhere underneath, in the part of her that does not lie because it cannot, the thing she knows about these eyes — without a name, without a date, without one single fact she could say out loud — is that they are dead.

Not his.

That is the only sentence that surfaces, clean and cold and complete, the way they surface, unbidden, fully formed.

*Those eyes are not his.*

"You all right?" says Rhodri. He's still holding the broken goggle. He's gone a half-step back, the gentle alarm of a kind boy who thinks he's said something. "You've gone a bit—"

"Fine," she says. "Sorry. Long way from a coffee."

"God, yeah, don't drink the coffee." He grins, relieved to be on solid ground, the whole strange second already gone from his side of the counter, never having landed there at all. "Carwyn does it on purpose."

"So I hear."

And he goes — knocks the goggle twice on the counter for luck, the way you'd ring a little brass bell, *ta, you're a star* — and pushes through the swing doors into the chlorine and the echo, and the eyes go with him, into the water, where she can't follow them, and Goldie stands with both hands flat on the counter and waits for the floor to decide whether it is a floor.

* * *

Dil is back. Dil is always back; she travels the building on rails.

"That's Rhodri," she says, helpfully, to no question, picking a thread off her cardigan and finding the counter to lean on like a sailor finding a rail in weather. "Huw's lot — great-nephew, or great-great, I lose count, Huw's the lifeguard, big soft article, he's training the boy up. Lovely family." A small click of the tongue. "Gave us a fright this morning, mind, that one."

Goldie's hands are still flat on the counter. She does not lift them. She keeps her voice exactly where she has kept it for twelve years, in the dry middle register where nothing can get a grip. "Did he."

"Went a funny colour at the deep end. White as that wall. Huw had him sat down with his head between his knees before you could say boo." Dil shakes her head, fond, untroubled, filing it under the long warm catalogue of small town nothings she carries the soft human way, wrong on every date and right on every feeling. "Dehydration, bless him. He never drinks enough water, that's boys, you tell them and you tell them. He's fine now, look at him." She nods through the door's little window, where the boy with the borrowed dead eyes is hauling a float net across the shallow end, laughing at something, alive as anything, alive as Tuesday. "Right as rain, see. They bounce, that age."

"They do," says Goldie.

She picks up the pen. She writes, in the ledger, in her own small forensic hand, where Dil cannot see and would not understand it if she could: *flip-flop, size 9, left, pronator. STATUS: unclaimed.* She has already written this. She writes it again because her hand needs somewhere to go and the shelf is the only confession she has ever been able to make, and it isn't one.

Twelve years of training herself not to summon one particular face — the only act of forgetting she has ever attempted, the single thing in all the world she has made herself stop being able to see — and she has come to the quietest counter in Wales, to the one job where no face would ever cost her anything, where the whole point of the arrangement was that the seeing could finally stop.

And on the first morning a boy has leaned over the counter wearing somebody's dead eyes, and the seeing has started up again like a thing that was only ever pretending to sleep.

She tells herself it is the coffee.

She tells herself it is the new place, the strange shift, fifteen years of bad footage and a brain that learned to see ghosts in static and never quite unlearned it. She tells herself she will not think about the boy's eyes again, that it is nothing, that she is tired, that *those eyes are not his* is exactly the kind of three-in-the-morning sentence a worn-out mind serves up and a sane woman declines.

She is lying. She knows she is lying the way she knows everything — instantly, against her will, and without the smallest idea what it means.

Through the little window the boy straightens up from the float net, and for no reason at all, mid-laugh, he puts one hand flat to his chest — there and gone, a half-second, the way you'd press a stitch, or check a phone in a breast pocket, or feel for something that for the length of a single beat forgot to be there.

Then Huw calls him, and he's gone down the poolside, easy and tall and nineteen, and the water takes the rest.

---

> **FACE 0001** — male, 19, lifeguard trainee (RHODRI). Counter, 08:50, returns nothing — claims a left goggle, no lens. A graze, three days. Left-handed.
> *The eyes: set, cant, lid — exact. Not the colour, the build. I have held these before.*
> Not his. Whose, then.
> Earlier: "a funny colour at the deep end." Dehydration, they say.
> **STATUS: UNPLACED.**

---

# CHAPTER 2 — The Aqua Belles

Three days in, Goldie has perfected the art of not looking at the pool.

It is harder than it sounds. The lost-property desk faces the swing doors, and the swing doors have a little window in each, two portholes of wired glass at exactly the height to frame whatever is happening at the deep end, so that the pool comes to her in slices whether she wants it or not — a flash of water, a body, a float net dragged past — like a film she has paid not to see being projected on the back of her own eyelids. She has taken to sorting the shelf side-on. She has taken to doing the ledger with her chair turned a careful fifteen degrees, the angle of a woman pretending to admire the postcards. She is, she will admit, a grown professional who has spent two of her three working days arranging not to glance at a leisure-centre swimming bath in case a boy's eyes are in it.

So far it has worked, in the sense that she has not seen Rhodri's eyes again, and not worked, in the sense that she has thought about almost nothing else.

"You're hiding," says Dil, arriving at the hatch with two coffees and the settled expression of a woman bringing news. "I know hiding. I've watched forty years of it go past this desk. Men hide behind the paper, women hide behind the kiddies, and you—" she sets one coffee down, the colour of the building, the smell of regret "—you hide behind a flip-flop. Drink that, it's worse cold."

"What am I hiding from?"

"Don't know yet," says Dil, with relish, the way another woman might say *we'll know by Friday.* "But Tuesday you went a colour when that Rhodri leaned over, and I've been turning it over." She has, too. Goldie can see her turning it, the soft human machinery of her, all wrong dates and right instincts. "Did he say something? Boys his age, half of them don't know they're being—"

"He said don't drink the coffee."

"Well, he's not wrong." Dil takes the deflection the way she takes everything, on the chin and onward, already gone to the next thing, because the next thing is bearing down on the reception in a tide of towelling and chlorine and conversation, and the next thing is loud. "Oh, here's the Belles. Now you'll see something. Brace."

Goldie braces.

* * *

The Aqua Belles come down the corridor from the women's changing room the way a weather front comes off the bay — not as individuals but as a *system,* a low-pressure event with several distinct centres of menace, and the air changes ahead of them.

She has them before they reach the hatch. It is involuntary, the inventory; it runs its hands over the lot of them in the second and a half it takes them to cross the foyer, and she could no more switch it off than switch off the swing doors. Four of them at the front, the core, the rest trailing in floral caps like a comet's tail.

The one on point is seventy if she's a day and built like a harbour wall — short, broad, a swimming costume the deep municipal navy of a costume bought for *function,* a cap she has not yet put on but carries in one fist like a thrown gauntlet. She walks from the hips. She walks the way you walk when the floor has always, in your experience, got out of the way. Goldie has met three people who walk like that and two of them ran prisons.

"That's Maureen," murmurs Dil, unnecessarily.

Beside Maureen, a half-step back in the slot of the natural lieutenant, a tall soft woman of about sixty-eight with a hearing aid in the left ear and the lovely open over-attentive face of someone who has spent a lifetime gambling on what was just said and losing graciously — kind, you can read kind off a face the way you read a limp, it's in the set of the mouth at rest, the readiness to be pleased. *Glenys,* Goldie thinks, before Dil says it, and is briefly unnerved by herself.

Then a smaller, sharper article, sixty-something, swim cap already on and cinched, glasses *over* the cap on a cord, scanning the foyer with the bright suspicious eyes of a woman who reads the back pages and believes none of it and all of it at once. She has a folded newspaper rolled in her bag like a baton. *That one thinks the council is up to something,* Goldie thinks, *and the council probably is.*

And at the back, moving slowly, on the arm of nobody because she has refused the arm, a very old woman. Ninety, Goldie has her, and then revises, because the hands say more than ninety and the eyes say exactly however old she has decided to be — small, folded inward, dry as a leaf, a cap the faded violet of a flower pressed in a book decades ago and forgotten there. She does not scan the foyer. She has seen the foyer. She came in slowly and entirely under her own steam and she is looking at nothing and missing nothing, and out of the four of them she is the only one Goldie cannot read past — not a blank like the others are open books, but a closed one, a face that has decided, long ago and on purpose, to give nothing away. Goldie clocks it and files it and does not know yet why her attention snags there, on the quietest of them, the way a comb snags on the one knot.

"Eira," says Dil, dropping her voice for this one alone, with something underneath the gossip that isn't gossip. "Ninety-five. Don't let the quiet fool you. She's seen this town do everything it's ever done."

Maureen reaches the hatch and stops. She looks at Goldie. It is not a hostile look. It is worse than a hostile look. It is an *assessing* look, the look of a woman taking delivery of a new appliance she did not order and is not yet sure she'll keep.

"You're the new one," says Maureen.

"Goldie."

"Dilys says you were police."

"Filing," says Goldie.

"Hm," says Maureen, in a way that suggests she has filed *that,* and will be returning to it. She turns her head a precise few degrees, the turret of a slow-moving vessel acquiring a target. "Glenys. The board."

"The Lord?" says Glenys, leaning in, beaming.

"The *board,* Glenys. The bingo board."

"Oh, the board." Glenys nods, delighted to have arrived at the board. She turns to Goldie with the air of someone passing on a great confidence. "We do the bingo Thursdays. In the function room. You'll come."

It is not a question. None of it, Goldie is learning, is ever quite a question.

* * *

What happens next, Goldie will reconstruct afterward and still not entirely believe.

She is conscripted. It takes under a minute and there is nothing she can do, the way there is nothing a swimmer can do about a rip. It begins with Maureen observing, to the air, that the bingo has been *short a marker* since Beryl's hip, and proceeds through Pam — the sharp one, the newspaper — declaring that you can't trust the machine to call fair, the machine is *programmed,* and somebody told her the balls are weighted, and arrives, with the inevitability of tide tables, at the discovery that the new one was *police,* and the police are exactly who you want marking a board where there is, Pam has reason to believe, *cheating.*

"There is not cheating at the bingo, Pam," says Maureen, in the tone of a woman who has had this conversation since the Falklands.

"Then where's Vera's full house from last week, when she only wanted the two?"

"Vera lied about wanting the two."

"That's what I'm saying," says Pam, vindicated by something. "It's all of it."

Goldie opens her mouth to say that she does the lost property, that she is not, in fact, available to invigilate a leisure-centre bingo night against the deep-state interference of weighted balls, and Maureen turns the full navy weight of her attention on, and says, "Good. Thursday. Seven. We start at half past but you'll want to be early or Maldwyn takes the good chair," and that, it transpires, is the end of it. She has been hired. She did not apply. She has lost a job interview she was not having and the prize is bingo.

Dil is openly enjoying this. "You don't say no to Maureen," she explains, kindly, once the system has moved off toward the pool. "Nobody says no to Maureen. Maureen said no to the *council* once. They moved the meeting."

"What was the meeting about?"

"Closing us," says Dil, and for half a beat the warmth goes out of it, the way the sun goes off the water when a cloud crosses, there and back. "They've been trying for years, mind. She's held them off for years."

And then it's gone, the cloud, and Dil is folding a lost cardigan and telling her about Maldwyn and the good chair, and Goldie files the closing of the place under things she will not think about, alongside the boy's eyes, in the drawer with the others, the one that does not lock.

* * *

She goes to watch the class. She tells herself it is because Maureen will check.

This is a lie, but a small one, the kind she allows herself. The truth is that the pool has been a closed door for three days and the door has just been propped open — there is a *reason* to look now, an alibi, a class to observe, a job adjacent to her actual job — and the thing behind her eyes that has spent three days starving for the water leaps at it like a dog off a lead.

The Aqua Belles in the water are a different proposition from the Aqua Belles on land. On land they are a weather system. In the water they are a *congregation.* The instructor, a young man with a microphone and a waterproof speaker the size of a brick, calls the moves from the side — *and reach, and reach, lovely, Glenys other arm, your other arm, lovely* — and the class moves in the warm green chlorinated light with the unhurried devotion of people who came here for each other and stayed for the company, the exercise a kind of liturgy they perform so they're allowed to keep meeting. Float belts cinched round soft middles. Foam noodles wielded like ceremonial staves. Maureen at the centre, not following the instructor so much as permitting him.

Goldie stands at the rail above the shallow end with a clipboard she doesn't need, performing a supervisor, and lets herself look, and for a few minutes it is only what it is — old women in a warm pool on a grey afternoon, the steam rising, the slap and echo, the radio-brick playing something from before any of them were sad — and it is, she thinks, with the small surprise of a woman who came here to feel nothing, genuinely lovely. *Lovely, that.* The phrase arrives in her own head in a cadence she has spent thirty years sitting on, and she lets it, this once, because nobody can hear her think.

Then the two come down the pool side, late, and the warmth tilts.

* * *

They come from the changing rooms at a half-run, the guilty quick walk of people who know they're late for Maureen, two young women, twenty or close to it, in matching navy club costumes with PELICAN SC across the chest, and Goldie has them before they hit the water the way she has everyone — and stops.

She does not stop because of who they are. She doesn't know who they are. She stops because of the thing her attention does, the involuntary thing, the thing she has never in fifty-one years been able to switch off: it reads the first face, and it reads the second face, and somewhere behind her eyes a drawer that has been open and burning since Tuesday slides another quarter-inch wider, and it is *cold* in there.

They are sisters. Twins — not identical, she'd put folding money on fraternal, the bones agree and the soft tissue argues, one a stone heavier and a shade fairer, one with a mole below the right jaw the other lacks. Ordinary lovely girls. Good skin, club swimmers, a graze on one elbow, a friendship bracelet on the fairer one's wrist gone grey with chlorine. She could tell you their mornings. She could tell you the fairer one bites the inside of her cheek and the darker one doesn't, that they share a kit bag, that one of them has just had a row with a boy by the way she's holding her shoulders and the other one knows about it by the way she isn't asking.

And across the two of them, split clean down the middle, parcelled out one half each like a thing divided between heirs, is a face she has seen before.

The cheekbones.

Not a feature you'd notice. That is the horror of it, the quiet workmanlike horror, nothing dramatic, nothing a normal person would clock in a hundred years of looking — but she is not a normal person, she is the broken kind, and she reads bone for a living, or did, and these cheekbones she *knows.* The high wide set of them, the particular shelf of the malar where it meets the temple, the angle the light catches and makes a plane of, that exact plane — the fairer twin has it on the left side of her face and the darker twin has it on the right, as if one original face had been cut down the philtrum with a blade and the two halves handed out to two different girls at birth, and each of them grew the rest of herself around her half and never knew she was carrying somebody else's bone in her own cheek.

It is the same bone she saw in the boy.

Not the eyes. The boy had the eyes. These two have the cheek — the same source-face, *she would stake the rest of her sight on it,* the same underlying architecture she held in her attention on Tuesday and filed and could not stop filing, scattered now across three young people instead of one, distributed the way you'd distribute an inheritance among children, a bit to you and a bit to you and a bit to your brother, and none of you the whole.

You cannot do this.

This is the thing that arrives, clean and cold and complete, the way they arrive. You cannot project a face onto three strangers. You can fool yourself with one — a tired brain, a poster you've walked past too many mornings, a coincidence of grey-green eyes; one she could explain away, she *had* explained away, she'd told herself a dozen sane things about the boy. But you cannot misfire *symmetrically.* You cannot imagine the same cheekbone onto the left side of one girl and the right side of another, split down a line a forger would have to measure, because imagination doesn't do precision — imagination smears, it generalises, it wants the whole face or none. This is not a smear. This is a *measurement.* This is the same bone, divided, walking around a swimming pool in two bodies that have never met the body it came from.

"You all right?" says someone. It's the instructor, microphone lowered, looking up at her on the rail with the mild concern of a man whose supervisor has gone still.

"Fine," says Goldie. The dry middle register, where nothing gets a grip. "Just wondering who I'm marking on Thursday."

"Oh, them?" He follows her look to the twins, now in the water, getting a low-voiced earful from Maureen about timekeeping that they're absorbing with the bowed cheerful penitence of the young. "Megan and Mali. The Pryce-Pughs. Huw's lot — you'll have met Huw, big fella, lifeguard." He grins. "Whole family swims. Always have. There's a board of them upstairs, the gala photos, going back. You'd think they were born in the water, that lot."

Huw's lot.

The boy was Huw's lot. *Great-nephew,* Dil had said, *or great-great, I lose count.* The boy with the dead eyes and these two with the divided bone are the same *family,* and the cold in the drawer goes colder, because now it isn't a coincidence of strangers, it's a *line,* the face is running down a bloodline like water finding the low ground, and somewhere up the top of that family, at the spring of it, is a person whose cheekbones and whose eyes are walking around Aberglas in the grandchildren while the person themselves is—

She doesn't finish it. She has had a lot of practice not finishing things.

"Pryce-Pugh," she says instead, lightly, writing nothing down. "Half the bay's Pryce, I'm told. And the other half's Pugh."

"And the trick is which," says the instructor, pleased, an old joke he didn't invent and is glad to pass on, and turns the microphone back up — *and reach, and reach, Maureen lovely* — and Megan and Mali Pryce-Pugh reach for the side of the pool with their grandmother's stolen cheekbones, one half each, laughing now, the row forgiven, twenty years old and alive as the afternoon.

* * *

"You'll want the basement," says Sai.

She has come to find him because she needs, suddenly and badly, somewhere to put her hands and her eyes that isn't the pool, and because the instructor said *a board of them, going back,* and the word *board* lodged in her like a hook. Sai Okafor is the new duty manager, twenty-four, in a centre-issue polo shirt and the haunted patience of a young man who has read the council's projections and knows exactly how long this building has left and is the only person in it who does. She has decided she likes him, in the wary provisional way she decides she likes anyone, which is to say she has decided he is not a threat to her anonymity and will leave her be.

"The basement," she says.

"For your overflow. The shelf's full — Dil told me you've a margarine tub of teeth you won't deal with—" he says it without flicker, a man past being surprised "—and there's metric tonnes of storage down there nobody's touched since the nineties. Half of it's the swim club's. Galas, photos, the lot. It's a fire hazard and a half but if you want shelf space, that's your shelf space." He hands her a key on a fob shaped, for reasons lost to time, like a small plastic dolphin. "Light switch is at the bottom of the stairs and it's temperamental. Whole building's temperamental. I'm closing a temperamental building." He says this last with the flat despair of a man stating a known cause of death. "Mind the third step."

The basement is cold the way only the underneath of a seaside building is cold, a damp that comes up out of the foundations and the bay beneath them, and it smells of paper going soft and chlorine that has soaked down through eighty years of floor and the particular mineral nothing of a room the sun has never once been in. She finds the light — it comes on reluctantly, two of four tubes, flickering, settling — and she stands at the bottom of the temperamental stairs and looks at the memory of the town.

It is in boxes. Banker's boxes and biscuit tins and a steamer trunk and a filing cabinet with no drawer fronts, ranked along the walls and stacked in islands, and someone, decades ago, with the doomed tidiness of the well-meaning, labelled them. Marker pen gone brown. GALA. GALA. CHRISTENINGS. SWIM CLUB 60s–70s. SWIM CLUB 80s. FUNCTIONS. And on the side of each, where the doomed tidy person ran out of nouns, the years.

She doesn't mean to look for it. She has come down for shelf space. But the eye goes where the eye goes, and it runs along the brown-marker dates the way it ran along the twins, helplessly, in order, counting — 1971, 1984, a fat split box for the eighties, the nineties bursting its lid — and snags.

The boxes thin toward the end. That's natural; the photographs stop somewhere in the nineties, the lido closed, the club shrank, fewer galas, fewer christenings, the town doing less of everything. The decline is right there in the cardboard, a civilisation's worth of joy tapering off into a couple of slim folders. That isn't what snags her.

What snags her is the one box for the early two-thousands — GALA / FUNCTIONS 00–04, the brown marker says — and it is *light.*

She doesn't open it yet. She just sees it: the box for the years either side of a number she does not yet have a reason to care about, the box that ought, by the run of the others, to be as fat as the nineties or fatter — the centre still half-alive then, weddings, the Trust's good works, all the function-room business Dil keeps half-mentioning — and it sits on the shelf with the slumped underfed look of a box that has had things taken out of it. The others bulge. This one caves. You can read a removed weight in a cardboard box the way you can read a removed ring off a finger; the shape remembers. Something was in here, by the give of the sides and the fade-line on the cardboard where a fuller load once sat, and now it isn't, and the lid sits a clean inch lower than it was built to.

She stands in the cold with the dolphin key in her fist and looks at the thin box and feels the afternoon arrange itself into a question whose first half she already has — *whose cheekbones, whose eyes, scattered down a family* — and whose second half is starting, just starting, to take a shape she can't pretend isn't there.

She came here for shelf space. She tells herself that's still why she's here.

She is lying, and she knows she's lying, the way she knows everything, instantly and against her will and without the smallest idea yet what it means.

* * *

Upstairs, the swing doors are still doing their little film, the pool coming to the desk in slices, and Dil is at the hatch when Goldie comes up from the cold with the dolphin key and a face she has put back into the dry middle register the way you put a book back on a shelf.

"Find your space?" says Dil.

"Plenty," says Goldie.

"Anything good down there? It's all the club's old galas, that — Huw'd have your hand off for it, he loves all that, who-won-what-in-nineteen-seventy-three." Dil shakes her head, fond. "Whole families on those photos. Half of them gone now. The other half still coming in Tuesdays, look." She nods through the porthole, where two young women in PELICAN SC navy are hauling themselves dripping up the steps at the shallow end, twenty years old, laughing, sharing a towel and a secret, each carrying half of a face that nobody alive has thought about in years.

"Lovely family," says Goldie, because it's true, and because it's the safe thing, and because the pen does the swerving for her even when there's no pen.

She needs a name. Not the twins' names — she has those, Megan and Mali, the instructor handed her those for free. She needs the *other* name, the one at the top of the family, the spring of the bone, the source-face she has now held in her attention three separate times in three days and filed three times and cannot file *away* — because a face she can recognise and not name is a splinter she can feel and not reach, and she has spent three days now with this one working its way in, and she knows herself well enough to know it will not stop. It never stops. That is the whole rotten miracle of it.

She is going back down to that box. She knows it already, standing at the hatch with Dil narrating the tide of the town, the way you know a thing you have not yet admitted you've decided. Tonight, or the next quiet hour, with the dolphin key and a torch, into the cold, to open the one box that caves where the others bulge, and find the face she keeps seeing, and give it a name, so it will let her sleep.

"Tea?" says Dil.

"Go on, then," says Goldie, and the Welsh of it lands in her own mouth before she can sit on it, and Dil grins like she's won something, and through the little window the water takes the twins, and the afternoon goes grey and warm and ordinary and continues.

---

> **FACE 0002** — female, 20, club swimmer (one of two; the fairer). Pool, Thurs class. *Cheekbone, LEFT side — the malar shelf, the temple-plane, exact.* Pryce-Pugh. Huw's lot.
> **FACE 0003** — female, 20, her twin (the darker; mole below R jaw). Same pool. *Cheekbone, RIGHT side — the other half.* The face was cut down the middle and given out.
> Same bone as 0001? The set is the same. The source is the same. Not the eyes — the cheek. Different child, same architecture.
> You cannot misfire symmetrically. So I am not misfiring.
> Three now. All young. All Huw's lot.
> **STATUS: UNPLACED.** *(Whose face am I giving out, one bone at a time?)*

---

# CHAPTER 3 — The Name in the Bunting

The basement of the Pelican is where the building keeps the people it used to have.

Goldie knows this now the way she knows everything about a place she has decided not to leave — completely, against her better sense, and at half past ten at night with a head-torch she bought in the Spar between the firelighters and the dog chews. The torch was £4.50 and came with a smaller torch she does not understand the purpose of. She has the small one in her pocket as a hostage.

Three days ago she came down here for storage reasons, which were a lie she told the building, and found instead the archive: forty-odd cardboard boxes shelved by decade along the back wall, swollen with damp at the corners, each one a slab of the town's face pressed flat and left to brown. GALAS, somebody printed once in marker that has gone the colour of weak tea. CHRISTENINGS. PRESENTATIONS. A box that just says MISC and has clearly absorbed the overflow of every other box for forty years, the way one drawer in every house in the world absorbs the batteries and the foreign coins and the keys to doors that no longer exist.

She is here for 2003.

She is here for 2003 because she is, as of this week, a woman with a problem she cannot put down, and the problem is that she has now seen a dead girl's face on three living people who have never met each other, and one of those people is a boy whose heart she watched stutter at the deep end, and the only way she has ever known to lay a face to rest is to find where she first saw it. Find the original. Put the copy against it. Make the drawer behind her eyes agree to close.

The 2003 box is thinner than it should be.

She notices this the way she notices everything, which is to say she does not decide to notice it; it simply registers, a wrongness of weight, a box that has been into more than it gives back. 2001 is fat. 2002 is fat. 2004 onward, fat, fat, fat, a whole bay's worth of children growing up in chlorine. But 2003 is light in the hand, light enough that she checks the label twice, and there is a softness to one inner flap, a give, the particular give of cardboard that has been opened more times than a gala photograph from twenty-two years ago has any business being opened.

She files the wrongness. She does not yet have anywhere to put it.

She takes the box to the floor, sits cross-legged like a much younger and more flexible woman, and begins.

* * *

"You know there's a light."

Goldie does not startle. She has not startled since 2011, when a man she was identifying off a frozen frame walked, in the flesh, past the café window where she was sitting, and her whole nervous system used up its lifetime allocation of fright in one go. So she does not startle. She simply goes still, the way she went still at the counter on the first morning, and turns the head-torch — slowly, so as not to blind him — onto Sai Okafor.

Twenty-four. She had him in full the first day: the lanyard worn high and tight like a man who is hoping the lanyard will do some of the talking for him; the trainers that cost more than the job; the careful, exhausted set of a person who came here to manage a leisure centre and has been quietly informed he will instead be switching one off. He has the duty-manager torch, the proper one, a black rubberised brick, and he is holding it the way you hold a thing you are not sure you are allowed to be using.

"There's a light," he says again, in case the first time was a fluke, and reaches past her and flips a switch, and a single fluorescent tube two ranks over comes on with the reluctance of something woken on a school night. It does almost nothing. It improves the dark in the way that a single sad balloon improves a hospital ward.

"There's a light," Goldie agrees. "It's a tremendous light. You should put it on the brochure."

"You're not meant to be down here."

"No," she says. "I'm absolutely not."

She had, briefly, considered lying. She has, in fifteen years, lied to police officers, barristers, a deputy assistant commissioner, and her own husband, and she could lie to a twenty-four-year-old in nice trainers without breaking the rhythm of her breathing. But there is a photograph in her lap, half-pulled from a brown envelope, and there is a boy somewhere across town sleeping with a stranger's eyes in his head, and Goldie discovers, with the small surprise of a woman finding a fiver in last winter's coat, that she does not want to.

"I'm looking for someone," she says.

Sai looks at her. He looks at the boxes. He looks at the £4.50 head-torch, which she has, she realises, left pointed roughly at his sternum, so that he is being interrogated by a woman in a budget mining costume, and something in his face — the something that came down here at half ten at night himself, with the proper torch, for reasons she suspects are not on his job description either — decides to let it go.

"There's no one down here," he says. "It's just boxes."

"It's just faces," she says. "Same thing, mostly. Can you work that?" She nods at the dead computer terminal in the corner, a beige tower from a previous geological era, its monitor furred grey with dust. "Because I can't, and the girl I'm after has a name in a book somewhere, and the book is on that, and that hates me."

Sai considers the terminal with the weariness of a man who has been introduced to all of the Pelican's machines and has come off worse against each of them in turn.

"That's the membership archive," he says. "Goes back to about ninety-eight. It's on a server in the boiler room that should be in a museum, or a skip." A pause. "Mostly a skip." Another pause, longer, the pause of a young man recalculating his evening. "Who are you after?"

"I don't know her name yet," says Goldie. "That's rather the issue." She lifts the photograph the rest of the way out of the envelope. "But I'll know her when I see her."

* * *

She finds her at eleven minutes past eleven, and the world does the small tilt again, the one from the counter, the floor remembering it has the option.

It is a gala photo. SWIM GALA, the back says, in the careful round hand of someone for whom labelling was a sacrament, and then a date, spring 2003, and below it a list of names in the order of the rows, the way they always did it, *back row left to right,* a roll-call of a town's children frozen mid-grin in the second before the flash. Forty-some kids in caps and team costumes ranked on the tiled steps of the indoor pool, the very steps Goldie walks past every day, the bunting strung above them then exactly as it is strung now, except in 2003 the bunting was new and the render was white and the building was still a thing that won.

And in the third row, fourth from the left, a girl of sixteen is looking at the camera with eyes Goldie has held in her attention twice this week.

She does not need to compare. The comparison happens to her, instantaneous, involuntary, the two images snapping together behind her eyes with the soft heavy click of a drawer she did not open and cannot close. The set of them — the exact width, the slight downward cant at the outer corner that should read sad and instead reads patient, the heaviness of the upper lid that makes a face look always like it is about to forgive you. Rhodri's eyes. *Not his.* Hers. And not only the eyes. The cheekbones — there, in the original, both halves still in one face, the high architecture of them that the Pryce-Pugh twins now wear split between two skulls, one each, as if the girl in the photograph had been divided like an estate. Goldie has spent three days telling herself she could not possibly be projecting a face onto two siblings symmetrically, one cheekbone to each, because that is not how projection works, that is not how anything works.

Here is the proof. One girl. The whole face. The face she has been finding in pieces, intact, sixteen years old, alive enough in silver halide to be annoyed about it.

"Found her," says Goldie, and her voice is exactly where she keeps it, in the dry middle register where nothing can get a grip, and Sai — who has been gamely losing a wrestling match with the membership server for forty minutes and has reached the stage of typing *PASSWORD* into things — comes over and crouches and looks where her finger isn't quite touching, because you don't touch them, the oils on a finger are a slow murder of an old print.

"Which one?"

"Third row. Fourth in."

Sai looks. Goldie watches him look, and watches the particular thing happen that she has watched happen on a hundred faces over a counter — the moment a person sees what she is pointing at and gets *almost* there, gets a shadow of it, the brow tightening with a recognition that has nowhere to land.

"She looks like—" He stops. He tilts his head. "I dunno. She looks like half the kids out there. Doesn't she? They all look a bit—" He gestures at the air, at the bay, at the great intermarried tangle of a town where everyone is a Pryce or a Pugh and the trick is which. "Round here everyone looks like everyone. My nan says you can't throw a chip without hitting a cousin."

"Your nan's right," says Goldie, and means it more than he knows, more than she yet knows herself, the sentence going down into her like a stone into a well she has not finished measuring. "But not this. This isn't a family resemblance. This is the same face." She sits back on her heels. The torch swings its small bad light across forty dead-and-living children. "I've seen those eyes twice this week, on a boy of nineteen and on nobody, and those cheekbones split across two girls of twenty, and they're all here, in one girl, in two thousand and three, and that girl is—" she checks the back of the photo, the careful sacramental hand, runs her finger down the roll-call to the third row, fourth name in "—oh."

"What?"

"There's a name."

* * *

The Mermaid Café, the next morning, is doing the thing the Mermaid Café does, which is smell like the single best decision and the single worst decision a person could make in the same building. The bacon, Dil was not wrong, is holy — it comes to the pass in a fug of itself, a smell with a halo, a smell you could navigate home by. The coffee is the colour of the Pelican and a sermon about sin. Goldie has learned the knack Dil taught her, which is to not taste it but simply decide it has happened to you, and she has decided it has happened to her, and she is sitting in the corner with a roll the size of a fist and a name in her notebook she has not said out loud to anyone, waiting, because she has learned in three days that in this town you do not chase a fact. You sit very still near the bacon and the fact comes to you, carried in the warm irrigation of other people's mouths.

It comes carried in Carwyn's.

Carwyn runs the Mermaid the way a lighthouse keeper runs a lighthouse, alone and with grievance, a big slab of a man with forearms like a butcher and an apron that has survived things. He is talking to the bacon, to the griddle, to the back of Dil's head, to the general air, in the seamless low monologue of a man who has not stopped talking since approximately 1994 and sees no reason to start.

"—because I said to her, I said, that's not a sausage roll, Glenys, that's an *artisan* item, you can't be charging the over-sixties artisan for a sausage roll, they'll have my windows—"

Goldie waits. She turns her notebook so the name faces up. *Pryce, C., third row.* She does the thing she came to do, which is the only honest thing she has ever known how to do, the sideways thing, the thing that is the precise opposite of a database and a badge: she sets the bait and waits for the town to walk into it.

"Carwyn," she says, into a gap. "You've been here a while."

"I've been here," says Carwyn, with feeling, "since the *flood.*" He does not specify which flood. There is the strong implication that there has only ever been the one, and that it was personal.

"Did you know the Pryces?"

It is, in Aberglas, a question of almost no information at all, like asking a man on a beach whether he has met any sand. Carwyn snorts. "Did I know the Pryces. There's three hundred Pryces, bach, you'll have to—"

"This one." She slides the notebook nowhere; she just says it. "Carys. Carys Pryce."

And the griddle does not stop. The bacon does not stop. But Carwyn stops, for half a second, the spatula going still over the pan, and Dil — at the next table, mid-sentence with one of the Bevan sisters about the bins — Dil stops too, and Goldie, who reads stillnesses the way she reads earlobes, watches two people who have known each other forty years arrive at the same buried place at the same moment without either of them moving toward it.

"Now there's a name I've not had over the counter in a while," says Carwyn, and starts the spatula again, deliberately, the way you start whistling. "Carys Pryce. Duw. Where've you had that, then?"

"An old gala photo," says Goldie, which is true, and offers nothing, which is the skill.

"She'll be in plenty of them," says Dil, leaning into it now, turning her whole warm bulk toward the conversation the way she turns toward all conversations, magnetised. But there is a softness gone into her face that wasn't there for the bins, a thing she carries the soft human way, wrong on the date and right on the wound. "Lovely swimmer, Carys. County, nearly. Nerys's girl — Nerys did the hair, you'll not have met her, she shut the salon, oh, years back, gone right into herself she has, you can't get a *bore da* out of her now and you used to not be able to stop her." Dil shakes her head. "Broke her mam's heart, that whole business. You never get over a thing like that. I don't think Nerys ever has, no, not really, no."

"A thing like what," says Goldie, gently, gently, the way you'd put your hand flat to a nervous dog.

"Ran off," says Carwyn, to the bacon. "Didn't she."

"Ran off," Dil agrees, and there it is, laid down between them like a coat over a puddle, the cover story, the shape the town poured itself into twenty-two years ago and let set. "Spring it was. Year of— oh, when was it. Sent to an aunt's, they said, Carmarthen way, and then she never— she just didn't come home. Sixteen. Boy-mad, they reckoned, or she'd had a row, or— you know how it is at that age, you think the world's somewhere else and it's only ever the next town and a worse boy. Police didn't make much of it, mind. She'd gone off before, see, the once. So they thought—" Dil makes a small gesture, a *you-know*, a whole institution's shrug compressed into a flick of the wrist. "Well. They thought she'd turn up. And she never did. Years and years. They had to— there was a thing, eventually, a court thing, declared her, you know." Her voice drops on the word the way voices drop in a chapel. "*Dead.* Couldn't have a funeral, of course. No—" the smallest pause "—no nothing to have it over. Nerys put a little notice in the paper. That was all there ever was of her. A notice in the paper and a name on a list of people who didn't come home."

The café holds it for a moment, the bacon hissing into the silence.

Then one of the Bevan sisters says, "Is that my tea, Carwyn, or has *that* gone to an aunt's an' all," and the spell breaks, and Carwyn says something unrepeatable about the Bevan sisters' tea, and Dil is off again, restored, narrating the bins, and the warm flow closes back over the place where Carys Pryce surfaced for thirty seconds and went under.

Goldie sits with her roll and does not eat it.

* * *

She finds Sai by the lido — the dead one, the outdoor one, leaning on the rail above the drained basin where the leaves and the rainwater and the ambition have collected into a dark soup at the deep end. He has a clipboard. He is meant to be counting something. He is, in fact, looking at the empty pool the way she has caught him looking at it twice now, a man visiting a thing before it goes.

"Carys Pryce," she says, coming up beside him, not bothering with hello. "Sixteen. Vanished, spring two thousand and three. Sent to an aunt's that I'd put a week's wages didn't exist, never came home, written off as a runaway because she'd bolted once before so the file went thin. Declared dead by a court, years on. No body. A notice in the paper." She puts both forearms on the cold rail beside his. "Twenty-two years ago."

Sai does the arithmetic before she has to make him, which is one of the reasons, she is starting to understand, that she has not lied to him. "Twenty-two years," he says, slowly. "So if she had a kid—"

"Stop there," says Goldie, "because that's the part that's wrong, and I want you to feel exactly how wrong it is, because I've been feeling it since eleven minutes past eleven last night and I'd like the company." She turns the notebook to him, though there is nothing on it now but a name and a row. "Rhodri's nineteen. The twins are twenty. The eyes I saw on Rhodri are Carys's eyes — not like Carys's, *Carys's*, the same build to the millimetre, the same cant, the same lid. The cheekbones split across those two girls are Carys's cheekbones, one half each, which is a thing a face does when it's *handed down*, not when it's copied. These are her features, Sai. Hers. On the bodies of children."

"Right," says Sai, carefully, the way you agree with someone you are not certain is well.

"And Carys vanished in two thousand and three." Goldie lets it sit. The gulls do something raucous over the harbour. Somewhere behind them, through the building, the indoor pool slaps and echoes and a whistle goes, Huw's whistle, three short, *walk don't run.* "Rhodri was born in two thousand and six. The twins, two thousand and five. They were not *alive* when she disappeared. They never met her. Nobody under twenty in this town ever laid eyes on Carys Pryce, because she was gone three years before the eldest of them drew breath."

She watches him get it. She watches the clipboard lower an inch.

"So how," she says, and her voice does not rise, because the thing about the real ones is they never need you to raise your voice, "does a girl who vanished in two thousand and three — who never came home, who's a name on a list and a notice in a paper, who's been legally dead for a decade — walk around this building, today, wearing the faces of children who were born after she was gone?"

Sai is quiet for a long moment. The leaves stir in the dead pool.

"Maybe you're wrong," he says, and he says it kindly, and he says it as the only mercy on offer, the door she keeps for everyone and never walks through herself. "About the eyes. Maybe it's just— like I said. Round here everyone looks like—"

"I'm not wrong about whether I've seen a face before," says Goldie. "That's the one thing I have never once been wrong about in my entire life." It is true. It is so true it is almost the whole of her. It is the thing she has built a life on and the thing she hides behind, and standing at the rail of a dead pool she does not yet know which it is, only that it is load-bearing and that it is holding. "I might be wrong about what it *means.* God knows I might be wrong about what it means. But I have seen that girl's eyes, twice, this week, alive. And I'd like to know how a dead girl keeps her appointments."

The gulls wheel. Sai turns the clipboard over in his hands.

"I came here," Goldie says, more to the leaves than to him, "for a job where no face would ever cost me anything." She watches the dark water that isn't water. "Three days."

"You don't have to do anything," Sai offers. "About it. Do you? You could just— it's a sad old story. Girl ran off, years ago. People look like their cousins. You could just—" he shrugs, the whole town's shrug, the flick that buried a girl "—leave it where it is."

"I could," Goldie agrees.

She means it the way she means most things she says about herself — exactly true on the surface and a lie all the way down, the way she meant *filing,* the way she meant *the coffee,* the way she means the small torch in her pocket she is keeping as a hostage against a darkness she has already decided to go back into. She will look into it just enough to stop seeing the eyes. That is the whole of the plan. Just enough to lay the face down and close the drawer and get back to a counter where nothing matters, which is the only place she has been able to breathe for twelve years, and she believes this plan completely, with the clean total belief of a woman who has never once in her life managed to un-see a single thing.

Across the leaf-dark basin, on the far wall, the old painted depth markings are still legible under the grime: 6FT. 8FT. And at the deep end, where the soup has gathered, faded but holding, the words DEEP END NO DIVING, and under them, smaller, in a hand that has outlasted the swimmers and the summers and the girl in the third row, the careful sacramental lettering of someone for whom even a warning was a thing worth doing properly.

Goldie looks at it a while.

Then she pushes off the rail, and goes back in, to the warmth and the chlorine and the echo, to find out how deep it goes.

---

# CHAPTER 4 — Sister Branwen

"Settling in fine," Goldie says into the phone, which is the second lie she has told before nine o'clock, and she isn't counting the coffee.

"You sound like you're settling in fine," Tom says, in the careful voice he uses when he doesn't believe a word and has decided, after twenty years, that it isn't his to disbelieve any more. There is a clatter behind him — a spoon, a bowl, the particular morning sounds of a man who got the kitchen in the divorce and uses it. "Sea air. Quiet job. Match any goggles to any toddlers yet?"

"One goggle. Half a goggle. The toddler's at large."

"There's my girl," he says, and the fondness in it lands somewhere under her ribs and she lets it, briefly, the way you let a draught in if the room's stuffy and then you shut the window. He asks is she eating. She says yes. That's three. "Talk soon, Goldie." "Talk soon, Tom." And the line goes, and she is alone again behind the counter with the boiler doing its slow disappointment and the morning coming through the swing doors in chlorinated waves, and the boy with the borrowed eyes is somewhere out there hauling a float net, alive as Tuesday, and she has spent four days now not looking at him and failing.

She has a name for the eyes. That is the trouble with a name. A face is just weather until you name it, and then it's a country, and you have to live there.

*Carys Pryce.* The runaway. Broke her mam's heart. Twenty-two years.

You don't, she has discovered, get to half-investigate a thing. You think you can — you tell yourself you'll just pull one thread, just enough to stop the seeing, snip it neat and walk away — and then the thread is attached to a jumper and the jumper's attached to a town, and here you are at the counter at nine in the morning, half-ashamed, asking Dil who'd know about a girl who vanished before the millennium was properly warm.

"Who'd *know*," says Dil, as if Goldie has asked who'd know about water. She is restocking the lost-property tags, licking her thumb between each one with the unhurried sacrament of a woman who has all day and intends to spend it here. "Everyone knew Carys. Everyone our age. It was a *thing*, love, you don't forget a thing like that, a girl just — gone." She snaps her fingers, soft. "But who'd *know* know." She considers it the way she considers a difficult crossword. "Branwen."

"Branwen."

"Sister Branwen, we called her, though she was never a nun, it was just the way she — she had that *manner*. Bethan Maddox, really. Midwife. She brought half this town into the world, that woman, and laid out the other half, I shouldn't wonder, she did everything in those days, the births and the deaths and the bits in between nobody talks about." Another tag, another thumb. "She knew all the families. Knew which babies were whose before the mothers had decided to admit it. If anyone knew what became of that girl—" Dil stops. Something moves over her face, the small shadow of a woman remembering that this is not, after all, a crossword. "She's in St. Cadog's now. The hospice. It's the cancer." She says *the cancer* the way the Welsh say *the cancer*, with the definite article, as if it were a person who'd moved in. "She hasn't got long."

"Would she see me?"

"She sees Pam on Thursdays for the rosary and she's not even Catholic, Pam, she just likes the wafers." Dil licks the last tag. "She'll see anyone, love. It's the not-long. You get curious, at the not-long. You start wanting to put things down before you go." She presses the tag flat. "I'd take her some of the lemon ones from the Mermaid, mind. Not the coffee. You don't send a dying woman the coffee. There's cruelty in it."

* * *

St. Cadog's is a low pebble-dashed building on the road out, with a car park the council has lined for many more cars than ever come, and a smell when you go in that is the exact opposite of the Pelican's — not chlorine and chips and old grandeur but lavender and antiseptic and, underneath, faint and patient, the thing the lavender is for. Goldie clocks the woman on the desk in a quarter-second and files her without effort — agency, not staff, drives in from Lampeter, two children, a wedding ring worn on the wrong hand because the right one's got eczema — and hates, briefly and uselessly, that she cannot walk into a building full of the dying without taking everyone's inventory at the door. There is no off switch. There never has been. You'd think a hospice would be the one place a person could stop seeing, and it is the one place you can't.

Branwen is in a chair by the window, not the bed, which Goldie reads at once as a decision rather than a stage of recovery — *I will be sat up, thank you, I will meet things with my spine* — and she is small the way the very ill go small, folded down to her essentials, and her face is a wreck of fine lines over very good bones, and her eyes, when they come up off her lap and find Goldie in the doorway, are the most awake eyes Goldie has seen since she came to this town.

She files nothing off them. That's the first surprise. Goldie reads everyone — the structure arrives whether she wants it or not, the set and the cant and the architecture — but what she gets off Branwen's face is not data, it's *attention*, the woman's and her own, the way two professionals clock each other across a room and each know the other knows. Branwen looks at Goldie the way Goldie looks at everyone. It is, for once, mutual, and it is deeply uncomfortable, and Goldie understands in that second exactly what it must be like to be on her side of the counter.

"You're the new one," Branwen says. Her voice is a thread but it's a strong thread. "At the Pelican. The English one."

"Cardiff."

"That's worse." A flicker that might once have been a smile, on a face that has retired most of its expressions to save the energy. "Sit, then. You've brought me something. They always bring me something now, as if I'm to be fattened for it." But she takes the paper bag, and looks in, and something in her settles. "Lemon. Good girl. The ones from the Mermaid?"

"Carwyn's. I was told not to send the coffee."

"There's wisdom in this town yet." She sets the bag on the windowsill, unopened, the way you save a thing. Out the window is a strip of car park and then the gorse and then, grey on grey, the sea. "Sit down, I said. You're making the door look untidy."

Goldie sits. She has rehearsed nothing, which is unusual for her; she rehearses everything, she walks into rooms with the conversation already filed. But there's no filing a woman like this. So she does the thing she is worst at, the thing the whole job at the desk was meant to spare her: she tells a little of the truth.

"I keep seeing a face," Goldie says. "At the pool. On the young ones. It's an old face on young people and it shouldn't be there and I can't make it stop, and I've put a name to it now, which was stupid, because now I can't leave it. I was told you'd know the families. I was told you'd know—" She makes herself say it. "—Carys Pryce."

The name goes into the room and the room changes.

It is not dramatic. Branwen does not gasp, or clutch the chair, or do any of the things the body does in stories. She goes *still* — a particular, total, professional stillness, the stillness of a woman who has spent fifty years keeping her face shut in front of frightened girls and frightened families, holding the worst news behind her teeth until the moment came to give it — and her awake eyes go somewhere far off and come back, and Goldie, who reads faces for a living and broke herself doing it, watches a door close behind a person's face in real time and cannot, for the life of her, read what's on the other side of it.

Only that there *is* an other side. That is the whole of what she gets. Branwen Maddox knows something, and is, this minute, in this chair, by this window, *deciding what to do with it.*

"Carys Pryce," Branwen says, at last, and it comes out flat and even and terribly careful, a woman walking on a floor she knows the weak boards of. "Now there's a name I've not been asked in a long while."

"You knew her."

"I knew everyone. I told you. I knew everyone." She looks at Goldie a moment, and there's calculation in it, and something that might be the beginning of hunger — the not-long hunger, the wanting to put things down. Then she looks back out at the sea. "She ran away. That's the story. You'll have had the story."

"I've had the story."

"And you don't believe the story, or you wouldn't be sat here making my door untidy." It isn't a question. Branwen's hands move in her lap, two thin birds settling. "You've got that look. I've seen it. The look of someone who's found a thread." She turns her head, and the awake eyes pin Goldie, and for a moment the dying woman is the one taking inventory. "What is it you do, exactly, at a lost-property desk, that has you driving out to bother an old woman about a girl who's been gone twenty-two years?"

"I notice things," Goldie says. "It's a sort of memory. For faces. I see one once and I keep it." She has never, in this town, said it this plainly. She doesn't know why she's saying it now, except that lying to this woman feels like a thing you'd be punished for. "I've a memory for faces I never asked for and can't get shot of, and it's showing me a dead girl's face on living children, and I came here to ask a woman who'd know whether I'm mad."

Branwen is quiet for a long moment. The sea does nothing. The lavender does its patient work.

"You're not mad," she says.

And then, before Goldie can move on that — before she can do anything but sit very still herself, the way you go still when a wild thing comes close and you don't want to be the reason it bolts — Branwen says the rest of it. Quietly. Looking at the sea, not at Goldie, the way you'd say a thing you'd decided long ago and were only now letting out for air:

"Some girls don't run."

The room holds it.

Goldie waits. She is good at waiting; the desk taught her that, or rather the desk let her stop pretending she'd ever been anything else. She waits, and the silence stretches, and she watches the moment hang there, the moment where Branwen Maddox could say the next sentence, the one with the weight in it, and Goldie can *feel* the next sentence in the room the way you feel weather coming, and—

—and Branwen closes. Just like that. The door that had drifted an inch ajar swings shut, and the awake eyes go ordinary, go tired, go *old*, and the woman in the chair is just a dying woman by a window with an unopened bag of lemon biscuits and forty years of other people's secrets folded down small inside her, and whatever she was deciding, she has, for now, decided the other way.

"I'm tired," Branwen says, which Goldie does not believe for one second, because she's said it herself ten thousand times and it has never once been true. "You'll forgive me. They like me to rest."

"Of course." Goldie stands. The door, untidied, releases her. At the threshold she turns, because she can't not — because there's a thread now and she's holding it and she has never in her life been able to put a thread down. "You worked with them, didn't you. The Trust."

It is a guess, dressed as a fact. Dil said *the births and the deaths and the bits nobody talks about.* Goldie said *the Trust* the way you put a card on the table face down and watch the other player's hands.

Branwen's hands don't move. That's the tell. They go *deliberately* still, two birds holding their breath, and the stillness is the answer.

"The St. Non's Trust," Branwen says, and the saying of the name costs her something visible, some small toll paid at the gate of it. "We did good work. Whatever you've come to think, sat behind your desk noticing things — we did good work. We helped girls who had nobody. That was the *whole* of it." A breath. "It was a long time ago. They were kind."

It is the second time in two minutes the woman has said a true thing in a way that makes Goldie's skin go cold, and the cold has nothing to do with the lavender or the sea. *We helped girls who had nobody.* Said like a defence. Said like a thing you'd been rehearsing, alone, in a chair, for the day someone finally drove out to ask.

"Thank you for the time," Goldie says.

"Take the look off your face before you frighten my nurse," says Branwen, and shuts her eyes, and is, very deliberately, asleep.

* * *

She drives back along the gorse road with *some girls don't run* sitting in the passenger seat like a hitchhiker she shouldn't have picked up, and by the time she's back at the Pelican she has nearly talked herself into the comfortable version — that it's an old woman's riddle, that the dying say strange things, that *some girls don't run* could mean a hundred soft sentimental nothings and she is, as ever, hearing a code where there's only grief.

She has nearly got there. She's parked, even. She's reaching for the door.

And then the upstairs of the building intervenes, the way the Pelican is always intervening, refusing to let her have the comfortable version of anything.

* * *

The function rooms are on the first floor, up a sweep of stairs that was clearly built for women in good frocks to descend slowly while a band played, and that now smells of damp and is hung with a banner reading SAVE THE PELICAN in letters cut by someone who ran out of green felt halfway through the second word. The grandeur is all still there — the high coved ceiling with its plaster wreaths, the tall windows looking out at the dead lido, a parquet floor that must have been glorious — and over the grandeur, like a tarpaulin over a chandelier, is the present use of the place, which is bingo, and storage, and the storage of bingo.

Goldie comes up because Sai texted that the 2003 archive boxes she's been pestering about are "probably in the cabbage room," which turns out to mean the room with the green walls, and she is threading between stacked trestle tables and a tower of orange stacking chairs and a bingo machine under a tea towel like a parrot put to bed, when the side door opens and a delegation of the Save the Pelican committee comes through it mid-meeting, and at the head of it, in full churchwarden sail, is a woman Goldie has never seen in her life and recognises instantly, completely, and wrongly.

She is seventy-three. Goldie has her at once — the hands give it, the neck, the particular upholstered solidity of a woman who has chaired things for forty years and won — and she is dressed for committee in a blouse with a brooch that means business, and she is saying, to the room, in the carrying voice of the constitutionally important, "—well, *I* shall be writing to the council, because if *we* don't, who—"

And she turns her head to find the better light, the unconscious vanity of a woman who has always known her good side, and the high window catches her, and there it is.

The brow.

Goldie's whole certainty staggers like a man missing a stair in the dark.

It is *Carys's brow.* Not like it. Not a cousin of it. It is the exact high, surprised arch she has spent four days seeing in fragments and three nights seeing whole — the same clean lift at the inner end, the same fine taper out toward the temple, the same small architectural miracle of a brow that makes a face look perpetually about to ask a delighted question. She knows it to the millimetre. She held it in the 2003 gala photo. She has held it on the living children. And it is sitting, now, in the wide pleasant pouchy seventy-three-year-old face of a churchwarden with a brooch, over a pair of eyes that are nothing, nothing at all, ordinary brown eyes that have never been Carys's and never will be, set in skin gone soft and creped with seven decades that Carys Pryce, sixteen forever, never got and never will.

The brow is exact. The face under it is seventy-three.

And Goldie stands very still among the stacked chairs with the parrot-machine sleeping under its towel and feels the floor of her certainty do the thing it has not done since the bad year, the thing she came to this counter precisely so it would never do again: she feels it *not hold.*

Because a seventy-three-year-old woman cannot be a sixteen-year-old's daughter. Cannot be her granddaughter, her cousin, her anything-by-blood. The arithmetic is a wall and there's no climbing it. Carys Pryce vanished twenty-two years ago at sixteen. This woman was already past fifty when it happened. There is no line you can draw on any family tree in any town in the world that puts Carys's brow on this face by inheritance.

And yet there it is. Exact. Worn by a stranger who is too old, by decades, to wear it.

"—Olwen, *cariad*, are you stopping or going, you're holding the door—" someone calls from the stairs, and the churchwarden gathers herself with a little affronted swish, *I am chairing this, thank you,* and sails on past Goldie without a glance, because nobody glances at the woman by the chairs, that is the whole point of the woman by the chairs, and the brow — Carys's brow, on a body Carys never lived to grow — goes out the door and down the glorious staircase to write a strongly worded letter to the council.

Goldie stands in the cabbage room a long time after they've gone.

She tells herself it's a coincidence. Brows are brows; there are only so many; a high arch is hardly rare; she is tired, she is in a strange town, she has a name in her head now and a name makes you see its owner in clouds and toast. She tells herself she is doing the exact thing she swore off doing, the thing that broke her — building certainty out of a partial pattern, hanging a whole story on the architecture of a single feature, the way she once hung a man's whole life on the structure of a jaw and was, it turned out, wrong, catastrophically wrong, nearly-an-innocent-man wrong. She tells herself the brow means nothing.
