She does not believe one word of it, and she knows she doesn't, instantly, against her will, and without the smallest idea what it means — only that it *means*, that a seventy-three-year-old churchwarden is wearing a dead girl's brow and there is no version of the world she has been building in which that is allowed, and the version of the world she has been building has just developed a crack she cannot un-see, running straight through the middle of it, neat as a part in the hair.

The brow on the children, she could explain. The brow she was almost ready to explain. The brow on the old woman explains nothing, and breaks everything, and she hates it, and she stands among the sleeping bingo chairs hating it, because she came to this town to stop the seeing and the seeing has just shown her a thing she would give a great deal to have not seen, which is that whatever is happening to Carys Pryce's face, it is happening in two directions at once.

Downstairs, distantly, the boiler resumes its slow disappointment.

Outside the tall windows, the dead lido fills, patiently, with rain, and *some girls don't run* settles into the seat beside her certainty and stays for the duration.

---

> **FACE 0004** — female, 73, churchwarden (OLWEN). Function rooms, committee in full sail. Returns nothing; claims nothing; chairs everything.
> *The brow — the lift, the taper, the inner arch — exact. Carys's, to the millimetre.*
> But the face under it is seventy-three. The eyes, ordinary; never hers.
> A 73-yr-old cannot be a 16-yr-old's blood. The arithmetic won't bend.
> So how is the brow here. The brow can't be inherited — wrong by fifty years — and yet.

---

# CHAPTER 5 — The Closure Notice

The notice goes up on a Tuesday, taped to the inside of the glass doors with the same brown parcel tape that holds the building together everywhere else, and Goldie reads it on her way in the way she reads everything, against her will, before she has decided to.

*PUBLIC NOTICE. Ceredigion County Council. Re: The Pelican Leisure Centre. The Centre will CEASE OPERATION on a date to be confirmed within the current financial year, pending review of the council's leisure provision. A public consultation will be held —*

There is more, in the dead grey language councils use when they are killing something they would rather not be seen to kill, and Goldie does not need the rest of it. She has read enough closures in her life — units, stations, whole departments — to know the font of a thing already decided. The consultation is not a question. It is the receipt.

Behind the glass, Dil is watching her read it through her own reflection, arms folded under her bust, lanyard hanging its little dead dog. When Goldie comes through the door Dil says, before good morning, before anything, "Forty-one years I've been on that reception," in a voice gone flat and strange, "and they put it up with *tape.*"

"It's always tape," says Goldie.

"They could have *posted* it."

"They never post the ones that matter."

Dil looks at her then, a small sharp look, the look of a woman recalibrating an opinion mid-sentence. "No," she says. "No, they don't, do they." And she turns and goes to put the kettle on, and Goldie hears her blow her nose once, hard, in the back office, and then come out again with her face arranged, because Dil is the sort of woman who would rather die than be seen, which is one of about four things Goldie has ever genuinely liked about another person.

* * *

The meeting is Thursday, the function room, half past seven, *ALL WELCOME* in Dil's looping marker on a sheet of A4 that has been photocopied so many times the letters have started to break up at the edges, like a face on bad footage, like everything Goldie ever spent fifteen years trying to make hold.

She does not want to go.

She has spent four days not-wanting various things with great discipline — not wanting to think about the boy's borrowed eyes, not wanting to make Olwen Tudor's eyebrow fit a pattern it refuses to fit, not wanting to hear *some girls don't run* in a dying woman's flat voice every time the building goes quiet — and she has been failing at all of it, steadily, the way you fail at not-thinking, which is to say completely and in the dark and alone at the counter at four in the afternoon with a single fogged goggle in her hand.

She is going to skip the meeting. She has decided. She tells Tom this on the phone Wednesday night, because Tom is the one person she still says true things to, partly because he is six years and a hundred and ninety miles away and cannot do anything about them.

"There's a town meeting about the place closing," she says. "I'm not going."

"You're going," says Tom.

"I'm the lost-property woman. I've been here a fortnight."

"You're going," says Tom again, fond and exhausted, the way he says most things to her now, a man reading the weather off a barometer he's owned thirty years. "You always go to the thing you say you're not going to. You went to my mother's. You went to that retirement do for the man you said you hated."

"I did hate him."

"You cried."

"I did not cry, I had a thing in my eye, it was a function room, they're full of things." She switches the phone to the other ear. Out the window the lido sits black and full of leaves under a sky going the colour of a bruise. "I'm not going, Tom."

"Marigold," he says, gently, "you don't go to be part of it. You go to *watch.* You've never in your life been able to not-watch." A pause, the old pause, the one with a closed door in it. "It's the only thing you let yourself have."

She doesn't answer that. After a while he says he has to go, the kettle, the boiler, his own quiet Cardiff evening, and they hang up the way they always do now, kindly, like two people returning a thing to lost property that neither of them has the heart to throw away.

She goes to the meeting.

* * *

The function room, upstairs, is where the Pelican keeps its grandeur the way some old men keep a good suit — folded, faded, smelling faintly of the last good occasion. Once, the postcards say, there were chandeliers. Now there is a drop ceiling with two tiles missing, a parquet floor scuffed pale in the shape of forty years of stacked chairs, and a hatch through to a kitchen that hasn't done a wedding since the pound coin was new. Along the back wall, in towers, the bingo equipment lives under dust sheets — the cage, the board, the little numbered balls — and Goldie clocks, because she cannot not, that the room has been set out by someone who has set out a great many rooms: chairs in arcs, an aisle, a top table with a jug of water and four glasses and a microphone that will not work.

It is, by twenty-five past seven, full.

She had not expected full. She had expected the dozen damp souls who attend the death of anything. But the whole town has come up the stairs — coats still on, the warm wet-wool smell of a wet country gathering indoors — and they are loud, and they are arguing already, and there is a queue at the urn, and Goldie stands at the back by the bingo sheets and does the only thing she has ever known how to do in a crowd, which is read it.

The Bevan sisters, front row, dressed for grievance. Dai Post, who lost his wife to a postcode, alone three seats from anyone, which is where he always is now. Carwyn from the café, apron still on, smelling of the holy bacon, looking like a man at a funeral for a relative he'd quarrelled with. Huw the lifeguard, vast and soft and already wet around the eyes though nothing has happened yet, because Huw cries at the *idea* of things. And the Belles — the Aqua Belles, in mufti, in cardigans, which is somehow more alarming than the swim caps — taking up an entire row near the front with the territorial calm of women who have sat in better seats than you will ever be offered.

Maureen is in the middle of them like a keel.

"They'll not get a word in," Pam is saying, leaning across two people to be heard, "not a *word*, I'm telling you, it's all signed and sealed, my cousin's girl works in planning and she says — well, she can't say, can she, but you can tell, by *how* she can't say —"

"Pam," says Maureen, without turning her head.

"— it's the developers, see, it's always the developers, they want the *frontage*, it's a hundred yards of seafront, you don't think they want a *swimming pool* there, do you, they want flats, they want flats for people from *away* —"

"*Pam.*"

Pam subsides. Glenys, on Maureen's good side, leans the other way, the deaf side toward the room, and says brightly to no one, "Is it about the *dog?*"

"The lido, Glenys," says Maureen. "The *lido.*"

"That's what I said. Has Lido been handed in? Only Maureen said there was a meeting about Lido and I've been worried *sick*, she's a little Westie, she belongs to the woman in the bungalows, she got out at the —"

"Not Lido the *dog*, Glenys, the *lido*, the *pool*, the whole —" Maureen stops. Closes her eyes briefly, the way a woman does when she has explained the same thing for ninety years. "It's about the pool, love. Not the dog."

"Oh," says Glenys, visibly relieved and slightly disappointed, the dog and the building both saved and both lost in the same breath. "Well, that's all right then. I thought it was the dog."

And Goldie, at the back, with the dust sheets and the dead chandeliers and the whole damp loud aching town in front of her, feels her face do the thing it has not done in some time, the small involuntary upward thing, and has to look at the floor to make it stop.

* * *

The man who comes in at twenty-nine minutes past, who crosses the room to the top table the way a tide comes up a beach — unhurried, total, arriving everywhere at once — is the only face in the building Goldie cannot read.

She knows this the way she knows everything: immediately, and without being able to say why.

He is seventy-one, and that much she gets, the ordinary way, off the good clean ruin of him: a big, well-kept man gone soft and dignified at the edges, white hair barbered close, a fisherman's weathering on a face that has spent its years out of doors and in committee rooms in roughly equal measure. A good jaw, a little jowled. Hands that have done work and now do handshakes — she watches him do nine of them on his way to the front, each one different, each one *right*, the small bend toward the older woman, the two-handed clasp for Carwyn, the hand briefly to Huw's shoulder that stops the big man's lip wobbling cold. He is dressed like a man who knows a town is watching him and has decided, kindly, to make it easy: not too smart, not too plain, a good coat carried over the arm.

She has him in three seconds, the whole exterior of him, the way she has everyone.

And there is nothing underneath.

That is the only way she can put it to herself, and she distrusts it the instant it surfaces, because it is exactly the kind of thing the worn-out part of her brain serves up at the back of a function room — *a face with no give* — the kind of thing that means nothing, that is fatigue, that is the bad coffee and the strange town and a fortnight of borrowed eyes. Every face gives her something. Every face is a structure under tension, and the tension shows — at the eyes, at the mouth-corners, in the held breath of a man performing his own ease. She has read defendants who'd practised serenity in a cell for a year and still she got the held thing underneath, the small permanent clench of a person managing a face across time.

This man is not managing a face. He simply has one, and it is the one he is showing her, all the way down, smooth as a closed drawer.

Forty years of reading people, and the only blank in the room is the man at the front holding the microphone that does not work.

"Friends," he says — and the dead microphone whines and dies, and he sets it down without a flicker and lifts his unaided voice, which carries to the back like a man used to carrying it, "we'll do it the old way, shall we, the way we did before they gave us the toys." A warm ripple. "You can all hear me. You could hear me in Aberaeron."

"You can hear him in *Aberystwyth*," somebody calls, and the room laughs, the loose grateful laugh of frightened people glad of a man who isn't frightened, and Goldie watches the laugh travel the room and arrive at the top table and break against him and change nothing in his face at all.

"That's Gareth," says Dil, materialising at Goldie's elbow the way Dil materialises, on rails, out of the wall. "Gareth Hollis. He runs the place — well, *ran*, manager, has been donkey's years — and he's the Trust, and he's the council liaison, and he's the one keeping the whole thing breathing, between you and me." She says it the way you'd describe a saint, or a load-bearing wall. "Lovely man. Buried his wife two summers back and you'd never know it to look at him, never let it slow him down. *That's* a man."

"The Trust," says Goldie.

"St. Non's. The charity. They do — did — all the quiet good in this town, the Trust, christenings, the old folks, the — you know. The quiet things." Dil's hand flutters, gathering decades of unspecified mercy into a small vague gesture. "Gareth's been a trustee since the year dot. There's nothing in this town hasn't got his hand somewhere on it. He christened half the bay, near enough. Stood godfather to the other half."

Goldie watches the man at the front, who is now naming numbers — the council's numbers, the running cost, the deficit, the date that is coming — saying the killing arithmetic in a voice that makes it sound survivable, and she thinks: *his hand somewhere on it,* and files the phrase, the way she files everything, in a drawer she did not open and cannot close.

* * *

He is good. That is the thing. He is so good that she nearly lets herself be carried with the rest of them.

He does not lie to the room — she'd have caught a lie, even off a blank face; lies have a shape, a small recoil, the body declining its own sentence — and he does not lie. He tells them it is bad. He tells them the date is real, the money is real, the council's mind is mostly made up. And then he turns it, the way a strong swimmer turns at the wall, and gives them something to do — a Friends committee, a campaign, a consultation to fill with so many bodies the council cannot pretend the room was empty. He gives them Lido Ponty, up the valleys, six million pounds and shut for years and now open and full and beating targets nobody believed. He does not give them Cold Knap, demolished and filled in despite everything, and Goldie notes the omission with professional respect: he is too kind, and too clever, to give a frightened room the corpse.

"This building," he says, near the end, with one big hand laid flat on the top table as if on the back of an animal that might bolt, "has done the work of this town for ninety years. Married you. Buried you, after, downstairs, when the chapel was full. Taught your children to swim and your grandmothers to keep moving. Every christening, every gala —" and here, for a half-second, no longer, Goldie watches his eyes go to the floor between his feet, to the parquet, to the dark below it, and come back, "— every quiet kindness this town ever did, it did under this roof. And they want us to let it go for a *spreadsheet.*" He lets that sit. "Well. I've kept this place going through worse than a spreadsheet. I'm not minded to stop now."

The room comes apart with it — applause, the Bevan sisters on their feet, Huw frankly weeping, Pam shouting something about developers that nobody can hear — and in the middle of it Maureen rises, slow and total, like land appearing, and the noise drops away from her the way it does, and she says, in the voice that has run the bingo since before half the room was born:

"Right. We'll need a committee, and we'll need it tonight, before everyone goes home and forgets they care. Gareth, you'll chair the campaign, you've the council's ear and the good suit. Dil's secretary, she's the only one who can read her own writing. Huw, you'll stop crying and do the heavy. The Belles'll do the funds — a swim, a raffle, a do — and Pam, you can do the leaflets, it'll keep you off the developers." Pam opens her mouth. Maureen does not look at her, and the mouth closes. "And we'll want someone for the *records.*"

There is a small silence, the silence of a committee discovering a hole in itself.

"The what?" says Carwyn.

"The records. The history. The archive, downstairs, the boxes — the galas, the photos, all of it. If we're saving the place we want the *story* of the place, don't we, for the council, for the papers, for the — for the case. Someone to go through it and make it tell." Maureen's eye, having travelled the whole room with the unhurried menace of a lighthouse, arrives, without surprise, without mercy, on the woman at the back by the dust sheets. "You. The new one. Marigold."

"Goldie," says Goldie, faintly.

"You were *police*, Dil says. You'll know how to make a box of old photos say something. You'll do the records."

It is not a question. Nothing Maureen says is a question. And Goldie — who came to this town to be no use to anyone, who chose the one counter in Wales where no face would ever cost her a thing, who has spent four days losing a quiet private war about a dead girl's eyes and has just been handed, by a terrifying woman in a cardigan, official cover to go through the entire photographic memory of the town under the flag of civic virtue — opens her mouth to decline.

"I'll do the records," she hears herself say.

"Course you will," says Maureen, and sits.

* * *

The committee disperses into the café after, because nothing in Wales ends without a second, worse cup of something, and Goldie is at the urn end of the room, willing herself toward the door, when the blank man finds her.

He does it kindly. He does everything kindly; that is the texture of him. "You're the one Dilys keeps telling me about," says Gareth Hollis, and his hand comes out, warm and dry and enormous, and she takes it, and gets — she notes, clinically, even as she shakes it — nothing off the contact at all, no read, the way you get nothing off shaking hands with a very good door. "The lost-property lady. Met, she says. London."

"Filing, mostly," says Goldie.

"Don't do that," he says, gently, smiling, and something in her goes very still. "Don't sell yourself short. Dilys says you were one of the special ones. The ones who never forget a face." He says it warmly, admiringly, the way you'd remark on a singing voice, and he holds her eyes while he says it, easily, all the way down, a man with nothing whatever to hide looking at a woman whose whole gift is finding the thing people hide. "That's a rare thing, that is. A useful thing." A beat. "It must be a terrible burden, mind. Remembering everyone. Never being let to forget."

"You learn to live with it," says Goldie.

"I'm sure you do." He releases her hand. Behind him Huw is telling Carwyn something that has them both laughing, and the Belles are arguing about the date of the raffle, and the whole warm wrecked surviving town is going on being itself in the failing light off the bay. "Well," says Gareth Hollis, "I'm glad you'll be doing the records. You'll do them properly. We could do with someone who looks at things properly, in this town." He smiles, and it reaches everywhere a smile is meant to reach and stops, exactly, at the threshold of his eyes, and goes no further in. "There's a great deal here worth looking at, Marigold."

He says her name like a man returning something to her she didn't know she'd lost.

Then he is gone, swallowed warmly back into the room he runs, a hand to a shoulder here, a christened head there, the good man of Aberglas moving through his grateful town, and Goldie stands by the urn with the closure notice she can still read off the inside of the doors downstairs in her head, word for grey word, *cease operation, date to be confirmed,* and finds she is rubbing her right hand slowly with her left, the hand he shook, the way you'd rub a hand that had touched something cold, or nothing at all.

Out the window the lido fills slowly with the dark, and somewhere below all of it, under the parquet, under the boxes she has just agreed to open, the building keeps whatever the building keeps.

---

# CHAPTER 6 — The Wrong Man

The building is trying to die again, and this time it has chosen the ladies' showers to do it in.

Goldie hears it before Sai does — a knock in the pipes that starts somewhere under the function rooms and travels down through the walls of the Pelican like a man falling slowly down a flight of stairs, getting louder and more personal as he comes. Then a hiss. Then, from the changing-room corridor, the unmistakable sound of water arriving where water was not invited.

"That's not—" says Sai, who is twenty-four and still believes buildings can be reasoned with. He is holding a clipboard and a biro and the particular expression of a man whose induction did not cover this. "Is that—"

"That's the ladies'," says Goldie, not looking up from the ledger. "Cubicle three. It does it on a Thursday."

"It's Wednesday."

"It's keen."

Huw is already moving, which is a thing to see — a man that size has no business being quick, and Huw is quick the way weather is quick, the whole front of him arriving somewhere a half-second before you've registered he's left. He goes past the desk at a tender thunder, a wrench in one fist, calling *now then, now then* to the pipes the way you'd gentle a dog off a postman, and the swing doors take him, and there is a brief silence, and then a sound from cubicle three like the building changing its mind, and then Huw's voice, muffled and serene: "She's all right. She's just letting off."

"She's *flooding*," says Sai.

"She's expressing herself," says Goldie.

This is the Pelican's other heartbeat, the one nobody fights to save in the meetings — not the grand frontage or the postcards of 1951, but the plumbing, sixty years of it, gone sentient and resentful in the walls. Dil has names for the worst of the pipes. There is one behind reception she calls *the Bishop*, because it only goes off during something solemn. There is a radiator in the function room that knocks during the two-minute silence, every Remembrance, regular as a soul that can't sit still. The town fights to save the Art Deco. What it will actually be saving, Goldie has worked out in three weeks, is a haunted boiler and a cubicle with opinions.

She likes it more than she'll say. That, too, is the arrangement.

* * *

The flood is mopped — Huw and a teenager called Carwyn-not-the-café-one and forty minutes of toweling — and the afternoon goes loose and grey the way they do here in the off-season, the light going out of the high windows by half three as if the day has somewhere better to be. The pool empties. The Belles have long gone, leaving behind them the smell of two competing perfumes and a single float-belt nobody will claim. The vending machine hums its one note. Goldie is alone at the desk with a shelf of other people's leavings and a list, on the back of a flyer for the Friends of the Pelican Grand Christmas Raffle, of all the things she has not let herself think about since five o'clock yesterday.

Sai pulls up a chair that isn't his and sits in it like it might be load-bearing.

"Can I ask you something," he says, "and you don't have to—"

"You can ask."

He turns the biro over in his fingers. He has the carefulness of a kind person who has learned that kindness, deployed wrong, is just another way of pressing on a bruise. She's watched him learn it, three weeks of it, the duty manager sent to close the place down discovering that he can't, that the closing is going to be done *to* him as much as *by* him, and that the only revenge available is to be decent to everyone on the way out.

"The boy," he says. "Rhodri. The eyes thing."

She'd told him. Not all of it. She'd told him the way you tell a true thing in a shape small enough to put down: that she'd seen a face on the lifeguard trainee that she *knew*, the bare yes of it, and couldn't place, and that it had bothered her, and that she'd gone hunting through his archive at midnight like a lunatic and found the bother had a name and a date and a missing-person poster the colour of weak tea. She'd told him because he'd caught her in the basement and not reported her, and a secret shared in a basement at midnight is a kind of marriage. She has regretted it precisely as much as she expected to.

"What about him," she says.

"You said you're sure. Like — *sure* sure. That it's her face, the girl from the photo, on him and the twins and—" he checks the flyer, where she has been an idiot and let him see her workings "—and now this Olwen woman, the brow, except she doesn't fit, you keep saying she doesn't fit." He looks up. "If you're that sure. Why don't you just say something. To someone. The police, or — I don't know. Her mam's still alive. The hairdresser. Nerys." He says the name gently, a local boy's gentleness, a name with a sorrow attached to it that even the corporate twenty-four-year-old has absorbed by osmosis. "If you're sure, why's it a secret?"

It is a fair question. It is the fairest question. It is the question her whole life has been built to never be asked, and she has been asked it before, in a room with worse coffee than this and a great deal more at stake, and she has learned that the honest answer takes about three days to say properly and the dishonest answer takes one second and a shrug, and that people will let you have the shrug if you give it to them confidently enough.

She gives him the shrug.

"Because I'm a woman at a lost-property desk," she says, "and what I'd be saying is *excuse me, I think your dead daughter is a teenage boy now.* Try that on a Tuesday."

Sai laughs, because it's funny, because she made it funny on purpose, the way you'd make a wound funny so the person looking at it stops looking. And it works for about a second and a half, and then it doesn't, because Sai is kind and kindness is patient, and he doesn't laugh it all the way out. He lets the end of it go quiet.

"That's not why, though," he says. "Is it."

* * *

Here is the thing she does not say, that the desk does not say, that the prose folds away as neatly as a towel.

There was a man.

She will not give him a name even now, even in the privacy of the inside of her own skull, because the name was the whole disaster — because she gave him a name once, in a courtroom, with her hand on a book, in a voice she had spent fifteen years making unimpeachable. She was *certain.* She is always certain; that is the entire content of the gift, the one thing she has that nobody can take, the bare bright yes-or-no that has never failed her — *seen before, not seen before* — clean as a struck bell. She knew his face. She had held it on a screen for nine hours of bad footage off a bus that had been on fire, and she had built him out of grain and shadow the way she builds everyone, the ear, the set of the brow, the way the jaw arrives in a frame, and she had said: *that man.* That one. I'd know him anywhere. I have never been wrong.

She was right about the face. She wants that understood, in the empty pool, in the failing light, by no one. She was *right about the face.* It was the same face. The recognition did not fail. The recognition has never once failed her in fifty-one years and it did not fail her then.

What failed was everything the face was supposed to mean.

Because a face is not a deed. A face is not a where-you-were or a what-you-did or a why. A face is just the thing on the front of a person, and she can read it to the millimetre and she cannot read one single thing *behind* it, not guilt, not innocence, not the ordinary alibi of a man who was four streets away holding his daughter's hand while the bus burned, which is where he was, which is where he could prove he was, eleven days later, after eleven days of being the man Marigold Okonkwo-Webb was certain about. Eleven days is not long. Eleven days is long enough. She has thought, since, about the inside of those eleven days for him, the thing she did to the middle of his one life with the clean bright yes that has never failed her, and she has understood the exact and permanent shape of what she is: a woman who can tell you it is the same face and cannot tell you what that means, and who spent a career letting people believe those were the same sentence.

They are not the same sentence.

That is the whole of it. That is what she got wrong. Not the seeing — she does not get the seeing wrong — but the second step, the human step, the step where a face becomes an accusation, the step where *I know him* turns into *it was him*, which is a leap across a gap she cannot see the bottom of and used to make every day for money and certainty and the small clean pleasure of being the one in the room who was never unsure.

So no, she will not say something about the boy's eyes. She will not stand in front of Nerys Pryce, whose daughter ran away twenty-two years ago and broke her heart, and turn a face into a claim. She has done that. She knows the price, and it isn't hers to pay; it's some innocent's, eleven days at a time, and she has retired from charging it.

What she says, out loud, to Sai, in the dry middle register where nothing can get a grip, is:

"Because last time I was sure, I was wrong about what it meant. And a man went home to find out what I'd cost him."

It is the most she has said about herself since she got here. It is, she registers, almost true. She watches him not know what to do with it, this boy, this decent boy holding a biro, and she feels the old animal urge to take it back, to make it a joke, to hand him the shrug after all.

"Right," he says, instead, very carefully, the way you'd accept something fragile someone's handed you across a counter. "Okay. So you want to be sure what it *means*, not just—"

"Before I ever say it. Yes." She looks at the flyer, at her own small forensic handwriting, the brow that doesn't fit, the eyes that do. "I want to know what the face means before I let it cost anybody anything. I've about earned that, I think."

"Yeah," says Sai. "You have."

And then, because he is twenty-four and the silence has gone somewhere neither of them can stand to follow it: "The pipe's gone again, by the way. I can hear it. That's the Bishop."

"It's not the Bishop, it's nothing solemn happening."

"It's *me*," says Sai, with dignity. "I'm solemn. I've just had a *moment.*"

She laughs, then, properly, and it lets the air back in, the way it does, the way the building floods and is mopped and floods again, and the day goes on being ordinary and ridiculous on the bones of the thing underneath it, which is the only way she's ever found to carry the thing underneath it at all.

* * *

She does the shelf at the end, because she cannot not.

It has been a poor day for lost property — a single earring, a paperback gone to a tide-line of damp, a child's left trainer, size 10, the Velcro worn furry, the heel crushed flat by a small person who steps on the backs of their own shoes rather than do up a strap. She holds the trainer and reads it the way she reads everything, the wear and the worn and the future it's headed for, and the reading runs on past where she means it to, the way water runs past a drain that can't take it, and there is a place it wants to go, there is a small flat thing it wants to become, a sandal, a particular afternoon, a heel exactly that crushed because the strap was a battle they'd stopped fighting—

She puts the trainer on the shelf.

*Trainer, child's, single, size 10.* She writes it. *STATUS: unclaimed.* The pen does the swerving. The pen is very good at its job, the same job it did on the armband three weeks ago, the same job it has done for twelve years: it gives the hand somewhere to go that is not the place the hand wants to go, and it asks no questions, and it never once writes down the thing it is for.

Because there is a ledger inside the ledger, and it has one entry it has never made.

She has, in the course of her life, indexed an entire city. She has faces in her that the people wearing them have forgotten — the man on the 2009 platform, the rioters, four hundred swimmers before breakfast, every face that has ever crossed her counter signed and filed and kept past all reason and all mercy, because the keeping is not a choice, the keeping is the weather, she has explained this to herself ten thousand times. She forgets nothing. She forgets nobody.

She has made herself forget one face.

Not gone — it isn't gone, it could never be gone, she is the last woman on earth a face could go missing from. Made herself *not look.* Trained herself, twelve years, the single act of forgetting she has ever attempted, the one drawer she has learned to walk past without opening, the way you learn to walk past a door in your own house if what is behind it is unbearable and yours and the only thing you have left. She can summon a stranger glimpsed for half a second in 2011. She does not summon the face she would give every other face in the cathedral of her head to keep, because to summon it is to have it, fully, in the forensic light she gives to everyone, every millimetre, the heaviness of the lid and the cant of the eye and the back of a small neck turning to check she is still—

The pen stops.

She does not write it down. There is no FACE number for it. There is no STATUS. There is, in the place in the ledger where it should be, nothing, a clean ruled line going on into the next clean ruled line, and she leaves it there, the way you leave a chair, the way you leave a room exactly as it was, and she closes the book.

* * *

The lido at dusk is the one place in the Pelican that is honest about itself.

She goes out to it because the desk is shut and Sai has gone and the indoor pool is dark behind its glass, and the old outdoor lido sits there the way it has sat since before the millennium, drained, the deep end full of leaves and a single traffic cone somebody threw in as a joke in about 2004, the diving board still bolted to the sky over nothing, going to rust the colour of the rest of it. The sea is just there, over the wall, doing its weather. The bunting somebody's strung for the campaign snaps once in the wind and goes still.

This is what they're fighting to save. A grand drowned thing with a cone in it.

She stands at the edge of the empty deep end, where a thousand people once went off this board like seals off a rock, all of them dead now or nearly, and she does the arithmetic she came out here to do, because it is the only honest work left to her, the only kind she trusts. Not *who killed her.* Not *what does it mean.* Just the bare bright yes, the thing that does not fail: the eyes on the boy are the eyes in the 2003 photograph. The cheekbones on the twins are the cheekbones in the photograph, split between them, one each. She is not wrong about that. She is never wrong about that. There is a girl who vanished twenty-two years ago, and her face is here, undrowned, walking around in the warm part of the building behind her, in children too young to have known her, and that is true, and that is impossible, and the gap between *true* and *impossible* is exactly the gap she fell into once before and swore she'd never lean over again.

She is leaning over it. She knows she is. She knows it the way she knows everything — instantly, against her will, and without the smallest idea what it means.

But she will not say a word — not to Nerys, not to the police, not to the boy with the borrowed eyes — until she knows. Not until the face is more than a face. Not until she can stand the gap between *I know* and *it was* and put her weight on it and have it hold. She owes a man eleven days she can never give back. She will not owe another.

She turns to go in. Behind her the diving board holds its pose over the leaves, and the cone keeps its joke, and the bunting snaps once more and is taken by the dark, and somewhere deep in the walls the Bishop begins, slowly, to knock — for nothing, for no one, for a building full of other people's lost things and one woman who has finally agreed to go looking for what they mean.

---

> **FACE —** *(no number)*
>
> *Trainer, child's, single, size 10, heel crushed — steps on the backs. STATUS: unclaimed.*
>
> *(That one's the boy's archive, the eyes, file it tomorrow.)*
> *(That one's the brow, the Tudor woman, won't fit, leave it.)*
>
> *(The one that goes here, you do not write down. You know the number. You have always known the number. Leave the line.)*

---

# CHAPTER 7 — What Eira Saw

The thing nobody tells you about being good at faces is that it makes you no good at all at people.

Goldie knows this the way she knows the boiler is about to do its disappointed-man noise — early, certainly, and with no power to stop it. She can stand at a counter and take a stranger apart, ear by jaw by the wear on a left shoe, in the time it takes them to say *all right.* What she cannot do, what she has never once managed in fifty-one years, is the soft slow opposite: sit across a small table from one known person and let them, in their own time, take *her* apart. That is the part she came to Aberglas precisely to never do again.

So naturally she is doing it now, on a Thursday, in a bungalow, with the heating set to greenhouse and a plate of Welsh cakes between her and a ninety-five-year-old woman who has not yet said a single word.

* * *

It is Dil's fault, the way most things are turning out to be Dil's fault — Dil who, when Goldie let slip that she "wanted to know a bit more about how the Pelican used to be," had set down the bingo cage with the air of a woman laying a foundation stone and said, "Then you'll want Eira. Eira *was* the Pelican. There's nothing happened in that building since the war that didn't happen in front of Eira, and she's got it all up here still, sharp as a tack, only she won't give it to a stranger, will she, so you can't go on your own, you'll frighten her, you've got a face like a closed shop—"

"I've got a—"

"—so we'll all come," Dil finished, satisfied. "Make a do of it. She loves a do."

Which is how Goldie comes to be conducting the most delicate interview of her recent life with a full audience of Aqua Belles arranged around the room like a tribunal that has decided in advance to enjoy itself.

Maureen has taken the good chair by the window and sits in it the way a magistrate sits, swim bag at her feet as though she might be called to the pool at any moment to pass sentence. Glenys is on the settee with her better ear angled hopefully into the room, a posture Goldie has learned means she will hear roughly two words in three and supply the rest herself, often improving them. Pam is perched forward on a pouffe with a notebook — an actual notebook — already open on her knee, because Pam has waited her whole life for someone to take her seriously about anything and has decided, fatally, that this is the day.

And Eira, in the centre of all of it, in a high-backed chair with a crocheted blanket over the arm, very small, very upright, watches them all the way you'd watch the sea: with interest, and without any intention of going in.

Goldie has her in three seconds, the involuntary inventory running soft and slow because the face is so old it is almost a landscape — ninety-five and every year of it earned and kept. The eyes have gone the pale milky blue of very old skies but the look behind them is not milky at all; it is a younger woman in there, sitting very still, taking the room's measurements. The hands are folded on the blanket, the knuckles a geography of work, and on the left one a wedding ring worn to nothing, the same knife-thin band Goldie inventoried off a dead man's finger on her first morning, only this one is still on, still belongs, has outlived whoever put it there by thirty years and stayed. Goldie reads it and looks away, fast, the pen behind her eyes doing the swerving.

"Now then," says Eira. Her voice is small and dry, like a page turning. "Who's the dark one?"

A short, electric silence. Pam's pen hovers.

"That's Goldie, Eira," says Dil, loudly, settling herself onto the settee's free cushion. "She's new. On the lost property. Lovely girl. Her mam was Aberglas — a Webb, before — and her dad was from off."

"From Lagos," says Goldie.

"From off," agrees Dil, who considers anywhere past Carmarthen to be a single undifferentiated abroad.

Eira looks at Goldie for a long, frank moment. It is not rude. It is the look of a woman who has earned the right to look at anything she likes for as long as she likes, and who is, Goldie realises with a small lurch of recognition, *doing exactly what Goldie does* — reading the face, taking it apart, filing it. The only difference is ninety-five years of practice at not letting it show.

"Webb," says Eira at last. "Your gran had a temper." And she reaches out one geological hand and pushes the plate of Welsh cakes an inch towards Goldie, which is, Goldie understands, the whole of her being granted entry.

* * *

The Welsh cakes are the genuine article, griddle-blackened at the edges and sugared while warm, and Goldie eats one because she has been told by Dil in the car, with the gravity of a woman briefing a spy, that *not eating Eira's bara brag is the one thing she'll never forgive, my own brother-in-law's still paying for it, dead nine years and she still brings it up.*

For a while it is just a tea. This is the part Goldie is bad at and the part, it turns out, she cannot skip. You cannot drive at the thing. Eira gives nothing to a question pointed straight at her; Goldie tries one — "And what was it like, the Pelican, when you—" — and watches the old woman close like a shop at five, the shutter coming down behind the milky eyes, *that's enough of that.* So Goldie does the unbearable thing. She stops asking. She lets it be a tea.

Glenys carries it, mostly, because Glenys cannot bear a silence and will fill one with whatever's nearest, and what's nearest is forty years of grievance, gently held. The bins. The doctor's, since it went over to the phone-first. A nephew in Bristol who married a girl who "doesn't do gravy." Maureen adjudicates each topic with a single word — "Disgraceful." "Typical." "Well." — and Pam, deflating slowly on her pouffe as no conspiracy presents itself, writes nothing.

And Eira, warming, begins — sideways, the only way that works — to remember.

"We had the King here," she says, to her tea, apropos of the nephew's gravy. "Nineteen thirty-seven. Not *the* King, the brother, the one before, but he came, opened the new diving boards, big do, banners all up the front. I was on the teas." A pause, the page turning. "I was always on the teas." She says it without complaint, the way you'd state your height.

"Go on, Eira," says Dil, soft, and Goldie understands that Dil — gossip, irrigator, knower of PINs — is also, quietly, the best interviewer in the room, because Dil loves the woman and the woman knows it, and that is the whole of the technique Goldie does not have.

So Eira goes on. The Pelican in its glory comes up out of her in pieces, out of order, dated wrong and felt exactly right, which is the only way the town ever remembers anything: a thousand people in the open-air water on a Whitsun. The galas, the medals, the honours board. A wedding where the bride went in the lido in her dress for a bet and the vicar pretended not to see. Funerals after, in the function rooms, ham sandwiches and the good urn. Christenings.

"They did everything there," Eira says. "Whole of a life, you could have it at the Pelican. Christened upstairs, married downstairs, buried out the back if you weren't fussy about the chapel." A small dry laugh, half a cough. "It was the heart of the place. People forget. They look at it now, all that rust, and they think it was always dying. It wasn't always dying."

"No," says Goldie, who came to it because it was, and finds she means it anyway.

* * *

It is Pam who knocks the table.

Not on purpose. Pam, sensing the conversation drifting away from her into nostalgia where no plot can live, leans further forward to stay in it, and her knee catches the table leg, and the teaspoons jump, and into the small clattering pause Pam says, too eagerly, the thing she has clearly been holding in for forty minutes:

"My mother always said there was funny business in those back rooms."

Maureen turns her head a single, terrible degree. "Pamela."

"Well, she *did.* The Trust." Pam plunges on, pen suddenly busy, vindicated by motion. "The charity. The do-gooders. My mother said they had girls up there nobody ever saw come or go. *Funny business*, she said." Pam looks round the tribunal, daring it. "I'm only saying what she said."

And Goldie, who has spent the whole hour failing to drive at the thing, feels the thing arrive on its own — not from her question, never from her question, from Pam's idiot elbow and her mother's old gossip — and she does the single hardest piece of work she has done all week, which is to keep her face a closed shop, to keep her tea exactly where it is, to give the room nothing while the room decides whether to give *her* something.

She does not look at Eira.

She looks at her Welsh cake.

The settee creaks. Glenys, who has caught *funny business* and *back rooms* and assembled from them something a notch saltier than intended, says comfortably, "Ooh, it was always going on, that, in the old days. My cousin Beryl—"

"Not that kind of business, Glenys," says Maureen.

"—oh."

And in the quiet after the *oh,* very small, very far back in her chair, Eira says: "They helped girls in trouble."

Goldie keeps her eyes on the cake.

"Quietly," Eira says. The dry page-turning voice has gone slower, gone down somewhere, dredging. "There was a — they did it kind, mind. You have to understand it now to understand it then. A girl that fell wrong, in those days, in a place like this, with the chapel up the hill watching — there was nowhere for her. So the Trust took them. Quiet. Up in the function rooms, the back of them, where the offices were. They'd go away, a girl, to stay with an aunt, everyone said *gone to an aunt's,* and she'd be up there the whole time, behind the glass, and you'd not ask." A breath. "You didn't ask. That was the kindness of it. Not asking."

Nobody at the tea is breathing wrong except Goldie, who is not breathing at all, and is being very careful that it not show.

"And then?" she says. Quiet as she can make it. A teaspoon of a question, no weight on it.

"And then the baby went to a good home," Eira says, simply, as though that were the natural end of a thing, the only end, the kind end. "And the girl came home changed and we never said a word and that was that. They were *kind,*" she says again, and now there is something under the word, a hand pressing on a door from the inside. "You're sitting there with a face on you. You don't know what it was. They had nowhere, those girls. The Trust gave them somewhere. It was a *good thing.*"

"I'm sure it was," says Goldie, and is careful to mean it, because she can hear, plain as a faint at the deep end, that Eira needs it to be true and has needed it to be true for a very long time, and that the needing is itself the most frightening thing said in this room all afternoon.

"It was a long time ago," Eira says, smaller now, the shutter coming down, the shop closing. She folds her geological hands back onto the blanket. "They were kind. People forget that. They were *kind.*"

And she will not say one word more about it, not to Goldie, not to Dil, not to anyone, and Goldie knows better than to push a closed shop. You don't get a second answer out of a woman who has frightened herself with the first.

* * *

Pam is radiant.

It is, Goldie will think later, the only genuinely funny thing about the whole afternoon, and thank God for it, because the afternoon needed one: Pam, who has spent her entire adult life being shushed at meetings, who has been wrong about the water fluoride and the new bins and the man from the council and the contrails, has been, for once, in one particular, *right* — her mother's daft old gossip about funny business in the back rooms has turned out to have a true thing standing behind it — and Pam is so happy she can barely hold the pen.

"I *told* you," she says, to Maureen, to the room, to history. "Funny business. I *said.*"

"You said a great many things, Pamela," says Maureen, rising, gathering her swim bag, declaring the tea adjourned by sovereign authority. "Most of them about chemtrails."

"But this one was *true.*"

"Even a stopped clock," says Maureen, and sweeps out to put the kettle on in someone else's kitchen, which is a thing Maureen is somehow entitled to do in every house in Aberglas.

But Goldie has clocked it, the way she clocks everything — the small terrible arithmetic of it — that Pam is right and does not know *what* she is right about, that she has hold of the true thing by entirely the wrong end, the way the whole town has hold of Carys by the wrong end, the way Goldie herself is currently holding something by an end she cannot yet name. The town is full of people who are right about the wrong thing. It is going to be the whole job, she suspects, sorting the true ends from the wrong ones. She has a sudden, unwelcome flash of the Met, of a wall of grey faces and a colleague's voice saying *but are you* sure, *Goldie,* and she puts it down, fast, the way you put down something hot.

* * *

She nearly misses the last face of the afternoon, which would have been a shame, because it is the only one that matters.

They are leaving — coats, the long Welsh goodbye that takes nineteen minutes and three returns to the doorstep — when a young man comes up the path of the bungalow next door, key out, shopping bags cutting white lines into his fingers, calling something cheerful over his shoulder to a dog already losing interest in him. Eira's neighbour, or her neighbour's lad; Dil supplies it without being asked, low, on the doorstep — "That's the Beavan boy, helps his nana with her shop, lovely family" — and Goldie is not even trying, is genuinely not trying, is tired and full of Welsh cake and wanting only the car, and the gift does not care what she wants. It never has.

The boy turns his head to manage the bags and the low afternoon light comes off the side of the house and lands flat across his forehead, and there it is.

Not the eyes. She braces for the eyes — she has been braced for the eyes since Tuesday — and it is not the eyes. It is the hairline. The widow's peak: a sharp, deliberate downward point at the centre of the brow, the hair driving to a little dark spearhead above the bridge of the nose, the two sweeps of the temples lifting clean away from it. A born thing, that. You cannot buy a widow's peak. No salon on God's earth can give you the place your own hair decides to grow; it is written before you are, in the part of a person that decides such things in the dark, months before a first breath. She has the geometry of it instantly, the exact degree of the point, the symmetry of the lift either side — and she has *held it before,* the drawer slides open, it is in the 2003 photograph, it is on the girl in the bunting, the same point, the same degree, the same dark spearhead driving down a sixteen-year-old forehead in a swim-gala line-up of the dead.

The boy is — twenty? Younger. Nineteen, twenty. Born long, long after Carys Pryce walked into the sea or wherever the town decided she'd walked.

Always young.

She doesn't know who he is. She files him — *the Beavan boy, the peak, exact, born, young* — and she does not say a single word, because she is good at faces and no good at all at people, and because a teenage boy carrying his nana's shopping does not need a strange dark woman on a doorstep telling him whose forehead he's wearing.

"You coming, Goldie?" says Dil, from the gate. "You've gone—"

"Coming," says Goldie.

The boy lets himself into his nana's house. The dog gives up on him entirely and trots off down the lane after a gull. And Goldie stands one second longer in Eira's nineteen-minute goodbye doing the arithmetic she cannot stop doing, the arithmetic she came to the quietest counter in Wales precisely to retire: a girl who died — or vanished, the town says vanished — twenty-two years ago, her exact face arriving piece by piece on the children of this town. The eyes on a lifeguard. The cheekbones on two sisters. And now a peak, a born thing, an unbuyable thing, on a boy fetching tins for his grandmother, decades too young to have ever met the girl whose forehead he is wearing.

And in the back room of the building where Goldie keeps the lost property, a charity once kept girls who'd fallen wrong, quietly, behind glass, and sent the babies to good homes, and never, ever asked.

The two facts have not yet touched, in Goldie's head. They are sitting on the table near each other, the way the eyes and the faint sat near each other on her first morning, near and not yet joined. But she can feel them leaning. She can feel, the way you feel weather coming in over Cardigan Bay before the first gull turns inland, that they are going to touch, and that when they do something is going to make a terrible amount of sense.

"Was it worth it, then?" Dil asks in the car, pulling out past the chapel, cheerful, oblivious, wonderful. "Eira and her teas. Get what you wanted?"

"More than," says Goldie.

"She liked you, mind. Pushing the cakes over. She doesn't do that for everyone, that. My brother-in-law—"

"I heard about your brother-in-law."

"Dead nine years," says Dil, with relish, "and still paying," and the car goes on down the front past the great curved wreck of the Pelican the colour of a bad tooth, where a charity once did the quiet work, and Goldie sits with two facts leaning towards each other in the dark behind her eyes, and does not yet let them fall.

---

> **FACE 0019** — male, ~20, neighbour's grandson ("the Beavan boy"). Eira's lane, afternoon, carrying shopping. Not a swimmer, not the Pelican, nothing to do with any of it.
> *The widow's peak: point, degree, the lift either side — exact. Not a feature you can buy. Born, not made. Held it before — 2003, the gala girl.*
> Young again. Always young.
> *The eyes on the young. The cheekbones on the young. Now the peak. Never on the old — when does it ever land on the old—*
> **STATUS: UNPLACED.**

---

# CHAPTER 8 — The First Theory

The Pelican at half six in the evening is a different animal from the Pelican at half eight in the morning. The aqua's done. The schools are gone. The boiler has stopped its slow disappointment and started a new noise, a low cyclical groan, like the building turning over in its sleep and not waking. The pool, through the swing doors, lies flat and lit and empty, the lane ropes hauled to one side, and the only person left in it is the chlorine smell, which clocks off later than anyone.

Goldie has the desk, the hatch down, the lights off in the foyer and on in the little back office, and a problem she has decided to stop pretending is the coffee.

She has the ledger open on the counter. She has, beside it, an A3 sheet of flip-chart paper she liberated from the Save the Pelican supplies — the kind with the faint grey squares — and on it, in the dry forensic hand nobody is meant to read, she has begun, against every instinct she came to this town to protect, to build a board.

"You're sure about this," says Sai.

He is sitting on the wrong side of the desk, on the high stool from the café that Carwyn lets nobody move, which Sai has moved, with the air of a man committing the first of several crimes he intends to deny. Twenty-four years old, duty manager, sent here by a leisure trust to switch the lights off on the whole century with as little fuss as possible, and instead spending his Tuesday evening unpaid, with his corporate lanyard off and his laptop open and a expression of someone watching a colleague reverse slowly off a pier.

"No," says Goldie.

"Right. Good. As long as we're clear."

"I'm not sure about anything. That's the entire problem. Pass me the green one."

He passes her a bingo dabber. The board, it should be said, is being assembled out of what the Pelican has, which is bingo dabbers, a roll of café receipts, the back of three closure-consultation leaflets, and a set of laminated swim-badge stickers — TADPOLE, GUPPY, DOLPHIN — that she has been using as labels because they are the only adhesive in the building that has not perished. The whole apparatus of a major incident room, rendered in the materials of a dying lido. Somewhere in the Met there is a wall she stood in front of for fifteen years, magnetic, backlit, hierarchical, and it identified fewer people with more money.

"Talk me through it again," Sai says. "Slowly. Pretend I'm the spreadsheet."

"You are worse than the spreadsheet. The spreadsheet does what it's told."

But she does it, because saying it out loud to a second human being is the thing she has been avoiding for a week and the thing she has, evidently, driven across town in the dark to do, and there is no use pretending the desire to be talked out of it is the same as the desire not to talk.

She uncaps the green dabber and starts at the top.

"Faces," she says. "I see faces. You know this much. It's not a feeling and it's not a guess and I'd thank you not to call it a gift, it's the same as you knowing a song off two notes — it just resolves, it arrives, I don't go looking. And what's arriving, in this building, since the morning I started, is the same face, over and over, on people who are not the same person."

"The dead girl."

"Don't call her that either. I don't know she's dead. I know one thing." She taps the ledger. "I've seen these eyes before, somewhere, sometime, and the somewhere is the part that's broken, because I can't place it, and I never can't place it, and that's its own ulcer I'm not dealing with tonight. But the eyes are real and they are exact and they are on a nineteen-year-old boy who has had them his whole life and they are *not his.*"

She dabs a green circle, top left, and labels it with a DOLPHIN sticker, and under the dolphin she writes: *0001 — RHODRI — eyes.*

"Then," she says, "the cheekbones."

Two more circles. Two GUPPY stickers — she's run out of dolphins. *0002 / 0003 — TWINS — cheekbones, split.* "Megan and Mali. Twenty. One got the left, one got the right, like someone took the face and tore it down the middle and gave each of them half, which is the bit that put paid to me telling myself I was tired. Because you can fool yourself into seeing a feature on one stranger. You cannot project the same bone onto two people *symmetrically* — that's not me seeing a ghost, that's geometry, and geometry doesn't get tired."

"And the others." Sai has, without admitting it, started typing. She can hear him not admitting it.

"The peak." A circle, lower. *0019 — peak — (Eira's, the neighbour's grandchild).* "A widow's peak, the exact one, on a kid at the old girl's the other day. Sixteen, maybe. Young. They're always—" she stops, the dabber an inch off the paper, the word arriving and being declined. She writes it instead. Underneath the circle, small: *young again. always young.*

She looks at the three green circles and the words under them and feels the particular discomfort of a pattern being right at her, the way the earlobe on the 2009 man was right at her, an answer she has not earned looking back at her with total confidence.

"So that's three," says Sai. "Eyes, cheekbones, peak. Three young people wearing bits of a face. And your theory is—"

"Hold on. There's a fourth."

And here is where her hand slows.

Because there is a fourth circle she has to draw, and she has spent the better part of three days not drawing it, the way you don't probe the tooth, and she dabs it now, off on its own at the edge of the sheet, away from the others, with a deliberate ribbon of white space between it and the rest, and she labels it with the last sticker, a TADPOLE, smallest of the three, which feels about right.

*0004 — OLWEN — the brow.*

"The churchwarden," says Sai.

"Mrs. Olwen Tudor. Seventy-three. Reads the lesson at St. Cynog's and tells you she does. And she has the brow — the same high, surprised, lifted brow, the *exact* arch, I've held it, it's not a coincidence of bone, it is the same brow as the rest of the face." Goldie taps the lonely tadpole twice, harder than she means to. "And she is seventy-three years old."

Sai stops typing. "So she can't be—"

"She can't be anything. That's the trouble with her." Goldie hears her own voice go flat and clipped, the register it goes into when something doesn't fit, the register Sai has not heard before and sits up slightly at. "If the others are young — fourteen, sixteen, nineteen, twenty — and they're wearing a face, then the face came from somewhere they could plausibly have got it from. A grandparent. A great-aunt. Something. But there is no version of the arithmetic where a sixteen-year-old and a seventy-three-year-old churchwarden are wearing the same inherited face for the same inherited reason, because the seventy-three-year-old is two generations the wrong way round. She's not a leaf on the tree. She's older than the root."

A silence, in which the boiler turns over in its sleep again.

"So she's a coincidence," says Sai, helpfully, the way you'd offer someone a chair.

"Yes," says Goldie. "She's a coincidence."

She says it the way Dil says *dehydration, bless him.* She says it the way she said *it's the coffee.* She hears herself say it and she knows, with the same machinery that knows everything, instantly and against her will and without the faintest idea what it means, that she is doing the thing she has watched a hundred guilty men do in interview rooms: taking the one fact that ruins the story and setting it gently to one side of the table, face down, *we'll come back to that.*

She does not come back to it. She draws a thin grey box around the tadpole, a little quarantine, and writes beside it, neatly, the worst word in any investigation: *(noise?).*

The question mark is, in its way, the most honest thing on the board. She'll give herself that, later.

* * *

"Right," says Sai. "Theory."

"Theory." Goldie stands back from the board. Three young carriers in a loose green constellation; one old one boxed off in the corner like a dish you've decided not to order. "Here's where I've got to. And I want to say first that I don't like it, and I want to say second that I can't make it not fit, and those are two different problems."

"Go on."

"The girl in the photograph." She has the gala still, the 2003 one, propped against the lamp where it's been since she found it — a wall of teenagers in swim caps, bunting, a banner, and one face in the third row that is all the green circles at once, eyes and cheekbones and peak in a single sixteen-year-old, the original, the whole, before it scattered. "Carys Pryce. Sixteen, that summer. And then, the town tells me, gone. Ran off. Broke her mam's heart, never came back, and after enough years of nothing they had the courts call her dead." She taps the photo, the third row, the face. "Twenty-two years ago."

"Okay."

"And here's what's been at me since the basement. The carriers are too *young.*" She says it slowly, because it's the hinge, and she wants to hear it land. "Rhodri's nineteen. He was born after she was gone. The twins are twenty — born after. The peak, the kid at Eira's, fourteen or fifteen — that child wasn't *thought of* when Carys Pryce walked out of this town. So how does a dead girl's face turn up, exact, on the faces of children who were born after she vanished? You can't catch a face. It's not the measles. There's only one way a face gets into a body that's born after you've gone."

She lets it sit. Sai gets there himself, which she wanted — she has learned, this week, the difference between telling someone the answer and letting them arrive, and she is testing whether the arriving works on anyone but her.

"She had kids," he says.

"She had kids."

The words go out into the cold foyer and don't come back any softer.

"That's the theory," says Goldie. "That's the whole shape of it, and God help me it fits the green. Carys Pryce didn't run off boy-mad, or not only. Carys Pryce was sixteen and pregnant in a chapel town in 2003, and a girl who's gone *that* kind of wrong, in *that* kind of place, in *that* year — she doesn't get a sympathy and a sit-down. She gets sent to an aunt's in Carmarthen. She gets a story put round. And maybe she came back and maybe she didn't, but the *child* stayed, somewhere, in this bay where everyone's a Pryce and the other half's a Pugh, and the child grew, and had children, and the face came down the line the way faces do — not whole, never whole, you don't get her back whole — but a piece here and a piece there. The eyes to one. The cheekbones split between two. The peak to a fourth. A girl scattered across her own grandchildren, and not one of them with the faintest idea whose face they're wearing to the swimming baths on a Tuesday."

She stops. She is, she registers distantly, slightly out of breath, which is not a thing that happens to her describing a pattern, and she puts it down to the cold and the standing and does not put it down to the truth of it, the awful neatness, the way it slots.

Sai is very quiet. He has stopped pretending not to type and started just looking at the board.

"That's—" he says, and stops, and starts again. "Goldie, that's actually—"

"Don't say good."

"I was going to say sad."

"Don't say that either." She caps the dabber. "Sad is for after. Right now it's just the only shape that holds the green."

* * *

What she does not say — what she would not say to Sai, to Dil, to Tom, to anyone, what she barely lets surface in the dry middle register where she keeps the things that have grips on them — is that the theory is *clean,* and that clean is what frightens her.

She has built clean stories before. She built one, once, with this same machinery, this same total certainty, this same lovely lockstep of fact into fact, and she stood up in a room with her hand on a book and gave it, every confident millimetre of it, and a man very nearly went to prison for the geometry of his own face being briefly, catastrophically convenient to a story Goldie found beautiful. That one had been clean too. That was the trouble with clean.

So she stands in the dark foyer of a dying lido at a quarter to eight, looking at a board built out of bingo dabbers, holding a theory that fits, and the thing she has learned — the one thing, the expensive thing — sits in the back of her throat and says: *the brow.*

The brow doesn't fit. The brow is in the corner in its little grey box marked *noise* with a question mark she put there herself because some honest part of her wouldn't let her write it without one. Seventy-three years old. Older than the root. If the theory were true — if this were only blood, only a girl come down through her grandchildren — then the brow on Olwen Tudor has no business existing, and it exists, and Goldie has decided, tonight, in the warm and the relief of a shape that holds, to not let that matter yet.

She knows what she's doing. That's the part she'll have to live with. She is doing the exact thing that nearly jailed a man: she is loving the clean story enough to file the dirty fact face-down.

She just isn't, tonight, going to stop.

* * *

"Don't you have to tell someone?" Sai says, later, packing the laptop, the office light buzzing. "Police. If a missing girl had a — if there are *children,* shouldn't that go to—"

"Go to who, and say what." It comes out drier than she intends. "I'm the lost-property woman. I'd be ringing a desk sergeant to report that I've matched a 2003 swimming photograph to a teenager's eyebrows from memory, and that on the strength of that I'd like them to reopen a twenty-two-year-old case the courts already closed. I'd be a crank, love. I'd be the lady who thinks the lifeguard's a ghost." She rolls the flip-chart sheet carefully and slides a rubber band over it, the whole incident room reduced to something that fits behind the dentures. "And I'd be wrong before, mind. There's that. They've got a long memory for the ones who were wrong before."

"You weren't wrong about the cheekbones. Geometry doesn't get tired, you said."

"No. The cheekbones are gospel." She looks at him, and for a second lets him have the actual distinction, the one that costs her, because he's earned a Tuesday of it. "That's the trap, see. That part of me's never been wrong, not once in fifty-one years, and so I trust the next part — the part that says *and therefore* — and the next part's a liar." She nods at the rolled-up sheet, at the grey box she can't see through the paper but knows is in there. "That brow's the one honest thing on that board, and I've shut it in a box marked *noise* because it spoils a story I like. A man went in the dock once for a story I liked. So no. I don't ring anyone over a thing I want to be true. I find out why the brow's wrong first — the bit I can't fit, not the bits I can. Then I say. Never the other way round again."

Sai considers this. He's a kind boy, under the corporate, the same way Rhodri is, and she watches the kindness do its arithmetic.

"That sounds lonely," he says, finally.

"It's efficient," says Goldie, which is the answer she gives, and which is not a denial, and which she rather hopes he's tired enough not to notice isn't one.

* * *

She rings Tom from the car. She doesn't know why she rings Tom, except that the board is rolled up on the passenger seat like a body, and the foyer was very dark behind her when she locked it, and there is exactly one number in her phone belonging to someone who has known her long enough to hear what she isn't saying.

He picks up on the fourth ring, the telly going somewhere behind him, a fondness in his hello that twelve years and a divorce have worn smooth and not worn out.

"Goldie. You all right? It's gone eight."

"I'm fine. Settling in fine." It's automatic, the same coin she gave Dil, the same exact change. "Just ringing. The job's grand. It's a — it's a quiet little place. Lost property. Nobody needs me to be sure of anything."

There is a pause on the line of the specific quality only Tom can produce, a pause with twenty years of her in it.

"You found something," he says.

Not a question. She watches the harbour lights through the windscreen, the black water past the wall, the rolled-up sheet in her peripheral vision being, stubbornly, exactly the shape of a thing she's carrying.

"I found a flip-flop," she says. "Size nine. Pronator."

"Goldie."

"It's nothing, Tom. It's a — it's an interesting nothing. There's a face that keeps turning up. I'm being daft about it. You know me and a face."

"I do know you and a face," he says, and there is no edge in it, only the long tiredness of a man who once shared a house with the gift and never once got to switch it off either. "Marigold. You went there for quiet."

"I know."

"You promised yourself a year. You said one ordinary year."

"I know what I said."

"And it's been a fortnight, and you're ringing me at eight at night to tell me you found a flip-flop, in a voice you only use when you've already not slept about something." A breath. The telly murmurs. "I'm not telling you off. I gave that up. I'm just — I can hear it. You should hear it. You sound like you did before."

And there it is — the cost of having been known, once, by somebody. He can hear the thing the prose of her own evasions cannot keep out of her voice. She came to this town to be a woman who does filing, and her ex-husband has listened to fourteen seconds of *settling in fine* and heard the whole board through the phone.

"It's a flip-flop," she says again, softly, because it's all she's got, and because if she says one more true word she'll say six.

"Okay," says Tom, who knows when to let her keep the crusts. "It's a flip-flop. Go home, Goldie. Eat something that isn't from the café."

"The bacon's holy," she says.

"Goodnight, you daft article."

She sits in the car a moment after he's gone. The sheet on the seat does not stop being the shape it is. Somewhere up the coast, in a bed she's never seen, a nineteen-year-old with somebody's eyes is asleep, and a fourteen-year-old with a widow's peak, and twins who got half a face each, and not one of them dreaming they're wearing a girl who got sent to Carmarthen and never properly came home, if the clean story's true, which it is, mostly, in the way the clean ones always are: true enough to chase, and wrong in the one place she's decided not to look.

She starts the engine. *If Carys Pryce had children,* she thinks, and the thought has the cold completeness of the ones she can't unthink, *they're in this town, wearing her face. So find them.*

She drives home and does not eat anything that isn't from the café.

---

> **FACE 0001 — UPDATED.** male, 19 (RHODRI). The eyes — set, cant, lid — now matched, exact, to the 2003 gala photograph, third row. Source named at last: **CARYS PRYCE.** Sixteen that summer. "Ran off." Declared dead.
> *But he's nineteen. Born after she was gone. A face cannot postdate the source unless it is inherited.*
> Working theory: descendant. (cf. 0002/0003 cheekbones, 0019 peak — all BORN AFTER. all young.)
> *And 0004 — the brow — seventy-three. Doesn't fit. Boxed off. Noise.* I have done this before, the boxing-off. It cost a man eleven days he'll never get back and me the lie I tell about being sure.
> **STATUS: UNPLACED → working: BLOOD.**
> *(The brow is still in the corner. I can see it from here.)*

---

# CHAPTER 9 — Rhodri

The trouble with deciding to befriend someone, Goldie is discovering, is that you then have to do it.

She has done surveillance. She has stood in a Croydon control room at four in the morning watching the same forty seconds of a stairwell until she could have drawn the suspect's molars from memory, and she has knocked on doors with a warrant card and a face she'd practised in the rear-view, and she has sat across a table from men who knew exactly what she was and lied to her anyway, beautifully, for hours. None of it required her to be liked. That was the mercy of it. The Met never once asked her to be liked.

The lost-property desk, it turns out, asks for nothing else.

So at twenty to nine on a Thursday she is loitering by the lifeguard hut with two coffees she does not want, performing the most unnatural act of her life, which is *casualness*, and the coffees are from the Mermaid, which means they are not bribes so much as small grey threats, and she is waiting for a nineteen-year-old to finish hosing down the footbath so she can ambush him with warmth.

"Now then," says Huw, who has appeared beside her the way the sea appears beside you, without a sound, all of it at once. Fifty-eight, eighteen stone of him, a man built along the lines of a friendly outbuilding; she has him to the inch — the old break in the nose set slightly proud, the broad pads of swimmer's hands gone to permanent prune at the fingertips, thirty years of this water in the very grain of him. "You're up the wrong end. Lost property's down by reception."

"Stretching my legs."

"With two coffees." He considers this, untroubled, the way he considers everything, as a thing the world has simply done and which it would be rude to question. "Brave."

"They're not for me."

"No," he agrees, gravely. "You wouldn't do that to yourself twice."

Rhodri kills the hose and straightens, and there it is again, the thing that arrives before he does — not the boy, the eyes, the involuntary inventory running its hands over him before she can stop it, the same as the first morning, the same as every morning since: the set of them, the cant, the heavy patient lid, a face she has held in the full forensic weight of her attention before and filed and cannot place and knows, in the part of her that does not lie because it cannot, the way she knows the colour of the sea, are *not his.*

She has stopped trying not to see it. That ship went, she thinks, at a quarter to eight in a dark foyer with a board built out of bingo dabbers.

"All right, Goldie!" Rhodri lifts a hand, easy, glad, the gladness of a boy who is glad about most things and hasn't yet been taught better. "Is that for me? You're a saint. Don't tell Carwyn, he'll do you for theft."

"Carwyn can do me for whatever he likes. I'm doing him for the coffee." She holds it out. He takes it in his left hand — left-handed, she clocks it again, the way you clock the tide is in — and folds round it gratefully, and her whole investigation, such as it is, the entire towering edifice of it, begins with a paper cup and a lie about stretching her legs.

* * *

Here is how you befriend someone sideways, if you are Goldie and you have never once in fifty-one years done it on purpose. You let the desk do it.

The desk is unimpeachable. The desk gives her *reasons.* She does not have to manufacture an interest in Rhodri Pryce-Pugh, lifeguard-in-training, great-nephew or great-great-nephew of Huw, possessor of a dead girl's eyes — she simply has to possess the one thing in Aberglas that Rhodri loses more reliably than anyone alive, which is goggles, and then wait.

"I've lost it again," he says, on the Monday, with the particular shame of a man confessing a thing he knows is funny. "The goggle. The—" he holds up finger and thumb "—the one without the lens. I had it. I genuinely had it."

"You had half of it. You've never in your life had all of it."

"I had the strap." He says it like an alibi. "The strap was on me at half nine."

She finds it on the shelf — it's always on the shelf; she has begun, without entirely admitting it, to *put* it on the shelf when it turns up elsewhere in the building, a small custodial fraud she performs on behalf of a boy who would lose the sea if it weren't screwed down — and she slides the ruined thing across the counter, one lens gone, the other crazed grey, an eyeglass for a man with one eye, and he receives it like a relic.

"You're a legend. Seriously. I'd lose my head."

"You'd find it on this shelf Tuesday," she says, "with a label on it, *one (1) head, slight concussion, owner careless,*" and he laughs, the real laugh, the ordinary one, and goes off down the poolside knocking the goggle twice on his own knuckles for luck, *ta, you're a star,* the way he rang it twice on her counter the first morning, the way you'd ring a small brass bell, and she watches him go and does not let herself feel the thing that is trying to be felt, because the thing that is trying to be felt is twelve years old and walled and not invited.

It is not the eyes that do it. She could armour the eyes. She has armoured worse.

It is the goggle. It is that he keeps losing the same broken half-thing and she keeps quietly giving it back, and that there is, in all the world, a finite and decreasing number of mornings on which a careless boy comes to a counter to be handed his ruined goggle by a woman who is glad, against her will, to be the one holding it.

She writes it down, because writing it down is the only confession she has. *Goggle, single, no lens. Returned (3rd? 4th?). STATUS: keeps coming back.* It is not, technically, a lost-property entry. She lets it stand.
