* * *

The hut, she learns, is its own small country.

It smells of neoprene and orange squash and the inside of a kettle. There is a corkboard furred with rotas and a poster about spinal injuries gone the colour of weak tea and a kettle that Huw guards the way other men guard a marriage. There is a radio that plays one station, always, and a biscuit tin with a fierce and unwritten constitution. And there is the banter, which moves through it like weather, and which Goldie — who can reconstruct a stranger's decade from his earlobe and cannot, for the life of her, do small talk — finds she can stand at the edge of and let run over her, the way she let Dil run over her, the way you stand in the shallows.

"He went in after a *float*," Huw is saying, of Rhodri, to the squash, to Wales, the morning she finally gets properly inside the hut on a maintenance pretext she does not bother to make convincing. "Full clothes. October. A child let go of a float, see, down the deep end, and this article" — a vast thumb at Rhodri — "decides the float needs rescuing, and goes in after it, in his trainers."

"The kid was *upset.*"

"The kid was three feet from the rail."

"She was *attached* to the float."

"You ruined a phone for a float."

"The float," says Rhodri, with dignity, "is fine. The float lives. You're welcome, the float."

Goldie, at the edge of it, with a clipboard she is not using, finds she is smiling, and clocks herself smiling the way she clocks everything, instantly and against her will, and does not know quite what to do with the information except note it, the way she notes a faint, the way she notes a graze going yellow at three days: *something here is healing that she did not give permission to heal.*

"He's a soft one," Huw tells her, later, by the chemical store, quietly, with the unembarrassed tenderness of a huge man who has cried at every retirement do this building ever threw. "Rhodri. Soft as butter, that boy. Takes after his mam's side that way." A pause; a shadow goes over it, brief, the kind of shadow that crosses a man when he says a true thing he hasn't examined. "Funny lot, the mam's side, mind. Lovely. But funny."

She keeps her face exactly where she has kept it for twelve years. She does not lift the clipboard. "Funny how?"

"Ah, you know." He doesn't, quite, mean it the way she wants it. He means it the soft human way, the Dil way, the way the whole town keeps its truths. "The Beynon side. They came in — Rhodri's grandmother, his mam's mam, she was took in, see, as a baby. Adopted, like. Local couple, the Beynons, no children of their own, lovely people, gone now. And nobody ever — well. You didn't ask. There's families round here you don't ask. The Beynons were one." He scratches the back of his neck, a man who has wandered further into a thing than he meant to and would like a way out. "It was all done proper, mind. I'm not saying. Only it's a branch of the family with no roots showing, if you follow. Lovely people. Bit of a mystery, that branch. Always has been."

A baby. Took in. The Beynons.

Goldie writes nothing. She has learned, this past fortnight, the precise difference between a fact she can chase and a fact she must let *sit*, untouched, in the light, the way you don't grab the bird, and this one she lets sit so hard her hand actually flattens on the clipboard to keep itself still. *A branch with no roots showing.* A baby, adopted in, locally, a generation back or two — she does not let herself count it, will not let herself draw the line from a girl in a 2003 photograph to this hut, not yet, not on the strength of a soft man's shadow by a chemical store, because she has drawn beautiful clean lines before and stood up in a room and given them and been wrong.

She just files it. Adopted-in. The Beynon side. A mystery.

She files it next to the eyes.

* * *

It happens on the Friday, at the deep end, in the ten unbearable seconds it takes a thing you have been told about to become a thing you watch.

She is at the desk. She has the hatch up and the ledger open and a queue of precisely no one, and through the swing doors' little window she can see the pool, lit and busy, the schools-session churn of it, forty children frothing the shallow end and a knot of better swimmers doing lengths up the deep, and Rhodri on the side in his red shorts with the whistle, walking the long edge the way Huw has taught him, slow, eyes down, reading the water the way she reads a face.

And then he stops.

He stops the way a film stops when the projector slips — not a stumble, nothing so dramatic, just a sudden subtraction, the walking simply *not continuing*, and he puts one hand out and finds the rail and isn't there, isn't behind his own face, the eyes she would know in the dark gone to two flat windows with the lights off behind them, and the colour goes out of him from the top down like water out of a glass, and Goldie is up and through the swing doors before she has decided to be, because fifteen years of the worst footage in London taught her one thing her body never forgot, which is that the half-second you spend being surprised is the half-second the person on the other end of it does not have.

"Huw." She doesn't shout it. You don't shout it. You say it so it carries flat across water, the way they're trained, the way she was never trained but learned, watching. "*Huw.* The boy."

Huw is already moving — he has seen it too, the great bulk of him suddenly fast, appalling and graceful at once — and Rhodri's knees go, and he doesn't fall so much as *fold*, sliding down the rail with the boneless give of someone the strings have been cut on, and there is a moment, a single grey suspended moment, where his head tips back and his hand comes up off the rail and presses flat to his own chest — there and gone, the way you'd press a stitch, the way you'd feel for a phone in a breast pocket, the way you'd reach for something that for the length of one beat forgot to be there — and Goldie, kneeling now on wet tile with her hand going to the side of his throat the way you do, finds under her two fingers a pulse that is not a pulse so much as a panic, a small fast wrong scrabbling thing, here and gone and here too fast and gone again, a heart doing something a heart should not be allowed to do this close to the surface of a healthy nineteen-year-old boy.

"Rhodri." Huw has the boy's shoulders, has him down properly, flat, legs up, the old certain choreography. "Rhodri, *bach.* Come on now. Come back to us."

And he does.

That is the thing she will keep, afterwards — the thing the shelf will not be able to hold and the ledger will not be able to say. He comes back. The colour climbs back into the face like a tide turning, and the lights come up in the two dark windows, and Rhodri blinks at the ceiling of the Pelican and says, thick and far away and already ashamed, "...did I go? I went, didn't I. Oh, *God.*"

"You went," says Huw, whose enormous hands are not quite steady. "You're all right. You went."

"In front of the *kids.*" He tries to sit up. Huw and Goldie put him back down with one hand each, in perfect wordless agreement, and he subsides, mortified, alive, nineteen. "Is the — did the kids see? Tell me the kids didn't see."

"The kids," says Goldie, whose own voice she is keeping in the dry middle register by an act of will she will feel later in her jaw, "are having the time of their lives. You're the most interesting thing that's happened to a Year Four all term. You'll be a legend by Monday. *The day the lifeguard fell over.*"

He laughs — a weak, wet, grateful ghost of the real one — and it is the worst sound she has heard in a long time, because it is so exactly like a sound she heard out of a different throat at a bingo table she has not yet learned to dread, and because she is two fingers deep on the throat of a living boy feeling a heart she now understands she has been afraid of since the first morning without knowing it was a heart she was afraid of.

* * *

They get him into the first-aid room, which is a converted store with a too-high bed and a blood-pressure cuff and a window that looks at the bins, and they sit him up against the wall with a juice he doesn't want, and the colour stays this time, and the eyes stay, and the awful little scrabbling pulse under Goldie's memory of it settles back to something a nineteen-year-old's pulse is allowed to be.

"Dehydration," says Rhodri, before anyone has said anything, reaching for it the way you reach for a rail. "It's just — I don't drink enough water, everyone says, my mam says, it's a whole thing. I went funny like this a few weeks back and all, the deep end, Dil'll tell you. Dehydration. I just need to—" he lifts the juice, a man producing his own evidence "—drink the juice."

"Drink the juice," Huw agrees, too quickly, wanting it to be true the way you want it to be true, the way Dil wanted it, the way the whole soft loving lot of them want every frightening thing to be a small thing you can fix with a juice and a sit-down. "You frightened the life out of me, you daft article."

"I'm sorry."

"Don't be sorry. Drink the juice."

Goldie says nothing. Goldie is standing by the window that looks at the bins, with her arms folded against the cold of the room and the colder thing the room is not saying, and she is doing the arithmetic she did not let herself do at the chemical store, and she is hating it.

Because here is what she has, now, that she did not have an hour ago. She has the eyes — not his, exact, held-before, a dead girl's eyes on a living boy. And she has, in the same boy, in the same hour, in the same body, *this:* a heart that twice now, weeks apart, has simply stepped out of the room and had to be called back. A "weak turn." A "funny colour." *Dehydration, bless him.* The whole town's word for it, soft and sufficient and wrong, the word they put on a boy the way Goldie put a grey box round a brow and wrote *noise.*

And the two things are on the same boy. The eyes and the turn. The face she is chasing and the heart that keeps stopping. For the first time she lets herself set them down side by side on the same line, the way she set Carys's whole face down beside the green circles, and the line, when she looks at it, is not a line about a face at all.

It is a line about a body.

She has spent a fortnight thinking she was tracking a *resemblance.* A scatter. A dead girl's features turning up like lost property on the living. A puzzle of bone, a thing she could solve and file and be, finally, done with — match the fragments, name the source, prove the descent, hand it gently to nobody and go back to her shelf.

She has not been tracking a resemblance.

She has been tracking an *inheritance.* And an inheritance is not only a face. An inheritance is everything that comes down the same line — and something has come down this line, to this boy, attached to the eyes the way the eyes are attached to the bone, and it is not a feature, and you cannot put it on a shelf, and it does not get better with a juice.

"Goldie." Rhodri, against the wall, colour back, eyes back, watching her with the gentle alarm of a kind boy who thinks he has caused a fuss. "You all right? You've gone a bit—"

She catches it. The funniness, she means. The grim line of her own mouth. She puts it away, where she puts everything, and gives him back a face he can drink the juice in front of.

"Long way from a coffee," she says, which is what she said the first morning, the lie she has, and he grins, relieved, on solid ground, and drinks the juice.

* * *

She finds Dil at reception, because Dil is the town's memory, the soft inaccurate sacred memory of it, and because there is a question she needs answered that no archive can answer and no badge could ask.

"That second turn of his," she says, when she has manufactured a reason to be there, when she has signed a thing and complained about the boiler and earned her way to it sideways. "Rhodri. Today. And the one a few weeks back, the morning I started. You said dehydration."

"Bless him, yes. Always going a colour, that one. He's better when it's not so warm in there." Dil clucks, fond, filing it where she files everything, in the long warm catalogue of small-town nothings. "His mam was the same as a girl, mind, now you say it. Marged. Used to go off in chapel — out like a light, down she'd go, the once or twice, give the whole vestry a fright. They had her looked at, course they did, all sorts. *Nothing wrong,* they said. *She'll grow out of it.* And she did, more or less." A shrug, the great soft shrug the town gives every frightening thing it has decided to survive. "Runs in them a bit, the fainting. The mam's side. You know what families are."

*You know what families are.*

Goldie does. Goldie, God help her, is the one person in this building who knows *exactly* what families are — what comes down them, unasked, in the bone and under it; what gets handed on with the eyes whether anybody wants it or not; what a face is, finally, which is not a resemblance and not a coincidence and not a ghost, but a delivery, generation to generation, of everything the body was too honest not to keep.

"Yes," she says. "I know what families are."

She goes back to the desk. She does not, this time, write down a flip-flop. She sits with her hand flat on the closed ledger and looks through the little window at the pool, where a boy with somebody's dead eyes is sitting on the side now, benched, drinking a second juice he doesn't want, laughing at something Huw has said, alive as Friday, alive as the water — and going, twice now, a colour the whole town has agreed to call dehydration, out of a heart that came down to him on the same line as the eyes she has never once in her life seen on anybody who was still alive.

She picks up the pen.

---

> **FACE 0001 — UPDATED.** male, 19 (RHODRI). The eyes — set, cant, lid — exact, *not his*, held before. Today, 11:40, deep end: a turn. The real thing — folded, grey from the top, the lights gone out behind the eyes; came back. Two fingers on the throat: the pulse all wrong, fast and skipping, here-gone-here. Earlier this fortnight: another. *Dehydration,* they say. They say it the way I said *noise.*
> Hand to the chest as he went. Same as the first morning. There and gone.
> The mam's side — "the Beynon side." A baby took in, adopted, local, generations back. "A branch with no roots showing." And the mother — Marged — *fainted as a girl. Runs in them. She grew out of it.*
> Eyes + the grey turn. SAME BOY. Same line.
> I came here to track a face. This is not a face.
> cf. heart? cf. the mother? cf. *whatever this is that came down with the eyes.*
> **STATUS: UNPLACED.**
> *(He keeps losing the goggle. I keep giving it back. There are only so many Fridays.)*

---

# CHAPTER 10 — The Pryce Brow

There is a way to hunt a face that does not look like hunting, and Goldie has been perfecting it for a week.

You do not stand in the square with a photograph. You do not stop teenagers and turn their chins to the light, however much the hand wants to. You take your terrible coffee to the one outside table at the Mermaid that gets the morning sun, and you sit with your back to the wall and the whole square laid out in front of you like a slide under glass, and you let the town walk past, and you read it.

It is, she has decided, research. She has even told Sai it's research, which made him put his head in his hands.

The square is the kind of small that means everything happens across it twice. The war memorial. The shut bank that's a charity shop now, the charity shop that's shut. A bench where two old men sit not speaking with the comfortable thoroughness of men who said everything worth saying in about 1986. The chip shop, dark until eleven. And, on the far side, between the chemist and a unit with whitewash on the inside of the glass, the salon — PRYCE OF ABERGLAS in a font that was modern once, the gold gone the colour of weak tea, a closed sign curling in the door and behind it, just visible, the ghosts of two chairs and a basin and a mirror with a swag of something draped over it, a building holding its breath since before Goldie ever heard the name.

She does not look at the salon. She is good at not looking at things. She looks at the square, and she catalogues, and the cataloguing is a comfort the way it has always been a comfort, a thing her hands can do while the rest of her decides nothing.

Mother with a buggy, thirties, a caesarean's careful walk, exhausted, a good coat she can't afford. Postman she's seen before — no, not seen, *worked,* he's the 2009 man from her first morning, the train-platform man, he posts now, the world is the size of a stamp. Two girls from the comp on a free period they have not been granted, sharing one set of earbuds and one cigarette, fizzing with the specific joy of getting away with nothing much. A man crossing on the diagonal toward the chemist with a prescription in his hand and a face she has, the instant he steps off the kerb, *already filed* —

and she stops with the cup at her mouth, because the face that has just stepped off the kerb is wearing Carys Pryce's brow.

* * *

He is forty-four. She has him before the kerb, the way she has everyone — the soft creep of weight at the collar, the grey come in at the temple in that uneven way men go grey, one side first, a wedding ring and the pale band beside it where a second one isn't, builder's hands gone slightly to office, a back that's been bad and been ignored. Forty-four if he's a day. A man halfway through a life, ordinary as a Tuesday, walking to the chemist with a green prescription slip for somebody, his own knee or his mother's blood pressure, nothing on him that anyone in this square would turn to look at twice.

And over his ordinary brown eyes, his ordinary tired forty-four-year-old eyes, sits the brow.

*The* brow. The high clean lift at the inner end, the fine architectural taper out toward the temple, the small surprised arch that makes a face look perpetually a half-second from asking a delighted question — the exact one, the one she held in the gala photo and on the children and, four days ago, in a cold green room over the pouchy face of a seventy-three-year-old churchwarden. She knows it to the millimetre. She has held this brow more times now than she has held her own coffee.

It is on him. It is on a balding man in a fleece going to get a prescription, and it sits over his eyes like a thing borrowed for the day from a younger, finer, briefly-living face, and Goldie sits very still at the outside table at the Mermaid with the cup not quite at her mouth and feels the small cold slide of a fact arriving that she has already, somewhere underneath, refused.

He does not see her. Nobody sees the woman at the lost-property desk and nobody sees the woman with the coffee; she has been invisible in two professions now. He goes into the chemist, the brow and all, and the door's little bell does its tin sound, and Goldie sets the cup down without drinking and does not move for a moment, the way you don't move when something has settled on your arm and you are deciding whether it stings.

* * *

"That's Idris," says Carwyn, who has come out to collect the cup she hasn't finished and stayed to lean in the doorway, because the Mermaid is the kind of establishment where the proprietor's interest in your custom does not survive your having paid for it. He is a wide, calm man with forearms like a dock and a tea-towel he uses for everything including, she suspects, things best not enumerated. "Idris Tomos. You'll have seen him about."

"I haven't," says Goldie, truthfully, which is its own small vertigo — *I have never seen this man before in my life and I have seen his eyebrow three hundred times.*

"Lives up Maes-yr-Awel, the new ones. Does the boilers, half the town's boilers, did ours till the part it needs stopped being made, now it does what it likes." Carwyn says this without rancour, the boiler one more weather system to be endured. "Lovely fella. Buried his mam in the spring, Beti, you'd have liked Beti, sharp as a tack and twice as likely to draw blood. He's getting her prescriptions cancelled, I shouldn't wonder. They keep coming for months, you know, after. The chemist rings the names of the dead off a list till somebody tells them to stop." He says it the way the town says everything, the awful folded into the ordinary, a fact about chemists. "Cake?"

"No. Thank you."

"You never have cake." He is not offended; he is gathering data of his own. "You sit out here with a coffee you don't drink and you watch the square like the telly. I'm not saying it's not nice to be watched. After thirty years a man takes what he can get." And he goes back in, the tea-towel over the shoulder, and the bell does its sound, and Goldie is left with Idris Tomos's name and the salon's blind windows across the way and a brow that has just, very quietly, become a problem with a body count of two.

She writes nothing down. She has learned that much. You do not, in a café in a small town, take out a notebook and start writing while looking at a man's house; that is how you become the thing the town talks about instead of the woman who listens to it. She holds it in the place where she holds everything, the involuntary cabinet, and she files Idris Tomos under the brow, beside Olwen Tudor, and the drawer they share is the wrong drawer, the drawer that should be empty, and she sits there feeling it be the wrong drawer.

Forty-four. Seventy-three. A man and a churchwarden, thirty years apart, both of them decades past any version of the arithmetic that would let them be Carys Pryce's anything.

The children, she could explain. The children she was *almost* ready to explain — three days ago she stood in a dark foyer and built a clean and lovely story out of the children, *she had kids, the face came down the line.* The children fit the story. A face comes down a bloodline to the young; that is what blood does; the young are where you'd look. But the brow is not coming down to the young only. The brow is on a man getting his dead mother's prescriptions stopped, and there is no line on any tree that runs from a girl who vanished at sixteen *up* and *backward* to a forty-four-year-old plumber.

The brow, she thinks, and does not want to think, is not behaving like the eyes.

The eyes are only in the young. She has seen the eyes once, exact — on the boy, on Rhodri, nineteen and warm and alive and wearing them like a thing he'd been lent. The cheekbones, the twins, twenty. The peak, the kid at Eira's, fifteen. Every born feature, every one, on a body young enough to have got it down a line from somewhere. The born features keep to the children. They cluster there, in the young faces, the way leaves cluster at the ends of branches and never on the trunk.

But the brow is everywhere.

* * *

She is still sitting with that — *the born features keep to the young; the brow keeps to no one* — turning it the way you turn a stone to find what's under it and then, finding what's under it, wishing you'd left the stone — when a girl comes across the square on the diagonal toward the chip shop that won't be open for an hour, and Goldie's involuntary cabinet opens its drawer again and there is Carys Pryce's widow's peak.

The girl is seventeen, maybe. Goldie has her at once: a swimmer, the chlorine-fade in the ends of the hair, a school bag worn on one shoulder the way you carry a thing you've outgrown caring about, the loose-limbed half-finished walk of someone whose body is still arriving. And at the hairline, where the hair parts and lifts, the peak — the precise downward point of it, the exact lift to either side, the small clean V that you cannot draw on and cannot buy and cannot ask a hairdresser for, that a body either grows or doesn't. Born. Unmistakably, structurally born. The peak is Carys's. The girl is young. So far the story holds; so far the story is beautiful; the peak is exactly where a peak should be, on a child, coming down the line —

and then the girl turns her head to check the road, and the light comes across her, and she has the brow as well.

The brow *and* the peak. The made thing and the born thing, on one seventeen-year-old face, the surprised high arch sitting over a hairline that grew its own point — and for a half-second Goldie's whole clean story sings, because here it is, here is a young one wearing the brow, here is the brow being young after all, here is the brow behaving like blood —

except.

Except that twenty minutes ago the same brow walked into the chemist on a man of forty-four. Except that four days ago it chaired a committee on a churchwarden of seventy-three. The exact same brow, to the millimetre, the identical arch — Goldie does not get *similar*, she does not get *a family resemblance*, she gets *the same*, she has never in fifty-one years confused *the same* with *alike* — and the same brow cannot be inherited blood on a seventeen-year-old and *also* inherited blood on a seventy-three-year-old, because the seventy-three-year-old was alive and grown and chairing things before Carys Pryce was a thought, and a feature does not run forward and backward through time and arrive identical at both ends of a town.

The peak does one thing. The peak is on the young, only on the young, born and structural and clustered in the children. The brow does another thing. The brow is on the young *and* the middling *and* the old, and it is exact on all of them, and exact-on-all-of-them is not how blood works. Blood scatters. Blood gives you the eyes and not the nose, the peak and not the chin, a piece here and a piece there, never the same piece whole to everyone. *Exact, to everyone, at every age* is not inheritance.

*Exact, to everyone, at every age,* says the cold clean machine at the back of her skull, in the voice it uses when it has the answer and is holding it out to her with both hands, *is a copy. Somebody is copying it.*

And Goldie, sitting at the outside table at the Mermaid in the morning sun with a cooling coffee and a square full of strangers wearing pieces of a dead girl, does the thing she promised Sai she had stopped doing, the thing she watched a hundred guilty men do across a hundred interview tables, the thing that nearly put one of them in a cell:

she takes the answer her own brain is holding out to her, and she sets it gently down, face down, off to one side, and she does not pick it up.

Because the answer breaks the story. The story is *blood* — the clean thing, the lovely thing, the dead girl come down whole-in-pieces through her grandchildren, the thing that fits the green circles and the youth and the eyes. And the answer the machine is holding out is *two things at once* — blood for the peak and the eyes, and something else, something *made*, for the brow — and two things at once is not clean, two things at once is the kind of fact that has no bottom, that you fall through, and she came to this town precisely so she would never again stand somewhere bright and certain and feel the floor not be a floor.

So she looks at the girl with the peak and the brow until the girl is gone past the chip shop and round the corner, and she does not let herself finish the thought, and she knows she isn't letting herself, instantly, against her will, and without the smallest idea what it will cost her, which is, she is coming to understand, the only way she ever knows anything in this town.

She picks up the cold coffee. She drinks it. It is, even cold, a sermon about sin.

* * *

Dil finishes the brow off, the way Dil finishes everything off, without being asked and without knowing she's done it.

It is half eleven and Goldie is back behind the counter with a returned inhaler and a single child's sock, navy, age 6–8, the heel worn paper-thin (*a dragger, this one, scuffs the back foot, the parents will be told and told*), and Dil has arrived at the hatch with a cup of the coffee for herself and a piece of news she is carrying the way you carry a full glass through a crowd.

"—and *she* says to *me,* well, if the council won't pay for the railings then the council can carry the coffins down to the beach themselves, and I said, Maureen, it's a lido, not a crematorium—" Dil breaks off, because Goldie has said *Idris Tomos* into the gap, casually, the way you drop a coin in a slot to see what comes out.

"Idris Tomos," says Dil, delighted, because there is no name in this town that does not open like a flower for Dil. "Beti's boy. Up Maes-yr-Awel. Did our boiler. Lovely fella, terrible back, married a girl from Tregaron and you'd think *that* was a foreign country the fuss the mother made, God rest her. Why, what's he done?" Hope, bright and unbecoming, lifts her whole face. "He's not in *trouble?*"

"Nothing. I saw him in the square. He's—" Goldie picks the tool from the box, the lightest one, the one that won't show. "He's a good-looking man. Striking. That brow."

She says it idle. She says it the way you'd remark on a hat.

And Dil — Dil who knows everyone the soft human way, wrong on every date and right on every feeling, Dil whose head is forty years of who-sat-in-whose-chair and who-married-whose-cousin and whose-baby-came-early and everyone-said-nothing — Dil laughs, a short fond snort into her terrible coffee, and hands Goldie the entire craft channel across the hatch wrapped in nothing but gossip.

"*That* brow," she says. "Bless you, love, that's not *his* brow. Half the town's got that brow. That's the *Pryce* brow, that is — Nerys's. She gave it to everyone." She licks her thumb, settling in, the pleasure of a woman with a good seam to mine. "Oh, you wouldn't remember, you weren't here, but in her day — Nerys Pryce, the salon, before it all — there wasn't a woman in this bay went anywhere *near* the chapel or the club or a wedding without Nerys had done her, and Nerys did them all the one way, she had a *look,* see, she was *famous* for it, the lift here—" Dil draws it on her own forehead with a wet thumb, a ghost arch, and her face when she does it is briefly, accidentally, the brow, and Goldie's skin moves "—she'd take any old droopy thing you came in with and she'd make you that brow, surprised-like, *up,* it took years off you, everyone wanted it, it was *the* brow for thirty years. My sister had it. Beti had it — Idris's mam, she's where he gets it, his mam went to Nerys from a girl, sat in that chair every Friday from nineteen seventy-something, and Nerys'd have done little Idris too, the boys came in with the mams in those days, she did everyone's, the whole — " Dil flaps a hand at the window, at the square, at the town, at the decades. "Everyone. You'll see it on half the over-fifties from here to Aberaeron if you know to look. It was a *fashion,* love. Like a perm. It was just hers."

She drinks her coffee, pleased with herself, with the morning, with the inexhaustible richness of a town she has been reading for forty-one years.

"Why," she says, "you thinking of getting it done? You'd want to go to Cardigan now, mind. There's nobody here can do it since Nerys went into herself. That look died with the salon." A small click of the tongue, the warm reflexive elegy the town keeps on tap. "Shame. It was lovely, the Pryce brow. You knew a woman'd been *looked after,* you saw that brow."

* * *

Goldie says something. She doesn't, after, know what. *Lovely. No. Maybe Cardigan, then.* Something to put in Dil's hand so Dil will go on being Dil and not start, even for a moment, wondering why the lost-property woman has gone quiet over an eyebrow.

Because Dil has just, in the time it takes to drink half a cup of bad coffee, handed her the thing she has spent three nights not letting herself reach.

*She gave it to everyone.*

The brow is not blood. The brow on Olwen, the brow on Idris, the brow on the seventeen-year-old in the square — they are not pieces of a dead girl come down a bloodline. They are the *same brow* because they were all *made the same,* by the same hand, in the same chair, over thirty years — a woman with a look and a thumb, painting one arch onto a town's worth of foreheads, Friday after Friday, the over-fifties of two villages walking around to this day wearing a fashion she set before the millennium. The brow is not inheritance. The brow is *craft.* It is a perm. It is a haircut. It is the one thing about a face that a person with skill and a steady hand and thirty years can put there, on anyone, at any age, exact —

which is exactly why it turns up exact at every age, on the young and the old alike, *behaving like nothing blood would ever behave like.*

And the peak does not. And the eyes do not. The peak and the eyes keep to the children, born and structural, scattered the way blood scatters, a piece here and a piece there, never whole, never to everyone. You cannot draw a peak on. You cannot give a seventeen-year-old the precise downward point of a hairline that grew its own. No hand, no thumb, no thirty years of fashion put Carys's eyes on Rhodri. *That* came down. That is the line.

Two things. Two channels. The made and the born, walking the same square this morning, one of them on a plumber and a churchwarden and the other only ever on the children — and Goldie stands behind her counter with a navy sock in her hand and understands, with the whole cold machinery she has never once been able to switch off, that she has been told the shape of it, plainly, by a woman licking lost-property tags, and that the understanding sits in her like a swallowed stone, because if it's true — if the brow is *made* and the eyes are *born* — then the clean story she built in the dark three nights ago is half a story, and the half she boxed off in the corner marked *noise* was never noise at all. It was the other half. It was the half that proves there are two of them.

She does not write it down.

She wants to. The hand wants the pen the way the hand at the café wanted the girl's chin. She wants to take the flip-chart sheet out from behind the dentures and unroll it and draw the line straight through the middle — *made* on this side, *born* on that — and she does not, because to draw it is to admit the story she's in love with is wrong, and she is not, yet, this minute, with the boiler resuming its slow disappointment and Dil sailing off down the building on her rails, ready to be wrong out loud. She has been wrong out loud before. There was a man, and a book, and her hand on it. *Never the other way round again.* She finds out first. Then she says.

So she puts the sock on the shelf. Age 6–8, navy, a dragger, the heel gone paper-thin. She writes it in the ledger in her small forensic hand. *Sock, child's, navy, single. STATUS: unclaimed.* A sock she can write all day. The hand will give you a sock, a goggle, a single armband, the whole green inventory of a town's small losses, and never once give you the word *made.*

And then she stands there, and she lets herself, just for a moment, have the one piece of it she can bear — not the whole crack, not the falling-through, just the one usable edge of the thing. If the brow can be faked and the eyes cannot, then there is a way to be sure. There is a way to cut the made from the born without a single guess. You find a fragment that no hand on earth could put there. You find the thing a thumb can't draw — a tooth that came in crooked, a gap that was never closed, a piece of a face so plainly grown that there's no salon, no fashion, no thirty years of Friday that explains it — and you hold it against the photograph, and if it's *the same,* then it isn't made, it isn't a perm, it isn't a look that died with a shut salon.

It's blood. And blood is a line. And a line has a beginning.

* * *

The girl with the peak and the brow does not come back across the square. Goldie watches for her until two, the way you watch a junction after you've seen one near-miss, and she does not come, and the salon's blind windows hold their breath across the way. The woman with the thumb made a town look after itself for thirty years and then went into herself and shut the door — and the brow lived on without her, going about the square on borrowed foreheads, exact, ownerless, a fashion that outlasted the woman the way a perfume outlasts the room.

A made thing keeps. That's the trouble with a made thing. It keeps just like a born thing keeps. From across a square, in the morning light, on a man going for a prescription, you cannot, with the naked eye, tell the difference between a face you were *given* and a face you were *grown.*

Goldie can.

That is the whole rotten miracle of it, and for the first time since the boy leaned over her counter wearing somebody's dead eyes, she thinks it might, God help her, be useful.

---

> **FACE 0009** — male, 44, plumber (IDRIS, Beti's boy, Maes-yr-Awel). Square, ~09:40, claims nothing — collecting a dead mother's stopped prescriptions. Grey one side. A back.
> *The brow — lift, taper, inner arch — exact. Carys's, to the millimetre.*
> But the face is forty-four. cf. **0004** (Olwen, 73). Same brow, two strangers, thirty years apart. Both decades too old to be hers by blood.
> Dil, unprompted: *"That's the Pryce brow. Nerys gave it to everyone."* A fashion. A salon. Made.
> So the brow is NOT the eyes. The brow goes anywhere, any age, exact. The eyes, the peak — only the young, only scattered. **Two things, not one.**
> Next: find a fragment a thumb couldn't draw. The born ones can't be faked.
> **STATUS: UNPLACED.**

---

# CHAPTER 11 — Lowri's Gap

You cannot draw a tooth-gap on. That is the thought that has been carrying Goldie across the square since half past two, the thought she went to bed with and woke up still holding, the way you wake holding a phrase from a song you didn't choose.

A brow, you can do. A brow is a decision a woman makes in front of a mirror twice a week, a line lifted, a tail thinned, an arch raised to a place the bone never put it; Goldie has spent a fortnight now watching the Pryce brow ride past her counter on faces that could not possibly share a grandmother, on a churchwarden of seventy-three and a man of forty-four with the brow sat over his eyes like something borrowed for a wedding. A cut, you can do. A laugh, it turns out, you can apparently catch off a chair the way you catch a cold. But the gap between two front teeth — the actual width of it, the specific generous quarter-inch of dark that a particular dead girl let show every time she found the world funny — that is not a service Nerys Pryce or anyone else could ever have sold across a counter. You are born into a gap or you are not. So if Goldie can find Carys's gap on a living mouth, untouched, never closed, then she will finally have her hands on the one thing a salon could not make. She will have a fragment that can only have been *grown.*

She has been looking for it for two days. She has been looking, if she is honest, the way she used to work a wanted board: not hoping, exactly. Hunting.

She finds it in the Mermaid Café at the worst possible moment, which is to say while it is laughing.

* * *

The Mermaid is the size of a generous hallway and run on the principle that the coffee is a punishment and the bacon is the reward, and that a person who has come through the one has earned the other. Carwyn presides over it the way a lighthouse keeper presides over weather — necessary, immovable, faintly disappointed in the sea. There is a hatch through to a kitchen the council would close if the council ever came, a chalkboard advertising a Special that has read CAWL £4.50 since before Goldie arrived and possibly since the flood, and four formica tables, three of them holding the wreckage of a late breakfast and one of them holding a baby.

The baby is the loud one. The baby is perhaps two, harnessed into a high chair that has done forty years' service and resents every minute of it, and the baby has been given a teaspoon and a saucer and has discovered percussion. He is conducting. He is conducting with total seriousness, the way the very young do everything, and the woman wiping the next table along is laughing at him over her shoulder, a proper laugh, head going back, and—

There.

Goldie does not decide to see it. She never decides. It arrives the way the man at the turnstile arrived on her first morning, the way they all arrive, weather over a field: the woman tips her face up to laugh at her son and her top lip lifts and there, exactly there, between the two front teeth, is the gap.

Carys Pryce's gap. To the millimetre. Goldie has it from the 2003 gala photograph the way she has everything, frozen and filed, a sixteen-year-old in the third row caught mid-grin with the same dark generous quarter-inch showing, the same slightly forward set to the lateral incisors that frames it, the same — and this is the part that puts Goldie's stomach somewhere near her shoes — the same *untouched* quality, the edges of the two front teeth ever so faintly chipped and uneven where a life has happened to them, nothing filed flat, nothing veneered shut, nothing *done.* A mouth that no orthodontist was ever sent for. A mouth that grew this way and was left this way and is, right now, in a café that smells of bacon and burnt milk, laughing at a baby with a teaspoon.

And the smile around it is Carys's smile. Not the gap alone — the whole shape the gap sits in: the way the lift is uneven, slightly higher on the left, so the grin always looks as if it's deciding whether to commit. Goldie has watched that exact unevenness on a dead girl in a photograph for a fortnight. She is now watching it move.

"Two minutes for the bacon," says the woman, to Goldie, because Goldie is standing in the doorway like a draught. "Carwyn's doing it fresh, which means he forgot. Sit anywhere that isn't him." A nod at the baby. "He's not for sale, mind, no matter what he tells you."

Her name, Goldie gets from the apron, which says LOWRI in marker over a fading printed MERMAID, and from Dil, later, who will supply the rest the way Dil supplies everything, unasked and approximately accurate. Lowri. Twenty-two, going by the skin and the tiredness and the particular way a young woman carries a body that has recently grown and surrendered a person; a worn wedding band that Goldie clocks is on the wrong hand, the right, which is a thing people do when a ring matters and the marriage didn't last to match it; a tattoo of a swallow on the inside of one wrist, amateur, a teenager's, half-regretted. A swimmer's shoulders, once. The cowlick fought into a bun. And the gap. The gap is the thing the whole rest of the face is arranged around, and the face does not know it.

"Ta," says Goldie, and sits, and the baby looks at her with frank professional interest and offers her the teaspoon, which she declines, and which he therefore throws.

* * *

Here is the trouble with the gap, and Goldie knows it before the bacon comes.

The trouble is that it is exactly what she has been hunting, and that she does not get to enjoy it, because a fragment you cannot fake is also a fragment that means something, and meaning is the thing she got wrong once with a man's whole life in her hands. A brow she can hold at arm's length. A brow is *made* — she has decided this, over the fortnight, sitting in the dark with her ledger — and made things lie. But the gap is *born.* The gap is bone and growth and the lottery of which face a person is dealt, and the only way Carys Pryce's exact dealt face turns up, untouched and laughing, in a café twenty-two years after Carys Pryce stopped having a face at all, is the way faces always travel: down. Through. Into bodies that came after.

There is no salon that closes that loop. There is only blood.

She makes herself not lean forward. This is a discipline she has, the not-leaning; it is the difference between a woman doing the crossword and a woman working a case, and she would rather Lowri saw the first. She picks up the laminated menu she does not need and reads CAWL £4.50 with the deep attention of a scholar.

"You're the new one," says Lowri, coming back with the bacon — a bap the size of a small dog, the bacon genuinely, scandalously good, Dil was not exaggerating about the holiness of it — and not asking, the way the whole town does not ask, the way they have all already decided who you are and are only confirming the spelling. "Up the Pelican. The faces woman."

Goldie's hand does not stop on the way to the bap. Twelve years of training. "The lost-property woman," she says.

"Pam says you're a faces woman. Pam says you were a detective." Lowri delivers Pam's intelligence with the flat scepticism of a person who has known Pam her whole life and has accordingly developed antibodies. "Pam also says the council's putting fluoride in the lido to make us compliant, so." She shrugs, which is the local way of finishing that sentence. "You want sauce, there's red and there's brown, the brown's gone a bit to glue."

"Red," says Goldie, who would, frankly, agree to almost anything to keep this woman standing at this table, laughing at intervals, showing the gap.

It is not, she tells herself, the worst way she has ever surveilled a person. She once spent eleven hours of a Tuesday matching one balaclava across forty seconds of CCTV. This is a bacon bap and a baby. This is, by the standards of her old life, a holiday.

* * *

The baby is called Macsen, which Lowri pronounces with the resigned grandeur of a woman who let her ex name the child and has regretted it in the registrar's queue ever since, and Macsen, having got nothing out of Goldie, has turned his attentions to the lost property, because lost property follows Goldie around the way iron filings follow a magnet, even here, even off duty.

What has happened is that a soft toy — a knitted, ambitious, possibly-octopus — has been left behind by some earlier family on the windowsill, and Macsen has spotted it, and Macsen wants it with the clean total wanting of his age, and is making this known. Lowri retrieves it without breaking stride, without looking, a mother's radar, and inspects it for hygiene with an expression of profound mistrust before surrendering it.

"Some kiddie'll be crying for that tonight," she says. "Half of Aberglas is crying for something it left at the Mermaid. You should see what Carwyn's got out the back." She jerks her head at the kitchen. "Box of it. Dummies, mostly. One shoe. A set of teeth."

"Always a set of teeth," says Goldie, with feeling.

Lowri laughs — *there,* the gap, the uneven lift, higher on the left — and Goldie files it again, the live one over the dead one, the match holding, the way a key holds in a lock you'd convinced yourself was changed.

"My nan's are in a tub up the cupboard, God rest her," says Lowri, comfortable now, leaning a hip on the table, baby on the other one, the way she'd talk to anyone, because Goldie has done the one thing Goldie is worst at and the one thing that works, which is to be a quiet place a person can put words. "Couldn't throw them. Daft. You can't throw teeth." She says it lightly and it lands in Goldie like a stone in a well, because it is the truest thing anyone has said to her in a fortnight and the woman saying it has no idea, no idea on earth, that she is wearing a dead girl's teeth and could not throw them away if she tried, because they are, irrevocably, hers.

"You can't throw teeth," Goldie agrees.

* * *

She gets the photographs the way she gets everything she actually needs, which is sideways and by accident, by being dull enough that a person reaches for their phone to liven her up.

It is Macsen who does it. Macsen, bored of the octopus, reaches for his mother's apron pocket where the phone lives, because the phone is the thing the giants are always looking at and is therefore the best thing, and Lowri, fishing it out before he can post it into the bacon, says "you want to see a proper handful," and turns it round, and there they are. The whole tangle of them. A small town's worth of family, thumbnailed and scrolling, christenings and a barbecue and a paddling pool and somebody's wedding and a great many babies who are all, to the untrained eye, the same baby.

They are not, to Goldie's eye, the same baby.

She does not ask to hold the phone. She does the thing she does, which is look once, completely, at the speed of a shutter, and keep it. A boy at the edge of a barbecue photo with a widow's peak that drops to a point Goldie has seen drop to that exact point on the Beavan boy outside Eira's; *cf. 0019, born, not made.* A girl, fourteen perhaps, half-turned from the camera at a christening, and the line of her jaw — Goldie banks it without a name and without a place, *young, again, always young* — and a flicker of something at the corner of the eye that she will not let herself finish, not over a bacon bap, not yet. And Lowri's own face at sixteen, in a swim cap, on a podium, third place, grinning the grin with the gap wide open and the whole future of her in it, before Macsen, before the apron, before the ring went onto the wrong hand.

"That's me," says Lowri, catching where Goldie's looking, and there is in her voice the particular tenderness people keep for the version of themselves that still thought it was going somewhere. "Hundred metres. I was quick, once. We were all in the water, our lot — you had to be, round here, or what else is there." She scrolls on, fond. "Half of these are cousins. You lose track. We're all related round here, aren't we, if you go back far enough. My gran used to say if you marry an Aberglas boy you want to draw a chart first." She laughs again, easy, the gap, the lift. "There's worse places to be everyone's cousin, mind. Nobody goes uncwtched."

*We're all related round here.* She says it the way you'd say the tide comes in, a fact too obvious to weigh, a small joke about a small town, and Goldie sits very still with a bap in her two hands and the floor of the Mermaid holds steady under her, which it would not have on her first morning, which is its own kind of progress, and she thinks: *truer than you know. Truer, cariad, than anyone in this room could stand to know with the lights on.*

Because the chart Lowri's gran joked about is real, and Goldie is, slowly, against her will and her better judgement and the entire reason she took the quietest job in Wales, beginning to draw it.

* * *

It is the last thing Lowri says that does it, that turns the screw, and she says it as an aside, the way the worst news always comes — not announced, just dropped, on the way to clearing the saucer.

Macsen has gone over. Not far — the high chair holds him — but he's flung himself sideways after the octopus and gone briefly upside down and come up purple with outrage, and Lowri rights him with one hand and says, to him, not to Goldie, in the rote exhausted singsong of a tired mother, "Oh, you're all right, you're all right, you're not your Auntie Lowri, you don't get to keel over for attention."

"Keel over?" Goldie says it before she can decide not to. The not-leaning fails. Twelve years and it fails over a baby and a teaspoon.

"My cousin. Other Lowri — there's two of us, course there is, this town." Lowri rolls her eyes, settling Macsen, not feeling the weight of any of it. "She faints. Always has, since she was a kid. Goes down like the lights went off — at the school sports, once, halfway down the track, scared the life out of everyone. Doctor says it's nothing. Says it runs in the family, some of them just do it, low blood pressure or growing too fast or — I don't know. Half the Beynon side's done it at one time or another. You learn to catch them." She says it the way you'd mention a family chin, a family temper, a thing that gets handed down with the photographs. "Macsen here'll probably do it and all, knowing his luck. Won't you. You'll keel over at your own wedding."

She kisses the top of the baby's head, where the cowlick is, the same cowlick, fought into the same swirl, and Goldie sits with the bacon going cold in her hands and does not write anything down, because there is nothing in this café to write with that would not look exactly like what it is.

*It runs in the family.* The gap and the faint. The face and the falling. Born together, travelling together, down the same line, into the same children — the ones who carry her smile are the ones who go down like the lights went off, and not one doctor in twenty-two years has thought to ask *whose* family, because nobody knew the family had a ghost in it, and the ghost is the only one who ever found out what the falling meant, alone in a back room with her heart, on a night this town decided to call running away.

Goldie does not have the word for it. She will not have the word for chapters yet. But she has, now, the shape of it, cold and clean and complete the way they come: the gap that cannot be drawn on, and the faint that comes with it, are the same inheritance. You do not get the one without the risk of the other. The face is the warning. Nobody read the warning.

"Right-o," says Lowri, oblivious, lovely, doomed in a way she has no notion of, stacking the saucer onto the cup. "You've gone quiet on me. Was the bacon all right?"

"The bacon," says Goldie, with total honesty, because she has learned that the only safe ground is the literal, "is the best thing in this town."

"Don't tell Carwyn. He's insufferable enough." Lowri tucks the phone away, hoists the baby, and gives Goldie the gap one last time, full and uneven and entirely her own and entirely not, over her shoulder, on her way to the kitchen, like a thing handed across twenty-two years by a girl who never got to grow up and never got to know she'd had a daughter at all, let alone a town full of grandchildren laughing in cafés with her mouth.

The bell over the door goes as a family comes in out of the wind, and the Mermaid swallows them, warm and loud and crying-for-something, and Goldie puts a fiver under the saucer and leaves before her own face does anything she'd have to explain.

* * *

> **FACE 0011** — female, 22 (LOWRI), Mermaid Café. The smile, exact: lift uneven, higher L. **The gap — NATURAL. Never closed. Edges chipped, untouched. No orthodontist, ever.**
> = BORN. cf. **0004** (Olwen, 73, brow) / **0009** (Idris, 44, brow) = MADE.
> Two kinds of carrier. One you can buy. One you cannot.
> This is the one you cannot. Proof.
> Also: a cousin who "keels over." "Runs in the family." "Half the Beynon side." The faint follows the *born* ones. Always has.
> **STATUS: UNPLACED — but no longer unplaceable.**

---

# CHAPTER 12 — Carol-Anne's Laugh

Bingo at the Pelican is run the way other places run a reactor, and Maureen is the rods.

Goldie understands this within ninety seconds of the function room, which has been transformed since the closure meeting from a place where a town grieved into a place where a town concentrates. The dust sheets are off the cage. The little numbered balls go round in their wire drum with a sound like the sea trying to remember something. Forty-odd people sit in the arcs of stacked chairs with their books squared and their dabbers uncapped, and the air over all of them is thick with the particular reverence of folk who have come, on a wet Thursday, to win eleven pounds and a meat voucher off each other and would, every one of them, fight you for it in the car park.

She is here under the flag of the records. This is the lie she has settled into — that she is *making the case for the building,* photographing the old galas, getting the story to tell — and like all her good lies it is true enough to wear in public. The actual reason she is here is on the seat beside her, rolled and rubber-banded, the flip-chart sheet with its green constellation and its one grey-boxed shame, and she has brought it because she has reached the part of the work where she needs the room. She needs to stand in a crowd of the town's faces and *sort* them. Born or made. Blood or craft. Lowri's gap, never closed, sitting on one side of a line; Olwen's brow, and Idris's brow, and the whole high surprised arch of it that Dil swears Nerys *gave to everyone* — sitting on the other. Two columns. She has half a column each and a hunger for the rule that fills them.

"You'll keep the score," says Maureen, putting a clipboard in front of her with the finality of a sacrament. It is not the records score. It is the bingo score, the prize ledger, names against amounts, and Goldie looks at it and understands she has been conscripted again, sideways, the way she is always conscripted, and that there is no version of the evening where she gets to merely watch.

"I'm meant to be doing the photos," she says.

"You can do the photos when you've done the score. Glenys did the score last week and we had a *situation.*"

"It was *legs eleven,*" calls Glenys, from two seats down, on the good ear's wrong side, so that she has it slightly late and slightly loud. "I said *eggs eleven* the once and you'd think I'd —"

"You said it four times, Glenys."

"— you'd think I'd robbed the till. *Eggs.* It's a number, isn't it, eleven, whatever you put in front of it." Glenys settles her cardigan over her swim-cap hair with great dignity. "I'd have given them the eggs."

Goldie takes the clipboard. She finds, to her irritation, that the small involuntary upward thing is happening to her face again, and she lets it, because there is nobody at a bingo night who needs her to be sure of anything, which is the whole point of the arrangement, and the arrangement, for once tonight, is holding.

* * *

The caller is a man named Selwyn who has, Dil informs her in a stage whisper that travels the length of the room, *been doing the numbers since the foot-and-mouth* and is therefore beyond criticism, beyond hurry, and beyond the reach of the dead microphone, which whines once and dies and is ignored, the room having long ago agreed to do it the old way. He calls in Welsh and English both, the numbers sliding between languages without a seam, *dau ddeg saith, two-and-seven, twenty-seven,* and the dabbers come down in their soft little volleys, and Goldie keeps her score and reads her room and does not, for whole minutes at a time, think about a girl in a 2003 photograph.

She is three games in, sorting faces almost idly now — *that one's blood-or-not-at-all, never been near a salon in her life; that one's the brow, made, file her; that one's nothing, thank God, a face that is only its own* — when the laugh comes across the room and stops her hand on the page.

She does not see who it is first. That is the thing she will keep, after — that the laugh arrives before the face, the way the eyes arrived before the boy, the way it always comes at her in the wrong order, the recognition first and the person trailing behind it like a kite. A laugh, three rows up and to the left, over the call of *legs eleven* and Glenys's muttered loyalty to the eggs: a quick rising thing, three notes and a catch, a laugh that ducks its own head halfway through as if embarrassed to have started, and tilts — *tilts,* the chin going down and to the right, the whole head canting on the neck, shy and bright at once.

Goldie has heard that laugh before.

She has never heard that laugh before in her life.

She knows it the way she knows a face. Not the sound of it exactly — sound is the soft sense, the fallible one, she has never trusted her ears the way she trusts her eyes — but the *shape* the body makes around it, the choreography, the duck and the tilt and the rising three notes and the catch, the geometry of a gesture as exact as the geometry of a brow. She has held this before. She has given it the full forensic weight and filed it, and the drawer it comes from is the same drawer the eyes came from, the same drawer the cheekbones came from, the old wrong drawer that is full of 2003 and a girl in the third row, and what surfaces, clean and cold and complete, is not a name and not a date but a single flat fact she would have sworn was impossible:

*That is Carys Pryce laughing.*

She turns in her seat, slow, the way you turn toward a sound you hope you imagined, and finds the face that goes with it, and the face is sixty-one years old.

* * *

"Carol-Anne Beddoe," says Dil, before Goldie has asked, because Dil reads the turn of a head the way Goldie reads the head itself. "Sixty-one. Did the flowers at St. Cynog's till her knees went. Lovely woman, no harm in her, lost her Brian to the heart —" a small reflexive Welsh blessing, half-swallowed "— three years back, give or take. Why, what's she done?"

"Nothing," says Goldie. "She laughed."

"She does that," says Dil, comfortably, missing the entire weight of it, and reaches across to dab a number on her own card. "Always has. Carol-Anne'd laugh at a funeral. Did, in fact, at her own Brian's — set the whole chapel off, mind, which is what he'd have wanted, he was a terror." Dil chuckles, and her chuckle is just a chuckle, her own, nothing in it, and that is somehow the thing that makes the hair stand up on Goldie's arms — that the woman three rows up is laughing with a dead girl's throat and the woman beside Goldie is laughing with her own, and only Goldie can hear the difference, and the difference is twenty-two years deep.

Because the laugh is *wrong* in the way the eyes were wrong, and it is wrong in a worse way, and Goldie sits very still with the bingo clipboard on her knee and lets herself, finally, name what the worse way is.

The eyes were bone. A widow's peak is bone, and cheekbones are bone, and a tooth-gap a salon never closed is the absence of bone where a body grew without it — those she could understand, in the end, even the impossibility of them, because bone comes down a bloodline whether anyone wills it or not, a face scattering through children like coins down the back of a sofa. Born. You cannot help what bone you are given.

But a laugh is not bone.

A laugh is *learned.* Nobody is born laughing in three rising notes with a duck and a tilt to the right. You catch a laugh the way you catch an accent — off the person you sit nearest, week on week, year on year, until their music is in your body and you have no memory of a time it wasn't. Carol-Anne Beddoe was not *given* Carys Pryce's laugh by any bloodline. Carol-Anne Beddoe was given it by *sitting somewhere,* a long time, near someone, often enough that a dead girl's gesture took root in a living woman's throat and grew there for two decades and laughed, just now, over the bingo, at a number with eleven in it.

And Goldie thinks: *where do you sit, week on week, year on year, near the same person, long enough to catch how they hold their head.*

She thinks: *Olwen had the brow. Idris had the brow. The brow is everywhere, Dil said. Nerys gave it to everyone.*

She thinks: *the chair. The salon chair.*

* * *

"You've gone a colour," Dil observes. "Is it the heat? It's the heat. They never fix the heat and then they put forty people in here breathing."

"I'm grand," says Goldie. "Dil. Carol-Anne. Did she go to Nerys? The salon. Pryce of Aberglas."

"Go to Nerys." Dil says it the way you'd repeat a question with an obvious answer to give yourself time to be amazed by it. "Cariad, *everyone* went to Nerys. There wasn't another salon to go to, not for thirty years, not unless you fancied the bus to Aberaeron and a girl who didn't know your head. Carol-Anne was Nerys's *Friday,* near enough — half ten, every Friday, set and a natter, the pair of them thick as —" Dil stops. Something moves behind her eyes, some soft human recalculation, the kind that arrives in her wrong on the date and right on the feeling. "Well. Before. Before Nerys went into herself. After the girl, you know. After Carys. She let it all go after that, the Friday crowd, the lot. But for *years,* love. Carol-Anne sat in that chair for years."

"For years," says Goldie.

"What's this about, now?" Dil's eyes are on her properly, the gossip-engine idling, sensing a richer seam. "You've that look. You had that look about the photographs. Marigold, if you're going to go funny about Carol-Anne Beddoe of all the harmless —"

"It's the records," says Goldie, which is the lie, the good warm true-enough lie, and Dil, who wants it to be the records so she can go back to her card, lets it be the records.

But Goldie has stopped sorting her room into born and made.

She has started sorting it into *who went to Nerys,* and the answer, the cold completing answer she did not earn and cannot unthink, is that the made ones — the brow, the brow, and now the laugh — every single one of them, all of them, sat in the same chair. The craft has a *door.* The craft has an address, a half-past-ten on a Friday, a woman with scissors and thirty years and a habit Goldie cannot yet name of giving the town a dead girl back one head at a time. The brow she could call fashion. The laugh she cannot. You do not do a woman's *laugh* by accident.

*Born ones come down the blood,* she thinks. *Made ones come through the door.*

It is the cleanest thing she has thought in a fortnight, and clean, she has learned, the expensive way, is exactly what she is meant to distrust. But this one she cannot box off in a grey corner. This one has a Friday and a witness and a woman three rows up laughing with somebody else's mouth.

* * *

"*Eyes down,*" calls Selwyn, "*for a full house,*" and the room goes taut, and into the taut silence Pam leans across two people, the way Pam leans, and hisses at Goldie with the fervour of the truly informed:

"You heard it too. The laugh. I *saw* you hear it."

"Pam," says Maureen, not turning her head, on instinct, the way you'd say *down* to a dog.

"I'm only saying. There's a *thing* in this building." Pam's eyes are bright and entirely sincere, and Goldie has learned, this fortnight, that Pam is wrong about almost everything and occasionally, terribly, right, and never able to tell which. "My nan would've called it a *fetch.* When the dead come back wearing the living. You laugh like the dead and it's a sign, that's what my nan —"

"It's a *sign* you've had two sherries before the bingo, Pam," says Maureen.

"— a sign somebody walked over a grave. *Somebody's grave.* You mark me. There's been a face going round this town that's no business going round, and now there's a laugh, and you can call me what you like, Maureen Probert, but I notice things."

And Goldie, who notices things for a living, who has a rolled-up sheet on the seat beside her that is a more careful catalogue of exactly the thing Pam is babbling about than Pam will ever produce, sits with her cold completing fact in her throat and thinks that the most unbearable part of Pam is how close she gets while being entirely wrong, like a woman describing the colour of a sound. *The dead come back wearing the living.* It is nearly it. It is so nearly it that Goldie has to look at the bingo cage, at the small mortal numbers going round and round, to keep her own face from giving Pam the satisfaction.

"Nobody's walked over a grave," she says.

"*House!*" shouts Carol-Anne Beddoe, three rows up, delighted, and does the laugh again, the whole bright ducking tilt of it, throwing her dabber down in triumph, and the room groans and turns and a dead girl laughs out of a sixty-one-year-old throat across a function room full of the people who loved her and never knew her, and Goldie writes the win on the clipboard with a steady hand because that is what you do, you keep the line moving, and her own face does not move at all.

* * *

Afterward, in the lull while Maureen adjudicates a disputed line with the cold authority of The Hague, Goldie goes out to the step.

The smoking step is round the side, between the function room and the dark mass of the old lido, and nobody is on it but the cold and the sea-smell coming up over the chlorine and the fried-onion ghost of the urn. She does not smoke. She came out to stop her face being in a room. And it is there, on the step, that she finds the thing the building keeps handing her whether she asks or not — because propped against the wall by the fire door, in a stack of damp boxes labelled *FUNCTION RM — CLEAR,* somebody mid-clearance has turned up the salon's leavings, the way a dying building turns up everything, and on the top of the nearest box, swollen with damp and curled at the corner, is an appointment book.

*PRYCE OF ABERGLAS,* it says on the cover, in a hand that did a thousand fringes. A year she can't read. She opens it because she cannot not, and the pages are a ruin of pencil, half gone to mould, columns of half-tens and elevens and names she is starting, God help her, to know — and there, on a Friday she can still make out, in the salon's own hand, a name written so many Fridays it has worn a groove in the woman who wrote it: *Carol-Anne. Set.* And below it, and above it, and threaded all through the book like a refrain, the other names, the brow names, the made names, all of them, kept, weekly, for thirty years, in a hand that gave them something across that page she is only now learning to read.

She does not take the book inside. She stands on the step with it open in the cold and understands that she has been looking for the rule behind *made* and that the rule is sitting in her hands, in pencil, in half-tens on a Friday — that there was a chair in this town, and a woman in it, and everyone who wears the dead girl came through that door and sat in that chair and got given a piece of her, on purpose or otherwise, and that the woman who held the scissors is the next face she has to find.

Behind her the function room roars — Glenys has called *eggs* again, gloriously, on purpose now, a woman who has decided to own her crime — and the warmth of it comes through the fire door at Goldie's back, the whole loud surviving town winning meat vouchers off each other an arm's length away, and she stands in the cold with the appointment book and does not go back to the noise just yet.

She closes the book on the worn Friday name and holds it shut, both hands, the way you'd hold a door.

---

> **FACE 0014** — female, 61 (CAROL-ANNE BEDDOE). Function room, bingo. Carys's *laugh* — three notes, the duck, the tilt right — and the head-cant, exact. NOT a feature. A gesture. I have held it before; it is hers; it is twenty-two years old and it is sixty-one years old at once.
> *You don't inherit a laugh. You learn it. Sat near it. Week on week.* cf. 0004 (OLWEN, brow), 0009 (IDRIS, brow) — all MADE.
> Nerys's Friday, thirty years. The brow, the brow, the laugh — every made one came through the same door.
> Born comes down the blood. Made comes through the salon.
> **STATUS: UNPLACED — MADE.** Find who held the scissors.

---

# CHAPTER 13 — Some Girls Don't Run

St. Cadog's hospice has done the thing hospices do, which is to spend a great deal of money making a place look as though no one in it is dying.

There are watercolours. There is a fish tank in reception with one optimistic angelfish and a great many pebbles. There is a smell laid down over the other smell — something floral and corporate, a meadow that has been to a meeting — and Goldie clocks it the way she clocks everything, against her will and for no money, and files it under *lies we agree to.* The carpet is the blue councils buy when they want a colour that won't show anything. She knows that blue. She has spent her life in buildings the colour of not showing anything.

She signs the visitors' book. She does it slowly, because the visitors' book is the only thing in the building she actually wants, and a woman who wants a thing must be careful not to look at it directly. *Time in. Name. Visiting.* Three columns and a Biro on a chain, and her eyes go down the page the way water goes downhill, taking the lot, and she does not let her hand pause once.

"She's having a good morning," says the girl on the desk, twenty-three, a fringe she cuts herself, a lanyard, kind. "She had her hair done. Wednesdays a lady comes in and does the hair, isn't that nice."

"Lovely," says Goldie, who is reading the page.

"I got her the good biscuits in, mind," the girl adds, not to be answered, just to say it, the way the young say a thing they're quietly proud of. "The proper ones, the foil ones, not the rich tea — she did a face at the rich tea, you've never seen a face like it on a woman, and I thought, well, that won't do, will it. She has two with her cup and tells me off for spoiling her and has a third." She smiles, and it is a real smile, no edge on it, a girl who has decided to love a stranger for the few weeks she'll have her, and Goldie, who arms everything, finds she has nothing to arm against it, and lets it be exactly what it is. "She's a love when she's not telling you off. You'll see."

Most of the names are family. You learn to spot family in a visitors' book the way you spot it in a face — they come back, they come often, they stop writing the surname after the third visit because the desk knows them now. And then there are the others, the once-or-twice names, and Goldie takes those too, and one of them is up near the top of last week, neat capitals, a hand that has signed a great many official documents in its time and learned to make the letters carry, *G. HOLLIS,* and under *Visiting* it says, in the same careful hand, *a friend.*

She does not pause. The pen does the not-pausing for her. The pen is very good at its job.

"Down the end," says the girl. "Past the day room. You'll hear the telly."

* * *

You do hear the telly.

The day room is four armchairs the colour of stewed tea and a television the size of a small allotment, and on the television, at a volume calibrated for a room of people who have stopped being asked what they want, a man in a blazer is auctioning a commemorative thimble to a studio of pensioners who would, you sense, give anything to be anywhere else, which they have in common with everyone watching. Two residents sit before it like stones in a stream. Neither is looking at it. The thimble goes for eleven pounds. Nobody in the room or out of it feels anything at all, and the man in the blazer is delighted for all of them.

And in the corner chair, half-turned from the screen, in a cardigan the soft violet of a sweet you'd find at the bottom of a handbag, is a Belle.

"Don't you say a word," says Pam, who has the conspiratorial calm of a woman who has done nothing wrong and intends to keep it that way through superior preparation. There is a flask between her feet. There is, Goldie can see, very much not tea in the flask, because Pam has not bothered to disguise the smell, on the grounds that nobody under this roof is going to report her and most of them would want some. "Visiting. I'm allowed to visit. It's a free country, last I heard, though you'd not know it from the parking."

"Pam."

"She likes a drop of the sherry," says Pam, lowering her voice to the register of national security. "Branwen. Always has. And they give them nothing in here but the squash and that wicked tea, and a woman that's dying is entitled, Goldie, she is *entitled,* to a small sweet sherry of an afternoon, and if the powers that be don't like it they can come and have words with me." She says this last bit at a slightly raised volume, in the general direction of the corridor, on the off-chance the powers that be are listening and fancy their odds.

"You're a good woman, Pam," says Goldie, and means it, and Pam, who is braced for an argument, looks briefly disarmed, the way certain people do when you give them the thing they were going to fight you for.

"I'm a *menace,* is what I am, Maureen says so," Pam says, recovering. "Go on, she's been asking after you. Wouldn't say why. She's gone funny on me this last week, mind." The conspiracy drains out of her face for a second and something older comes up underneath, something that has sat in a lot of these chairs and watched a lot of these televisions auction a lot of these thimbles to people on their way out. "Funny like she's got something on her chest. You know how they get. They go to put it down before they go." She picks the flask up, screws the lid, unscrews it again, a small fretting of the hands. "I never can tell if it's a mercy, letting them. Putting a thing down."

It is, Goldie thinks, exactly the kind of sentence Pam doesn't know she's said, the way half the truest things in this town arrive — not announced, not underlined, dropped on the way past, on the way to the sherry.

"Go on in," says Pam. "And Goldie." The conspiracy comes back, because conspiracy is how Pam loves people, and she'd no more say the soft thing straight than fly. "If anyone asks, you never saw the flask."

* * *

Branwen is smaller than three weeks ago.

That is the first fact, and Goldie takes it the way she takes all first facts, completely. Three weeks ago the woman had been a presence in the bed, a column, a churchwarden of a person sitting upright against the truth. Now the bed has more of her than she has of herself; the blanket has won an argument. But the hair is *done* — set, brushed, a soft silver wave with a roll at the front, the work of a hand that knew exactly the shape it was making — and the contrast is so cruel and so kind at once that Goldie has to stand a second in the doorway and let it be.

The eyes, though. The eyes are all there. Branwen turns them on her and they are the same eyes that went still at a name three weeks ago, sharp as a tack pushed through paper, and they take Goldie in, and the mouth does something that isn't quite a smile but is in the same family.

"The woman who remembers," Branwen says. Her voice is a held thing now, rationed, each word counted out of a small reserve. "Sit, then. I can't be doing with people standing. Makes the place look like a funeral."

Goldie sits. The chair is the day-room chair's cousin, the same stewed tea. Through the window the sky over Cardigan Bay is doing its undecided thing, light and slate at once, and below it, two streets down and a world away, the curved back of the Pelican catches a bit of sun on its bad render, and beyond that the long grey-gold beach, and the harbour wall with the gulls on it like punctuation. From here you can see the whole of it. From here you can see the gala field at the side of the Pelican grounds where, in a fortnight, the town will hold its sponsored swim and its raffle and its last great gasp at staying open, the bunting already going up between the lamp standards, small and brave and far away.

"They've put the flags up," Branwen says, following her eyes. "For the do." A pause to spend a breath. "I used to run the cake stall. Forty years, the cake stall. Bara brith and a Victoria sponge and a tombola you could not win, I made sure of it, the prizes were *shocking.*" The not-quite-smile again. "You'll have come about Carys Pryce."

She says it plainly, in the open, the way you'd put a cup down on a table you'd decided to put it on.

Goldie has learned, in fifty-one years, that the worst thing you can do to a person who is finally about to say a thing is to look like you want them to say it. So she does the deflection she does best, the one that has kept the world at arm's length her whole life, and turns it, for once, to mercy — she goes dry, she goes sideways, she gives Branwen somewhere easy to put her feet.

"I've come about a lot of faces," Goldie says. "Carys Pryce's keeps turning up on people she never met. It's doing my head in, if I'm honest. I came here for a quiet life."
