"There's no quiet life," Branwen says, "for anyone who looks." She says it without weight, an old woman's flat remark, and Goldie, who knows an aphorism when one is being handed to her and declines them as a rule, finds she can't deflect this one, because it is simply true and the woman is simply dying and there is nothing clever to do with it. She lets it sit. Branwen watches her let it sit, and something settles in the old face, some small test passed.

"You see them, then," Branwen says. "The faces."

"I see them."

"On the young ones."

It is not a question, and the not-question lands in Goldie's chest like a stone going into still water, the rings going out and out. She keeps her voice flat as a filed page, the voice that has given nothing away across a thousand counters. "On the young ones," she agrees. "Mostly the young ones."

Branwen closes her eyes. For a moment Goldie thinks she's gone to sleep, that the reserve has run out mid-sentence, and she sits very still in the stewed-tea chair and listens to the thimble man two doors down getting eleven pounds for the next thing, and waits.

"I delivered half this town," Branwen says, eyes still shut. "Forty-one years a midwife. The Trust, and then the proper service when the Trust gave it up, and then back to the Trust at the end for the quiet ones, the ones nobody — " She stops. Spends a breath. Opens her eyes, and they are wet now, and furious about it. "You catch them. That's the word for it, *catching,* the old word. You catch them coming into the world. And you'd think, wouldn't you, you'd think that meant you got to *keep* them, after, the ones you caught. You'd think a thing like that would buy you the right to know how they all turned out." Her hand moves on the blanket, finds nothing to hold, lies still. "It doesn't. They go off into their lives and you never. You only ever catch them the once."

"Branwen," Goldie says. Gently. "Who did you catch?"

And the old woman looks at her, and Goldie watches the decision happen — watches it the way she watches everything, every muscle, the small abandonment around the mouth, the chin coming up, a churchwarden squaring herself one last time against the truth and then, slowly, deliberately, setting the truth *down* — and Branwen says:

"Some girls don't run."

Goldie goes very still.

"That's all I'll tell you," Branwen says, and there is steel in it now, the reserve spent on iron instead of words. "Because you're not the one to tell. You're a stranger, however much you see, and what I've got to say isn't yours to be given. It's *hers.*" Her eyes go to the window, to the bunting, to the gala field, to something out there. "There's a girl who ought to know whose she is. Before I'm gone. She's owed her own name, that one, and I'll not go into the ground with it in my mouth. I've carried it long enough. I'm putting it down."

*Hers.* A girl. A girl who ought to know *whose she is.*

The rings go out and out and out. Goldie sits in the blue carpet and the meadow-that-went-to-a-meeting and the thimble man's distant joy and feels the whole shape of it move under her, vast and slow, a thing turning over in deep water — *not who is this face but how is a dead girl alive in a child* — and the answer Branwen has just not-quite-given her, the answer in the word *whose,* is so simple and so enormous that for a second Goldie cannot make her mouth work, which is a new thing, for a woman who has spent twelve years using her mouth to keep her chest shut.

A child who is *owed her own name.*

You are only owed a name you were not given.

"Who," Goldie says, and it comes out wrong, it comes out cracked, she who is never cracked, and she clears her throat and tries to flatten it back down and it still won't go level. "Branwen. The girl. Just — point me. I'll not take it to her, I swear it, that's yours, but let me make sure she's — let me make sure nothing happens before you can — "

"I've a call to make," Branwen says, and there is something almost gentle in how she says it, the gentleness of a person who has decided and is at peace with deciding, the worst gentleness in the world. "Today. I've waited for a good morning and this is one, my hair's done and my head's clear and I'll not get many more. I'm going to ring her mother. Ask them up. Tomorrow, the day after. While I can still get the words in the right order." The not-quite-smile, and now it nearly makes it all the way. "You can stop fretting, woman. I'm not going to die before I've done the one good thing I've left to do. God wouldn't be so untidy."

* * *

Goldie does not, in the end, stop fretting.

She stays another twenty minutes because you do not leave a dying woman the second she's said the thing; she stays and lets Branwen tell her about the tombola you could not win and the Victoria sponge and a christening in 1979 where the baby was sick down the vicar's surplice and it was, Branwen maintains, the best christening the town has ever had. She laughs at the right places. She is good at the right places; it is the human part she has always been able to fake, the part that comes after the seeing. And the whole twenty minutes a clock she cannot hear is going somewhere behind the wall, and she does not know it, she only knows that her hands want something to do, and there is nothing on the blanket to hold.

When she leaves, the corridor has a second visitor in it.

Not a face she knows. A care assistant, mid-forties, badge, a trolley of the squash and the wicked tea, perfectly ordinary, doing rounds. But the door to Branwen's room is ajar where Goldie has left it ajar, and the trolley is parked outside it, and the woman is not in any hurry at all, and Goldie clocks her the way she clocks everyone, and files her under *staff, harmless,* and walks on, because that is the thing, that is the whole rotten thing — Goldie reads everyone, instantly and completely and forever, and a person you have filed under *harmless* is a person you have stopped looking at.

And as she passes the day room, where Pam is now openly sharing the flask with one of the stones-in-the-stream and the thimble man is auctioning a porcelain spaniel, she hears, through Branwen's ajar door, behind her, the small particular sound of an old woman picking up a telephone receiver — the click, the rising tone — and Branwen's voice starting up, low, careful, getting the words in the right order, *Marged, cariad, is that you, it's Bethan, don't fuss, I'm grand, I've something to* — and Goldie does not stop, because it is not hers, because she has sworn it is not hers, because a woman who has spent her life listening past doors is trying very hard to be a better woman than that.

She does not see who else is in the corridor to hear it.

She is too busy not listening.

* * *

She comes out into the day, and the day has decided to be bright, the slate burned off, and the gulls are up over the harbour wall, and she stands on the hospice step with her heart going and tries to think.

A girl. Fourteen, fifteen — Branwen said *that one,* looked at the field, looked at something young. A girl owed her own name. A girl the midwife caught and never kept and has watched, somehow, from a distance, for years, and cannot die without telling. The eyes are real. The eyes were always real. And now there is a person at the centre of the eyes, a living person, a child walking around this town wearing a dead girl's face and not knowing the first thing about it — not knowing whose she is, not knowing what she carries, not knowing the way the dead girl's heart kept stopping at the deep end of a pool—

And it is then, on the step, that Goldie sees her.

Across the road, at the bus shelter, a knot of kids in the maroon of the comprehensive, bags and crisps and phones, the four o'clock release. And among them, half-turned, laughing at something on a screen, fourteen if she's a day, a girl Goldie has never seen before in her life.

And Goldie knows these eyes.

Not the colour, though the colour is part of it — the wet-slate green with the darker ring at the rim, the eye that would change with the weather and the shirt. It is the *build*: the precise width of the set, the slight downward cant at the outer corner that should read sad and instead reads patient, the heaviness of the upper lid that makes a person look, always, like they are about to forgive you. She knows the geometry of them. She has held these eyes before — once over a lost-property counter on a nineteen-year-old lifeguard, and once, whole and sixteen and 2003, in the third row of a swim-gala photograph, fourth in.

The same eyes. A different child.

The girl glances up, feeling nothing, feeling the ordinary nothing of a fourteen-year-old waiting for a bus, and looks straight through the woman on the hospice step the way the whole town looks through her, and goes back to her phone, and laughs again, and somewhere under the laugh — Goldie cannot have seen it, the distance is wrong, she could not possibly have seen it and she sees it anyway, the way she sees everything she is not allowed to see — the girl's free hand comes up, light, unconscious, and presses flat to her own chest, there and gone, the length of a single beat.

Then the bus comes. And the maroon knot folds itself up into it, the girl with the eyes among them, and the doors hiss, and it pulls out past the harbour wall and the gulls, and takes her wherever fourteen-year-olds go, into a life she does not know the shape of. And Goldie stands on the step of the place where Bethan Maddox is two doors down putting a thing down before she goes, and she does not yet know — how could she, she is only a woman who never forgets a face — that behind her in the building, the clock that has just started on an old woman's life is already counting down.

She only ever knows the eyes.

---

> **FACE 0023** — female, ~14, comprehensive uniform, bus shelter opp. St. Cadog's, 16:02. Returns nothing. Hand to chest, once, at distance.
> *The eyes — set, cant, lid — exact. Not the colour, the build.*
> Again. The eyes, again, on a *second* child. cf. **FACE 0001** (Rhodri, 19). Same eyes. Different child. Twelve, fourteen years between them.
> A dead girl's eyes cannot be in two living children at once.
> Impossible — unless—
> **STATUS: UNPLACED.**

---

# CHAPTER 14 — The Brow on the Churchwarden

The board has started lying to her, which is a thing boards do when you love them too much.

Goldie stands in front of it at quarter to nine, before the building wakes, before Dil, before the first verruca of the day comes hopping to the hatch with a question. The flip-chart Sai liberated from the function room is propped against the lost-property cage, and on it the town is laid out in bingo dabber and swim-badge stickers the way she left it last night: a TADPOLE for Rhodri, two GUPPIES for the twins, a DOLPHIN for the Beavan boy with his widow's peak, a line of dabbed dots she has connected and reconnected until the marker bled through to the cardboard behind. *Carys's descendants.* It is a beautiful board. It is the cleanest thing she has built in twelve years. Everything on it agrees with everything else, which is exactly what a lie does, in her experience, right before you stake your professional life on it and a man four streets away with an alibi and a daughter spends eleven days finding out what your certainty costs.

In the bottom corner, face down, is the sticker she will not look at. She put it there herself. She knows the trick — she invented the trick — and she still does it, the small administrative murder of an inconvenient fact: a GUPPY with Olwen Tudor's name on it, turned over so the cartoon fish grins at the cardboard and not at her. *We'll come back to that one*, she told Sai, in the voice she uses for things she has no intention of coming back to.

She comes back to it.

She turns the sticker over.

And there it is, the brow she has been refusing for ten days, drawn from memory in her own forensic shorthand in the margin — *lift, taper, inner arch, exact, to the millimetre* — Carys Pryce's high surprised arch sitting in the middle of the face of a seventy-three-year-old churchwarden who reads the lesson at St. Cynog's in a hat like a docked boat. Goldie looks at the board. She looks at the sticker. She does the arithmetic she has been declining to do, the way you decline a second helping of something you are going to eat anyway.

Carys vanished at sixteen. Twenty-two years ago.

Olwen Tudor is seventy-three.

There is no version of a family tree in which a girl of sixteen is the grandmother of a woman fifty-seven years her senior. The arithmetic doesn't bend. The arithmetic isn't *interested* in bending. She has spent ten days treating the brow as noise because the board is beautiful and the brow is ugly, and now, at quarter to nine, with the boiler making its noise like a man being slowly disappointed in the next room, she lets the ugly fact stand up and walk across the board and knock every dabbed dot flat.

"Right," she says, to the brow. "You've been trying to tell me something."

The brow, being a brow, says nothing. But the building has begun to fill with the small drowned sounds of the day, and somewhere in the basement a box of 2003 sits lighter than it should, and Goldie knows — the way she knows everything, instantly, against her will, with no idea yet what it means — that she is about to lose a theory she has been very happy with, and that she will be better for it, and that she will not enjoy it even slightly.

* * *

She needs to get into the salon, and to get into the salon she needs Dil, who arrives at the hatch at nine on the dot like a tide that has memorised the timetable.

"Pryce of Aberglas," Goldie says, before Dil has even got the lid off her own coffee. "The salon. The shut one, by the chapel turn. Who's got the key?"

Dil regards her with the frank delight of a woman whose whole life has been leading to a question like this. "Now what," she says, settling both forearms on the hatch, "would you be wanting in Nerys's salon."

"Storage," says Goldie.

"You said storage about the basement and I found you up to your elbows in the gala box at midnight."

"Different storage."

"You're a terrible liar, Goldie, and I say that with love." Dil takes a meditative sip of the café coffee and visibly decides it has happened to her. "Nerys keeps the key, of course she keeps the key, it's her shop, but Nerys won't see you. Nerys won't see God. She's gone right into herself, that one, since — well." A small click of the tongue, the click that in Dil's grammar means *the daughter, the runaway, the thing we don't pick at.* "But." And here Dil's eyes go bright, because here is the part she has been saving. "*I* know where Olwen keeps the spare."

Goldie holds very still. "Olwen Tudor has a key to the salon."

"Olwen Tudor has a key to everything. She's churchwarden, she does the flowers, she did the keys when the Trust had its do's in the function rooms, she'd have a key to my chest of drawers if I let her near it." Dil is already reaching for the great freighted lanyard at her own hip, thumbing through fobs the way other women thumb through a hymn book. "Olwen had Nerys do her hair for forty years, fortnightly, never missed, not once, not even—" the click again "—not even *then*. She's the nearest thing that salon's got to a next of kin, is Olwen, and she keeps the spare under the second cake tin in the church kitchen, which she thinks is a secret, bless her, and which is the worst-kept secret since the vicar's diet."

And there it is, dropped into Goldie's lap by the soft human database, warm and gossipy and utterly load-bearing: *Olwen had Nerys do her hair for forty years.* Goldie files it the way she files everything, in the part of her that does not lie. The board cracks a little further. She doesn't say so.

"Get your coat," says Dil, delighted. "I'm coming. You'll never find the cake tin."

* * *

The salon has been shut for two years and it smells like 2003.

That is the first thing, the thing that arrives before the eyes can sort the room — the smell, sealed in, undisturbed, the particular cocktail of a place that closed its door one ordinary afternoon and was never aired again: setting lotion and singed hair and that sweet chemical perm reek, and under it, the way grief gets under everything, the cold mineral smell of damp coming up through a floor nobody heats. Dil flicks a switch and one strip light at the back stutters on, buzzing, throwing the room into the grey of an aquarium.

"Mind the chair," Dil says. "It bites."

The chairs are the kind that pump up and down, four of them, cracked maroon vinyl, gone stiff and shiny with age, and in the long mirror that runs the wall Goldie sees herself and Dil arrive — two women of a certain age in a drowned salon, one of them holding a key she had to steal from under a cake tin — and she thinks, not for the first time, that her life has taken her to some genuinely unimprovable places.

It is a time capsule. It is the most complete time capsule she has ever stood inside, and she has stood inside the Met's evidence store, which is a different kind of mausoleum. Here the posters are still up, curling off the wall at the corners: women with the architecture of two decades ago, the chunky highlights, the kicked-out fringe, a man with frosted tips smouldering at a camera that has long since stopped being able to hurt anyone. On the trolley by the second chair, abandoned mid-thought, a tub of mousse has fossilised — the foam gone to a yellow concrete rind, a little geological record of the exact moment the town stopped coming. There is an appointment book on the desk, open, a biro lying in the gutter of it where someone set it down meaning to pick it up again. A wall calendar two years dead. A radio. A jar of combs standing in a blue fluid gone the colour of pondwater.

"There's Mrs. Ellis," Dil says, drifting to the wall of photographs by the till, where the clients are pinned in their scores — a Polaroid economy of finished hair, decades of it, the proud after-shots a hairdresser keeps. "Dead now. There's the Bevan sisters, look at the state, that's the perm that started the feud. There's me." She taps a small square fondly. "Nineteen ninety-one. I had a *waist*." She moves along the wall the way Goldie moves along the shelf, naming, naming, the soft archive answering the hard one. "There's Carol-Anne. There's Idris's mam, God rest her, and there's little Idris done up like a choirboy, he'd have been—"

Goldie has stopped listening to the words and started reading the wall.

Because the wall is the case. The wall is the whole case, pinned and dated and laughing, and Goldie stands in front of it and lets the involuntary inventory off the leash, lets it run its hands over forty years of the town's heads, and what it finds, what it cannot stop finding, is the brow.

The brow on Mrs. Ellis, dead now, who would have been sixty in the photo. The brow on a bride from a colour print so old it has gone the rust of dried blood. The brow on a woman in a Christmas paper hat, on a girl confirmed in 1994, on Olwen Tudor herself in a sequence of photographs marching down the years, the same arch, the same exact lift and taper and inner curve, fortnightly, never missed, not even then — the brow appearing and reappearing on face after face after face, on the young and the old and the long dead, on women who could have been Carys's mother and women who could have been her great-aunt, the brow *everywhere*, scattered down the decades like a signature.

And the eyes are nowhere.

That is the thing that stands her hair up, here in the buzzing grey, with Dil still chuntering happily about waists. The eyes are nowhere on this wall. Not one. She has been seeing Carys's eyes for a fortnight — on Rhodri, on the Beavan boy's whole patient downturned set of them, on a fourteen-year-old she glimpsed once and can't stop seeing — and there is not a single pair of them on forty years of this salon's clients. The eyes don't come from here. The brow comes from *only* here.

She stands very still, the way she stood still on the first morning with both hands flat on the counter, and she lets the board in her head turn itself over and start again from nothing.

The brow is everywhere. The eyes are only in the children.

A face that was *born* would not behave like this. A born face goes down a bloodline — narrow, young, clustered, two generations of one family. It does not appear on a dead woman of sixty and a bride in a rust-coloured photograph and a churchwarden across four decades of fortnights. The brow is not descending. The brow is *spreading*, the way a fashion spreads, the way a song you can't stop humming spreads, the way a thing gets into a town when one pair of hands puts it there, head after head after head, in a chair, in this chair, in this drowned maroon chair with the cracked vinyl that bites.

"Goldie." Dil has noticed. "You've gone a bit—"

"How many heads," Goldie says. Her voice is doing the dry-middle-register thing, the thing she keeps it at so nothing can get a grip. "Forty years. Fortnightly clients. How many heads did Nerys do, would you say."

Dil considers it the soft human way, wrong on the date and right on the scale. "All of them," she says, simply. "Every woman in Aberglas under a certain age and most over it. Nerys did the lot. There wasn't another shop till the one in the precinct opened, and that was for the young ones wanting it pink. No. If you'd hair in this town, Nerys had her hands in it." She says it with a kind of civic pride, and then, because she is Dil, with the small click: "Till she stopped. She just — stopped, one day. Locked it up. Wouldn't say why. Grief takes people funny."

*All of them.* Goldie looks at the wall of the lot of them, the whole town's worth of heads, and on a great many of them, far too many of them, far more of them than any bloodline could account for, sits a sixteen-year-old's surprised eyebrow, given out by hand, fortnight after fortnight, year after year, like a small free gift with every cut.

She has been wrong.

She makes herself say it inside, plainly, the way you press on a bruise to be sure: she has been *wrong*. Not about the eyes — never about the eyes, the eyes are real, the eyes are *seen*, the eyes are not his — but about the meaning. The board was a beautiful lie. "Carys's secret descendants" cannot hold the brow, because the brow is on the dead and the old and the bride in the rust-coloured print, and a girl who vanished at sixteen does not have a grandmother. The theory she stayed up making, the theory she dabbed and stickered and connected and loved — it was the meaning, and the meaning was where she has always, always gone wrong.

There are two of them. Two mechanisms. One that *is* faces — born, young, clustered, the eyes, the heart she daren't think about — and one that *makes* them, here, by hand, in a chair, by somebody whose name is on the door.

"Whose was the hand," she says, mostly to herself.

"Nerys's," says Dil, who has stopped pretending she doesn't know this is a strange morning. "Whose else. It was her shop."

* * *

By half past ten she is back at the board, and Sai has appeared with two coffees that are an insult to the word, and Goldie is dismantling, methodically and with no pleasure at all, the only beautiful thing she has built since she came here.

She moves the dabbed dots. She separates the wall into two. On the left she lines them up — Rhodri's eyes, the twins' split cheekbones, the Beavan boy's peak — and they are all young, all under twenty-two, all bone, all the kind of feature you are issued at birth and stuck with, the set of an eye-socket, the shelf of a malar, the precise point a hairline drives down into a forehead. You cannot buy a widow's peak. You cannot draw cheekbones onto a face. These are *born*.

On the right she puts Olwen, seventy-three. She puts Idris Tomos, forty-four, the brow sitting square in the middle of a middle-aged man's face — a man too old to be a teenage runaway's child and far too old to be her grandchild, a man whose late mother Nerys did for thirty years and who got "done as a boy," the brow handed down not by blood but by *salon*, the way you'd inherit a turn of phrase off a parent or a way of holding a fork. She puts Mrs. Ellis, dead, sixty. She puts the bride in the rust print. And the right-hand list is every age there is, the whole human span of it, with only one thing in common: a chair, and a pair of hands, and a door with NERYS on it.

"You're taking the board apart," Sai says, in the careful voice of a duty manager who has learned that this woman dismantling things is not, on balance, a thing to interrupt lightly.

"The board was wrong."

"The board was great. The board had stickers."

"The board," says Goldie, "told me what I wanted to hear, which is the only thing a board is really for." She steps back. Two columns now. *Born* and *Made.* It is uglier than the descendants-board. It does not agree with itself. It is the first true thing she has put up there. "Look. The eyes only land on the young. Always the young, never the old — you'd find them on the children of this town and nowhere else, because that's what a bloodline does, it goes *down*, narrow and young. But the brow—" she taps the right column "—the brow's on a churchwarden of seventy-three and a man of forty-four and a woman who's been dead since the Blair government. The brow doesn't go down. It goes *out*. It's not inherited. It's *given*."

Sai is quiet for a moment, doing the maths of it, getting there. He is twenty-four and he is doomed to close this building and he has somehow become the only person she trusts to do the maths, which is its own small unlooked-for thing she hasn't examined. "So they're not all related," he says slowly. "The ones with the brow — they're not Carys's family at all."

"Some of them might be. Some of the young ones, the bone ones, yes. But the brow's not a clue about blood. The brow's a clue about—" she stops. The word arrives and she sets it down carefully on the desk between them, because it is the new shape of the case and she wants to hear how it sounds out loud. "About somebody making her. On purpose. Over and over. For forty years."

The strip light in her memory buzzes. The fossilised mousse. The wall of the lot of them, the whole town wearing a dead girl's eyebrow and not one of them knowing it, and the door with the name on it, and the woman behind the name who *just stopped, one day*, and locked it, and wouldn't say why.

She does not yet have the why. She is honest enough, this morning, to know that she does not have the why — that she is standing where she stood with the man on the burning-bus footage, certain of the face and reaching, reaching, toward a meaning. She assumes design. She assumes a *reason* — something cold, something deliberate, a hand that knew what it was doing. It does not yet occur to her that the answer could be grief; that a wall of a thousand stolen eyebrows could be the opposite of cold; that you can rebuild a daughter on the heads of strangers without ever once admitting, even to yourself, that that is what your hands are doing. That tenderness is twenty chapters of someone else's life away, and she will not be ready for it when it comes.

"Goldie," Sai says, and there's something gentle and slightly frightened in it now, the way you'd speak to someone standing closer to an edge than they realise. "Can I say a thing."

"You're going to."

"You were *really* sure about the descendants thing. Like — really sure. You had it solved. You had it stickered." He turns one of the coffees round in his hands, not drinking it, nobody drinks it. "And it was wrong. And you only worked out it was wrong because you made yourself look at the one fish you'd turned face down." He doesn't say *the wrong man.* He doesn't know about the wrong man, not the whole of it. But he has been listening, this clever doomed boy, the way Dil listens, and he has the feeling-shape of it even without the facts. "So how do you know this one's right? The making thing. How do you know you're not just — doing it again. The being-sure."

And Goldie, who has spent twelve years behind counters precisely so that no one would ever again ask her to turn a face into a claim, stands in the buzzing morning with two terrible coffees going cold, and lets the question land, and does not deflect it.

"I don't," she says.

It costs her more than the board did. She watches it land on Sai, watches him not know what to do with a Goldie who admits a thing, and she lets that cost too. "That's the whole — that's the *thing*, Sai. I'm never wrong about whether I've seen a face. Never, not once, not on the worst night the Met ever fed me. But what it *means* —" She stops. The dry register wants to make this a joke, a tourniquet, *that's where I keep my mistakes*, and for once she lets it go past without grabbing it. "That's where I get people hurt. So I'm not going to be sure. I'm going to find out who made the brow, and why, and *then*, maybe, I'll let myself be sure. In that order. Find out, then say. Not the other way. Never again the other way."

Sai nods, slowly. "That," he says, "is the least efficient thing I have ever heard a former Met analyst say."

"There's lovely," says Goldie. "Drink your coffee."

"Nobody drinks the coffee."

"No," she agrees. "Nobody does."

She looks back at the board. *Born* and *Made.* Two columns that don't agree, and a name on a locked door, and a whole town walking past her hatch wearing the scattered face of a girl two of them are dying of and none of them can see. Somewhere out past the harbour wall a dying woman in a hospice has, this very morning, lifted a telephone and decided to stop carrying a thing to her grave — and has been overheard by the one person in the world who needed her not to — but Goldie does not know that yet. Goldie knows only that she has lost a theory and found a question, which is the trade her whole life has been, and that the question now has a door on it, and that behind the door is the woman who made a thousand copies of a face the whole town agreed to forget.

She takes the GUPPY with Olwen's name on it off the *Born* column, where it never belonged, and presses it firmly into the *Made* one, where it does. The cartoon fish grins up at her, finally facing the right way.

"Right then," she says to it. "Now then. Let's go and find out who taught you that eyebrow."

---

# CHAPTER 15 — The Tide Comes In

She is halfway to the hospice with a tin of bara brith on the passenger seat when she finds out she is too late, and she finds out the way you find out anything in Aberglas, which is that the town has already finished telling everyone else and is only now getting round to her.

It is Dil. Of course it is Dil. The phone goes against the gearstick and Goldie answers it on the second ring with the dry middle-register thing ready in her mouth — *I'm five minutes off, tell Maureen the bara brith's Eira's not mine* — and Dil says, without the usual run-up, without the parking-space feud or the weather or the funeral on Thursday that is now a different funeral, "Bach. Don't go up. There's no point you going up." And then, because she is Dil and the news will out of her like water finds a crack: "Sister Branwen's gone. In the night. Slipped away peaceful in her sleep, the matron said. Wasn't she lucky, in the end. They're saying she went lovely."

Goldie pulls the car in at the harbour wall because the alternative is to keep driving it, and a woman who keeps driving when a thing like that arrives is a woman who ends up four streets away explaining herself to a daughter.

"When," she says.

"In the night, I told you. They found her at the half six obs, all settled, like she'd just—" Dil stops. Even Dil, whose grammar for death is forty years deep and mostly cheerful, hears something off the line, the wrong kind of quiet coming back at her. "Goldie? You all right? You've gone—"

"Fine," says Goldie. "Long way from a coffee." It is the oldest lie she owns and it does not even slow down on the way out. "I'll come in. Tell Maureen I'm coming in."

She sits with the engine ticking and the tin of bara brith warm against her thigh and looks at the tide, which is out, a long brown reach of mud and sea-wrack with the harbour boats lying over on their sides like animals that have given up. *Slipped away peaceful.* *Went lovely.* Two days. Branwen had two days, three at the outside, and she had spent twenty-two years not telling and had finally, finally lifted a telephone and decided to stop carrying it to the grave — *a girl who ought to know whose she is, before I'm gone* — and now the grave has very obligingly arrived ahead of schedule, ahead of the telling, on the one night between the deciding and the doing, and the matron is saying *wasn't she lucky.*

Goldie does not believe in lucky. She has never once in fifty-one years met a coincidence she liked the look of, and she does not like the look of this one, and she sits with both hands on the wheel of a parked car and waits to see whether the world is going to be a place where dying women slip away peaceful the very night they choose to talk, or whether it is the other place, the place she has spent her whole life not being able to stop seeing.

The gulls do not help. The gulls never help. They wheel over the dead boats and scream at nothing and the tide keeps coming, the way the tide does, indifferent and on time, filling the harbour back up over everything that was lying there exposed.

* * *

The Pelican, when she gets there, has already become a wake.

This is the thing about a town that loves its dead: it does not wait for permission. By the time Goldie comes through the turnstile the function room above the pool — the faded-grand one, the bingo store, the one where the Trust did its quiet work two decades ago and where nobody now standing in it knows that a girl gave birth and died behind the very partition they've stacked the spare chairs against — the function room is full of women putting out a buffet for a body that is not yet cold.

"There she is." Maureen has Goldie by the elbow before she's three steps in, which is Maureen's way, a grip like a docking clamp. Maureen runs the Friends of the Pelican committee the way she runs everything, by annexation, and she has clearly decided that Sister Branwen's send-off is now a Friends matter. "We're doing the tea here Thursday after the service, the hospice room's too small and the chapel hall's got the damp. You'll do the urn. You're good with the urn."

"I've never done the urn."

"You'll be good with it." It is not a compliment. It is a deployment. Maureen has the seating already on a sheet of A4 and a biro behind her ear and the particular high colour of a woman for whom grief and logistics are the same muscle and both are flexing. "Glenys. *Glenys.* Goldie's doing the urn."

"She's doing the *urn*?" Glenys, across the room, turns her good ear and her better one and gets, as she gets most things, the shape and not the sound. She is laying out sandwiches in concentric rings of disapproval. "Well she can't do the hymns as well, she doesn't know them, you can't have an urn person on the hymns."

"Nobody said the hymns."

"You said hymns."

"I said *urn.*"

"There's no need to shout, Maureen, I'm not deaf in both."

And there it is, the first one, the laugh coming up Goldie's throat before she can stop it, treacherous and warm and absolutely the wrong response to a dead nun, and she swallows it, the way you decide a coffee has happened to you, because the alternative to laughing in this room is the other thing, and she will not do the other thing here, not with the urn in her future and Maureen's biro and the whole loving terrible machine of the town grinding into gear around a coffin.

Pam is at the window with the order of service already drafted on the back of a flyer for a sponsored swim. Pam, who reads three newspapers and believes most of them, has the look she gets, the bright fixed look, and when Goldie comes near her she says, low, not to the room, "She knew something, you know. Branwen. She'd gone funny these last weeks. My sister-in-law does her cleaning at St. Cadog's and she said Branwen had the priest in twice and turned him away twice, said she wasn't ready, said she had to do a thing first." Pam taps the side of her nose, which on Pam is less a gesture than a tic, a thing her finger does when the rest of her is excited. "And now she's gone before she could do it. You tell me that's nothing. *I* won't tell you it's nothing."

"It's nothing, Pam," says Glenys, who has the better ear pointed the wrong way and has heard exactly the part she wanted to. "She was seventy-eight and full of cancer. People die, it's not the *Pelican*."

"I never said it was the Pelican."

"You're always saying it's the Pelican."

But Goldie has stopped, half a sandwich-ring away, with the bara brith still in her hands like a hot brick she's forgotten to put down, because Pam — Pam who is wrong about the council and wrong about the bins and wrong about the man from the water board — has just put her excitable finger on the exact place it hurts. *She had to do a thing first.* And she did not get to do it. Someone made very sure she did not get to do it. And the only people who knew there was a thing to do were the people Branwen had told she meant to do it — which was Goldie, and a granddaughter she'd been watching from a bus shelter, and a mother on the end of a telephone, and whoever, whoever, had been close enough to that hospice corridor to hear an old woman lift the receiver and say *Marged, cariad, it's Bethan, I need the family to come up.*

She had been seen. That is the cold thing arriving now, sliding into place the way they slide, fully formed, unbidden. Not Branwen — Goldie. Goldie has spent a fortnight asking. Goldie has gone up that hospice corridor twice with her dry questions and her counter and her terrible reputation for never forgetting a face, and she has been seen doing it, in a town that watches the way this town watches, and somewhere in all that watching is a person who looked at a burned-out woman pulling threads and decided the threads had got long enough.

She puts the bara brith down on the buffet table, between the egg ones and the fish-paste ones, very carefully, because her hands have decided they would like something to do that isn't shaking.

* * *

The visitors' book is the kind of thing nobody thinks to hide because nobody thinks of it as a thing at all.

She is not supposed to be in the hospice and she goes anyway, Thursday-urn business her cover, a tin to return that she has cynically not returned, *Eira sent this up for Branwen, only she'll not want it now, will she, where do I—* and the girl on the desk, twenty-two and exhausted and grateful for anyone who isn't crying, waves her toward the day-room with the awful telly and does not watch where she goes. The book is on a lectern by the lift, open, a Biro on a string, the well-meaning Victorian survival of it — *please sign in for fire safety* — and Goldie reads it the way she reads the shelf, the way she reads a face, completely, against her will, and for no money.

She does not need a name. She needs the last week, and the last week is right there in Biro on a string, every soft loop of it.

She takes it the only way she can take anything, which is to look once and keep it. Tuesday: two from the chapel, a niece from Aberaeron, a Pam (*P. Probert, a friend* — that'll be Pam's sister-in-law with the cleaning). Wednesday: the Wednesday hairdresser, *re hair, 11am*, because even the dying get their hair done in this town and a kindness like that is its own small unbearable thing. Wednesday evening: a care agency relief, the looping institutional hand of someone signing a dozen of these a shift. And there, Thursday — the day before, the last full day, the last day Branwen Maddox was alive to be visited — three lines from the bottom, in a hand she has seen before, on a "Save the Pelican" clipboard and a council letter and the top of a public meeting, square and unhurried and giving nothing away, the way the man himself gives nothing away:

*G. Hollis. A friend. 3.40.*

Goldie stands with her eyes on it and does not let her face do anything, because there is a CCTV dome in the corner of the foyer the colour of a dead eye and fifteen years have taught her that the camera is always the thing you forget. *A friend.* He had signed himself *a friend.* Not trustee, not Pelican, not the long civic title he wears like a good coat — *a friend,* the smallest, warmest, most unarguable word in the language, the word you cannot question without looking like a monster, and he had put it there at 3.40 on the last afternoon of a woman who had decided, that very week, to dig up the one thing in this town he has spent twenty-two years keeping in the ground.

It means nothing. She makes herself say it inside, plainly, the way Sai made her say it: *it means nothing.* A beloved old man visits a dying woman he has known for decades; of course he does; he visits everyone, he buries everyone, he leads the campaigns and reads at the funerals and is the first to the buffet and the last to leave; that is who Gareth Hollis *is,* that is the entire point of who he has made himself, a man so woven into the town's care of its own that his name on a dying woman's visitor list is the least suspicious thing in the building. She has been wrong before. She has stood exactly here, certain of a face, reaching toward a meaning, and got a man's eleven days taken off him. She will not do it again. Find out, then say. In that order.

But she looks at the dome in the corner and the book on the string and the square unhurried hand that gave nothing away, and she thinks — not as an accusation, she is very careful, even alone, even with her face doing nothing, to keep it from being an accusation — she thinks only: *he was the one face that gave me nothing.* From the first night, in the function room, the whole crowd open to her like a wall of lit windows and one face shuttered, one face with no give, the one set of features in Aberglas she could not read past. And she had filed it, on that first night, under the dozen harmless reasons a face goes still — tiredness, grief, the natural reticence of a busy man — and she has just understood, standing at a lectern that asks her politely to sign in for fire safety, that there is a thirteenth reason a face gives nothing away, and it is that the face has spent twenty-two years learning not to, because it is keeping something, and a face that is keeping something is, in the end, the only kind of face her whole ruinous gift was ever really built to find.

She does not sign the book. She puts the bara brith tin on the windowsill by the lift, where someone will find it and not know whose it was, and she leaves the way she came, and the camera watches her go, and gives nothing away either.

* * *

The chapel grounds at low tide are the worst place in Aberglas and Goldie goes there because she cannot, for the moment, go anywhere with people in it.

It is round the back of the Pelican, past the dead lido full of leaves, down a slate path nobody mends: a little Victorian chapel gone to the damp, boarded, its noticeboard still announcing a harvest festival from a year she'd have to count back to, and beside it the small forgotten burial ground that came with it, a few dozen leaning stones furred green, the names sponged off by a hundred years of sea-weather. Beyond the low wall the mud reaches out grey and shining to a sea that has pulled all the way back, and the smell is the smell of everything Aberglas keeps under: salt and rot and the cold mineral breath of ground that never dries.

She does not know — she could not know, not yet, not for chapters of someone else's grief away — that she is standing within thirty feet of Carys Pryce. That somewhere in the unmarked margin of this ground, in the part beyond the leaning stones where the grass grows ranker and a person could walk a hundred times and never read a thing, a sixteen-year-old has lain twenty-two years with no name on her, no stone, no STATUS, nothing the eye can catch and keep. Goldie reads the stones she can read — *Tudor, Pugh, a Pryce, beloved, fell asleep, gone to a fairer shore* — and the ground beyond gives her nothing, because that is the whole point of the ground beyond, it was chosen precisely because it gives nothing away, and she stands among the named dead with her collar up against the sea and does not know that the one face this whole town is wearing in pieces is six feet under the grass at the edge of her vision, keeping the longest silence of all.

What she knows is smaller and worse. She knows that a woman who wanted to speak is now permanently not speaking, and that the not-speaking was arranged, and that the arranging was clean — no blood, no mark, an old dying woman who *went lovely,* a death so soft and so on-schedule that the matron called it luck and the town is already buttering sandwiches for it. That is the part that turns her stomach, standing here in the rot-smell with the tide coming in: not that it was violent but that it was *kind.* That it would have looked, to anyone, like mercy. A frightened girl's heart stops in a back room and they call it a runaway; an old woman's breath stops the night before she talks and they call it peace. The same hand, she thinks — and stops, because she does not know that, she does not have that, that is reaching — the same *town,* then, the same soft machinery that loves its dead so much it cannot bear to look at how they actually died, papering the wall over the frailty, thin and floral and warm, *fell asleep, gone to a fairer shore, wasn't she lucky in the end.*

The water is at the foot of the wall now, and rising.

And Esme would have been twenty now.

It comes the way it comes, the way the tide comes, unasked and on time. Not a face — she will not summon the face, she has not summoned the face in twelve years, the face is the one thing in all the world she has made herself stop being able to see — just the arithmetic, the plain flat number of it, twenty. The hospice girl on the desk was twenty-two. The granddaughter at the bus shelter is fourteen. Branwen at seventy-eight got to make her peace, got to lift a phone, got to *decide,* and even her, even her they would not let. And Esme would have been twenty now, and Goldie keeps to the number, the way she keeps to the crusts, because the number is a thing you are allowed, the number does not have a face on it, and she stands at the wall and lets the cold flat fact of the number stand there beside her in the rot-smell and asks it nothing, and it asks her nothing back, and the tide comes up over the both of them.

Then a gull screams, and it is just a Tuesday, and she has an urn to learn.

* * *

She goes back up to the lit room and the sandwiches because the alternative is to keep standing at a wall, and a woman who keeps standing at a wall is no use to a living girl in a bus shelter.

The wake has progressed. Maureen and Glenys have reached the hymns, which is to say the war, and Goldie comes in on the back of it, the warm ridiculous din of it closing over her head like the tide closing over the harbour, and for a moment she just stands inside the noise and lets it be loud.

"*Calon Lân,*" Maureen is saying, "and the twenty-third, and we'll have *Cwm Rhondda* to send her out, that's three, you can't have more than three, people start looking at their watches."

"You can't have *Cwm Rhondda* for Branwen," says Carol-Anne Beddoe, who has done the flowers at St. Cynog's for thirty years until her knees gave out and considers herself, on the strength of it, the ranking authority on every send-off in the bay. "She was chapel-chapel, not chapel-rugby. *Cwm Rhondda's* for the men. You want *Arglwydd, dyma fi.*"

"What number's that, then?" says Glenys, pencil poised.

"It's not a number, Glenys, it's a hymn."

"They've all got numbers. Everything's got a number. What number."

"Two-thirteen in the green book."

"*Two-thirteen?*" Glenys writes it down with the air of a woman entering it into evidence. "Well that's no good, two-thirteen in the green book's *Bread of Heaven,* which is *Cwm Rhondda,* which is the one we're not having, so you've gone round in a circle, the pair of you, and I've got two of the same hymn and an urn person who can't sing."

"I never said I'd sing," says Goldie, from the door.

"You see," says Glenys, triumphant, to the room. "She can't even *do* the hymns."

And the laugh comes up again, the warm treacherous thing, and this time Goldie lets a little of it out, because the room needs it and she needs it and Branwen — Branwen Maddox who turned the priest away twice because she wasn't ready, because she had a thing to do first — Branwen would, Goldie suspects, have enjoyed nothing in the world so much as two old women fighting to a standstill over the number of a hymn at her own buffet. There is a kind of love in it that the gift cannot see and the ledger cannot hold. Glenys cannot hear the hymns; Maureen cannot bear to let anyone else run the grief; Carol-Anne is laughing now — three notes, the duck, the head tilting right, a dead girl's laugh coming out of a sixty-one-year-old throat that learned it in a chair on a Friday at half ten for thirty years and does not know it, will never know it — and none of them know what Goldie knows, and that is the mercy of them, that is the whole warm unbearable mercy of a town that recognises its dead in pieces and has the grace not to know.

She takes the seating sheet off Maureen. She finds the urn. It is enormous and dented and older than several of the mourners and she fills it and switches it on and it begins, slowly, to tick and creak its way toward boiling, a great civic samovar warming up for a woman who will not drink from it, and Goldie stands by it with her hand near the little orange light and lets the room be loud around her.

But she is not, any more, doing the thing she came to Aberglas to do.

She had come here to be no use to anyone. She had taken the quietest counter in Wales precisely so that no face would ever again cost her anything, and she had let the gift fire on a boy's borrowed eyes and chased it for a fortnight the way you'd chase a tune you couldn't place — to *satisfy* it, to make the seeing stop, to file the impossible face and be done. A puzzle. A cold case the size of a town, worked sideways through bingo and gossip and a wall of stolen eyebrows, because she could not *not.*

That is over. She knows it is over the way she knows everything, instantly, standing by a boiling urn with two old women still arguing about the green book. It stopped being a puzzle the night someone went up a hospice corridor and sat for twenty minutes at 3.40 with a dying woman and made her go lovely. There is a girl at a bus shelter with a dead woman's eyes and a heart Goldie cannot yet name, and a mother somewhere who got a phone call from Bethan that was never finished, and a whole soft loving machine of a town that will paper any death over with hymns sooner than look at it — and one face in all of it that gives nothing away, that signed itself *a friend,* that buries everyone and is first to every buffet, and Goldie is no longer trying to put the face down.

She is trying to get to the girl before he does.

Across the room Glenys licks her pencil and says, to nobody, with the deep satisfaction of a woman who has won an argument by sheer endurance, "Right. Two hymns and a reading and we'll have done her proud," and writes it down, and the urn ticks over toward the boil, and outside the high windows the tide is in, all the way in now, bright and full and giving nothing away, smoothing the harbour over everything that lay there exposed.

---

# CHAPTER 16 — The Collector

The town has not changed and that is the unbearable thing.

Four days since they found Branwen gone soft in her chair with the squash untouched on the side, four days since the word *peaceful* started doing the rounds like a collection plate — *she went so peaceful, mind, no fuss, the way she'd have wanted* — and the Pelican opens at seven as it always opens, the boiler grumbling its slow disappointment, Dil narrating the tide of the morning through the hatch, Carwyn burning the coffee on purpose, the gulls doing their gull business over the dead lido. Goldie stands at her counter and watches the building be exactly itself and cannot forgive it for it.

Because one of them did it.

That is the sentence that arrived on the hospice step and has not left, the way the bad ones never leave; it surfaced clean and cold and complete on the second morning and now it lives behind her eyes with all the other things she cannot put down. *She was days from telling. And then she wasn't.* Goldie does not have a name for the hand that did it. She has, instead, the much worse thing — she has *all the faces*, the whole warm filed catalogue of this town, and any one of them could be it, and she has spent four days unable to stop running them, the way you run a tongue over a tooth that's gone wrong, and every face she runs is somebody's nan, somebody's good neighbour, somebody who'd lend you a fiver and feed your cat.

A person you have filed under *harmless* is a person you have stopped looking at. She thinks this at the hatch, at the shelf, at the boiler. She thought she was being kind, leaving the town its faces. She was being lazy. There is a difference, and Branwen is the difference, two doors down from where Goldie stood being a better woman than the one who listens past doors.

"You've gone all gothic on me," says Sai.

He has taken to bringing his laptop to the desk in the dead hour after the schools start, on the pretext that the office Wi-Fi is worse, which is not true; the pretext is that he is twenty-four and he has worked out, without either of them saying it, that she should not be left alone in a quiet building with the thing she is carrying. He is a good boy. He files the campaign spreadsheet and he watches her not eat her sandwich and he does not ask.

"I'm always gothic," says Goldie. "It's the bone structure."

"You've been standing there for ten minutes doing a face." He does not look up from the screen. "Your investigating face. The one that ends with me getting cautioned by my area manager."

* * *

The comprehensive comes on Thursdays, two coaches of them, Year Tens for their statutory thirty metres and a lifetime's chlorine trauma, and the whole front of the Pelican turns into weather. Goldie has learned to time her shelf-tidying to it, not despite the chaos but for it; you can read forty faces in twenty minutes when forty faces are streaming past a hatch shouting about whose towel is whose, and nobody, not one of them, looks at the lost-property desk, which suits her down to the wet tiled ground.

She is not, this morning, tidying the shelf.

She is watching for the boy.

She found him a fortnight ago and has been keeping half an eye since — Gethin, the badge said, before the badge went under a hoodie; sixteen, Year Eleven helping shepherd the little ones, a tall folded-up boy all elbows and apology, and Goldie clocked him then the way she clocks them all and filed him with the cold small jolt she has stopped pretending is anything else. Now she watches him come through the turnstile in the maroon stream, ducking the way the very tall learn to duck, and she gives him the full forensic weight, the involuntary inventory running its hands over him, because she has to, because the seeing does not ask.

The ear first. It is always the ear with this one. The left ear sits a particular way against the skull — a long lobe, attached not free, and at the top of it that small backward fold of the helix, the flattened curl like a wave that broke and didn't finish, the exact wave she has held before in the third row of a 2003 swim gala and on a nineteen-year-old lifeguard who keeps losing the same goggle. You cannot draw an ear. Nobody has ever gone into a salon and asked for a wave in the top of the helix. An ear is a thing the body decides in the dark before you are born and then keeps, forever, while everything else gives up — the ear of a man on a 2009 platform has not moved a millimetre in sixteen years, and the ear of this boy is sixteen years old and somebody else's ear, somebody dead, the same way the goggle on the shelf is half a thing pretending to be whole.

And the cowlick. At the back, where the hood doesn't quite cover, the hair breaks the wrong way and won't be told — a left-handed cowlick, a small stubborn whorl spinning anticlockwise against the run of the rest, the kind of thing a mother licks her thumb at for the school photo and gives up on by eight. Goldie knows the spin of it. She knows it the way she knows the ear, which is to say absolutely and without one fact attached.

He reaches across his own body for the turnstile bar. Left-handed.

*Not his,* she thinks. *The ear isn't his. The cowlick isn't his.* The same flat true sentence that surfaces every time, and underneath it now, four days into the worst week, a second sentence, uglier, that she lets stand because she is past pretending it isn't there: *and whoever's they are, somebody made very sure they got buried.*

Then it happens.

He is halfway across the wet apron between the turnstile and the changing-room door, in among the streaming kids, and he stops. Just stops. One hand goes out and finds the tiled wall the way you'd find a rail in weather, and he stands a half-second too long with his head down, and the colour leaves him — not pale, that is too slow a word, it *leaves* him, the way water leaves a sink, top to bottom, his lips going the grey of the lido render — and a girl behind him says *Geth, you div, move* and bumps him and he comes back, blinks, swallows, says something, laughs it off, and is folded back into the maroon and gone toward the boys' changing room before any teacher has clocked a thing.

Two seconds. Maybe three.

Goldie has both hands flat on the counter.

She knows that grey. She held it a fortnight back on a lifeguard at the deep end, the white-to-grey of a boy whose heart forgot for a beat what it was for; she has the whole sequence filed, the find-the-wall, the head-down, the colour going out like a tide, the coming-back-confused. *Dehydration,* they said about the other one. *He never drinks enough, that's boys.* And here it is again, the same grey, on a second boy who wears a second piece of the same dead face, and the two of them have never, she'd put folding money on it, so much as met.

She does not write *dehydration* in her head.

She writes nothing in her head. She just stands there while the floor decides whether it is a floor, and watches the door swing shut on a sixteen-year-old who carries a dead girl's ear and a dead girl's cowlick and, it appears, a dead girl's habit of nearly dying in a swimming pool.

* * *

"Two of them," she says.

It is gone six. The schools are long flushed out, the building emptied to its echo, and she has Sai trapped at the desk with the flip-chart turned to its clean back, because she could not bear, tonight, to look at the *Carys's descendants* side and the murdered fish in the corner. She has drawn it new. She has drawn it the way the thing in her chest is drawing it, which is to say badly, in dabber, with arrows.

"Rhodri," she says. "Nineteen. The eyes. Goes grey at the deep end, August, they call it dehydration. And now this one. Gethin. Sixteen. The ear, the cowlick. Goes grey on the apron this morning, and if there'd been a teacher near him they'd have sat him down and given him a squash and called it dehydration too. Two boys. Same face — bits of the same face. Same..." She stops. She does not have the word, she has only the picture, the colour leaving a boy like water leaving a sink. "Same fault. Whatever it is. It's in them both."

"Right," says Sai, carefully, in the voice of a man watching a vase travel toward the edge of a shelf. "And four days ago a woman who knew about all this died."

"A woman who was about to talk about all this died."

"You don't know—"

"I know she picked up a phone to ring a family and tell them something. I know she was dead inside a week of it." Goldie hears her own voice and does not much like it; it has gone somewhere she swore off, the flat hard somewhere of the years when she sat in a dark room and hung names on shapes in the static and a wrong call put a real man four streets from his alibi. "I'm not saying I know. I'm saying watch."

And then her mind, which has been a good and careful instrument for four days and is very tired, does the thing she has been braced against, the thing the dark room taught it: it makes a shape. It takes the missing girl and the scattered face and the dead midwife and the two grey boys and the buried truth, and it builds, out of those true pieces, a monster.

She lays it out. She hears herself lay it out.

A man — it is always, in the dark room, a man — who took Carys. Took her, twenty-two years ago, when she was sixteen and gone-off-before and easy to write down as a runaway; who did to her whatever such men do; who *kept* her, or kept the idea of her, and could not stop, the way they cannot stop, and over twenty-two years found others, made others, *built* others — groomed the look-alikes, steered the young ones, collected the eyes and the ear and the cowlick the way a certain kind of man collects, a face he liked once and went on liking, on body after body after body, a whole town quietly stocked with copies of a girl he—

"And a collector," she says, the word arriving with a click she distrusts even as she says it, because it is too neat, too much the shape the dark room likes, "a collector would *kill* to keep the collection. That's the missing piece. That's why Branwen. She was going to point at him. So he—"

"Goldie."

Sai has stopped typing.

"You've built a serial killer," he says, "out of a hairdresser's town and a dead nun. In about four minutes. From a flip-chart." He is not mocking her. That is the worst of it; he is twenty-four and frightened and trying to put a hand out and find a wall for her. "Listen to it. Listen to what you just said out loud. A man's gone round Aberglas for twenty-two years *collecting eyebrows*?"

"Eyes. The eyes are the—"

"I don't care if it's eyes or eyebrows or *ankles*, you can't hear yourself." He shuts the laptop. The desk goes quieter. "You said it yourself, the first week. You're never wrong about whether you've seen a face. And you're—" he picks the words up like they're hot "—you told me. You're worse than anyone at what it *means*. You told me that. A man four streets away with his daughter. Eleven days."

The boiler knocks, once, in the next room. *The Bishop,* Huw calls that knock, the one that lands in the gaps. Goldie stands in the gap it leaves.

"I know what I am," she says, very quietly.

"Then *be* it," says Sai, gentler now, almost pleading. "Be the one who knows the face and admits she doesn't know the rest. Don't be the dark-room one. Please. Not on a Thursday."

* * *

It is Maureen who breaks it, the next morning, the way Maureen breaks everything, with the unanswerable.

The Belles have got wind. Of course they have got wind; Pam has had the theory of Branwen-was-Done-In since approximately the hour the squash went cold, and has been circulating it through the changing rooms with the diligence of a woman who has waited her whole life to be right about one murder, and Goldie, fool that she is, has let slip to Glenys — to *Glenys*, of all the watertight vessels — that there might be, possibly, conceivably, a person in this town who has been gathering up a certain look over the years. Glenys heard *gathering* as *gardening* and reported to the changing room that the new lost-property woman thinks someone's been doing topiary, which took twenty minutes and a diagram to walk back, and is, Goldie privately concedes, no more deranged than the truth.

So now they are at her, four wet women at the hatch in their caps and their cossies, smelling of chlorine and Deep Heat, demanding the theory entire.

"A *collector,*" says Maureen, when she has it. She says the word the way you'd hold up something the cat brought in. Maureen is the harbour wall of this town; nothing has got past her in seventy years and she does not intend to start with Goldie. "Going round. *Collecting* girls' faces. For twenty-two years."

"It's a theory," says Goldie. "It's not a—"

"In my day," says Maureen, with the magnificent finality of a woman closing a book, "the only thing collected round here was the tombola. And the bins, Tuesdays." She turns to the others, case dismissed. "Some man's been *collecting faces*. Honest to God. Glenys, has anyone collected your face?"

"I've not had it done in a fortnight," says Glenys, touching her perm, wounded.

And Pam — Pam, who has been wrong about the moon landings and the water fluoride and the man from the council who is definitely a lizard, and who is, the bible of this town will one day record, occasionally and infuriatingly correct — Pam says, into the laughter, not loudly, fixing Goldie with her flask-bright conspiracy eyes:

"You don't catch a thing by deciding what it is first, bach. You catch it by watching where it goes."

The Belles move off to their aqua, Maureen at the head of them like a tug. Goldie stands holding half a goggle she has picked up without meaning to, and lets Pam's daftness sit on her, because Pam is daft and Pam is right, and the dark room is wrong, and somewhere underneath the monster she has built there is one true thing the monster cannot explain and she has been refusing to look at it the way she refused the brow.

A collector takes a face he likes.

A collector does not give two boys the same heart.

She comes back to it at the desk, with the building filling up and the chlorine coming through the swing doors in waves. A man could groom a look-alike. A man could, God help the world, steer a young one toward the eyes and the cut and the way of holding the head — could *make* a face, the way someone in this town has plainly made the brow, on Olwen, on Idris, on the dead and the old. She has *seen* the made faces; she knows now that faces can be made. But the brow does not faint. The brow has never once, in all her ledger, found a wall and gone grey and forgotten for a beat what it was for. Only the eyes faint. Only the ear and the cowlick and the eyes — only the *born* ones — go grey at the deep end. Rhodri faints. Lowri's cousin keels over, half the Beynon side, the doctor says it's nothing. And now Gethin, sixteen, on the apron, the colour leaving him like water leaving a sink.

You cannot collect a heart. You cannot groom a fault that lives where the ear is decided, in the dark before a person is born. A thing that travels with the ear and the eyes and the cowlick, that turns up grey in this boy and that boy and never once in the brow-on-the-old — a thing like that is not gathered. It is not chosen. It is not loved by a man across a room.

It is *passed down.*

She does not have the word *inherited* with the weight it will later carry. But she has the shape now, under the monster, where it has been all along: not a man who took a face he wanted, but a face going *down* — down through bodies, child to child, the eyes and the ear and the quiet grey fault all riding the same dark river — and a man, somewhere, who is not collecting it at all.

A man who is making very sure nobody traces where it runs.

Which is a different man entirely from the one in the dark room. Quieter. Kinder, even, to look at. The sort of man who organises a funeral beautifully and is on the visitors' book up near the top, *G. HOLLIS, a friend,* and goes round the town being its good heart precisely because—

She stops.

She does not finish it. She is, she reminds herself, looking at Sai's empty chair, exactly the woman who once finished a sentence like that with total confidence and put eleven days of hell on an innocent man. She will not name the kind face. She will not put a fish on the board with that name on it, not tonight, not on a Thursday, not until she has done the thing Pam said and watched where it goes.

But she takes the dark room and she folds it up and she puts it away, the predator, the collector, the whole neat monster, because the monster cannot explain a boy going grey on a wet apron, and the truth — whatever it is, sadder than a monster, older than a monster — can. The eyes are real. The eyes were always real. And the eyes carry something that does not get collected; it gets *given*, with the ear and the cowlick, down a line she cannot yet draw, to children walking around this town with a dead girl's face and a dead girl's heart, not knowing whose they are, not knowing what they carry, not knowing the river they are standing in.

She needs to find the salon. The made faces all came through one door. If she wants to know who made the brow — and, behind the brow, who made very sure the eyes were never traced — she starts where the faces were made.

She picks up the pen.

* * *

> **FACE 0016** — male, 16, comprehensive (Year 11), poolside apron, ~08:40, Thursday. Returns nothing.
> *The ear — long lobe, attached, the broken-wave fold at the helix, exact. The cowlick — left, anticlockwise, won't be told. Not the colour, the build. Held before.* Not his.
> Went grey on the apron. Found the wall. Came back. Three seconds. They'll call it the heat.
> The faints again. cf. **FACE 0001** (Rhodri, the deep end). cf. Lowri's cousin, half the Beynon side, "it's nothing." cf. **FACE 0023** (the bus-shelter girl, hand to chest).
> The faints follow the *born* ones. Never the made. The brow has never once gone grey. Why—
> Not collected. *Carried.*
> **STATUS: UNPLACED.**

---

# CHAPTER 17 — Nerys

A collector's workshop, Goldie has decided, will smell of something. Bleach, or fixative, or the cold mineral nothing of a room kept too clean for too long. She has built it in her head over four sleepless nights since they put Branwen in the ground — a place where a man kept faces the way some men keep birds, a wall of them, a method to it — and she is so braced for the method that it takes her a full thirty seconds, standing in the doorway of the flat above the dead salon, to understand that what the room smells of is setting lotion and old tea and a radiator turned up too high for one small woman in a cardigan, and that she has come to the wrong sort of place entirely.

"You'll want to mind the step," says the small woman. "Everyone goes over the step."

Nerys Pryce is sixty-nine and she has been described to Goldie, by everyone, in the past tense. *Nerys, who did the hair. Nerys, before. You'd not know her now.* Dil said it kindly and Carwyn said it kindly and even Maureen, who does not do kindly, dropped her voice for it, the way the town drops its voice for a building it has decided is finished. Goldie expected a hollow. She gets a woman who is entirely present, in the way of someone who has arranged her presence very carefully and would prefer you not move anything.

She reads her on the step, because she cannot not. Sixty-nine, smaller than she was — the cardigan is two sizes into a body that used to fill it; a woman shrinking on purpose, going quiet in the flesh the way she's gone quiet in the town. Hands that have done one thing for thirty-one years: the right thumb pad flattened and shiny, a callus you only get from a comb held the same way ten thousand times; the nails kept short and square the way a hairdresser keeps them and a widow stops bothering to, except she hasn't stopped, the square is exact. A face Goldie has, against every expectation, never seen anywhere in this town — not the eyes, not the brow, not one borrowed fragment — because Nerys Pryce, alone of everyone, wears nothing of Carys at all. She gave it all away. There was none left over for her own face.

"Goldie," says Goldie. "From the Pelican. Dil rang ahead."

"Dil rings ahead about everything." Nerys says it without heat, a fact about weather. "Cup of tea. I've only the bags, the loose went years ago."

* * *

The flat is the salon's ghost, one floor up.

Goldie has been in the salon itself — talked her way past the shutter ten days ago, stood among the fossilised mousse and the rust-print bride on the wall — and the flat is the same time, preserved warmer. A three-bar fire doing the work of two bars. A television with a doily on it. And the walls.

She sees the walls and her whole trade goes very still.

Photographs. Not framed — pinned, taped, propped, slotted into the frame of the mirror over the mantel and behind the clock and along the picture rail in a ribbon that goes round the room twice. Hundreds. Women, mostly, and a scatter of men and children, every one of them photographed in the salon, in the chair, under the dryer, in front of the same panel of mirror with the same string of fairy lights reflected in it across thirty years of fashion. A wall of heads. Goldie stands in the middle of it the way you'd stand in the middle of a congregation, and the congregation looks back, and she reads it.

She does not mean to. She has never in her life been able to mean not to. But this is not the involuntary flick of the turnstile, the man-shaped 2009 that arrives and is filed and gone. This is the gift turned up to the work it was made for and never had, two hundred faces at once and time to take them, and what it does to her is what a chord does, all the notes landing together, except the chord is wrong, the chord has been wrong all along, and she has just walked into the room where it was written.

The brow.

It is everywhere. She knew it was a fashion — Dil told her, *Nerys gave it to everyone, it was the brow* — but knowing it and standing in it are different countries. Here is a bride from a year of big sleeves with the high surprised arch sitting over a face that has nothing else to do with Carys Pryce. Here is a woman at a christening, here three on a hen do in matching sashes, here a grandmother with a perm like architecture, here a girl of eighteen and a woman of sixty in the same fortnight by the look of the print, and on every single one of them, drawn, shaped, plucked and pencilled and *built*, the exact lift, the taper, the inner arch a half-millimetre proud of where a brow naturally sits, that Goldie has been carrying behind her eyes since a churchwarden filed past her counter and broke her theory in half.

It is not inherited. She has known that since Olwen. But she has spent the days since the funeral letting it be sinister — a *signature*, a man's cold method, faces marked the way a brand marks a flank. And it is not that either. It is this. It is a woman with a comb and a pencil and thirty-one years and a wall of the results, kept, every one, because she could not throw a face away.

"That's my girls," says Nerys, coming back with the tea, following Goldie's eye to the wall without alarm, with something that is nearly the opposite of alarm. "Thirty-one years of them. I kept a snap of everyone, after. Carwyn said I was daft. But you do a head, see, you do it lovely, and then they walk out the door and the rain has it and it's gone, isn't it, all that work, an hour and a half of work walking off down the front in the wet. So I started keeping a snap. So it wasn't gone."

"There's a lot of them," says Goldie, which is the most careful sentence she has ever constructed.

"Forty a week, busy. Less, the end." Nerys sets the tea down and looks at the wall the way you look at a garden you planted, proprietary and a little surprised it grew. "I did this town's hair, near enough. Weddings. The dead, some of them — I did Mrs. Ellis for her funeral, I did a few for their funerals, it's an honour that, you don't say no. Brides. First days of school. You'd be amazed what comes through a chair." She picks a fleck off the doily. "I'm out of practice talking. You'll forgive me. I'm better with my hands than my mouth, I always was."

* * *

Goldie has come here to corner a workshop. She came up the stairs with the question loaded — *who taught you the look, who told you to give them that face, whose method is this* — and now she stands in the warm with a cup she does not want and watches the question dissolve, because there is no method here, there is no *who*, there is only Nerys, and Nerys does not know.

That is the thing that arrives, slow and terrible, while the kettle ticks itself cool in the next room.

Nerys does not know what she did.

Goldie tests it the only way she has — sideways, the way she does everything, the way she has learned to do everything since the day she learned that the front door is for people who can afford to lose. "You had a way with a brow," she says. "Everyone says. The Pryce brow."

And Nerys laughs — not the laugh Carol-Anne wears, that one's a different woman's, learned in a different chair — a short embarrassed pleased sound, a woman handed a compliment she's been hungry for so long she's forgotten how to hold it. "Well. You shape to the face, that's all it is. But I did like a good high brow, I'll own that. Opens a face up, see. A low heavy brow makes a woman look like she's cross at you before you've done anything." She lifts her own hand to her own brow, unconscious, demonstrating on herself, and Goldie watches the thumb do the exact movement it has done across a thousand foreheads — the lift, the taper, the inner arch a half-millimetre proud — and Nerys has no more idea she is drawing a dead girl than she has of the tide going out two streets away. "I'd say to them, let me give you a bit of a lift. They liked it. They came back for it. You give people what they didn't know they wanted, that's the trade." A small pride, banked low like the fire. "I gave a lot of women a nice brow."

She did. She gave a lot of women a nice brow. She gave them Carys's brow, week after week, fortnight after fortnight, for thirty-one years — *let me give you a bit of a lift* — pencilling her daughter's one surprised arch onto every forehead in a town that buried the girl and never knew it was wearing her, and she does not know, she will go to her grave not knowing, she thinks she has a knack and a fashion and a wall of nice work saved from the rain, and Goldie stands in the middle of it and has to put the tea down because her hand has stopped being reliable.

This is not a workshop. This is a mother who could not stop making her child.

* * *

She does not say it. There is no version of this room in which she says it. She has spent fifteen years learning which boxes not to open and the rest has been the trouble, and this is the most locked box she has ever stood next to, and she keeps her voice exactly where she keeps it, in the dry middle register where nothing gets a grip, and she lets the wall be a wall and the woman be a woman who likes a high brow, and the whole time the floor of her own certainty is going out from under her, because she came here for a monster and the monster is grief, and grief is not a thing you arrest.

She makes herself useful. It is the only mercy she has to hand.
