She admires a head — a real one, a genuine one, a woman with a beehive in a print from before Goldie was born — and Nerys lights, properly lights, and crosses to the wall and starts to tell her, this one was Glenda, married a sailor, this one's the chapel, this one I did for forty years till she went, and for ten minutes the flat is not a crime scene, it is two women in front of a wall of work, and one of them is happier than she has been in a long time because someone has finally, finally asked her about the thing she is proud of, and Goldie lets her have it, lets her run, the way Dil runs, the way the whole warm machinery of this town runs over her and through her, and underneath she is doing the cold arithmetic she came to do, and the cold arithmetic is this: the brow is accounted for. The brow, the cut, the way of holding the head — all of it, made, here, by these hands, out of love that never knew its own name. The made channel has a face now and the face is kind.

But the wall is innocent.

That is what stops her, mid-admiration, the tea going cold and the three-bar fire roaring. She has read two hundred faces off this wall and not one of them has the eyes. Not the colour — she's past colour — the *build*, the set and the cant and the heavy patient lid, the thing that is in Rhodri and in the boy by the chip shop and in a fourteen-year-old at a bus shelter opposite a hospice. It is not on this wall. Nerys could pencil a brow. Nerys could teach a head to tilt and a mouth to laugh in three notes. Nerys could not, with all the love and all the pencils in Wales, *give a stranger an eye she was born without.* The made things are here. The born things are not, and never could be, because no comb reaches the bone.

So where do the eyes come from.

Goldie holds her cold tea and looks at a wall full of a mother's grief, every fragment of it explained, and feels the floor she stood on yesterday — *a man, a method, faces taken and kept* — slide quietly off into the sea, and feels the new floor refuse to form, because the new floor needs a thing she does not have. The brow is craft. The brow is this room. But the eyes are blood, and blood does not come down from a girl who ran away at sixteen and never had a child. Blood needs a child. A runaway leaves a poster. A runaway does not leave grandchildren wearing her own eyes two streets from where she ran.

Unless.

* * *

"Can I ask you," Goldie says, when the wall has run its course and they are sat with the cold tea between them, "about Carys."

She watches it land. She has watched a great many faces take a great many blows and she knows the shapes they make, and the shape Nerys makes is not the one she has come to expect from the town, the long-rehearsed grief, the worn groove of *she broke my heart, she never came home.* Nerys goes still in a different way. She goes still the way a clock goes still — not slowing, *stopped*, a held thing, the hands fixed where someone fixed them.

"Carys went to my sister's," Nerys says. "Carmarthen way. To get herself sorted. She was always a one for — she'd had a wobble, see, with school, with a boy, you know how they are at that age, and we thought a change. My sister's." A pause, and the pause is wrong, the pause has a wall in it. "And then she didn't come home."

"She was at your sister's how long?"

"A while." The stopped clock. "Through the spring."

"And in the spring—"

"And then she didn't come home," Nerys says again, and it is not grief, it is recitation, it is a woman saying the words that were given to her to say, smooth as a stone in a pocket, smooth as something handled until all the edges are gone, and Goldie — who has spent her whole life telling the difference between a thing remembered and a thing rehearsed, who got it wrong once with a man on a burning bus and has never trusted herself the same way since but is not wrong about this, knows she is not wrong about this the way she knows a seen face from an unseen one — Goldie hears, under the recitation, the silence where the spring should be.

There is no spring in Nerys Pryce. There is a sister's house and a girl who didn't come home and a poster and twenty-two years, and where the spring of 2003 ought to sit — the months of it, the texture, the small true terrible details a mother keeps of the last time, the awful useless precious litter of an ending — there is nothing. Not pain. *Nothing.* A managed nothing. A nothing with a clean edge, the way a field has a clean edge where the council mowed it, where a hand came through and took the spring out of her and left the cover story, smooth, repeatable, hers to say at the hatch for the rest of her life without it ever once cutting her, because there is nothing left in there to cut on.

"Who told you she'd gone to your sister's," Goldie says, very quietly.

And Nerys looks at her, and for a half-second something moves behind the stopped clock — not memory, the absence of memory, a person reaching for a date in a drawer that has been emptied and finding the drawer and not the date — and then it closes, the way Branwen closed, the way a door closes that someone oiled long ago so it would always close the same.

"She went to my sister's," says Nerys Pryce, "to get herself sorted," and reaches for the cold teapot, and asks Goldie would she take a fresh cup, and the wall of all her girls looks down on the two of them, two hundred kept faces and not one of them the right one, and somewhere on the front the rain starts up against the window like a thing patiently trying the glass.

* * *

Dil is waiting at the bottom of the salon stairs when Goldie comes down, because Dil is always waiting at the bottom of something, and she has brought, against every instruction, two of Carwyn's bacon rolls in a paper bag gone translucent at the corners.

"Well?" says Dil. "How's she keeping? I'd not been up in — oh, a year. More. You feel awful, but you don't go, do you, you mean to and you don't."

"She showed me the wall," says Goldie.

"Oh, the wall." Dil's face does something complicated and fond. "She always kept the snaps. Daft, I said, you'll never look at them, who keeps a snap of a perm — and now it's all she's got, near enough, that wall and the telly." She hands Goldie a roll, which is warm, and holy, and the only entirely good thing that has happened in four days. "Cariad never got over the girl, see. You don't, mind. But Nerys, she — it's like the lights went off in the back of the house and she's still in the front room being polite. You know?"

"I know," says Goldie, and eats the holy bacon, and looks back up the dark stairwell to the flat where a woman is at this moment pouring fresh tea for no one, and does the arithmetic one more time, the load-bearing one, the one she has been not-doing the way she does not-do everything that frightens her.

The brow is made. She has the maker. The maker is grief and the maker does not know.

But the eyes are born. And a girl who ran away does not leave a bloodline. And the only way — the only way in all the world — that a dead runaway's own eyes are alive in a boy at the deep end and a girl at a bus shelter is if the runaway was never a runaway, if she did not go to a sister's in Carmarthen to get herself sorted, if there is a child somewhere who came out of those lost months that someone reached into Nerys's head and smoothed away, a child grown up and grown on, blood of her blood, and that means Carys Pryce did not run.

*Some girls don't run.*

Branwen said it in a hospice with her hands folded, and Goldie filed it under the maddening things the dying say, and Branwen is in the ground now where someone put her early, and the sentence comes back up out of the dark stairwell whole and cold and complete, the way they always come, unbidden, fully formed, and lands in the warm chlorinated middle of Goldie's chest where the bacon was meant to go.

She did not run. She did not run.

Then where, in the spring of 2003, in a town that took the spring out of her own mother's head and left a wall of borrowed faces and a poster on a lamp post, did Carys Pryce actually go.

"You've gone a colour," says Dil, frowning, reaching for her arm. "You all right? Was it the wall? It does you in, that wall, I should've—"

"I'm fine," says Goldie. "Long way from a coffee."

It is the most reliable lie she owns, and for once it does not even slow Dil down, who is already off — the funeral flowers, a feud about the wreath, the boiler, the warm flood of the town closing over the cold thing Goldie is carrying back down the front in the rain like a head an hour and a half in the making that the weather is going to have off her if she lets it.

She does not let it.

---

> Channel closed: made = grief, not a method. No collector. No workshop. A mother who couldn't stop.
> But the brow is not the eyes, and the eyes are not on her wall. The born ones aren't hers to give — no comb reaches bone.

---

# CHAPTER 18 — The Function Room Ledger

Faces have stopped telling her things, and Goldie does not entirely trust the silence.

It is the worst feeling she knows, this one — worse than the bad coffee, worse than the boiler doing its slow disappointment behind the wall — because it is the feeling of having read every face in the building and reached the bottom of what a face can give. She has the brow and where it comes from, which is a chair and a pair of grieving hands. She has the eyes and that they come from somewhere else entirely, somewhere young, somewhere in the blood, somewhere a salon could never reach. She has a wall of a thousand copies and a fourteen-year-old whose heart goes wrong at the deep end and a dead woman two streets away who decided to stop carrying a thing and was stopped instead. What she does not have is the bridge — the join between a girl who vanished in 2003 and the children walking past her hatch in 2025 wearing her face — and faces, for once in her life, are not going to hand it over. The face she needs belongs to nobody alive. It belongs to a filing cabinet.

"Some girls don't run," Branwen had said, in a hospice that smelled of disinfectant and waiting, the last full sentence the woman ever gave her. Goldie has carried it for a fortnight like a stone in a shoe. *Some girls don't run.* It is the kind of thing you say when you have spent twenty-two years watching a town grieve the wrong story and have finally, dying, run out of the strength to let it stand. If Carys didn't run — if Carys never left — then the eyes did not get into the children by being passed down across the years from a woman living some other life under some other name. They got passed down from a woman who was *here*. Who had a baby here. And there is only one kind of record that holds a thing like that, and it is not a face.

It is a function room full of boxes the developer's men are coming to clear on Thursday.

* * *

"Absolutely not," says Sai.

"You haven't heard it."

"I don't have to hear it. I can see it on you. You've got the face you had before the salon, the burglar's face, and the last time you had the burglar's face I aged four years and stole a key off a churchwarden." He is standing in the doorway of the duty office with a clipboard held to his chest like a breastplate, twenty-four years old and doomed to close this building and visibly aware that closing it would be a great deal simpler if the lost-property assistant would stop committing crimes inside it. "Whatever it is, no."

"The Trust kept its records up in the function rooms."

"No."

"For thirty years. Confinements, the bible says — I mean Eira says. Adoptions. The quiet work, all of it, the back offices, the filing." She lets it sit. "And on Thursday a man called Pugh with a van and a skip is coming to clear the upstairs before contracts are signed, because nobody's paying to store thirty years of a defunct charity's paperwork, and it goes in the skip, and then it goes nowhere, and then a girl named Seren Hollis faints at a gala one day and a doctor writes *dehydration* on a form because there is no paper left in the world to tell him different."

Sai's clipboard comes down an inch.

That is the thing about the boy, she has learned: you cannot frighten him with the case, because the case is abstract and a closing building is not, but you can stop him dead with a real child and a real form. He has the doom of the place in his bones — he has spent six months watching a thing he secretly loves be costed and condemned — and he cannot abide waste, the specific waste of a thing thrown out because nobody could be troubled to keep it. The waste is the lever. She is not proud of knowing exactly where his lever is. She uses it anyway.

"It's not even tampering," she adds, gently, watching it work. "We're not changing a record. We're *rescuing* one. From a skip. Before the skip. That's — Sai, that's basically archiving. That's heritage. There's probably a grant for it."

"There is not a grant for it."

"There's a grant for everything, you told me that. You said the whole town runs on grants and grief."

"That was a private remark." But the clipboard is all the way down now, against his leg, and he is doing the thing he does when he has lost — looking at the ceiling, as if the answer might be written in the water stain shaped like Wales. "If we get caught," he says, "I was never here."

"You're always never here. It's your superpower."

"And we do it quiet. We do it while there's something on. Bingo, or—"

"It's Thursday tonight," says Goldie. "There's bingo Thursday."

"Of course there's bingo Thursday." He shuts his eyes. "There's always bingo Thursday. This whole town is a front for bingo."

* * *

The thing about a heist conducted under cover of bingo is that bingo provides, without trying, the two things a heist most requires: noise, and an entire population accounted for in one room.

By half seven the function room's big sister — the function room, the *real* one, the faded-grand ballroom where the Trust once did its weddings and christenings and its other quieter business — is sealed off by a velvet rope and a folding sign reading STAFF ONLY in a font that has not frightened anyone since decimalisation. Below it, in the lesser hall, the whole of Aberglas over sixty has assembled under the strip lights with their dabbers and their lucky troll dolls and their flasks, and Selwyn, who has called the bingo since the foot-and-mouth, is warming up the machine. Goldie can hear him through the floor. *Two fat ladies, eighty-eight. Doctor's orders, number nine.* The numbers come up through the boards like weather, and under the weather of them, on the dark landing above, Goldie and Sai work a torch along a wall of cardboard.

"Lookout's in place," says Dil, arriving on the landing the way she arrives everywhere, without warning and intending to stay. She has appointed herself, naturally; there was never any version of this in which Dil was not on the landing. She has dressed for it, which is to say she is wearing her best cardigan and the expression of a woman who has waited forty-one years for the Pelican to finally be *interesting*. "Maureen's got the bottom of the stairs. Anyone tries to come up, she'll have them."

"Have them how," says Sai, with the strain of a man who needs to know the exact mechanism of his alibi.

"She'll talk to them about her hip." Dil says it the way you'd say *she's armed.* "Twenty-two years that hip's been going. She can do forty minutes on the hip before she's even reached the replacement. Nobody gets past Maureen's hip. It's a public service." She peers at the wall of boxes. "Ooh. Look at all the *dead admin.* Isn't it lovely. Every box a funeral."

The boxes are not labelled the way the basement is labelled, by decade and by hand. They are labelled the way a charity labels things when the charity is tired and the volunteers keep changing — half of them in a felt-tip that has gone to ghost, half of them in a typed sticker peeling at the corner, ST NON'S TR — FIN, ST NON'S TR — CORR, and then a run of them, a whole shelf of them, in a small fierce upright hand that Goldie's eye catches on and does not let go of, the way her eye catches on an earlobe. CONFINEMENTS. The word sits there in its careful capitals, doing its careful institutional work, meaning *we helped girls who had nobody*, meaning *the quiet work*, meaning a thing the town agreed not to ask about because the people doing it were kind and the alternative was a kind of cruelty everyone preferred not to picture.

"That one," says Goldie.

Sai lifts it down. It is lighter than it ought to be, the way the basement's 2003 box was lighter than it ought to be, and Goldie clocks that too, files it, says nothing. They set it on a trestle table that still has a paper cloth on it from some long-finished christening, a faint scatter of silver horseshoes, and Goldie opens thirty years of other people's worst and most secret afternoons under the torch, while beneath their feet the whole town shouts *house* and a woman wins a meat voucher and the night goes ordinarily, gloriously on.

* * *

It is, mostly, sad paper.

She had braced for it to be horror — for the predator, for the workshop, for the cold designing hand she has been chasing since they found Branwen with the pillow not quite where a pillow ought to be. The theory has been riding her for a fortnight: a man who *took* girls, who *kept* them, who arranged their scattered features about the town like a collector pinning moths, and who killed a dying midwife to keep the collection quiet. It is a good theory. It explains a murder, which her other theories never did. And she sits down in the function room with the CONFINEMENTS box and waits for it to be confirmed, for the paper to be a monster's paper.

It is not a monster's paper. It is worse, in the way the true thing is always worse than the lurid one. It is *forms*. It is a charity's careful, frightened, well-meaning forms, decades of them, a girl to a page, each one a small managed catastrophe filed under a heading and a date. Initials, mostly, not names — the Trust was discreet, the Trust was nothing if not discreet — a column for the date of arrival and a column for the date of the confinement and a column, God help them, for *outcome*, and Goldie reads down the *outcome* column the way she reads down the lost-property shelf, the whole human condition sorted and left to go quietly green: *placed. placed. placed. mother to family. placed. removed by mother. placed.* Girl after girl, fifteen and sixteen and seventeen, sent here by a parent or a vicar or a doctor or their own terror, confined in the back of a leisure centre that was once the civic heart of a town, and parted from a baby, and sent home *changed*, and never once, on any page, named in full.

"This is awful," says Dil, quietly, from the landing door. She has come to read over Goldie's shoulder. The bright gossip's delight has gone out of her entirely; she has the face of a woman recognising a thing she half-knew and never let herself add up. "I knew they did it. Everyone knew they did it. You just — you didn't *think* about it, did you, it was kind, they were being kind." She touches the edge of a page. "There's somebody's whole life. *Placed.* One word."

"Most lost things," Goldie says, "get one word."

She does not look up while she says it. She turns pages. She is looking for a spring, a spring twenty-two years gone, and she finds it the way you find the one face in a crowd — not by searching, exactly, but by letting the searching happen to her until it resolves — about three-quarters down the box, in a slim folder gone soft at the corners, with a date on it in that same fierce upright capital hand.

* * *

She knows it is the one before she has read a word of it, because of the gap.

Every other folder in the box is thin but complete — initials, dates, the *outcome*, a placement reference, the small bureaucratic full-stop of a thing concluded and closed. This one is thinner. This one has had things *taken out of it*. There is a place where a name should be and the name is not redacted, which would at least be honest, a black bar admitting a secret; the name is simply *gone*, a form with the mother's particulars excised at some later date by someone with a knife of a mind, leaving the shape of the absence behind. A column scraped clean. A date of confinement: spring, twenty-two years ago. A date of arrival some months before. And an *outcome*, in the careful hand, the only thing on the page anyone troubled to keep: *infant placed locally — closed adoption — records sealed at request of Trust.*

Records sealed at request of Trust. Not the family. Not the court. The Trust. The Trust sealed its own record of its own placement at its own request, which is a sentence that should not be able to exist, and which exists, here, in pencil that has not faded, under Goldie's torch, while two floors of bingo roar on.

And then the thing that is not on the page, which is the loudest thing of all. There is no *outcome* for the mother. Every other girl in the box has a discharge note, however terse — *returned home; collected by parent; transferred.* A line that lets her leave the page alive. This girl has nothing. Arrival, confinement, the baby placed and sealed away, and then the form simply *stops*, the way a sentence stops when the person speaking it dies. The mother arrives. The mother is confined. The mother has a daughter. And then the mother does not leave the building, not on this paper, not on any paper, because there is no paper in the world that admits a girl walked into the back of the Pelican to have a baby and never walked out.

"Goldie." Sai is over her shoulder now too, and his voice has gone careful and small. He is reading the *outcome* line. He is doing the arithmetic, the boy is quick, he is doing the same arithmetic she is doing and arriving where she is arriving, which is at a hole in the shape of a girl. "Spring. Twenty-two years ago. That's—"

"Don't," she says, not unkindly. "Don't say it yet. Once we've said it we have to be right."

But she is looking at the gap where a name was lifted out by a careful hand years after the fact, and she is thinking of a board upstairs in her memory with two columns on it, *Born* and *Made*, and the eyes that only land on the young, and the *funny colour at the deep end*, and a woman in a hospice who said *some girls don't run* and then died before she could finish the sentence, and the whole impossible thing is turning over in her, slow and enormous, like a tide deciding.

*She didn't run. She had a baby. Here. And the baby stayed in the town.*

The eyes are not a copy and not a coincidence and not a collector's trophy. The eyes are a *descent*. There is a girl two floors below her — fourteen, a hand pressed once to her chest at a bus shelter — wearing a dead girl's eyes, and the dead girl is not a face Carys ran away into the world carrying. The dead girl is a great-grandmother who never got to be one. The line goes down. The eyes go down. And the thing that rides down beside the eyes, on the same dark chromosome, the thing that stopped Carys's heart in a back room of this building nine days after she became somebody's mother — that goes down too, narrow and young and silent, into the children, into the bone, into a fourteen-year-old's chest pressed flat for half a second under a bus shelter on a Tuesday.

She has been chasing a monster. There is no monster. There is a *family*, and a thing in the family's blood that nobody has named, and a heart still ticking in living children that does not know it is supposed to stop.

* * *

There is more, because there is always more, and the more is paper too, and the more is what closes the second loop.

In the bottom of the box, under the folders, a banded bundle of correspondence: a clinic's letterhead, a dentist's, an *orthodontist's*, three towns over, the practice long since absorbed into something with a brand name and a parking problem. Referral slips. Decades of them. And every one of them — Goldie reads them under the torch with the strip light of the salon buzzing in her memory — comes from the same source, in the same world, *re: yr patient, kindly assessed at request of Mrs N. Pryce, hairdresser, of this town, with a view to improving the smile-line; the gap to be closed to the patient's wish.* Slip after slip. Girl after girl, woman after woman, sent across two counties by a hairdresser, to have a tooth-gap closed, to have a smile made to *sit right* — to sit, Goldie understands now, the way one particular smile sat, in one particular face, in one 2003 gala photograph, a smile with a gap in it that its owner never lived long enough to close.

This is the craft channel, in writing. This is Nerys, documented from the far side — not a forger working in secret but a referral on a letterhead, a paper trail of a grief so thorough it reached into the mouths of strangers and rebuilt a dead girl's smile in them, two counties over, by appointment. The made smiles, on file. The fixed gaps, dated and signed. And the difference between the made and the born, which she has had to read off living faces for a fortnight, squinting at the edge of a tooth to tell whether a thing was closed by a surgeon or left open by God — that difference is here, now, in paper, separable, provable, with a referral slip on one side and a sealed adoption on the other. The brow and the smile came from a chair. The eyes and the heart came from a body. One was *given* and one was *born*, and she can prove it now without a single face at all.

"Two boxes," she says, mostly to herself, the way she said *two of them* in the salon. "One holds the made ones. One holds the born one." She sets the orthodontist's bundle down beside the sealed folder, side by side on the silver-horseshoed cloth. "Same town. Same dead girl. Two completely different machines."

"What's the difference," Sai asks. He has stopped being frightened and started being something else, something she recognises and tries not to encourage, the thing that happens to people who get too close to her and the work — the leaning-in, the wanting-to-know.

"The salon made her *look*," Goldie says. "Forty years, head after head, a brow here, a smile there — a town full of women carrying bits of a girl who's dead, and none of them blood, and all of them kind, and one woman behind it all who doesn't even know she did it." She moves her hand to the other folder, the thin one, the one with the gap. "But the salon can't make an *eye*. Can't make a heart. You can close a gap with a surgeon and you can give a brow with a comb, but you cannot put Carys Pryce's actual eyes into a fourteen-year-old at a bus shelter unless that fourteen-year-old has Carys Pryce's actual blood. And she does." She taps the sealed folder once, gently, the way you'd touch a sleeping thing. "Because of this. Because a girl had a baby here, and the baby stayed, and the baby grew, and the line came down."

* * *

She nearly misses the last thing, because the last thing is in the margin, and the margin is where she has trained a whole career not to look until she has read the body of the text. But the torch slides as she lifts the folder to put it back, and the beam catches the inside edge of the cover, and there it is — a note in the careful upright capital hand, the *administrator's* hand, the same hand that wrote CONFINEMENTS on the box and sealed the record at the Trust's own request. A small dated annotation, years after the spring it concerns, written by someone going back through an old file and tidying it, the way a frightened man tidies a thing he cannot throw away: *file reviewed — no action — refer to G.H. only.*

*Refer to G.H. only.*

She does not know the hand. She wants to be honest with herself about that, here, in the function room, with the bingo roaring and Sai breathing over her shoulder and a meat voucher being awarded two floors down — she does not *know* this hand the way she knows a face. A hand is not a face. She has been wrong about meanings her whole life and she is not going to be wrong about this one by wanting it too much. But she has stood in a packed function room and watched a beloved man lead the fight to save this building, the only face in the room that gave her nothing, a face with no give, and she has read his name on a hospice visitors' book up near the top of a dead woman's last week. *G.H.* She files the initials in the part of her that does not lie and cannot yet explain. She closes the folder. She does not say the name out loud, because once you say a name out loud you have to be right, and she has buried one man already under a thing she was sure of.

She puts the box back exactly where it was. She is good at putting things back exactly where they were; it is, in a sense, the only thing she has ever been good at.

Below, the bingo ends. The roar resolves into the scrape of three hundred chairs and the rising tide of three hundred conversations, and somewhere under it, faint and territorial and immortal, Maureen is still describing her hip to a man who came to read the meter and will now never be the same. Dil pokes her head round the landing door, her colour come back, her gossip's brightness returning the way the light comes back after a dark beat, because the town is downstairs being ordinary and ridiculous and continuing, and a person can only hold a sealed adoption for so long before the meat voucher reasserts itself.

"Well?" she says. "Did we find the body?"

And Goldie, who has just found, in a box of sad forms, exactly the shape of a body — a girl who walked into this building to become a mother and is recorded as having done so and is recorded nowhere as having left — looks at Dil's hopeful ordinary face and feels the floor do the thing it did on the first morning, tilt very slightly, the way it does just before you understand a thing you are going to have to carry.

"Not the body," she says. "Not yet."

Because that is the last lost thing, and it is not in any box upstairs. The salon held the made face. The CONFINEMENTS box held the born one. The orthodontist held the smile and the Trust held the sealed child and the careful hand held the name it lifted out and the initials it left in. Three machines, three boxes, three decades of paper — and they all run on the same missing person, and not one of them says where she went.

A filed girl. A sealed child. An unmarked grave.

She does not know yet where the third one is. But she knows now, the way she knows everything, instantly and against her will and with the whole weight of it finally arriving — she knows the girl never left this town, and that means the girl is still in it, somewhere in the ground of it, waiting twenty-two years to be the last thing claimed.

---

> **FACE 0001 / 0011 / 0016 / 0023 — CROSS-REF.** Not a face tonight. Paper.
> All the BORN ones — the eyes, the gap-never-closed, the ear, the eyes again — descend from ONE 2003 confinement. Mother's name lifted out *after.* Infant placed locally; sealed at the Trust's own request; no discharge for the mother. *None.*
> Carys didn't run. She had a baby HERE. The baby stayed. The eyes are the bloodline; the heart rides with them.
> The MADE ones = Nerys, documented (orthodontist referrals, two counties, "the gap to be closed").
> A hand in the margin, years later: *refer to G.H. only.* I do not know the hand. I will not say the name.
> **STATUS: PLACING.**

---

# CHAPTER 19 — Same Eyes, Two Decades

There is a chair at the lost-property desk that Goldie has never once seen anybody sit in, and on Tuesdays at ten past four she watches it the way other women watch a kettle.

It is the visitor's chair, plastic, the orange of a feeling nobody has anymore, bolted at a polite angle to the front of the counter so that the bereaved of the swimming pool may lower themselves into it while she goes through the shelf for a thing they have already decided is gone. Mostly it holds coats. Today it holds nothing, and the clock above the turnstile says four-oh-nine, and Goldie has the page of the ledger open to FACE 0001 and a cup of Carwyn's coffee going cold beside it as a kind of offering, and she is waiting, with the specific dishonest patience of a woman who has told everyone including herself that she is only doing the records.

She has been afraid of this man for a month.

Not of him. She has never been afraid of a person in her life in the ordinary way; the gift took the ordinary way off her young, the way it takes everything, leaving her with the worse fear, the one that comes from seeing too much rather than too little. She is afraid of his *face.* She is afraid because he is the one face in this whole drowning town that her gift cannot file, and a thing her gift cannot file is, to Goldie, what a sound in the wall at night is to anybody else.

FACE 0001. The first entry in the book. The Tuesday Man.

She wrote him on her third day, before she had a single other thread, before Carys had a name or the twins had cheekbones or the whole rotten constellation had a shape — wrote him out of pure instinct, the way you flinch from a hot ring before the pain arrives: *too much. too much of her in one face.* Because that is the trouble with him, that has always been the trouble with him. The others carry a fragment. One borrowed feature each, a single page torn from a dead girl and handed unknowing to the living — the eyes on Rhodri, the gap on Lowri, the cheekbones split clean down a pair of twins. A page each. You can hold a page.

The Tuesday Man carries a *fistful.* The eyes — Carys's eyes, the build of them, the patient cant, the forgiving lid — and the brow over the eyes, the high surprised arch, *the* brow, the made one, the one Dil swears Nerys gave to half the bay; and something in the set of the whole face that her gift keeps reaching for and not closing on, the way your tongue keeps going to the gap a tooth left. Born features and made features on one face at once. A man wearing two channels. He has broken her confidence for a month precisely because he refuses to sort. He should be blood or he should be craft and he is somehow, impossibly, both, and a thing that is both is a thing she has got wrong, and the last time she got a face wrong a man four streets away spent eleven days finding out what her certainty costs.

So she watches the orange chair.

* * *

"You're doing it again," says Dil, arriving on her rails, settling at the hatch with a custard cream and the conspiratorial air of a woman about to be enormously helpful in the wrong direction. "Watching the door. You've a face on you like the clock's done something."

"I'm waiting on a man about a goggle," says Goldie, which is the literal truth and therefore the deepest possible cover, and Dil accepts it the way she accepts all weather.

"The Tuesday one." Dil dunks. "Left goggle. Never collects. I've got him down in the book going back to before you came, look — *Tues, left a goggle, says he'll be back for it* — and back he comes, regular as the tide, and does he take the thing? Does he heck. Stands there. Has a little look at the shelf. Asks is it come in. Says he'll leave it another week in case. Off he goes." She shakes her head, fond, filing him under the long warm catalogue of the town's harmless lunatics. "Forty years on this desk and I'll tell you the one thing I've learned, Goldie. It is never about the goggle."

"No," says Goldie, who is, professionally, the world's leading authority on the fact that it is never about the goggle. "It rarely is."

And at four-eleven the swing doors breathe and he comes through them, and Goldie's whole month arrives at the counter.

He is thirty-eight. She has the rest of him before he is clear of the door, the involuntary inventory running its hands down him the way it runs down everyone: a working man's forearms, a wedding ring worn thin on the inside the way the thirty-year ones go, a soft new weight at the belt that says a happy house and a wife who cooks; a navy fleece with a plant-hire logo gone pale at the shoulders; tired in the good way, the way of a man whose tiredness has people in it. A nice face. An open, decent, unremarkable, *settled* face — and across the top of it, unasked-for, undeserved, sitting over his eyes like something borrowed for a wedding, the Pryce brow, exact; and under the brow, looking out at her, patient and a little apologetic, eyes that her whole obsessed month wants to call *hers,* the dead girl's, and that are not quite — close, achingly close, the same patient cant and forgiving lid she has held exact on a nineteen-year-old at this counter and a fourteen-year-old at a bus shelter, but not the millimetre-match, not the thing she could swear to in a court, a near-miss her month has been polishing nightly into a certainty it was never entitled to.

"Sorry to be a nuisance," he says. "I don't suppose—"

"The goggle," says Goldie.

"The goggle." He smiles, sheepish, and the smile does a thing his face has not done while it was just standing there, and the thing it does is the answer to the whole month, only Goldie does not know that yet, she only knows the smile is *wrong,* the smile does not belong to the eyes, the smile is some other family's entirely. "I know. I know. My wife says I want my head looked at. Left one, black strap, the—"

"The lens is gone," Goldie says.

"The lens is gone." He laughs, properly now, surprised. "You've got me off by heart."

"I've got everyone off by heart," says Goldie. "It's a condition."

* * *

She does not go to the shelf. The half-goggle has lived on the shelf for the better part of two months and they both know it is not there, the way you both know the dead are dead, and she has decided, somewhere in the last sixty seconds, that she is finished being afraid of a man who comes to a lost-property desk every Tuesday and asks after a thing he will not take. She is too tired and the case is too close and there is a child somewhere in this town pressing a hand to a heart that does not know its own name.

"Can I ask you something," she says, "while you're stood there. Off the record. It's just—" she taps the ledger, the lie she lives in "—I'm doing the history of the place, for the campaign. The old galas. Faces."

"Oh, aye?" He brightens; everyone brightens; the town is dying and any stranger who wants to remember it is a friend for life. "I'm no good on the old days, mind. I'm not from here originally. Tregaron, me. Came over for the work and never went back." A shy grin at the building. "Married into it."

*Married into it.*

Goldie keeps her hand flat on the ledger and her voice exactly in the dry middle register where nothing can get a grip and asks, "Married into who, then?"

"Beynon," he says, with the small pride a man has in a name that isn't his. "Marged Beynon, was. Aled, I am — Aled Beynon now, I suppose, more hers than mine these days." He laughs. "You'll know the Beynons. Everyone knows the Beynons. Well. You'll know *of* them. They're a quiet lot." And then, because the town has trained him as it trains everyone, because a stranger asking about faces is owed the catalogue, he tells her, unasked, the one thing in all the world she has spent a month trying to learn: "Marged's side, see, they were always a bit of a— not a mystery, that's a daft word. But her gran was took in. Adopted, like. Long back. A local couple, the Beynons, never had their own, and they got the baby from— well, nobody ever said from where, did they. You didn't ask, those days. So Marged's never had a proper *back* to her family, if you follow. No grans-and-great-grans in frames up the stairs like normal. Branch with no roots showing, her dad used to say." He says it lightly, a thing about his wife's people, a Tuesday thing, and has no idea, none, that the floor of the world has just tilted very slightly under the orange chair he has still not sat in. "Why—does the campaign want the Beynons? I can ask her. She's got a box of bits somewhere."

* * *

There is a sound the town makes, Goldie has learned, when it hands you a thing without knowing what it is — a soft sound, a kindness sound, the sound of Dil filing a death under dehydration and Eira filing a buried girl under *they were kind* and now this good tired man filing the whole shape of the crime under *a box of bits somewhere.* It is the sound of recognition without knowing. The town has been making it at her for a month and she has only just learned to hear it, and hearing it is worse, because once you can hear it you understand that everybody is doing it all the time, walking around carrying things that would unmake them, handing them across counters to strangers, *you'll know the Beynons,* perfectly safe, perfectly doomed, recognising and recognising and never once knowing.

She has been chasing a monster.

She lets herself have the thought now, standing across the counter from Aled Beynon of Tregaron, *more hers than mine these days,* and she lets herself feel the whole stupid ugly architecture of it come down. For four days — since the tide came in on Branwen, since the town's warmth curdled and a dying woman was made to *slip away peacefully* the very week she'd decided to speak — Goldie has been building a thing in the dark in her head, a thing with a man at the centre of it, a thing that explained the violence. A collector. A man who took girls and *kept* them, who hoarded a face the way a sick mind hoards anything, who made the living wear a dead girl's features the way you'd pin a stolen brooch to a borrowed coat. It explained the murder. It explained the *too-much-of-her.* It was the kind of theory you build when there is a body on the ground and your hands are shaking, and it has been sitting in her like a swallowed stone, and she has half-suspected every man in this building of it, the lifeguard and the duty manager and the beloved grey article running the campaign, *which one of you,* and she has been, she sees now, entirely and confidently and catastrophically wrong.

Because there is no theory of a collector that produces *this.*

Look at him. Look at the face. The brow is real, and the brow is *made,* the salon brow, the one Nerys laid on half the bay — that much she would still swear to in a court, because a brow is craft and craft she can read to the millimetre. And under it the eyes are *near,* truly near, near enough that a tired woman could mistake near for same and has, nightly, for a month. A monster cannot do that. A monster cannot reach across twenty-two years and lay her mother's signature arch on a man from Tregaron who never met her — and *also* arrange that the same man, by no design of anyone's, happen to carry a family ghost of her colouring under it. No collection does that. No predation does that. No groomed look-alike, no kept trophy, no twisted shrine.

Only two things do that, working at once, neither of them a crime. A *family.* And a *salon.*

He is not a descendant. The brow is on him and the brow is craft and craft has no blood in it, she proved that in a shut salon a week ago, the brow on the dead and the old and a rust-print bride. And he is not a victim, and he is not a monster, and he is not the centre of anything. He married a woman whose grandmother was a baby nobody would say the source of, and he sat in Nerys Pryce's chair like every man in this town sat in Nerys Pryce's chair, *the boys came in with the mams,* and the salon gave him the arch — and the eyes, the eyes she has to be honest about now, here, finally, the way she was never honest in a witness box. *The eyes by marriage,* she told herself, the first awful week — as if a man could soak up his wife's people's faces across a kitchen table and start to wear them; as if love were heredity. It is not. You cannot catch an eye the way you catch an accent. A born thing is born or it is nothing, she has built her whole ruined gift on exactly that, and a salon could no more put Carys's true eyes on this man than it could put her heart in him. So she lets the easy lie go. What is actually here is smaller and truer and entirely innocent: a genuine scrap of family likeness — some in-law's colouring, some near cousin's set, the bay being three families deep and everyone married sideways into everyone — a real partial resemblance that was always only *partial,* sitting under the salon's exact arch, and worked over, night after night, by a woman who could not stop looking until the looking made more of it than was ever there. The brow, real and made. The likeness, real and faint. And a month of her own obsessed attention, doing the rest. He is not too much of one woman. He is an ordinary near-coincidence she magnified into a haunting, standing at her counter asking after a goggle, *the man beside the secret,* and she has been frightened of him for a month for the crime of being, of all the impossible things, *near.*

The relief of it is so total it is almost grief.

"Are you all right?" says Aled Beynon, because of course he does; they all do; she gives them all the same gentle alarm. "You've gone a bit—"

"Long way from a coffee," says Goldie, and finds, to her own surprise, that she means it as a kindness, and that he takes it as one.

* * *

"Oh, *Aled,*" says Dil, when he has gone — drifted off toward the doors with a *cheers anyway, I'll leave it another week in case,* the immortal phrase, the phrase that is never about the goggle — and Dil says his name the way you'd say the name of a soft old dog. "Aled Beynon. He's harmless, love, don't you be reading him. Married Marged. Marged that was took-in's granddaughter, you know, the Beynons—" and she is off, irrigating, the town coming through the hatch in its warm unstoppable flow, *Marged faints, mind, did as a girl, runs in them, the mother and the gran and all,* and Goldie stands very still inside the flood and lets the gossip finish the murder the archive could not.

"The goggle," says Goldie, when there is a gap. "Why does he keep coming for it. He never takes it."

"Oh, that." Dil's face does a thing, softens, goes briefly and surprisingly tender, the soft inaccurate sacred memory of the town reaching for the one fact that is not a joke. "That's not his goggle, see. That was Marged's da's. Old Beynon. Took-in's husband — well, the took-in's *son*, I'm getting myself in a twist, the families round here, you want a chart. Marged's father. He swam here every morning of his life, the lido and then the indoor when the lido went, sixty years near enough, and the week before he died he left a goggle at this very desk, the daft article, lost the one and went home in the other, and then he never—" she stops, dunks, doesn't, sets the biscuit down. "He never came back for it. And Aled started coming. For Marged, I always thought, so she didn't have to. Asking is it turned up. Course it hasn't. It's not going to, is it, after all this. But he keeps the slot, like. Tuesdays. Comes and asks and leaves it another week in case." She shrugs, the great soft shrug. "It's never about the goggle, Goldie. I keep telling you."

Goldie looks at the shelf, where among the dentures in their margarine tub and the wedding ring with no inscription and the single child's flip-flop she has not once let herself throw away, there is half a goggle, one lens fogged to crazed grey and the other gone clean out, a thing pretending to be a whole thing, that a dead man left on a Tuesday and a living man has come back for every Tuesday since, on behalf of a wife whose grandmother was a baby from a place nobody would say.

"No," says Goldie. "It's never about the goggle."

* * *

She does not write a flip-flop. She sits with her hand flat on the open ledger and she lets the whole thing assemble in her, the last beam dropping into the last slot, and for the first time since a boy leaned over this counter wearing somebody's dead eyes she can see the shape of it entire, and it is not a monster, and it was never a monster, and the not-a-monster is somehow harder to bear than the monster would have been.

A girl vanished at sixteen. A face came back, twenty-two years on, in pieces, scattered across the living. Half of it was *made* — laid on a thousand heads by a grieving woman behind a chair, the brow and the cut and the laugh, given to the old and the dead and a man from Tregaron, free, like a sweet, like a sermon, like the only prayer she had. And half of it was *born* — the eyes, the gap, the cheekbones, the ear — and the born half did not scatter at random, the born half came *down a line,* down out of a baby nobody would name the source of, into a quiet family with no roots showing in frames on the stairs, into Marged who faints and Marged's children who are out there right now in the maroon of the comprehensive and the red shorts of the lifeguard hut, wearing a great-grandmother's eyes and not one of them knowing whose, not one of them knowing what came down the line *with* the eyes, hand to the chest, there and gone, the length of a single beat.

Same eyes. Two decades. A dead girl's, and then again, alive, in children born long after she was gone.

There is only one thing in all the rotten honest world that does that, and it is not collection and it is not coincidence and it is not a ghost and it is not the worst thing Goldie spent four frightened nights imagining.

It is descent.

Carys Pryce did not run away. Goldie knows it now the way she knows a face, instantly and completely and without one fact she could yet say in a court: a girl who ran away does not leave grandchildren behind her in the bone. Carys had a baby in this town. The baby went, quiet, to a good home, *you didn't ask those days,* to a couple called Beynon with no child of their own. And the baby grew, and had a child, and that child is Marged who faints in chapel, and Marged had children, and the children are everywhere, and the eyes are everywhere the children are, and the only person in two decades who could see it, who could hold a 2003 swim-gala photograph against a living face and be certain to the millimetre, who could tell the born from the made and the blood from the craft and the page from the fistful — the only person was never meant to come to this town at all, and took the quietest job in Wales to make the seeing stop, and the seeing has just finished, on a Tuesday, ten past four, with a goggle nobody will ever claim.

She has the *who.* She has the *how.*

She does not have the *where.* That is the last thing. A girl had a baby in this building and then she was gone, declared a runaway, presumed dead by a court with no body and a notice in the paper — and if she did not run, if she never ran, if she has been here all along in the bone of the children, then her *body* is the one record nobody has read, and Goldie knows, the way she has been quietly knowing it for a week and refusing the knowing the way she refuses everything, exactly which unread ground in this town has been waiting twenty-two years for someone with the wrong gift and no sense to leave it alone.

She caps the pen.

Through the little window the pool throws its broken light on the ceiling, and the four-o'clock release is long gone, and somewhere out past the harbour wall the tide is going out the way it always goes out, uncovering the things the water keeps. On the shelf the half-goggle catches the light in its one crazed lens and gives it back, grey, the colour of the building, the colour of a thing that used to be loved.

Goldie reaches up and takes it down off the shelf, the dead man's goggle, the one his daughter's husband cannot stop coming back for, and she does not file it and she does not bin it and she does not put it in the box marked for the tip at the end of the quarter. She sets it on the counter where the orange chair is, square in front of the empty seat, so that next Tuesday at ten past four it will be the first thing he sees.

Then she goes to find Sai, because there is a piece of ground by an old chapel that the council's dig is going to open whether the town is ready or not, and she would rather get there first.

---

> **FACE 0001 — STATUS: PLACED.**
> male, 38 (ALED BEYNON, of Tregaron, m. Marged). The brow — Carys's, the arch, exact. The eyes — I wrote *exact* for a month. They are not. *Near.* A month of *too much of her in one face,* and the too-much was mine.
> Resolved: the brow is *salon* (Nerys's, like half the bay — craft, swearable). The eyes are a plain scrap of in-law likeness — a near-resemblance, partial, married sideways into the bay like everyone — that I over-read under the arch night after night until I made a haunting of a coincidence. You cannot catch an eye by marriage. Born is born. Not blood. Not a trophy. The man beside her.

> One record left unread. It is in the ground.

---

# CHAPTER 20 — The Unmarked Ground

The chapel keeps its dead the way the Pelican keeps its lost property: filed, unclaimed, and going quietly green.

Goldie finds it the long way round, because the short way is locked. The old chapel sits at the seaward edge of the grounds, behind the dead lido and a hedge of escallonia that has not been cut since a government you'd have to look up, a squat little box of slate and lime with its windows boarded and its noticeboard still advertising a harvest service for a year when she was at school. Huw gave her the back gate. *Now then,* he said, *mind the nettles,* and went off down the poolside to set up the gala chairs, six hundred of them, alone, the way he does everything, like a man carrying the same building on the same shoulder for thirty years.

She came for one thing. She has the rest of it already — the birth nobody recorded, the baby placed quiet and local, the brow that Nerys gave away on a thousand heads, the eyes that came down a bloodline and the heart that came down with them. She has the whole shape of it now, assembled out of fragments the way she assembles a stranger from an ear, and what she does not have is the last record, the one nobody filed because filing it was the crime.

She has the where.

The burial ground is the size of a tennis court and twice as forgotten. Proper stones at the front — *Pryce, Pugh, Edwards, asleep in Jesus, 1911, 1923, a whole drowned crew of the *Mary Glas*, 1938* — and then, past a sunk path where the grass changes, the part that has no stones at all. Just ground. A long unremarkable run of it, slightly higher than it should be in places, the way ground is where something was put in and the earth made up its mind to keep it.

"They buried the quiet ones up here," Eira told her, two days ago, holding her teacup in both hands as if it might bolt. "The ones the Trust couldn't put in the book. The babies that didn't take. The girls that—" and then Eira had looked at the door, at ninety-five, in her own front room, and gone where Eira goes when the memory gets too near the truth, which is silent.

Goldie stands on the higher ground now, in the wind off the bay, and reads it the only way she can, which is structurally. No name. No date. No face. For once in her life, nothing to recognise at all — and she has never in fifty-one years stood over a person and had less to go on, and never been more certain who it is. That is not recognition. Recognition is her gift and her gift has nothing to say here. This is the other thing, the thing she does not have a license for, the thing that nearly jailed a man four streets away with an alibi and a daughter.

This is a claim.

She takes out her phone to photograph the lie of the land — the rise, the changed grass, the corner where the developer's pegs are already in, little orange flags ticking in the wind, because the dig starts Monday and after Monday there is no asking the ground anything politely — and that is when Sai rings, and she knows before she answers, the way she knows everything, instantly and against her will, that the night has decided not to wait for Monday.

"You need to come," Sai says. He is not deadpan. Sai is always deadpan; it is his whole personality and his only defence against a job that is closing the love of his life. He is not deadpan now. "Goldie. The pool. It's the girl."

* * *

The gala is in full cry when she comes through the swing doors, and for one held second the room is exactly itself — exactly the thing they are all fighting to keep — and then it isn't.

The indoor pool, lit up for once, every bulb Huw could coax, bunting strung corner to corner in the centre's colours, a banner that says SAVE OUR PELICAN — GRAND GALA in letters Pam cut out herself and is therefore three sizes; the stand packed, the whole town in cardigans and good coats, a raffle drum, a tea urn the size of a depth charge, Carwyn doing bacon at a trestle as a public service. Children in the water in caps and numbered armbands. The smell of chlorine and chips and six hundred people who came out on a Friday because the alternative is letting one more loved thing die for the want of a fight.

And then the noise changes. A noise a crowd makes that you never have to be taught — the drop, the suck of breath, the way six hundred conversations become one held silence with a single edge of a scream in it.

A child is in the water at the deep end and she is not swimming.

Goldie is moving before she has decided to. Fifteen years of moving toward the thing everyone else moves away from; the body remembers even when the woman would rather it didn't. Through the gate in the rail, past a marshal too young to know what his hands are for, and she sees it all at once, files it whole the way she can't help: the girl face-up and going under, lips already the wrong colour, that specific dusk-blue that is not cold and is not breath-held, a fourteen-year-old's face she has logged before — *set, cant, lid; not the colour, the build* — Carys Pryce's eyes, open now, fixed on nothing, in a child whose heart has just done the thing that heart does.

"Get her *out*." That is Maureen.

Maureen is sixty-something and four foot eleven and has cleared the pool deck like the Red Sea. She has a wet whistle in her teeth and the absolute moral authority of a woman who has run bingo since Thatcher, and the crowd that would not move for a marshal moves for her, falls back, makes a lane, because some people are born to be obeyed in a crisis and the rest of us are born to obey them and be grateful. "*Back,*" she says. "Give the man room. Glenys — phone."

And here is the thing about Glenys, who is deaf in the left ear and has misheard every important sentence spoken to her since roughly the Coronation, who calls legs eleven *eggs eleven* and once thanked a man for his condolences at a christening. Glenys, who hears nothing, was the one who saw. Three rows up, knitting, half-watching, and it was Glenys who put her needles down and said, to nobody, in the small clear voice of a woman who has stopped second-guessing herself, *that girl's gone funny,* a full four seconds before the lifeguard's whistle — four seconds that are, it will turn out, the whole of it, the entire margin, the difference. The one time in this book it mattered to hear right, the deaf woman heard.

Huw hits the water like the building falling in.

He has the girl out in the time it takes Goldie to reach the edge — one enormous arm under, the lifeguard's haul, no panic in him at all, thirty years of pulling this town out of this water doing the work while his face does something Goldie will think about later — and lays her on the wet tile, and she is not breathing, and there is no pulse Goldie can find at the throat with two fingers, and the child's lips are the colour of the deep end.

"Seren," says a woman's voice, very high, from somewhere behind the rail. "*Seren.*"

"On her back. Tip the head." Goldie hears her own voice come out flat and Met and twenty years old. "Huw. Compressions. Now. Hard, in the middle, don't be gentle — you'll feel something give, that's meant. Sai — nine nine nine, tell them cardiac arrest, paediatric, fourteen, the Pelican, they know the Pelican. Tell them she went down in the water." She is counting already, under the words, the metronome she will never be able to unlearn. "Maureen — get the *defib*. The green box by reception. Run."

"It's an AED, love," says Pam, who has done a course. "Automated external—"

"*Run,*" says Goldie, and Pam runs.

* * *

There is a thing that happens to time, and Goldie has been inside it before, and hates it, because it is the one place her gift cannot follow her and so the one place she is briefly, blessedly, like everyone else — no faces, no filing, no involuntary inventory, just a child's chest under a man's hands and a number she is counting out loud so that Huw doesn't slow.

*One and two and three and four.* Huw's whole back working. The crowd a wall of held breath. Somewhere the tea urn still hissing, obscenely, the way ordinary things refuse to stop. A small green machine arriving in Pam's arms and Goldie peeling the pads and pressing them to a fourteen-year-old's wet skin — *upper right, lower left, the picture's on the pad, follow the picture* — and the calm robot voice of it, *analysing, do not touch the patient,* the most patient voice in the building, telling a roomful of terrified people to take their hands off a child.

*Shock advised.*

"Clear," says Goldie, and means it, and the child's body lifts off the tile a half-inch and falls back, and Huw is on her again before the machine has finished its sentence, and Goldie counts, and the woman behind the rail has stopped saying *Seren* and started making a sound with no words in it, and somewhere in the held dark of all that, a fourteen-year-old's chest takes a breath on its own.

A wet, dragging, furious breath.

And then another.

The colour does not come back the way it goes — it goes like a tide and comes back like a slow stain, an awful grey giving way to an awful white giving way, at last, to the ordinary blotchy pink of a living child who has just been very nearly dead, and Seren Hollis turns her head and is sick on the tile, which is, Goldie will think later, the most beautiful thing she has seen in twelve years, because the dead do not vomit.

The room exhales. Somebody starts to clap and is hushed, correctly. The paramedics come through the doors at a run with their bags and their certainty, and Goldie steps back and lets the competent take over, and stands with her hands held slightly away from her body the way you do when they have just been somewhere they cannot quite come back from, and watches them work, and feels the gift come back online like a light flickering on in a room she had hoped was finally empty.

* * *

She finds the woman who said *Seren* by the rail, and it is the mother, of course it is — younger than Goldie, a good face gone to ash, and Goldie reads her without consent, the involuntary hands running over her, and there is Carys in her too, not the eyes, the mother didn't get the eyes, but the *jaw*, the exact set of it, and the widow's peak under the disordered hair, and Goldie understands she is looking at one of them, a carrier herself, a woman who has worn a dead girl's bones her whole life and watched her own daughter nearly die of a dead girl's heart and has no idea, none, that the two facts are the same fact.

"She'll be all right," Goldie says, which she does not know, but which is the only sentence you are allowed.

"She's never," the woman says. "She's never done that. They said it was nothing. She faints, see, since she was small, the doctor said it was — they said she'd grow out of it, like I did, like my—" and she stops, and looks at the paramedics lifting her daughter, and goes after her, and the unfinished sentence hangs in the chlorine where Goldie can read it as clearly as if it were written on the pad. *Like my mam. Like my nan. Like it runs in us. Like a thing this family does and a doctor shrugs at.*

A thing this family does.

Goldie stands very still in the noise.

She has, until this exact second, been treating the heart as the proof — the eerie corroborating detail, the thing that made the bloodline undeniable on paper. She has been wrong about what it is, the way she was wrong about a man four streets away, certain of the face and stupid about the meaning. The heart is not the proof.

The heart is a child who will keep doing this.

The heart is a sixteen-year-old with an ear and a cowlick. The heart is a nineteen-year-old who goes a funny colour at the deep end and drinks more water and is fine, *look at him, right as rain, they bounce at that age.* The heart is whatever cousin keels over at weddings while a GP says it's nothing. The heart came down the same chromosome that carried the eyes, out of a girl who died of it alone in a back room of this building twenty-two years ago, and it is sitting right now, undiagnosed and unnamed, inside four children in this town, ticking, like a thing that forgot to be there for a single beat and one day won't start again.

And the only thing in the entire world that gets those children tested — that gets a name put to the silent heart, that gets them the wire and the drug and the small clever box under the collarbone that catches it — is the truth about where they came from. The truth she has been carrying like a stranger's goggle, half a thing, on a shelf, declining to claim.

She has spent twelve years believing that the merciful thing, the only survivable thing, is not to look. She built a whole life on it. She came to the quietest counter in Wales to do it professionally.

She turns a face into a claim.

* * *

The crew gathers in the lull after the ambulance, in the wrecked carnival of the function room — Dil with the urn, white as her perm; Sai still holding the phone he forgot to put down; Pam shaking; Glenys serene; Maureen counting the deck like a sergeant doing a head-check after a contact. And Goldie, who has spent every chapter of her life avoiding the moment where she stands in a room and tells people what she knows, stands in the room and tells them.

"Carys Pryce didn't run away."

She says it quietly, and the quiet is what makes them turn.

"Twenty-two years ago a sixteen-year-old girl didn't get on a bus to Carmarthen. She had a baby in this building — upstairs, in the rooms the Trust used, the quiet work Eira won't talk about — and nine days later her heart stopped, the same way that child's heart just stopped on the tiles, and nobody ever wrote it down. They buried her up by the chapel where the ground has no stones, and they let the whole town grieve a runaway, because a runaway is easier to bury than the truth." She makes herself keep going, against the wound, against everything, with Sai's eyes on her and Dil's hand gone to her mouth. "I know how that sounds. I know exactly how it sounds, because the last time I was this sure of something I nearly put an innocent man away, and I have spent twelve years not being sure of anything since, on purpose. So you can tell me I'm a madwoman off the lost-property desk seeing dead girls in the swimming."

"You're not," says Glenys, who heard.

"I'm not." Goldie looks at the wet floor where the child was. "Her eyes are alive. That's the whole of it. I have stood at that counter and watched Carys Pryce's eyes walk past me on living children born years after they put her in the ground — and the dead do not hand their eyes down the years to the living unless the living came up out of them. Those children are her blood. That girl tonight is her blood. And the heart that killed her is in them, and it is going to keep doing what it just did, until somebody says her name out loud and gets a doctor to listen."

Nobody speaks. The urn hisses. Bunting moves in a draught.

"Well," says Maureen, eventually, into the silence, with the flat finality of a woman closing a bingo book. "Then we'd best find out whose children they are, hadn't we."

And it is Dil — Dil, who has known this town the soft human way for forty-one years, wrong on every date and right on every grief, who has not said a word — who lowers her hand from her mouth and says, in a voice Goldie has never heard her use, "It's the Beynon side."

* * *

Goldie sees Gareth at the doors.

She has been so deep in the count and the claim that she has not clocked him until now, and that itself is the tell, because she clocks everyone, she cannot not, and the one face in this town her gift has never been able to read past has stood at the edge of a child's near-death for ten full minutes and given her *nothing.*

He is being magnificent. That is the only word. He is moving through the wreck of the gala the way he moves through everything — the beloved man, the safe pair of hands, seventy-one years old and entirely in command — gathering the frightened, steering the marshals, getting the urn moved and the children out of the water and a blanket over the mother and his arm, his kind enormous arm, around the shoulders of Seren Hollis's gran, who has come from somewhere at a run. He is saying the right thing to everyone. He is the right thing to everyone.

And Goldie watches him do it, and for the first time she sees what he is doing.

He is not comforting. He is *containing.* He is moving people away from the deep end and away from each other and away, she sees now, with a small cold drop of the floor, from *her* — putting his big body and his big voice between the crew and the rest of the room, herding the warmth into manageable pools, lowering the temperature, and all of it so smooth, so practised, so loving, that not one of the six hundred people in this building would ever call it anything but a good man being good in a crisis.

She has seen this before. She has done a version of it herself, at a counter, for twelve years. It is the management of a thing you cannot afford to have looked at directly. It is keeping.

He glances up, once, across the wrecked room, over the heads, and finds her looking — Goldie at the lost-property desk, Goldie who reads faces, Goldie who has just said *Carys Pryce didn't run away* loud enough for a town to hear — and for the length of a single beat, the face that has never given her anything gives her one thing.

It arranges itself.

She watches a seventy-one-year-old man, in the half-second before he decides what his face is going to do, *choose* what his face is going to do — the way you'd watch a hand reach for a card it had been holding all along — and settle, smooth and warm and grieving and helpful, into the good man, the safe pair of hands, the pillar of the town.

He keeps a face.

He does it the way she does it. He does it the way she has done it for twelve years, in the dry middle register where nothing can get a grip, the small administrative murder of an inconvenient fact, turned face-down so the cartoon fish grins at the cardboard and not at you.

And then he smiles at her, kindly, across the ruin, and mouths, *all right?* — and turns away to fold a frightened woman into his coat, and is, once more, to everyone watching, only the best man in Aberglas doing what the best man in Aberglas does.

Goldie does not smile back.

She picks up nothing, because there is nothing on the counter to pick up, and her hands have to do something anyway, so she presses them flat to the cold edge of the trestle the way she pressed them to the counter on her first morning, and waits to see whether the floor is a floor. Past Gareth's shoulder, through the swing doors, the ambulance light is still going, swinging its blue arm across the wet front of the Pelican, across the boarded chapel and the higher ground behind it where the developer's little orange flags are ticking in the dark, marking out, for Monday, the exact corner of the world that nobody has been allowed to ask a single question of in twenty-two years.

She watches the blue light cross the unmarked ground.

Monday, somebody is going to ask.

---

> **FACE 0023** — female, **Seren Hollis, 14**. The deep end, the gala, 19:50. Returned to her body on the tile at 19:54 — four minutes, the colour of the deep end, then a breath, then sick, which is the prettiest thing I've seen in years.
> *The eyes — set, cant, lid — exact. And the heart. The same heart. The one that stopped Carys nine days after, alone, in the rooms upstairs.*
> She nearly went tonight. If I'm right — and I have been wrong before, and a man paid for it — then saying it out loud is the only thing that keeps her breathing.
> cf. **0001** (Rhodri, eyes + the grey turn), **0016** (Gethin, the faints), **0019** (the peak, always young), **0023**. Same eyes. Four children. Children of a girl who's been in the ground since before they were born. **STATUS: PLACED.**
> Every face in this book placed now, bar one.
> *And it isn't in this book.* *(Leave the line.)*

---

# CHAPTER 21 — The Morning After

A hospital corridor at six in the morning is the most honest room in the world, and Goldie has never been able to bear it.

It does not let you keep a face. Everyone in it has put theirs down — the husbands gone grey under the strip lights, the woman asleep sitting up with her coat for a blanket, the porter who has stopped pretending the trolley is going anywhere quickly — and Goldie sits on a moulded plastic chair the colour of a swimming-pool armband and reads them all, helplessly, the whole exhausted parade, because that is what she does, she cannot not, she files the night shift of Bronglais General the way she filed the lost-property shelf, and there is no joy in any of it, only the involuntary accuracy of a woman who would give a great deal, this morning, to be able to look at a tired stranger and see nothing but a stranger.

Through the doors of the children's ward, somewhere she is not allowed, a fourteen-year-old is alive.

That is the entire content of the morning, and it is enough.

Seren Hollis came back at 19:54 last night on a wet tile with the colour of the deep end in her lips, and at half past three she was sitting up taking small offended sips of squash and asking whether the gala had been cancelled, which is, Goldie has decided, the second most beautiful thing she has heard in twelve years, the first being the sound the child made being sick. They have her on a monitor. They have her on a ward. They have used, the registrar said, carefully, to the family and to anyone in earshot who looked like family, which in the small hours of a Welsh hospital is everyone, the words *unexplained cardiac event.*

*Unexplained.*

Goldie sits with the word the way she sat with the coffee on her first morning. You don't taste it. You decide it has happened to you.

Because she knows exactly what it is, and she is the only person in this building who does, and the word *unexplained* is not a mystery. It is a hole shaped precisely like the truth nobody has been allowed to say for twenty-two years.

* * *

"They asked me was there any history," says the woman, "and I said no."

Goldie did not see her come. That is the tell, again — she clocks everyone, she cannot not, and Marged Hollis has crossed forty feet of corridor and lowered herself onto the next plastic chair and Goldie was so far inside the word *unexplained* that she missed it. She turns and there is the face she read off in the chlorine last night while a child died and came back: a good face gone to ash, younger than Goldie, the jaw exact — Carys's jaw, the set of it, twenty-two years and two generations and a dead girl in unmarked ground all sitting in the line of this woman's chin — and the widow's peak under hair that has not been touched since yesterday morning, when there was no reason on God's earth to do it.

The eyes are not Carys's. Marged didn't get the eyes. She got the bones and the heart and none of the warning.

"No history," Marged says again. She is not crying. She is past it, in that flat country past it where you say true things in an ordinary voice. "I sat there and a lovely young doctor with a clipboard asked me, very gentle, was there anything ran in the family, heart things, faints, anyone gone young — and I said no. And then I'm sat here thinking." She turns her hands over in her lap. "My mam fainted. Did I tell them that? I didn't tell them that. My mam fainted at her own wedding, they've got a photo, everyone laughed, *Auntie Glad's gone over again,* she was famous for it. And her mam before her, by all accounts, though nobody — you don't, do you. You don't write it down. It's just a thing the women do. Going over."

"Going over," Goldie says.

"Like a faulty light." Marged almost smiles. "Flicker and back. And I sat there and told a doctor *no history,* because there isn't one, is there, not a real one, not on paper, it's only — it's stories. It's *Auntie Glad went over at the wedding.* That's not a history. That's a *laugh.*"

And Goldie, who has spent her whole career turning blurred silhouettes into names and has paid for it, and who came to the quietest counter in Wales precisely so she would never again have to make a true thing out of a feeling, sits very still on a plastic chair and understands the exact shape of the thing she is now holding.

There *is* a history. It is the best-documented history in the town. It is walking around in living children — the eyes, the jaw, the ear, the cowlick, the same heart that flickered and did not come back in a girl in a back room in 2003 — and the only reason a doctor with a clipboard wrote *unexplained* is that the family that carries it has been told, for twenty-two years, by good kind people who loved them, that they descend from nobody worth recording.

The family doesn't know its own history.

So the heart goes *unexplained.* So Seren gets sent home, in a week, with a shrug and a leaflet and a *she'll grow out of it like the rest of us,* because there is nothing on paper, because the one piece of paper that would put a name to the silent thing in her chest is a death certificate that was never written, for a girl this town buried twice — once in the ground and once in a lie.

And Rhodri is nineteen and goes a funny colour at the deep end. And Gethin is sixteen and his cousin keels over and the doctor says it's nothing. And there are children of this woman's children, somewhere, in cots, in nappies, not yet old enough to faint, who carry it and don't know, the way nobody knew, the way Carys didn't know, nine days after a birth, alone, when her heart did exactly what Seren's just did and there was no Huw, no green box, no Glenys who heard.

"They'll find it," Goldie says. "Whatever it is. They'll keep her in, they'll do the tests."

It is the only sentence you are allowed. It is also, this morning, not quite true, and Goldie hears herself say it and hears the not-quite in it, and Marged — who is exhausted, and not stupid, and the granddaughter of a girl who saw too much — looks at her sideways.

"You were there," Marged says. "Last night. You did the — you got down on the floor with her."

"I used to do a job," Goldie says, "where you learned where to put your hands."

"Filing," says Marged, "they said," and Goldie laughs before she can stop it, one bark of it in the honest corridor, because of course they said filing, the town has decided she does filing, she has built a whole anonymity out of doing filing, and a tired woman whose child nearly died has just handed it back to her like a coat at a cloakroom.

"Mostly filing," Goldie says.
