Marged stands. She has a daughter to sit beside. At the doors of the ward she stops, and turns, and says the thing that will keep Goldie awake for the rest of the week — not as an accusation, there's no accusation in her, only that flat true country past crying:

"It's a funny thing, the women going over. You'd think somebody would've asked, all these years. Where it comes from." She pushes the door. "Nobody ever asks where anything comes from round here. We're all just *related,* and that's the end of it."

The door swings shut on the most honest room in the world, and Goldie sits with two facts that have spent the whole book leaning toward each other and have, in a six a.m. corridor, finally touched.

The truth is unbearable.

The truth is the only thing that saves the child.

* * *

The Pelican, the morning after a near-drowning that was not a drowning, smells exactly as it always does — chlorine and chips and the particular sorrow of a building that used to be loved — and is, against all decency, open.

"Course we're open," says Dil, who is behind the reception desk at eight where she has been behind the reception desk since 1983, white as her perm under last night, fierce with it. "What were we going to do, shut? It's a Saturday. There's the over-fifties at nine and the lengths swimmers at half-seven, they've come, look, they always come, you couldn't shut this place with the building on fire, we'd have someone wanting a refund on the smoke." She bangs the urn. "Sit down before you fall down, you look like the wall."

Goldie sits down. The lost-property shelf is exactly where she left it — the dentures in the margarine tub, the men's wedding ring worn to a knife-edge, the one-lens goggle that nobody will ever come back for because nobody comes back for half — and the ordinariness of it is so total, so blank and continuing and unbothered, that for a moment she could put her head down on the counter and not lift it.

Then the Aqua Belles arrive for the nine o'clock, and the morning after is over, because the Aqua Belles have decided to have a meeting.

They come in not in their swimming things but in their *outdoor* things — a tactical escalation Goldie clocks at once — Maureen in a mac like a field marshal, Pam with a clipboard she has produced from somewhere and is clearly thrilled about, Glenys with a tin, and Eira, ninety-five, brought in on the arm of Maureen's grandson and installed in the good chair by the radiator like a relic carried in procession, which, Goldie is beginning to understand, is more or less what she is.

"We're not swimming," Maureen announces, to the building. "We're *organising.*"

"Right," says Goldie.

"That child," says Pam, conspiratorially, leaning in over the counter with the clipboard, "nearly *died,* and the council still wants to shut the only place in twenty mile with a machine that brought her back. Now." She taps the clipboard. "I've made a list."

Pam has, indeed, made a list. Pam has spent a sleepless night that should have been spent grieving making a list, because Pam's particular conspiracy-minded genius — for once, this morning, entirely pointed the right way — has correctly identified that there is a war on and that wars are won by people who own clipboards. The list has columns. The list is titled, in Pam's careful capitals, *THINGS.*

"Casseroles," reads Maureen, taking over the way Maureen takes over, "to the Hollis house, because that family will not cook this week, that family will not *think,* you don't, when it's your child. Glenys is doing a lasagne the size of a coffin. No offence."

"None taken," says Goldie, who has had worse.

"Rotas," Maureen continues. "Someone with the gran. Someone at the hospital so Marged isn't on her own in that corridor at three in the morning like a — well." She does not finish it. She does not need to; she has clearly seen the corridor too. "Carol-Anne's got the WI phone tree going, half the bay will know by dinner, which is a curse and a use, and we'll *use* it. Lengths swimmers are doing a sponsored thing. Huw says he'll teach the little ones water safety free for a month, which is Huw all over, the soft article, he was up all night and he's down there now with the kickboards like nothing happened, you go and look."

Goldie goes, in her head, and looks, and there is Huw — fifty-eight, vast, gentle, who hit the water last night like the building falling in and pulled a dead child out of it and started her heart with his own hands — down on the poolside at eight in the morning showing a five-year-old how to float, *now then, now then, on your back, I've got you, I've always got you,* because some people do not have a face to keep, they only have the one face, and it is the kind one, all the way down.

She thinks: *this is the thing I came here to avoid.*

She has spent twelve years arranging her life so that no one would ever need her, so that she could sit at the quietest counter in Wales and recognise the whole town and be recognised by none of it, *that is the arrangement, that is the whole point of the arrangement* — and the arrangement is, this morning, in pieces on the floor next to a clipboard titled *THINGS,* because a town she did not want has decided, without asking her, to fold itself around a wound she found, and to put her, the woman who does filing, in the middle of it.

"And you," says Maureen, rounding on her with the full terror of a woman who has run bingo since Thatcher, "are doing the part nobody else can do."

"Am I," says Goldie.

"You're the one knows whose children they are," says Maureen, and the function room — which is a reception desk with delusions, but never mind — goes quiet, the way it went quiet last night when Goldie said *Carys Pryce didn't run away* in a voice too small to argue with. "You said it. In front of all of us. You said her eyes are walking around on living children and the thing that killed her is in them. Well." Maureen folds her arms. The little laminated dog on Dil's lanyard swings in the draught. "Then you'd best be the one who makes a doctor believe it. Because a lasagne won't fix that girl's heart. The *truth* will. And you're the only one of us who can carry it without dropping it."

It is, Goldie thinks, the single most frightening sentence anyone has said to her since a barrister in 2011 asked her, in a voice exactly this certain, whether she was *quite sure.*

She was quite sure. She was wrong.

And the difference — the only difference, the one she has spent twelve years too frightened to test — is that this time the thing she is sure of is not a stranger's face on a burning bus. It is the same eyes in two decades, the same heart that stopped twice, a fact so solid she could build a house on it. She is being asked, again, to turn a face into a claim. The last time she did it a man nearly went to prison. This time, if she doesn't, a child might not grow up.

"All right," says Goldie.

* * *

Gareth Hollis arrives at half past ten, when the casserole logistics are at their height, and he is, as ever, magnificent.

He comes in the way he comes in everywhere — the beloved man, the safe pair of hands, seventy-one years old and entirely in command — and the morning reorganises itself around him without his having to ask. Pam stops talking. Dil straightens. The grandson fetches him the other good chair before he's reached for it. He has been to the hospital already, of course he has, he has been to the hospital and the chapel and probably the council before most people had their toast, and he stands in the wreck of the Aqua Belles' war room with his coat still on and his big kind face arranged into exactly the right amount of grief, and says, "How is she. How is everyone. What can I take off you."

It is the perfect sentence. *What can I take off you.* It is the sentence of a good man, and it is also, Goldie now knows — having watched him last night in the half-second before he decided what his face would do — the sentence of a man who has spent twenty-two years taking things off people. Taking the truth off a town. Taking a daughter off a mother and folding the grief over so the cartoon fish grins at the cardboard and not at you.

"The family," he says, to the room, gentle, reasonable, "are going to be inundated. Casseroles are kind — Glenys, that's kind — but they'll be drowning in kindness by Tuesday and what they'll need is someone to *manage* it. The hospital, the press if it comes to it — a near-thing at a public gala, people will talk — the school for the other children, the gran's medications, the lot. Marged can't be expected to field all that. She's barely standing." He turns, warmly, to Goldie, the way you turn to the most capable person in the room because you have correctly identified her as the most dangerous one. "I'll handle the family liaison. I've known that family thirty years. I'm the natural — well. I'm the obvious one to be the go-between. Keep the chaos off their door."

And there it is.

*I'll handle the family liaison.* Said with total warmth, in front of witnesses, by the most loved man in Aberglas, and not one of the people in this room — not Pam, not Dil, not Maureen, who fears nothing — hears it as anything but a good man being good in a crisis.

Goldie hears it for what it is. She hears the management of a thing that cannot afford to be looked at directly. She hears a man positioning himself, smoothly, lovingly, between the truth and the only people it has to reach — between a doctor with a clipboard and the one fact that would make him write a different word than *unexplained.* He is not offering to help the family. He is offering to be the wall. To stand his big body and his big voice in the doorway of the Hollis house the way he has stood it in the doorway of this town for twenty-two years, herding the warmth into manageable pools, lowering the temperature, keeping.

"That's good of you, Gareth," she says. "But there's no need."

The room does not notice anything. Why would it. A woman who does filing has politely declined a kind man's kind offer; people do it all day. Dil is already saying something about who's got a big enough oven for Glenys's lasagne.

But Goldie is watching the one face in this town that has never given her anything, and for the length of a single beat — the same single beat she watched it take last night across the wreck of the gala — it gives her something.

It registers her.

She watches a seventy-one-year-old man hear *there's no need* from the lost-property desk and understand, in real time, that the lost-property desk is not declining a casserole rota. She watches him understand that she is keeping the liaison for herself. She watches him understand *why.* It is the smallest thing — a stillness behind the eyes, a half-second where the warmth holds level by main force the way a man holds a heavy thing level when he doesn't want you to see how heavy — and then it is gone, folded down, face turned over, and he is smiling at her, kindly, sorrowfully, the safe pair of hands, the pillar of the town.

"Of course," he says. "You know the family better than I gave you credit for." A beat. "You've a gift for knowing people, I hear. From the old job."

"Filing," says Goldie.

"Filing," Gareth agrees, gently, and holds her eyes one half-second longer than a man being kind needs to, and Goldie holds his back, and in the warm ruined function room full of casseroles and clipboards and a town learning the difference between recognising a person and knowing one, the two people in Aberglas who have each spent decades refusing to look at a single face look, for one beat, directly at each other, and each of them sees, with total clarity, exactly what the other one is keeping.

Then Glenys says, "Who's got the big oven, then," having misheard the entire exchange as being about ovens, and the spell breaks, and Gareth Hollis turns away to be magnificent at somebody else, and Goldie picks up the one-lens goggle off the shelf because her hands have to do something, and turns it over, half a thing pretending to be a whole one, an eyeglass for a man with one eye.

She is going to have to be sure.

She is going to have to be more sure than she has let herself be about anything since the morning a barrister asked her if she was, and she was, and she was wrong — sure enough to put a doctor's pen to paper, sure enough to walk into a happy woman's kitchen and take her whole life off her like a coat, sure enough to stand between a beloved man and the thing he has killed once already to keep buried.

And she cannot be sure of where Carys went on the strength of a face.

She has the eyes. She has the heart. She has the paper, the sealed adoption, the brow on a thousand heads, the whole shape of it assembled out of fragments the way she assembles a stranger from an ear. What she does not have — the one record nobody filed, because filing it was the crime — is the girl herself. The last unread thing. The higher ground behind the boarded chapel where the grass has changed colour and the developer's orange flags are ticking in the wind, marking out, for Monday, the exact corner of the world that no one has been allowed to ask a single question of in twenty-two years.

She sets the goggle down. Through reception's window, past the bunting nobody has taken down, the Saturday morning goes on being ordinary — Huw and the kickboards, the lengths swimmers, the town arriving the way it always arrives, because you couldn't shut this place with the building on fire.

Monday somebody is going to ask the ground.

Goldie has decided it is going to be her.

---

# CHAPTER 22 — Ffion

The girl has been standing at the lost-property hatch for four full minutes, and in four full minutes she has not once asked about lost property, and Goldie, who can tell you the wear pattern on a stranger's left shoe across a turnstile, has known from the first second exactly who she is.

It is the jaw that arrives first. It comes round the corner of the corridor a half-beat before the rest of the face, the way a certain jaw will — square at the angle, slightly under-hung, a clean shelf of bone you could set a spirit level on — and Goldie has seen that jaw before, twice. Once on a 2003 swim-gala photograph, third row, fourth in, on a girl who has been dead for twenty-two years. And once last night, on a woman white as the urn, saying *Seren* to the chlorine. The jaw is the mother's and the dead girl's and now it is the daughter's, and over it, under the disordered fringe that is doing its level best to hide the giveaway, sits the widow's peak — a sharp little V dead-centre of the brow, the point of it exact, the lift to either side exact, born and not made, no comb on earth gives you that one, you come up out of the ground with it. Seventeen. Carys's bones on a seventeen-year-old, worn the way the young wear everything, like it's nothing, like it's theirs.

She is not the mother. That is the thing Goldie clocks last and likes best: this is a wholly separate animal from the ashen woman at the rail. The mother wore the jaw the way you wear a thing that happened to you. This one wears it like a held breath. She is watching Goldie the way Goldie watches everyone — top of the head down, the involuntary inventory, the small frown of the filing — and that, more than the bones, is what makes the floor do its slow tilt under the counter.

"You're the one who saw," the girl says.

Not *good morning*. Not *has anyone handed in a goggle*. Straight to it, flat, in the register of someone who has decided talking-around-things is a tax she will not pay.

"I'm the one at the desk," says Goldie.

"You did the CPR. With Huw. You told him to push hard enough to break something." The girl puts a folder on the counter. An actual folder, cardboard, a school one, the sort that comes with a timetable printed on it, and on the front in biro, gone over twice so it's grooved into the card, somebody has written *FAMILY* and then, underneath, in smaller letters, the kind you write when you don't want anyone to read it: *WHO FAINTS.* "That's my sister. Seren. She's awake. They've got her on a monitor in Bronglais and they keep saying *unexplained.*" The frown deepens, the recogniser's frown, the one that has decided a word is wrong and is not going to let it go. "Three times now a doctor's said *unexplained* about my family and I'm starting to think it's a thing they say when nobody's done the work."

"And you've done the work."

"I've started it." She opens the folder. "You'll want to sit down, probably."

* * *

She has done the work.

Goldie comes round the counter and stands beside her at the hatch — there is one stool and Goldie gives it to the girl, who takes it without a thank-you, the way you take a thing that is obviously yours — and looks at what a seventeen-year-old assembles when nobody will tell her the truth and she is wired, by some grace or curse, to go and find it.

It is a family tree. Hand-drawn, ruled with a ruler, names in boxes, lines between them, and where she didn't know a name she has put a box with a question mark in it and drawn the lines to it anyway, because the gap is the point. Down one side, in red biro, a separate column with its own heading — *FAINTS / WEAK TURNS / "GREW OUT OF IT"* — and a list. Her mam, *Marged*, box at the top of the living end of the tree: *fainted at school, age 13, "low blood pressure," GP said nothing.* A cousin: *Lowri P — keels at weddings.* A name Goldie knows: *Gethin — twice in PE, sent home.* Rhodri, with a star next to him. And at the bottom, ringed twice, last night's date and one word. *Seren.*

"It's only on one side," the girl says. She taps the boxes down the left. "It's only ever Mum's side. Dad's lot are all fine, they live to ninety and complain about it. It's Mum's. And the thing is—" she stops, and Goldie watches her do the thing, watches her weigh whether the next sentence makes her sound mad, and then say it anyway, because she has clearly decided that sounding mad is also a tax she will not pay "—the thing is, you can't follow Mum's side up. It just stops. Nan's adopted. Took in as a baby, local, never knew her people, the Beynons had her and that was that and you don't ask. So the faints run up the family and then they hit a wall, because the family's got no top to it. There's a box up there—" she points to the highest box on the left, the one with the question mark "—and I don't even know if it's one person or a hundred. Nobody does. It got sealed."

Goldie looks at the box with the question mark.

She knows what is in the box. She has known for two chapters of her life. She has the gala photo and the Trust ledger and the sealed confinement with the mother's name lifted clean out and the orthodontist slips and the higher ground behind the chapel with the orange flags ticking, and she could put a name in that box right now, in biro, gone over twice — *CARYS PRYCE, 16, d.2003, this building* — and the girl would have her wall knocked through and her family delivered to her in a single sentence, and Goldie has spent twelve years learning precisely what a single sentence delivered to the wrong person at the wrong moment does to a life. She does not put a name in the box.

"What's your name," she says instead.

"Ffion." A beat. "Hollis." The surname comes out slightly armoured, the way a name does when you've spent a school career being one of three Hollises and two of them are your cousins and the teacher never gets it right. "Ffion Hollis. Mum's a Beynon-that-was, married a Hollis, so I'm a Hollis, so's Seren, but the *faints* are Beynon, the faints are Mum's, I keep telling them and nobody — " She stops. Starts again, lower. "Seren's fourteen. She was the colour of a corpse on a wet floor last night and a doctor wrote *unexplained* on a chart and sent her home to do it again. I'm not having it. So I'm doing the work. And you're the one who saw, so." She turns the folder a half-inch toward Goldie, an offering and a challenge in the same small shove. "What do you know that I don't."

Here it is. The thing the whole chapter of her has been walking toward without admitting it. A child has put a folder on the counter that says *WHO FAINTS* and asked the one person in Wales who could fill it in.

And Goldie, who solved this in a graveyard with the wind off the bay, who said *Carys Pryce didn't run away* out loud to a roomful of stunned cardigans not eighteen hours ago, who has a name for the box and a body for the ground — Goldie finds she cannot just *hand it over*, not the way you'd hand back a goggle, because the difference between her and this girl is exactly the difference the whole book is about, and she only sees it now, looking at a seventeen-year-old who came to *find* her people instead of waiting to be told.

"I know one thing you don't," Goldie says, slowly, choosing it. "I know your nan's box has a person in it. Not a hundred. One."

Ffion goes very still. The recogniser's stillness; Goldie knows it from the inside.

"How do you know."

"Because I've seen her face," says Goldie. "On you. On Seren. On a boy at the deep end who lost the same goggle three times. I've seen her face walk past this counter for a month wearing other people's lives, and a face doesn't do that unless it came down through bodies. Your wall's got one person on the far side of it." She taps, very gently, the highest box. The question mark. "And she fainted too. That's the bit you can't get to. The faints go all the way up — they go up *past* the wall — and the reason nobody's caught it is that the person at the top of your tree was filed under a different word than *family.*"

* * *

Glenys finds them there twenty minutes later, which is a mercy, because the air in the corridor had got to the point where it needed a window opened in it, and Glenys is a window in human form.

"Now then," says Glenys, who has appointed herself, since last night, the official bringer-of-tea to anyone Goldie is standing near, on the apparent theory that the gala has made them all veterans of something. She has two of Carwyn's worst, in the good cups. "I've brought you a coffee each, don't thank me, you can't, it's a war crime. Are you the Hollis girl? Seren's sister?" She does not wait. Glenys has misheard every important sentence since the Coronation and has consequently developed a magnificent disregard for whether she's been answered. "Terrible thing. *Terrible.* My sister's Bryn went the same way, you know, in the chapel, mid-hymn, down he went between *Cwm Rhondda* verse two and verse three, and they said it was the heat, and it was *March.*" She sets the cups down with the care of a woman delivering something precious and toxic. "Runs in some, that. The going-down. You'd be amazed who."

And she potters off, deaf as a wall, having said, by pure happy accident, the truest sentence anyone has said in the building all week.

Ffion watches her go. Then she looks at Goldie, and for the first time the armour slips a centimetre and something underneath it shows — not fear exactly, something more useful, the cold clean fury of a clever person who has just realised the grown-ups have been guessing.

"*Runs in some,*" Ffion says. "It's not low blood pressure. It's not the heat. It's not *unexplained.* It's a thing, isn't it. A real thing. With a name. That you can test for." She has gone pale around the jaw — Carys's jaw, going pale the way Carys's must have gone pale, the last time, in a back room, alone, with nobody who knew the word. "If it's got a name they can catch it. Before it does Seren again. Before it does me." She says the last bit absolutely flat, *before it does me*, a seventeen-year-old stating the actuarial fact of her own heart, and that flatness is the most frightening thing Goldie has heard since the deep end. "That's why it matters whose box it is. It's not — I don't care about *whose I am* for, like, a *story.* I care because the doctor needs the top of the tree to know what to look for."

Goldie looks at her.

She has spent the whole of this case explaining the medical truth to herself as the *clinching detail* — the corroboration, the thing that turned a face into proof. She has been getting it backwards, the way she got the man four streets away backwards: certain of the structure, slow on the meaning. The truth about Carys isn't the answer to a twenty-two-year-old riddle. The truth about Carys is a wire and a drug and a clever little box under a collarbone that catches the heart when it forgets itself. It is the only thing that knocks the wall through in time. A girl half her age has had to come to a lost-property desk to explain it to her, in a school folder, in red biro, under a heading she wrote so nobody else would read it.

"It's got a name," Goldie says. "I'm not a doctor and I'm not going to say it wrong, because the last time I was sure of something I wasn't a doctor about, a man nearly went to prison. But it's a real thing, in the heart, that runs down a family, that drops the young, that a GP shrugs at because it hides — and yes. They can test for it. They can catch it. That's the whole reason I'm not just minding my own business and matching flip-flops, which is, believe me, what I came here to do."

"So why aren't you telling them. The family. My mum." Sharp again, but not cruel; a person doing the work and finding a gap in someone else's. "If you know whose box it is, and the box is what saves Seren — why are you standing behind a *desk*?"

And there it is, the question, in the mouth of the only person young enough and frightened enough to just *ask* it.

"Because if I get the name in the box wrong," Goldie says, "I detonate a happy woman's whole life for nothing. And if I get it right—" she stops, and makes herself finish, because the girl has earned a finished sentence "—if I get it right, I detonate it for *something*, and either way it's not mine to throw across a kitchen like a brick. Your mum's been a happy person for thirty-eight years not knowing the top of her tree. I'm not going to be the stranger who knocks on her door and changes that with a sentence. *You* might be the person who can. That's different. That's family doing it gentle, with a name in the box and a doctor in the room, instead of a busybody off the lido doing it loud."

Ffion takes that in. Turns it over. Goldie can see her do it, the weighing, the same weighing Goldie does, the recogniser's habit of holding a thing up to the light from three sides before she trusts it.

"You want to use me," Ffion says. Not an accusation. A clarification.

"I want to *not* be the brick," says Goldie. "There's a difference. I've spent twelve years being careful about it from the wrong direction."

* * *

They make a plan at the lost-property hatch, two recognisers, fifty-one and seventeen, over two cups of coffee that neither of them drinks, and it is — Goldie notes, somewhere under the dread — one of the more companionable hours she has spent in twelve years, which tells you something appalling about how she has spent the last twelve years.

Ffion will keep building her tree, and Goldie will quietly give her the rungs she has — not Carys's name, not yet, not until there's a body and a date and no possibility of being wrong, but the *shape*: that the wall has one person behind it, that the faints predate the wall, that the family needs the cardiologist before it needs the story. Ffion is already three steps ahead. Ffion has, it turns out, been quietly recording who in the family does the head-tilt and who has the gappy smile and who keels at weddings, in the back of a maths book, for *months*, because — she says, with a shrug that breaks Goldie's heart precisely because the girl doesn't know it's heartbreaking — "I notice faces. I always have. I thought everyone did until I was about eleven and said the wrong thing to a supply teacher about where I'd seen her before."

Goldie does not say *me too.* She wants to, and the wanting is so unfamiliar it feels like indigestion, and she swallows it the way she swallows everything, and files it.

It's Ffion who says the name that makes the morning turn.

"The other one who knows things," she says, gathering the folder, "is Gareth."

Goldie goes still.

"Gareth Hollis. He's not — he's not really family family, but he's *family.* You know. He did Mum's wedding speech because her dad — Old Beynon, the one with the goggle — he'd gone by then, so Gareth stepped up, walked her down, said the thing about her people not being blood but being *chosen*, everyone cried." She says it warmly, the way you say a thing you've never once examined. "He's been around forever. He christened Seren — godfather, near enough, he's everyone's near-enough-godfather. He paid for Rhodri's lifeguard course, did you know that? Quiet, like. He does that. When Nan was dying he was there every day. He knew her people, see, because of the Trust — he was the one sorted her adoption, way back, so he's the only one who's ever known the top of the tree, and he won't talk about it, says it's *sealed*, says some doors are kind shut." A small frown. "I always thought that was him being decent. Protecting Nan's privacy and that. Mum says he's the best man in Aberglas."

Goldie stands very still in the corridor, with the gift coming online whether she wants it or not, and watches the whole shape of it tip from one thing into another, the way a face does when the light moves.

She had thought of Gareth Hollis as the Keeper. The man who hid a death to protect an institution. A cover-up with a kind face on it. And it is that — but it is not *only* that, and the not-only is what the girl has just handed her without knowing she held it.

He sorted the adoption. He walked the foundling's daughter down the aisle. He paid for the boy's course. He christened the youngest. He sat with the dying woman every day. He has spent twenty-two years not protecting a *thing* — not the Trust, not the Pelican, not his own skin first of all — but *these people*. This tangled Beynon-Hollis branch, his own by no blood at all and every other kind of fate, the children of the secret he is keeping. He killed a dying nun to stop her telling them, in the worst possible way, that they descend from the town's most famous ghost and carry her broken heart. He did it for *love.* The most deformed, the most administrative, the most catastrophic love, the kind that decides on someone else's behalf which doors are kind shut — but love, and Goldie of all people knows exactly what a person will do, and not do, and not *look* at, in the name of sparing someone a face they can't survive.

She knows, because she has done a smaller, quieter version of it, at a counter, for twelve years, and called it mercy.

"He offered," Goldie hears herself say, "to handle the family liaison. Last night. With your mum."

"Course he did." Ffion has the folder under her arm, already half-turned to the door, a girl with somewhere to be, and she says it as the most natural thing in the world, the kindest fact she knows. "He'd want to be the one to tell us. He always wants to be the one." She pauses at the swing doors, the morning light coming grey-gold off the wet front behind her, lighting up Carys's jaw, Carys's peak, on a living seventeen-year-old who is going to go on living, Goldie has just privately decided, whatever it costs. "He's the only one who knows what's in the box. If anyone's going to fill it in, it'd be him, gentle, the way he does everything."

She doesn't see Goldie's face change.

But Goldie sees the whole of it now, laid out clean as a tree in red biro: the kindest man in Aberglas is the one person on earth who knows exactly which face is in the box at the top of Ffion's family tree — and the one person on earth with twenty-two years of reasons to make absolutely certain nobody ever opens it.

And the box gets opened Monday, by a man with a digger, behind the chapel, in the higher ground.

"Ffion," says Goldie.

The girl turns.

"Before you tell your mum anything — even the shape of it, even the bit about the cardiologist — you tell me first. Not Gareth. Me." She keeps her voice exactly where she keeps it, in the dry middle register where nothing can get a grip, and she is not at all sure it works, because the girl is a recogniser, and recognisers read the thing under the voice. "There's an order this has to go in, or it goes wrong. Promise me. Me first."

Ffion looks at her for a long moment, jaw set, the peak catching the light, reading her the way Goldie reads the town.

"You don't trust Gareth," she says. Flat. Certain. The clarification again.

Goldie does not answer, which is its own answer, and they both know it, and the girl — clever, frightened, doing the work, seventeen — files it, the way Goldie files everything, instantly and against her will and without yet knowing what it means.

"Me first," Ffion says. "Right."

And she pushes out through the swing doors into the grey-gold morning, the cardboard folder under one arm, *WHO FAINTS* face-out to the whole of Aberglas, off to go and find her people the way Goldie never went to find hers — and Goldie watches the dead girl's jaw go down the front toward the harbour, alive and furious and seventeen, and reaches, out of forty years of habit, for the pen, because her hands have to go somewhere and the ledger is the only place she has ever been able to put a thing she cannot say.

---

> **FACE 0019** — female, **Ffion Hollis, 17**. The hatch, morning after. Returns nothing; brought a folder marked *WHO FAINTS* and a family tree she ruled herself.
> *The jaw — exact, the under-hung shelf of it. The widow's-peak — point, lift, born, no comb does that. Carys's bones, worn like they're nothing.*
> And the other thing, the thing not on the gala photo, the thing I have no business recognising and know on sight anyway: **she does what I do.** Watches the top of the head down. Frowns at a wrong word. Came to *find* her people instead of waiting to be told.
> The granddaughter is a recogniser too. It runs in more than the eyes.
> cf. the box at the top of her tree, sealed, one person behind it — and one man who's known whose box it is for twenty-two years, walking her mother down the aisle, christening her sister, sitting with the dying. Not greed. *Love.* The worst kind. The kind that decides which doors are kind shut.
> **STATUS: PLACED.**
> *(He still wants to be the one to tell them. He always wants to be the one.)*

---

# CHAPTER 23 — The Dig

The diggers come on a Monday, in rain, which is the only weather Aberglas has ever taken seriously.

Goldie watches them arrive from the lost-property hatch the way you watch a thing you have been dreading turn out to be small and orange and ordinary. Two men in hi-vis, a mini-excavator on a trailer, a yellow van with HALLEN GROUND SURVEYS down the side and a phone number with the wrong amount of digits in the rain. They are not, she has to keep reminding herself, the cavalry. They are a pre-purchase site survey, contracted by a developer who wants to know whether the ground will hold a block of nine apartments with a sea view and a name like *The Lido* that will be the only thing left to remember the lido by. They are here to take soil. That they will, in the course of taking soil, take the lid off twenty-two years is a thing none of them knows and one of them is about to.

She has had since the gala to think about how to be standing in the right place when it happens, and she has spent it badly, the way she spends most of her good ideas, in arguments with herself at three in the morning. *Tell the police.* And say what. *I run the lost-property desk and I'm certain there's a body up by the chapel because a teenager has the same eyes as a girl in a 2003 photo* is a sentence that ends, in her experience, with a kind constable and a leaflet. *Wait for the dig.* And let a developer's man with no idea what he's holding be the one to find a girl, alone, again. She has done the arithmetic of it forty ways and it always comes out the same, which is that she has to be there, and she cannot be there as a copper, because she isn't one, so she will have to be there as what she is, which is a nosy woman from the desk with a flask and no jurisdiction whatsoever.

She has brought Ffion.

* * *

"You don't have to come up," Goldie says, at the back gate, in the rain, and means it, and is selfishly glad when the girl ignores her.

Ffion Hollis is seventeen and built like a question she has decided to keep asking. She has Carys's jaw — the exact set of it, the clean line that arrives in a face a fraction before the chin does, *born not made, no salon ever cut a jaw* — and the widow's peak high under a hood pulled tight against the weather, and she has, which the jaw and the peak do not explain, the thing Goldie has only ever met in a mirror: a face that is reading the world while the world thinks it's just standing there. The girl has been doing Goldie's job in secret for two years, on her own, with a school exercise book and a family that won't answer questions, and she found half of it before Goldie found any. *It runs in more than the eyes,* Goldie wrote, after, and did not write the rest, which is that she likes the child, and liking a child is one of the doors she has kept bolted for twelve years and finds, now, has been quietly off its hinges for a while.

"She's my great-grandmother," Ffion says. Not a question. She has worked the tree the way Goldie worked the faces — slower, with paper, the human way — and she has it right, which the prose of this town does not, which the prose of this town keeps getting one generation short. "Carys. She's Mam's gran's mam. So." The jaw does something. "If she's up there, I'm not having a man from a van be the only person who came."

"It might not be today," Goldie says. "It might be a fortnight of soil samples and nothing."

"It's today." Ffion looks up the slope, through the escallonia, to the higher ground where the orange flags are still ticking, soaked now, drooping on their wires. "You know it's today. You've got your face on."

"I have not got a face on."

"You've got the face on you had at the gala. Before." The girl says it flatly, a colleague's observation, and Goldie understands she is being recognised, properly, by someone with the equipment to do it, and that it is the strangest and least comfortable thing that has happened to her in years, and that she does not, for once, reach for a joke to make it stop.

"Now then," says Huw, coming through the gate behind them with a flask of his own and a face like the morning. "Now then, now then."

He has appointed himself, the way Huw appoints himself to everything that involves the building being looked after, which a body in its ground apparently counts as. He is fifty-eight and enormous and he pulled Seren Hollis out of the deep end nine days ago and has not, Goldie thinks, been entirely dry-eyed since, in the way of very large gentle men who have spent thirty years being the one who is calm so that nobody else has to be. He doesn't ask why they're up here in the rain. He has decided not to know the exact shape of it, the way Eira decided, the way the whole town decided for twenty-two years, except that Huw's not-knowing is a kindness held over them like a coat and not a lie held over a grave, and Goldie loves him for the difference.

"You'll catch your death, the pair of you," he says, and then hears it, and looks at the higher ground, and says, quieter, "sorry," to nobody, to the ground, and that is the most anyone has said out loud all week.

* * *

The excavator finds her at twenty to eleven, and it is, mercifully, not the bucket.

Goldie has been dreading the bucket — the obscene yellow violence of it, the indignity, a girl come up in a clawful of clay in front of strangers — and the dread was, like most of her dreads, a story she told herself to keep the real thing at bay. What happens is quieter and worse. The survey is a trial pit, neat, a metre square, the operator going down in careful spits because that is how you survey, looking for made ground and clay and the water table, and at a little under a metre the careful spits stop being soil and start, the man can feel it through the controls before he can see it, being *not* soil — a give, a wrongness, the ground holding something the way ground holds something it was asked to keep — and he climbs down off the machine and goes to the edge of the pit and looks in, and goes a colour Goldie knows, the dusk colour, and says, "Boss," in a voice that has gone right back to being a boy's.

She does not let herself look. That is the discipline of twelve years arriving when it is, for once, of use. There is a thing in the pit and it is not her business to read it; she is not police, she has no license for this, and she has spent the morning rehearsing the one rule she could give herself, which is: *you do not look at her. You let them do it properly. You are not here to recognise her. You are here so she is not found alone.* So she keeps her eyes on Ffion, who has gone very still and very white and very upright, the jaw holding the whole face together by main force, and on Huw, who has taken his cap off, just taken it off and is holding it against his chest in two hands in the rain without being told, because some manners go deeper than knowing what they're for.

But she hears it. The survey men, who deal in drains and footings and have never in their lives expected the ground to do this, lean over the edge and say the small flat true things that frightened people say. *There's — that's not.* And, *is that.* And then, from the older one, the foreman, in the voice of a man whose Monday has just stopped being a Monday: "That's bone. Phil. Phil, get back, that's — don't, leave it, that's a person. Phone it in. That's a person down there."

A pause. The rain.

"There's — hang on." The boy again, lower, against his own will, the way you look at the thing you most want not to see. "It's small. It's not — boss, it's small."

"It's a young one," the foreman says, very gently, to the pit, the way you'd speak to it, and Goldie hears twenty-two years close like a drawer. "God love her. It's a young one."

And that is all. That is the whole of it, the great reveal she has chased through forty faces and a basement and a salon and a dying woman's room: not a skull grinning out of the gothic, not a TV exhumation with arc-lights and tents, but two men from a van with their phones out, standing in the rain over a metre of west-Wales clay, saying *young one* and *God love her* and *phone it in*, because in the end a buried girl is not a mystery. She is a child somebody put in the ground and did not mark, and the marking is what's left to do.

Goldie puts her hand on Ffion's shoulder. The girl is shaking, a fine high tremor, holding it the way she holds everything, and she does not turn round, but she reaches up with the hand that isn't holding her own elbow together and grips Goldie's fingers hard enough to hurt, and the two of them stand there, the recogniser and the recogniser, and look at the ground instead of into the pit, because the ground at least is something you are allowed to look at.

"Marged," Ffion says, after a while, very small. Her mam. Carys's granddaughter. The one nobody has told yet that her family is a grave. "Somebody has to tell Mam before she hears it off Facebook."

"I know," Goldie says.

"It can't be a man from a van."

"It won't be."

* * *

Gareth comes up the slope like the weather breaking.

She doesn't hear him over the rain and the idling engine; she feels the room change, the way she felt it at the gala, the way you feel a man enter a space he has decided to take charge of. When she turns he is already most of the way up the path from the Pelican, no coat, the rain darkening his good jumper, seventy-one years old and moving faster than a man that age should be able to on wet ground, and his face — the face that has never given her a single thing, the one unreadable face in a town she reads like a book — has, at last, given up trying.

It is not the villain's face she has half-expected, the one the cheaper version of this would put up the hill. There is no snarl in it. What is in it is worse, because it is recognisable: it is the face of a man arriving at a thing he has been outrunning for twenty-two years and finding it has stopped, finally, to let him catch up. He looks, Goldie thinks, the way she looked the first morning, at a counter, when a boy leaned over wearing dead eyes. He looks like a man whose floor has just declined to be a floor.

"Stop the dig," he says, to the survey men, in the voice that gets things done in this town, and they, conditioned, half-move to obey before the foreman remembers whose name is on the contract and whose isn't.

"Can't, butt," the foreman says, not unkindly, holding his phone up like a small shield. "There's remains. It's a police matter now. We've to stop and they've to come."

"I know what it is." Gareth doesn't shout it. That is the thing that stops Goldie's breath — he doesn't shout, doesn't deny, doesn't perform surprise; he stands in the rain over the open ground and says, simply, with the exhaustion of a man setting down a weight he has carried so long it has changed the shape of his body, "I know exactly what it is. I've known for twenty-two years. There's no need for the boy to look at her any more. Cover her up. Let me cover her up."

He looks at Goldie then. Only at Goldie.

"You did this," he says. There is no accusation in it, which is the most frightening thing of all. It is almost gratitude, the way the very tired are grateful to whoever finally makes them stop. "I knew it would be you. The minute they told me what you were — *Met, weren't you, before* — I knew you'd be the one. You can't help it, can you. You see them. The rest of us spend our whole lives learning not to look at a thing, and you walk in off the council list and you can't *not.*"

"Gareth," Goldie says. Carefully. The way you'd talk to a man on a wall. Behind her she is aware of Ffion gone rigid, of Huw stepping in close on her other side, an enormous quiet wall of a man putting himself, without fuss, between the girl and whatever this is going to be.

"Do you know what I've protected?" Gareth says, and now it comes, the thing he has been holding the whole twenty-two years, and it comes not as confession but as *argument*, a case made by a man who genuinely believes he is in the right and has the worse, kinder fear that he might not be. "Not myself. You think it's myself. It was never myself. I have protected three adoptions that turned into three good lives — real children, real families, who'd have grown up the children of a scandal, a body on the premises, a charity done for trafficking when all it ever did was help girls nobody else would touch. I protected a woman who is forty years old and happy and does not know she came out of a dead girl, and her children, who do not know their faces are a grave. I have stood at the front of this town for twenty-two years and been the good man, and do you know *why* I could afford to be the good man?"

The rain. The engine. The boy in the pit not looking at any of them.

"Because I was the one who paid for it," Gareth says, very quietly. "Somebody had to keep her. Somebody had to keep the whole thing from coming up out of the ground and ruining people who never did a wrong thing in their lives. It was a kindness. I have told myself every day for twenty-two years that it was a kindness, the kindest lie this town ever told, and most days—" his voice does something, the first crack in it, a hairline, "—most days I still believe it. So before you bring the whole of it down on a happy woman's head, I want you to tell me. You, who can't stop looking. What is the kind thing? Is it the truth? Or is it letting the dead stay where the living put them?"

And Goldie — who has spent twelve years being the world's foremost expert on exactly that question, who has built a life, a desk, a marriage's-worth of silence on the conviction that the merciful thing is not to look — feels the whole horror of it land, which is that she knows precisely what he means, and that there is a version of her, the version that came to this counter, that would once have agreed with every word.

She thinks of Seren on the tile, the colour of the deep end.

"The girl in the pit didn't get a choice about being kept," she says. "And neither did the four children walking round this town tonight with her heart in them. That's not a lie that protects the living, Gareth. Not any more. It's a lie that's *killing* them, one weak turn at a time, because nobody's allowed to put a name to what runs in that family — and a name is the only thing in the world that gets them to a doctor. You didn't keep a secret. You kept a girl from saving her own great-grandchildren."

For a second — and Goldie will go over this second for a long time, because it is the second the man she's been chasing becomes a man — Gareth's whole face caves, the kept thing finally not kept, and he looks past her, up the slope, at the open ground and the boy who won't look in it, with an expression she has no word for except the one the foreman used: *God love her.*

Then his eyes go to Ffion.

And change.

Goldie sees it happen the way she saw it happen at the gala — the half-second in which a man decides what his face is going to do — except this time it does not settle into the good man. This time it settles into something cornered. He has just understood, the way Goldie understood at the gala, who the girl beside her is: not a bystander, not a colleague of the nosy woman from the desk, but *one of them*, the jaw and the peak, a child of the very family he has spent his life protecting from this exact morning, standing on the higher ground in the rain finding out that her mother is a runaway's grave.

"Ffion," he says, and his voice changes too, gentles, terribly. "Ffion, *cariad*, you shouldn't be up here. Come down out of this. Come away from her. You don't want to see this, none of you want to see this, come down with me now and we'll—"

"I know what you did," Ffion says.

She is seventeen and shaking and her great-grandmother is a metre down in the rain and she says it clear, the recogniser's certainty in it, the family voice he has heard at a hundred christenings he helped arrange. "I know who she is. I worked it out before *she* did." A small jerk of the chin at Goldie. "And I know it was you that signed her into the ground, because I've seen your writing, Gareth, it's all over the Trust book, *refer to G.H. only,* I've had it photographed on my phone for a week." The jaw holds. The whole face holds. "So don't *cariad* me. Don't you come up here and *cariad* me over the top of her."

And Gareth Hollis, who has been the best man in Aberglas for twenty-two years, who held a frightened woman in his coat nine days ago and got the urn moved and the children out of the water, takes one step toward the girl with both hands coming up — to plead, to gather, to herd, to do the thing he does, the containing, the keeping — and Huw moves.

Huw doesn't say anything. Huw, who has called this man *Gareth bach* across thirty years of retirement dos and committee meetings and saved buildings, simply steps in front of Ffion, all fifty-eight gentle years and twenty stone of him, and puts one enormous hand flat on Gareth's chest, the way he'd hold a door, the way he held the girl up out of the deep end, and says, very low, very sad, the only thing he says in the whole of it:

"No, now. No, Gareth. You leave the child be."

The two old men stand there in the rain with one hand between them. Below, blue light is already coming up the front of the Pelican — the foreman's call gone through, the proper world arriving at last, twenty-two years late — and Maureen, who has appointed herself and four other women in plastic rain hoods to the chapel gate without being asked, plants her feet on the wet path between the dig and the world and folds her arms and says, to the first uniform up the slope, in the voice that has run bingo since Thatcher and will not be moved for anybody, "You'll want to be gentle up here, son. There's a young girl been on her own a long time, and she's not on her own any more, and we'd thank you to mind how you go."

Gareth looks down at Huw's hand on his chest.

And Goldie watches the best man in Aberglas understand that there is no face left to keep.

---

# CHAPTER 24 — The Kindest Lie

They get him inside out of the rain because nobody, in the end, knows what else to do with a confessing man in a downpour.

It is the sergeant's doing — a brisk woman in her forties with a face Goldie reads in one pass as competent and tired and somebody's mother, the kind of face that has talked a great many people down off a great many walls and has the patience worn into it like a stair. She takes one look at the open ground, the two officers stringing tape between the escallonia, the seventy-one-year-old man with no coat saying *let me cover her up* to a metre of clay, and she makes the decision a good copper makes, which is that the scene is the ground and the ground is now hers, and everybody who is not the ground can wait somewhere dry while she works out what she has. "Is there somewhere," she says, to Goldie, because Goldie is the one not crying and not digging, "we can put people. Out of this."

"There's a room," Goldie says.

There is, of course, a room. There has always been a room.

* * *

The function room upstairs is where the Pelican keeps its grandeur the way you keep a wedding dress — boxed, yellowing, too good to throw out and too sad to wear. It is where the Trust did its quiet work; it is where, twenty-two years ago, in a back office now stacked to the picture rail with bingo boxes and a broken Christmas tree, a frightened girl had a baby and, nine days later, a heart that simply stopped. None of which the sergeant knows. She knows it is large and dry and has chairs, and she puts them in it — Gareth on one of the stacking chairs with the orange seats, Ffion two seats along with Huw planted between them like a sea wall, the survey lad white and shaking with a cup of the Mermaid's worst, and the Belles, who have come up the stairs in their rain hoods without being asked or able to be stopped, ranged along the back like a jury that has already retired.

Then the sergeant goes back out to the ground, because the ground is the case, and she is gone perhaps twenty minutes, and in those twenty minutes the thing happens that no court will ever hear, because it happens in a damp function room over a faulty radiator between a man who has run out of road and a woman who knows the exact mileage.

Gareth looks at the floor of the room for a long time.

Goldie understands, watching him, that he is not looking at the floor. He is looking *through* it, down a flight of stairs and twenty-two years, to a back office and a camp bed and a girl, and that he has not let himself look at that particular place in all this time, has walked past the door of it five thousand mornings on his way to fight for this building, and that the dig has done to him exactly what a boy at a counter did to her: pried open a drawer he had nailed shut and made him look at what was in it.

"She was on her own," he says. To the floor. To the room. "When it happened. That's the thing I — " He stops. Starts again, lower. "Branwen was meant to be on at six. The girl took bad about four, in the night, and there was nobody but a sixteen-year-old and an arrangement, and by the time the morning came she was — " His hands do something in his lap, a small helpless folding. "Her heart. They told me after, the ones who knew about hearts, that it was always going to. That she could have been in the Heath with every doctor in Wales round the bed and it would have. That it wasn't the having of the baby and it wasn't the back room and it wasn't anything anybody did or didn't do. It was just in her. The way it's in them now." He lifts his eyes, and they go, without his choosing it, to Ffion. "The way it's in that one. And the little one in the water last week."

"Don't," Ffion says. Not loud. The jaw holds the whole face level. "Don't you look at me to make it softer."

"I'm not — " He stops. "No. You're right. I'm not allowed that."

* * *

"Tell me about Branwen," Goldie says.

She says it gently, the way you ask the one question that matters when there is no recorder running and may never be, and she watches him understand that this is the one he cannot argue his way around — that everything else he has done can be dressed as kindness, the unreported death, the unmarked ground, the long good-man years of paying for the town's peace with his own silence, but that this one cannot, because Branwen was not a scandal in 2003. Branwen was an old woman dying of cancer three weeks ago who had decided, at the very end, to do the one decent thing left in her, and somebody put a stop to it.

"She rang me," Gareth says.

The radiator knocks. Somewhere far below, faintly, the proper world is arriving — more vehicles, the particular slam of a van that means a tent and a generator and lights, the machinery of the state finally turning up twenty-two years late to a girl it wrote off as a runaway.

"She rang me, the Tuesday. Said she'd been thinking, with the building going and all of it coming up, that there was a girl who ought to know whose she was before — before Branwen wasn't here to tell her right. Said she'd carried it long enough. That she was going to ring the family. Marged." His voice on the name is careful, the way you'd carry something filled to the brim. "And I — " 

He stops.

And here is the place, Goldie thinks, where the cheaper version of this man would lie even now, would say *I never meant it, it was an accident, she was so frail* — and he does not. That is the horror of Gareth Hollis, the horror that has nothing to do with snarls. He looks straight at it.

"I went to see her," he says. "On the Wednesday. The afternoon. I signed the book — I didn't even think not to sign the book, that's how — *a friend*, I put. Three forty. Because I was. I'd known her fifty years. I held her hand and I told her she was tired and her mind was going and she didn't know what she'd be doing to a happy family, raking it all up, and she looked at me — " the hairline crack again, widening now, the good jumper dark with rain across the shoulders " — she looked at me with her whole self, clear as you like, no mind going at all, and she said, *Gareth bach, I held that baby and I held her mam while she died, and I am not going into the ground with the both of them still a secret. You can be cross with me if you like.*"

The Belles are very still at the back of the room. Maureen's arms are folded. Eira, ninety-five, who was *here*, who has known whatever she has known since 2003 and kept it folded small inside herself, watches Gareth with an expression Goldie cannot read and does not try to.

"And then she went to sleep," Gareth says. "She was tired. They get tired, at the end. And she went to sleep with her hand in mine, and I sat there, and I thought about Marged, who I've watched grow up, who calls me *Uncle Gareth*, who has never had a bad day she didn't earn fair, finding out off a dying nun that her real mother is the runaway every soul in this town has pitied for twenty-two years, and that her own children — "

He cannot finish it. Goldie does not help him. Some sentences a man has to carry to the end himself.

"There was a pillow," Gareth says, finally, to the floor. Flat. Done. "She was asleep. She didn't — it wasn't — she didn't wake. I want that to be true so badly I've decided it is, and I'll never know, and that's mine to carry, that not-knowing, for whatever I've got left. They said she slipped away peaceful in the night. I let them say it. I helped carry her coffin. I read the lesson." A breath. "I have done one unforgivable thing in seventy-one years, and I did it to the kindest woman I ever knew, to stop her doing a kindness. So don't — " and now, at last, something rises in him, not anger, something worse, an old man's grief turned outward because it has nowhere else to go " — don't you sit there and think you've caught a wicked man. I'd give my life this minute to have not gone up there with that pillow. But I'd give it to keep Marged off Facebook tomorrow morning, too. Tell me which of those is the good man. Go on. You're the one can see everything. Tell me."

* * *

And Goldie, who has spent twelve years being the world's leading authority on the subject of not looking at one face, feels the room tilt the way the floor tilted on the first morning, because she knows precisely what he is asking, and she has spent her whole adult life believing the same answer.

"You think I don't understand you," she says.

"I think nobody understands me. That's rather the point of it."

"No." She keeps her voice in the dry middle register, the one nothing gets a grip on, because it is the only thing holding her up. "You think I'm the law come up the hill to tell you the truth is always the kind thing. I'm not. I'm the woman from the lost-property desk, Gareth. I match goggles to toddlers. And I have spent twelve years keeping a face buried that nobody asked me to keep, because I decided — exactly the way you decided — that the merciful thing, the kind thing, the *bearable* thing, was not to look at it. So when you ask me which is the good man, I'm the last person in Wales who's going to lecture you. I know what it costs to keep one. I've got one of my own. I keep it every single day."

Something moves in Gareth's face. The thing she least expected and most dreads: recognition. He looks at her the way she has been looking at this whole town since the first morning — sees her, places her, knows her on sight.

"You as well," he says, softly. It is not a question. "I wondered. There's a — you've a way of not finishing a sentence. I know it. I do it in the mirror." He almost smiles, and it is the saddest thing in the room. "You know what it is, then. To keep a face you can't put down. To get up every day and walk past the one door you've nailed shut and tell yourself the nailing was a mercy. You and me, *cariad*, we're the same animal. We just kept different things."

And for one second — Goldie will go over this second for the rest of her life — she is back in the car on the wet road, twelve years gone, a few seconds and a turn, and the thing she has refused to look at since is *right there*, summoned by a confessing murderer in a bingo store, and she nearly says it. She nearly tells Gareth Hollis the one true thing she has never told Tom, never told the crew, never written in the only book she has ever been able to confess to. The drawer is open. He has opened it, on purpose, because the very tired know that the only thing that levels a thing is to make the other person bleed too.

She does not.

She thinks of Seren on the tile.

* * *

"You're right," she says. "We're the same animal. And here's where it stops being true."

She stands up. She doesn't decide to; her body does it, the way it puts both hands flat on the counter when the floor stops being a floor.

"The face I keep can't be hurt by my keeping it," Goldie says. "She's gone. There's nothing left to protect but me. That's the whole rotten luxury of my grief, Gareth — it's *private*. It only costs me. But the thing you've been keeping — " she takes a breath, because this is the one that matters, the one she got wrong once with a man's whole life in her certainty and swore she'd never do again, and she is doing it anyway, because the calibration runs the other way now, because some claims you have earned the right to make " — the thing you've been keeping isn't a dead girl any more. It walked into this building on the first morning I sat down. It's a nineteen-year-old who goes grey at the deep end and a fourteen-year-old who stopped breathing on the tiles nine days ago and a sixteen-year-old shepherding the little ones who's going to go down one Thursday with nobody knowing why. Four of them, Gareth. At least four, that I've seen, walking round this town wearing her eyes and her heart, and her heart is the thing that killed her, and it is *fixable* — it is a wire and a tablet and a name on a doctor's letter — and the only thing in the whole world that gets them that name is the truth you've spent twenty-two years burying."

The radiator knocks. Eira, at the back, makes a small sound and presses her knuckles to her mouth.

"You didn't keep a secret," Goldie says. "I'd have understood a man who kept a secret. You kept a girl from saving her own great-grandchildren. Carys's heart is loose in four children and you sat on the cure because the cure has her name in it. That's not the kindest lie this town ever told. Not any more. It stopped being kind the first time one of those children went grey in your pool and you decided their mother's peace was worth more than their pulse."

* * *

Gareth Hollis looks at her.

Then he looks, slowly, at Ffion — the jaw, the peak, the family voice — who is staring at him with seventeen years of a face he helped raise, a child whose christening he very likely arranged, whose great-grandmother he put in the ground; and Goldie watches the thing she has been waiting for since the gala finally arrive, which is not the moment the good man becomes a villain, but the far worse moment when the man understands that the love he committed murder to protect has been the very thing he was endangering all along.

"Seren," he says. Not to anyone. The little one in the water. "She nearly — " 

"She did," Ffion says. "She did nearly. And the doctor said no family history, and Mam said no, none, because Mam doesn't *know* her family, because you took it. So when they couldn't find a reason, they sent my little sister home with a leaflet and told her to drink more water." The jaw holds, but only just. "She's fourteen. She wears it now. The monitor. Under her school jumper. Beeps in double maths. And nobody can tell her why she has to, because you've got the why down a hole in the ground."

For a long moment nobody in the function room breathes.

Then Gareth puts his face in his two hands — the good man, the pillar, the keeper of the face nobody could read — and what comes out of him is not the confession of a murderer or the surrender of a villain. It is the sound of a man who has, after twenty-two years, finally looked at the thing in the drawer and found that it was never the scandal he thought he was keeping. It was a girl who died on her own in the dark, and a baby he meant to save, and four living children whose hearts he could have been mending since before they could swim, if only he had been able to bear the looking.

"I kept her for the town," he says, into his hands, wrecked, ruined, the words coming out broken-spaced and final. "For the families. For the girl who'd have grown up the runaway's bastard." He drags in a breath that shudders the whole of him. "And I was wrong. God forgive me, I was wrong, and I knew it every time one of them went pale, and I told myself it was the heat, the same as everyone, the dehydration — *bless him, he never drinks enough water* — because if it was the heat I didn't have to look. I'm so — " He cannot say it to Ffion. He says it to his hands. "I'm so sorry, *cariad*. I'm so sorry. Tell the little one I'm sorry. Tell her to keep the monitor on."

* * *

When the sergeant comes back up the stairs, rain off her in sheets, ready to take names and move people and run the machinery of an exhumation she is only beginning to grasp the size of, she finds a function room gone very quiet and a seventy-one-year-old man with his face in his hands, and a girl of seventeen sitting two seats away with her own face wet and level and unforgiving, and an enormous lifeguard between them with one hand resting — no longer a wall now, just a steadying — on the old man's heaving shoulder.

And she finds, ranged across the back of the room, five women in rain hoods who have arranged themselves, without anyone telling them to, into the shape a town makes around its own: Maureen with her arms folded and her chin up, daring the world to be unkind; Glenys with her good ear turned and her bad one toward the rain; Pam, for once not theorising, just holding Eira's hand; and Eira herself, ninety-five, the last living soul who was *there*, looking at Gareth Hollis at last with the thing Goldie could not read before and can now, because it is the same thing the foreman said in the rain over the open ground, the same thing the whole town will have to learn to feel for the girl it pitied and the man who hid her and the children it nearly lost:

not forgiveness. Not yet. Just the refusal to look away.

Sai is in the doorway behind the sergeant, soaked through, the Trust box clutched to his chest under his coat like a baby, *file reviewed — no action — refer to G.H. only* somewhere inside it in a fierce upright hand that will, by tomorrow, belong to a name. He catches Goldie's eye across the room and doesn't say anything, which is, for Sai, an entire speech.

The sergeant takes out a notebook. It is the wrong thing to do in a room this quiet and she knows it the second she's done it, but the machinery of the state has a notebook and the notebook has to come out, so she clicks the pen and says, to the room in general, in the careful voice of a woman about to ask a great many people for their dates of birth, "Now. I'm going to need to take a few details from everyone, if I —"

"You'll take a cup of tea first," says Maureen, from the back, in the tone that has closed bingo at half nine for forty years and will not be appealed. It is not a question and it is not, quite, a kindness; it is a woman in a dripping rain hood informing the constabulary of west Wales how this is going to go. "You're wringing. You'll catch your death and then where are we. Glenys, the urn."

"He's *what?*" says Glenys, who has her good ear turned to the radiator and her bad one to the sergeant, and who has caught, of the entire confession, the death of nobody and the threat of a wet policewoman. "Who's caught their death?"

"*Tea,* Glenys."

"There's no need to shout, Maureen, I'm right by you." Glenys pats the sergeant's arm, gravely, on her way to the urn, mistaking her for a person who has had bad news rather than a person taking a statement about a body. "You sit down, love. You've had a shock."

And the sergeant — competent, tired, somebody's mother, twenty years of walls behind her — looks at her notebook, and looks at the urn, and makes the second good decision of the evening, which is to put the pen away and let the town be the town for the length of time it takes a kettle the size of a dustbin to boil, because she has read the room the way Goldie reads a face, and the room is not going anywhere, and some statements keep better warm.

"Tea, then," she says. "Go on."

It is half a degree of air, no more — a wet sergeant, a fierce old woman, a deaf one patting her arm — and it is enough; the function room remembers, for a moment, that it is a room in a leisure centre and not a confessional, and Gareth, behind his hands, makes a sound that is not quite a laugh and not quite the other thing, the sound of a man who has loved these women fifty years hearing them be exactly themselves over the wreck of him, and Goldie watches the whole town close around the worst night of its year the only way it has ever known how, which is loudly, and with the kettle on.

"Right," the sergeant says then, when the cups are going round, gently, because she has read the room. "Let's take it slow, shall we. Nobody's going anywhere fast."

Goldie does not sit back down. She stands at the cold window of the function room with the rain coming sideways off the bay, and below her, in the grounds, in the white glare of a generator lamp they have got going at last, she can see the small square of disturbed ground where a girl has lain alone for twenty-two years and is, as of this evening, not alone — a tent going up over her, careful hands, the right people finally come.

She has spent the whole case learning the difference between recognising a thing and knowing it. She finds she would give a very great deal, just now, not to know what she knows; to be Dil, downstairs, who will hear about all this tomorrow and get every date wrong and every feeling exactly right; to be anyone who got to keep their drawers nailed shut.

Instead she watches them raise the tent over Carys Pryce, and she thinks about a wire and a tablet and a name on a letter, and four children, and one face she has kept for twelve years that nobody, after tonight, is going to let her keep much longer.

---

# CHAPTER 25 — Telling Marged

The hardest thing about telling a happy woman the worst news of her life turns out to be the oven.

Glenys's lasagne has been the question of the week — the lasagne the size of a coffin, *no offence,* none taken — and at half past three on a Wednesday Goldie is standing in Marged Hollis's kitchen holding a casserole dish she could bathe a child in, while three of the Aqua Belles conduct a low fierce summit over whether it goes in now or at five, and whether Marged's oven, which is a 1990s electric thing with one working ring and a door that has to be held shut with a chair, will even take it.

"It won't take it," says Maureen, who has assessed the oven the way she assesses everything, by standing in front of it and disapproving. "We'll do it at mine and bring it round hot."

"You can't bring a lasagne that size in a *car,*" says Pam. "It'll slide. You'll have it all over the dash on the Aberaeron road and then what."

"Then we eat it off the dash," says Maureen. "Goldie. Stop holding it like a font, love, you're not christening it. Put it on the side."

Goldie puts it on the side.

This is, she understands, what they do. This is the warmth doing the only thing warmth can do in the face of a thing it can't fix — it cooks, it rotas, it argues about ovens, it fills a kitchen so full of itself that the unbearable thing standing in the corner of the room has nowhere to sit down. They have been at it since the gala. There is a lasagne, a fish pie, a bara brith from Eira that arrived under a tea towel like a sacrament, a Tupperware of Carwyn's soup that nobody will eat because it is, like his coffee, a sermon about sin. The Hollis kitchen has not been this full of food since a funeral, which is, Goldie thinks, more apt than any of them are saying out loud.

In the next room a cardiologist is taking off her coat.

* * *

Her name is Dr. Anwen Rhys and she has driven up from the Heath, in her own car, on her day off, because a registrar she trained who now works at Bronglais rang her about a fourteen-year-old with an unexplained arrest and a family tree drawn in red biro by the girl's sister, and Dr. Rhys, it transpires, is the sort of woman who hears the words *family tree* and *unexplained* in the same sentence and cancels her plans.

Goldie clocked her at the door in half a second — fifty-ish, reading glasses pushed up into grey hair that has given up the fight, a soft Carmarthen voice, hands that have done a thousand of these and a face that has not stopped minding — and felt something she does not feel often, which is relief at meeting a stranger. Dr. Rhys does not want managing. Dr. Rhys wants the folder.

"Where's the girl who made the tree," is the first thing she says, in the hall, to Goldie, having sorted in one glance who in the house is family and who is furniture and who, against all the evidence of a lost-property cardigan, is the one who knows where the bodies are. "I'd like her in the room. She's done half my job for me by the look of it."

"She's making the tea," says Goldie. "She's seventeen and she has decided that managing the kettle is how she's getting through the afternoon, and I'd let her, if I were you."

"I'd let her too." Dr. Rhys looks at her over the glasses. "You're the one who saw it, then. Before any of us."

"I'm the one at the desk."

"Mm." She does not push it, which Goldie likes her for. "Right. Is the mother sitting down? She wants to be sitting down for this."

* * *

Marged is sitting down.

She is at her own kitchen table in her own kitchen with her own dead mother's clock ticking on the wall behind her, and she has a cup of tea in front of her that she is not drinking, and she has the particular stillness of a woman who knows that whatever is coming has already happened and is only now arriving to be said. She has not slept properly in nine days. The jaw — Carys's jaw, the clean shelf of it — is held the way Ffion holds it, the way the whole family holds it, by main force, and Goldie thinks, not for the first time, that the resemblance she has chased through forty faces is at its most unbearable here, in this kitchen, in the one face that wears the bones and does not know it.

Ffion has stopped pretending to be busy with the kettle and is standing against the counter with her arms folded, on guard, the way she stood on the higher ground in the rain. Seren is not here — Seren is in Bronglais, on her monitor, *beeps in double maths,* awake and offended and alive, with Huw's sister-in-law sitting beside her bed doing a crossword so the child is not by herself — and that absence is its own held breath in the room, the empty chair that is the whole reason for the meeting.

"You can all stay," Dr. Rhys says, to the kitchen, settling herself across from Marged with the folder, "or some of you can go and be loud somewhere else, whichever the family wants. I find these go better with company, myself, but I'm not the one it's about."

"They stay," says Marged. Flat. The country past crying. "There's no one in this kitchen doesn't already know more about my family than I do, by the look of it." Her eyes go, once, to Goldie, and there is no accusation in them, only that terrible level recognition. "Go on, then. Somebody's been working up to telling me something all week. I've watched you all do it. Casseroles and faces. You've the faces of people at a funeral, the lot of you, and nobody's died but a child nearly did, so." She wraps both hands around the cup she isn't drinking. "Tell me what the women going over is. Start there. Start with the women going over, because that's the bit I can carry. The rest of it I've half worked out and I'd rather you said it than I did."

* * *

So Dr. Rhys starts with the women going over.

She does it the way Goldie imagines she does everything, plainly, without drama, with the unhurried kindness of a person who has learned that the kindest thing you can do with a terrible fact is not to dress it up. There is a thing, she says, in the heart's wiring — not a weakness, not a damage, a *fault in the wiring*, present from birth, that runs silent in families for generations. It makes the heart, every so often, under strain, lose its place — *like a faulty light,* and Goldie watches Marged's hands tighten on the cup, because that is Marged's own word, the one she said in the corridor, *flicker and back* — and most times the heart finds its place again and you go down and you come up and everybody laughs and says *Auntie Glad's gone over.*
