"And sometimes," says Dr. Rhys, "it doesn't find its place. And that's the bit your family's been lucky about, mostly, for a long time, more lucky than they had any right to be. Until a fortnight ago, in a swimming pool."

"Seren," says Marged.

"Seren got unlucky in the one building in twenty miles," says Dr. Rhys, "with a defibrillator on the wall and a man who knew how to use it and a woman who knew where to put her hands." A small nod at Goldie. "If it had happened on the bus, we wouldn't be having this conversation. I'd be having a worse one. So before I tell you anything else, I want you to hold on to that one. Your girl is alive because of where she fell down. Everything else I'm going to say is so that the next one doesn't have to be lucky about where they fall."

Marged closes her eyes. Opens them.

"It's got a name, then," she says. "The going over."

"It's got a name."

"Tell me the name."

And Dr. Rhys tells her the name, and Goldie does not write it down, and will not, because it is not her place and because the having of the name out loud in this kitchen is a thing that belongs to the family and not to a ledger — but she watches it land, the long QT of it, the small clinical syllables, on a woman who has spent thirty-eight years being told it was low blood pressure and the heat and *just a thing the women do,* and she watches the most frightening word in the world become, in the space of one sentence, the least frightening, because a thing with a name is a thing you can fight.

"You can test for it," Marged says. It is not a question. Ffion has clearly already told her this much; Ffion has clearly told her this much at three in the morning, the two of them awake, the clever furious child and the exhausted mother, working the one true rung Goldie gave her. "Ffion said. You can test for it. And if you've got it—"

"If you've got it," says Dr. Rhys, "we watch it. We catch it. There are tablets that steady the wiring — simple ones, taken every day, the way some people take a thing for blood pressure their whole lives and forget they're doing it. And for the ones most at risk, there's a small device, smaller than a matchbox, sits under the skin here—" she touches her own collarbone, lightly "—and watches the heart all day and all night, and if the heart loses its place, it puts it back. A wire and a tablet and a check-up twice a year." She lets that sit. "It is not a death sentence, Mrs. Hollis. I want to be very clear, because the next hour is going to be hard and I don't want you carrying the wrong fear out of it. This is a thing we *manage.* This is a thing children grow up with and have babies of their own with and live to be a nuisance to their grandchildren with. The danger was never the condition. The danger was *not knowing it was there.*"

"Not knowing," Marged repeats.

"Not knowing," says Dr. Rhys. "Which is the part," she adds, gently, and looks, for the first time, properly at Goldie, handing the room across the table the way you'd pass something fragile, "I think somebody else has to tell you. Because the not-knowing in your family didn't happen by accident. And I can fix a heart, *bach.* I can't fix the reason nobody ever wrote the history down. That's not medicine. That's whatever the woman from the desk does."

* * *

There is a silence then, in the Hollis kitchen, of the kind that has weight.

Out in the hall the Belles have gone quiet — even Maureen, who can hear a held breath through a wall and respects it — and somewhere behind Goldie, very softly, she can hear Sai. Sai, who drove them all up, who has spent the afternoon making himself useful in the small ways a twenty-four-year-old makes himself useful in a house of grief he can't touch, carrying chairs, finding teaspoons, holding a door — Sai is at the kitchen doorway with a tea towel over one shoulder and the casserole logistics suspended on his face, and he has heard the whole of it, and he is, Goldie sees without turning fully round, crying. Not loudly. Sai is crying the way a young man cries who has decided not to, the kind where the face stays still and does the leaking on its own, a tea towel twisted in his two hands, and he catches Goldie's eye and mouths *sorry* and does not move, because there is nowhere to go and he is not going to be the one who makes a noise.

Goldie sits down across from Marged. She has not, until now, sat down. She has been holding casserole dishes and the doorframe and herself, and now she sits, because some things you cannot say standing up, and she puts her hands flat on the table the way she puts them flat on the counter when the floor stops being a floor.

"Your mam's adopted," Goldie says. "You told me that in the corridor. Nan was took in as a baby, local, never knew her people, the Beynons had her and you don't ask."

"You don't ask," Marged agrees.

"I asked," says Goldie.

* * *

She tells it the way Dr. Rhys told the heart — plainly, without dressing it, because the kindest thing you can do with a terrible fact is not to make it beautiful. She tells it small and true and in order, the way you'd file it.

She tells Marged that the wall at the top of her family tree — the box with the question mark, the one Ffion drew the lines to anyway because the gap was the point — has one person behind it, not a hundred. A girl. Sixteen. Local. She tells her there was a charity once, the St. Non's Trust, that did its quiet work in the back of the Pelican when the Pelican was still the heart of the town, and that it helped girls who had nobody, in the way they helped girls then, which was kindly and in secret and with the records sealed. She tells her that one of those girls had a baby in the spring of 2003 — a daughter — and that the daughter was placed, quietly, locally, with a childless couple, and grew up, and never knew, and had a daughter of her own, and that daughter is sitting at this table with her hands round a cup of tea she isn't drinking.

"The baby was your mam," Goldie says. "Nan. The one took in. And the girl who had her — sixteen, the spring of 2003, in the back of the Pelican—"

She stops.

Because here is the place where the cheaper version of this would reach for the drumroll, the em-dash, the floor coming up to meet you, and Goldie has spent twelve years learning that the floor coming up to meet you is a thing you do *to* people, and she is not going to do it to this woman. She is going to set it down gently, on the table, between the cooling tea and the dead mother's clock, the way you set down a thing that is heavier than it looks.

"You'll have walked past her face a hundred times," Goldie says. "There's a poster of it. There has been, on and off, for twenty-two years. Carys Pryce."

Marged does not move for a long moment.

The clock ticks. In the hall, nothing. At the doorway, Sai's tea towel.

"Carys Pryce," Marged says, at last, in a voice gone very small and very careful, the country past crying turned to something younger. "The runaway. Nerys's girl. The one that—" She stops. Her hand has come up to her own jaw, unconsciously, the fingertips along the clean shelf of bone, *Carys's jaw, under Marged's own hand,* and Goldie has to look at the table because it is the most naked thing she has ever watched a stranger do. "Nerys Pryce. Who did everyone's hair. Whose girl ran off and broke her heart and she never got over it and the whole town's been sorry for her my whole life." The fingers move along the jaw. "And she's—" Marged cannot say it. She looks at Goldie, and the level recognition is gone, and what is under it is a forty-year-old woman in her own kitchen finding out where her face came from. "She's *mine.* She's my—"

"She's your grandmother's mother," Goldie says. Carefully. Precisely. Because the precision matters, because this is the one thing the whole town keeps getting one generation short and she will not get it short here, not in this kitchen, not with the woman's own hand on the proof. "Carys is Nan's mam. Nan was the 2003 baby. So Carys is your great-grandmother. And Ffion's, and Seren's." A breath. "She was sixteen, Marged. She wasn't a runaway. She never went anywhere. She had a baby in a back room and nine days later her heart did the thing — the wiring, the going over, the thing that's in all of you — and it didn't find its place. And the people who were frightened of the scandal didn't tell anyone. They put her in the ground up by the chapel and let the town go on thinking she'd run." Goldie keeps her voice in the dry middle register, the only thing holding her up. "She didn't run from you. She didn't even know there'd be a you. She just had a daughter, and a bad heart she never knew about, and twenty-two years of being thought a girl who left. And the heart she didn't know about is the heart in your Seren. That's the history nobody wrote down. That's the thing the doctor needs the top of your tree to know."

* * *

Marged Hollis puts her face in her hands.

It is, Goldie thinks, exactly the gesture Gareth made in the function room — the good face going down behind the two hands — and the resemblance ought to be unbearable, the keeper and the kept making the same shape with their grief, except that it is not unbearable, because what comes out of Marged is not the wreck of a man who has hidden a thing for twenty-two years. It is simpler than that, and worse, and better. It is the sound a person makes when a wall they have stood against their whole life, leaning on it, complaining about it, never once questioning that it was a wall, turns out to have a door in it, and on the other side of the door is a sixteen-year-old girl who has been there the whole time, alone, in the ground, three streets away, wearing the same jaw, waiting to be claimed.

Ffion comes off the counter.

She crosses the kitchen and she does not say anything — the family voice, the recogniser, seventeen, who worked the tree the slow human way and got it right — she just folds down beside her mother's chair and puts her arms round her, the jaw against the jaw, the same bones held together by two generations at once, and over her mother's bent head she looks at Goldie, and Goldie sees in the girl's face the thing she has been watching the whole town reach for all week and has not, herself, in twelve years, been able to reach.

It is not grief. Or it is not only grief.

It is that the family has just got *bigger.* That a woman who thought the top of her tree was a sealed box has just been told it is a girl with a name, a real girl, somebody's daughter, who can at last be grieved properly, marked properly, *known* — and that the very fact that breaks her heart is the same fact that will keep her own children breathing. The truth has come into the kitchen wearing the worst possible coat, and underneath the coat it is carrying a wire and a tablet and a name on a doctor's letter, and the name on the letter is going to save the lot of them.

"She's been on her own up there," Marged says, into her daughter's shoulder, muffled, wet. "All this time. While we were—" She lifts her head. Her face is a ruin and it is also, Goldie sees, somehow lighter, the way a face is lighter when it has set down a thing it didn't know it was carrying. "She wants bringing in. She wants a proper stone. She's not staying up there with no name on her, not now we know, I won't have it." The jaw sets — Carys's jaw, decided — and Goldie understands that this is how the woman is going to survive the afternoon, by giving herself a job, by being, like her daughter, like the whole loved ridiculous town of them, a person who reaches for a thing to *do.* "And the children get tested. All of them. Tomorrow. Today. Rhodri and Gethin and the little ones and me and—" She turns to Dr. Rhys, fierce now, a mother with a cause. "How fast can you test a family, doctor. How fast."

"Fast," says Dr. Rhys, who has been sitting very quietly letting the woman from the desk do the part that isn't medicine, and who now leans in with the folder and the relief of a clinician given, at last, something to mend. "Fast, *bach.* We start with the ones we're worried about and we work out. I'll have Seren sorted before she's discharged, and the rest of you in clinic inside the month, and anyone who's positive on a tablet by the summer." She looks at the red-biro tree, lying on the table between the casseroles, the names in boxes, the lines drawn to the gap. "You've done my whole afternoon for me, the pair of you. I've never had a family hand me the tree already drawn. Usually I'm a year finding out what your girl found out in a maths book."

"She notices faces," says Marged, automatically, the way you say a thing you've said a hundred times without knowing it was load-bearing. "Always has. Drove her teachers mad. We never knew where she got it from."

And the whole kitchen goes still, just for a beat — Goldie still, Ffion still, even Dr. Rhys's pen still — because they all, in the same second, know exactly where she got it from now, the noticing, two generations up, a girl who saw too much and a great-granddaughter who can't stop seeing, and nobody says it, because some things you let a family find for themselves, in their own time, the slow human way, the way Ffion drew the tree.

Goldie does not say *me too.*

She wants to, and the wanting is so unfamiliar it still feels like indigestion, and she swallows it — and then, because the afternoon has done something to her, because she has just sat in a kitchen and watched recognition turn into relationship in real time and found she could not look away from it and did not want to — she does not, entirely, swallow it.

"It's a useful thing to be able to do," Goldie says, to Marged, drily, "noticing faces. Bit of a curse at parties. But useful." And she lets her eyes go, once, to Ffion, who catches it — the girl catches everything — and the corner of the dead girl's jaw does something that on anyone else would be the start of a smile, the two of them, the recognisers, fifty-one and seventeen, holding the room steady from either end of it the only way they know how. "She'll be all right, your one. Takes more than a wet floor to put down somebody who notices everything. I should know."

* * *

Later — when the lasagne has gone in at Maureen's and come back hot in a box on Pam's lap on the Aberaeron road and not, in the end, slid; when Dr. Rhys has gone back down to the Heath with the red-biro tree photographed on her phone and a clinic list already started; when Dil has arrived off the last appointment of the day and cried, frankly and enormously and without apology, into a cup of tea at the Hollis table because *that poor girl, that poor poor girl, all that time,* and been comforted, absurdly, by Marged, the bereaved comforting the weeper, which is how it goes in this town; when Eira, ninety-five, brought round on her grandson's arm because she would not be left out of it, has sat for a long time holding Marged's hand and said the only thing she has said all afternoon, which is *I knew her mam, cariad, I'll tell you about her mam, she was lovely, your great-gran, she had a laugh on her* — later, Goldie stands in the Hollis hallway putting her coat on, and Ffion comes out to her, folder under her arm, the eternal folder, *WHO FAINTS* gone soft at the corners now from a fortnight of being carried.

"You said her name right," Ffion says.

"What?"

"Carys. You said it like she was a person. Everyone else this week's said it like she's a—" the jaw works "—a thing that happened. A scandal. The doctor said *the affected individual,* even, bless her, when she meant Seren. You said *Carys Pryce, she was sixteen, she didn't run.* Like she was someone you knew." The girl looks at her, the recogniser's look, top of the head down, reading her. "Did you. Know her."

"No," says Goldie. "I never met her. I only ever saw her face."

"That's not the same as knowing someone."

"No," Goldie agrees, and something in her chest does a slow turn, because the child has just said, without knowing it, the whole of the thing Goldie has been failing to learn for twelve years. "No, it isn't. I've spent a long time not being clear on the difference, mind."

Ffion considers this. Then she says, in the flat practical register of a girl who has decided that talking-around-things is a tax she will not pay, "You should come to the stone. When it goes up. For Carys. You found her. You should come."

And Goldie, who came to the quietest counter in Wales precisely so that she would never again be the one who is needed, who built an entire anonymity out of doing filing, who has not let herself be *part* of anything since a wet road twelve years ago — Goldie says, "All right," and is appalled to find she means it, and that the meaning of it does not, as she has spent twelve years being certain it would, kill her.

* * *

It is dark by the time Sai drives them back down to the Pelican, and the building is shut, and Goldie lets herself in to the lost-property desk because she has left her own keys on the hook there like an idiot and because, she tells herself, she only wants the keys.

She does not only want the keys.

She wants the ledger, the way her hand has wanted the ledger her whole life when there is a thing in her she cannot say, and she takes it out from under the counter in the dark, and she does what she has done after every face for forty years, which is file it, because the shelf is the only confession she has ever been able to make, and it isn't one.

The case is closed. She lets herself feel that, plainly, the way Dr. Rhys feels a mended heart. Forty faces, a basement, a salon, a dying woman's room, a girl in the ground twenty-two years and a happy woman's kitchen this afternoon — and it is *done,* the great scattered face assembled and placed, every fragment home, the dead girl known at last and her living children safe. The forensic mind finishes its work and finds, for once, that finishing it has saved somebody instead of jailing them.

She writes it down clean and unaching, the way you close a file you are proud of.

Then she stops.

Because there is a thing the closing of the case has done that she did not plan for and cannot now un-notice, and it is sitting in her chest the way Marged's hand sat along her own jaw — naked, involuntary, the gift coming online whether she wants it or not. Every face in this town is placed now. She has spent a month learning the whole of Aberglas the way she learns everything, against her will and to the millimetre, and tonight, with Carys home and Marged told and the children getting their tablets by summer, the index is complete. There is not an unplaced face left in the building.

Except one.

And it was never this town's.

Goldie sits in the dark behind the counter with the pen in her hand and the ledger open, and for the first time in twelve years she does not, immediately, file the thing she cannot say and move on. She leaves the line. The pen, which has done her swerving for her for twelve years, which is very good at its job, hovers — and does not, tonight, swerve.

She closes the ledger.

She puts it back under the counter, where it lives, and she sits a while longer in the quiet of a building that used to be loved, listening to the boiler make its noise like a man being slowly disappointed, and through the dark glass of reception the lido water lies flat and black and full of leaves and ambition, and somewhere out there, three streets away, up on the higher ground behind the chapel, a girl who has been on her own for twenty-two years is, as of this evening, going to get a stone with her name on it.

It is only the having-no-name, Goldie thinks, that anybody ever truly can't survive.

She gets her keys off the hook.

She does not get the ledger back out.

But she knows, walking out into the cold off the bay with the gulls going over and the bunting nobody has taken down ticking in the dark, that she is going to have to, soon — that there is one face left unindexed in the only book she has ever been able to confess to, and that it has a name, and that she has spent twelve years being the world's leading authority on the subject of not writing it down, and that the town she did not want has just spent a fortnight teaching her, casserole by casserole, what happens to the ones you leave with no name on the ground.

---

> **FACE 0001** — male, 38, "the Tuesday Man" (ALED BEYNON). The brow, made — the eyes, married-in. The man beside the secret. *Placed.* He kept the slot for a goggle that was her father's. He can come collect it now; tell him it was never lost, only kept.
> **FACE 0019** — female, 17 (FFION HOLLIS), and the whole line behind her — the jaw, the peak, the heart, the noticing. The great-granddaughter who drew the tree. *Placed.*
> **CARYS PRYCE** — sixteen, 2003, the top of the tree. Home as of today. Stone to follow.
> **CASE: CLOSED.**
> Every face in this town, placed. The whole of Aberglas, indexed and home.
> Except one.
> And it was never this town's.

---

# CHAPTER 26 — The Face She Wouldn't Index

She goes back for the keys she did not, in fact, leave.

This is the thing about being a person who never loses anything. You cannot give yourself the kind alibi everyone else gets. You cannot stand on the harbour wall on a Friday evening and tell yourself, plausibly, that you forgot your keys at the lost-property desk, because you are the one human being in the county who has never once forgotten where a thing is, who could tell you to the inch where her own keys are sitting in the dish by Tom's old place in Cardiff, where her mother's wedding ring is, which drawer, which corner, where every flip-flop on that shelf came in and on whose foot. You do not get to be vague. The vagueness is the one mercy the gift has never extended her, and she has spent twelve years trying to forge it, and she has got, in all that time, exactly one document past herself.

So she does not pretend, walking back down to the Pelican on the second cold evening after Marged's kitchen, that it is about the keys. The keys are in her coat pocket. She can feel them. She walks anyway.

It is the dead end of the day and the dead end of the season at once, the worst light Aberglas has, the grey going to no colour at all off a grey sea, and the front is empty the way it is only empty in February — the amusements shuttered, the bunting nobody has taken down since the gala ticking against the lamp-posts, a gull on the war memorial giving her the flat professional contempt of a thing that has watched a great many people walk somewhere they did not want to go. She passes the Mermaid. Through the steamed glass Carwyn is wiping down for the night and a single customer sits hunched at the counter, and Goldie reads neither of them, on purpose, and is briefly, fiercely proud of herself for it, the way a person on a diet is proud of walking past a window, which lasts exactly as long as it takes her to clock — involuntary, against her will, the gift not asking permission — that the hunched customer has a stripe of paint dried on the back of one wrist and is therefore Idris Tomos, the plumber, the brow, the man too old to be Carys's anything, in for a late tea after a burst pipe somewhere, and that she will now carry the back of his wrist forever, which is the whole rotten arrangement, which has never once given her the night off.

She is not, she notices, walking past the window of the thing she has come to do. She is walking straight at it.

* * *

The building is shut and the building is hers.

Sai gave her a fob in the first week — a dolphin, the centre's font, the laminate already going soft at the corners the way everything here goes soft at the corners — and she lets herself in through the side door by the boiler, and the dark of the Pelican comes up around her like water that has been waiting. The boiler is making its noise. *The Bishop,* Sai calls the knock in the pipes, the one that comes at solemn moments and at no others, and the Bishop knocks once, low, somewhere under the deep end, as if to say *you again,* and goes quiet.

Out through the dark glass of reception the lido lies flat and black and full of leaves and ambition. There is a single light on across the square — Carwyn's, the Mermaid, where the man burns the coffee on principle and the bacon is holy and there is, even now, even at this hour, somebody in there she could read off the window in half a second if she let herself, which she will not, not tonight. Tonight she has come to read the one face she has spent a career not reading, and she would rather do it without an audience, even an unwitting one across a dark square.

She goes behind the counter.

She does not put the big lights on. The desk has a lamp, the gala photo still propped against the base of it where Sai left it weeks ago, Carys Pryce sixteen and third row and fourth in, the whole face in one girl, and Goldie sits down in the lamp's small yellow circle the way she has sat down a thousand times — facing the shelf, her back to the dark pool, the only arrangement she has ever been able to bear.

The shelf is the shelf.

One (1) pair of swimming goggles, the half a thing pretending to be a whole one, gone now from the slot where it lived for weeks because she took it down herself and set it square in front of an empty chair for a man called Aled who will come and claim it because it was never lost, only kept. One (1) set of dentures, upper, in the margarine tub she has still not opened, because some things you file and do not look at, and that is allowed, that is a skill, that is half the job. A child's armband, perished, one of a pair, the other already somewhere doing better.

And the flip-flop.

* * *

One (1) child's flip-flop, size 9, left foot only, the sole worn through on the inside edge.

It came in on her first morning. It is the first thing she inventoried in this building, the first thing she ever read off the shelf — *a pronator, this one, a roller, a small person already pounding the wrong part of the foot into the ground; a future of insoles* — and she has written it into the ledger more times than the ledger needs, *flip-flop, size 9, left, STATUS: unclaimed,* because her hand goes there when there is nowhere else for her hand to go, and because she has never, in a month of clearouts, in a month of Sai begging her to bin the dead stock before the developers do it for them, been able to put it in the skip.

She has told herself this is because someone might come for it.

Nobody comes for half. She knows that. She wrote it down on day one. The other flip-flop is somewhere doing better and this one is going green in a cage and no foot is coming back for it, and she has kept it anyway, on the shelf, where she will not throw it away, and she has spent a month not asking herself the obvious thing, the thing the gift would have answered for any other object in this building in a quarter of a second, which is: *whose foot does it remind you of.*

She picks it up.

She has not, in twelve years, let herself do this. Not look at a flip-flop — she looks at forty of them a week. The other thing. The thing under the flip-flop. The thing the flip-flop has been standing in front of since the first morning, the way a man stands in front of a stopped oven and disapproves of it so he does not have to think about what is behind him in the room.

She lets herself, now, on purpose, the way you would walk into cold water on purpose, and looks at the thing behind the flip-flop, and it is small, and it is plain, and it is this.

* * *

Esme would not wear the matching one.

That is the kind of thing you are allowed to remember. The summer she was seven, there was a pair of jelly shoes, pink, the left worn through on the inside edge because she ran on the wrong part of her foot, a pronator, a roller, a small person pounding the ground — and she would only ever wear the left. The right one lived in a bag by the door. She had a reason, the reason of a seven-year-old, which was the right one rubbed and the left one didn't, and Goldie used to carry the right one around in case, and never once needed it, and the right one is, presumably, still somewhere, doing better.

Esme had a way of standing with her weight on the worn shoe, hip out, waiting, while Goldie found the keys, found the bag, found the thing that was always exactly where Goldie said it was because Goldie never lost anything, and the back of her neck where the hair went up in a wet curl after swimming, and she would not eat the soft middle of a sausage roll, only the ends, the pastry, *I don't like the inside, Mum,* and the inside is the kind of thing you are allowed to remember, the pastry, the ends, the small ordinary preferences of a person who was eight for as long as she was ever going to be.

Goldie keeps to the flip-flop. She keeps to the worn edge of it, the inside edge, the roller's edge, the thing she can hold. She does not go to the road.

She is not going to go to the road.

There was a road, and there was rain, and there was a turn, and there were a few seconds in which a woman who has never in her life been wrong about what she is looking at could not make the thing she was looking at be other than it was, and that is the place the camera has always swerved, for twelve years, mid-stride, into a joke or a flip-flop or a churchwarden's brow to the millimetre — and the camera swerves now, too, the old reflex, the pen that does her swerving for her, and she lets it swerve, because some mercies you keep even when you are choosing not to keep the others. She does not go to the road. She keeps to the worn edge of the shoe.

The face, though.

The face she lets herself have.

* * *

Esme at eight. The last clear afternoon. Not the road, not the after — the afternoon, plain, the way you would file a thing you wanted to keep accurate: a kitchen, a Tuesday, the radio, a child on a stool with her weight on a worn shoe and her wet hair going up at the back, eating the ends and not the middle, telling Goldie a thing about a girl at school that went on for nine minutes and had no point and was the best thing Goldie has ever in her life been told.

She has not let herself see this in twelve years.

She has been able to see a rioter glimpsed for half a second from a burning bus in 2011, a man's ear on a 2009 platform, the exact attach of a stranger's earlobe sixteen years and a different country of a body later. She has carried four hundred faces before breakfast, every day, against her will, unforgetting, a cemetery she did not ask to keep and cannot close. And the one face — the one she would give the whole catalogue to be allowed to lose, the only one she has ever tried to lose — she has guarded so hard, so completely, so expertly, that she has gone twelve years without once looking at it, because looking at it was the thing she was certain would end her.

And here is the thing she did not know until tonight, sitting in a lamp's small circle in a dying building with a dead girl's flip-flop in one hand and her own dead girl's face, at last, behind her eyes.

It does not end her.

It is Esme at eight on a Tuesday with her hair going up at the back, and it hurts the way a held breath hurts when you finally let it out, all at once and everywhere, and it does not end her, and she sits with it, and the building keeps her, and the Bishop knocks once under the deep end and goes quiet, and she does not, as she has been certain for twelve years that she would, die of it.

* * *

She has spent a month, she sees now, learning this from strangers.

She thinks of Gareth in the function room, a good man going down behind his two hands, who spent twenty-two years refusing to look at one face — a girl on the floor of a back room, dead of her own heart, on his watch — and who built an entire good life out of the not-looking, the keeping, the management of the scatter, so that nobody would do to that face what Goldie does to every face, which is *see* it. She thinks of Nerys above the shut salon, who could not look at her daughter's death and so rebuilt her daughter's brow on a thousand heads instead, sketched and given away across thirty years, a face reproduced by hand because the original could not be borne. She thinks of a whole town that recognised Carys for twenty-two years — the eyes, the cheekbones, the brow, the laugh coming out of a stranger's throat at the bingo — and never once knew her, never knew the girl was three streets away in the ground the whole time, wearing the same jaw, waiting.

She has spent a month placing every face in Aberglas against a girl nobody would look at.

And she has done all of it — the refusing, the rebuilding, the recognising-without-knowing — she has done all three of them, for twelve years, to a child on a stool in a kitchen who would not eat the inside of a sausage roll. She has refused the face like Gareth. She has rebuilt around it like Nerys — built an anonymity, a counter, a whole second life out of the not-looking, the way Nerys built a salon. And she has been, to her own daughter, the most accomplished recogniser-who-never-knew in the whole of Wales: she who can place a stranger from across a dark square has not once, in twelve years, let herself simply *be* in a kitchen with the one face that was hers, and known it, and let it be known back.

The case was the mirror. It was always the mirror. She came to the quietest counter in Wales to stop seeing, and the building handed her, face by face, the exact shape of the thing she was not seeing, and made her place it, and now there is nothing left in the whole indexed town to place but the one face that was never this town's, and it has a name, and the name is not Carys.

* * *

She is not alone with it, which is the part she could not have managed, before.

The light is on across the square: Carwyn, at his urn, with somebody in there she could read and won't. Her phone is on the counter and the screen is lit and it says *Tom,* a missed call from an hour ago, the third this week, *just ringing to see how you are, no need, only ringing,* and she has not rung him back and she is, she finds, going to, because he is the one other person alive who carries this face the way she carries it, who had a daughter on a stool and a wet curl at the back of a neck and a child who would not eat the inside, and the two of them have spent twelve years being decent and exhausted and fond and never once saying her name to each other across the wreckage of it, and Goldie is tired, suddenly, all the way down, of being the world's leading authority on not saying it.

On the hook by the keys there is a flask.

It is Dil's. Dil left it Tuesday, the tartan one, and inside it is the tea Dil makes, the colour of the building, undrinkable, kept warm by a vacuum flask and forty-one years of a woman who has decided that the cure for everything is a hot drink you don't have to taste, only decide has happened to you. Dil left it on purpose. Goldie knows Dil left it on purpose the way she knows everything, instantly, against her will — Dil who remembers the town the soft human way, wrong on every date and right on every feeling, who clocked something in Goldie's face in the Hollis hallway that Goldie did not clock in it herself, and who left a flask on a hook the way other women leave a hand on a shoulder, because Dil does not have the words and would not trust them if she did, and a flask is a thing you can leave that says *I knew you'd come back here on your own, bach, so I left the kettle in a coat.*

Goldie does not cry. Or she does, a little, the way Sai cried into a tea towel, the kind where the face stays still and does the leaking on its own and you would not, across a square, be able to tell. It is not a breakdown. It is a held breath let out. It is the opposite of a wound reopening — it is a thing she has guarded for twelve years sitting in the room with her at last and not setting the room on fire, in a lamp's small circle, with a flask on a hook and a name lit up on a phone and a girl's flip-flop in her hand, the building keeping her the way it keeps everything, in the dark, sorted by date, going quietly green, waiting to be claimed.

* * *

She puts the flip-flop down. She does not put it on the shelf. She sets it on the counter, in the lamplight, by the gala photo of a girl who is going to get a stone with her name on it because the town learned, casserole by casserole, what happens to the ones you leave with no name on the ground.

And she stays with the face. That is the thing she did not come here able to do and is doing now, in a lamp's small circle, with the building making its noises around her: Esme at eight behind her eyes, on purpose, with the radio and the stool and the wet curl, and she does not look away, and the looking-away does not happen on its own either, the camera lying quiet for once where she has set it, on the kitchen, on the Tuesday, on the ends of a sausage roll, not the road.

She does not know how long she sits with it. A long time. Long enough that the lamp is the only warm thing left and the flask on the hook has gone past warm to merely kind, and across the square Carwyn's light burns on at nobody, and her phone says *Tom* in the dark, and a girl's flip-flop sits beside a girl's photograph in the small yellow circle, and the one face she has spent twelve years certain would end her does not end her, only stays — plain, and warm, and behind her eyes, the way a thing stays that has finally been let in out of the cold.

There is a page for it. She knows that now, the way she has known it, somewhere under the swerving, for twelve years: a page in a book under a counter, blank not for want of a face but because of the one face she has never let her hand go near — the gap a sharp reader would have felt long before she let herself feel it. She has spent the night looking at the face she would not look at. The writing of it is another night's courage, or this one's, or the small hours of it still to come.

For now she only reaches under the counter, in the keeping-dark of a building that used to be loved, and takes the ledger out, and lays it closed on the desk in the lamplight beside the flip-flop and the photograph and the lit name of the man a hundred miles west who carries the same face — and rests her hand flat on the cover, the way you rest a hand on a door you have decided, at last, you are going to open.

---

# CHAPTER 27 — STATUS

She does not write it the way she has written everything else in this book, which is to say against her will, with the pen doing the swerving for her, her hand going to the paper the way her hand goes to the bad coffee — because it is there, because it has happened to her, because the alternative is feeling the thing instead.

She writes it on purpose.

This is, she understands, sitting in the lamp's small circle with her hand still flat on the closed cover of the book where she left it the length of a long breath ago, the entire difference. Not whether she remembers — she has never had the option of not remembering, the gift took that off the table fifty-one years ago and has never once offered it back. The difference is whether she does the remembering with her face turned toward the thing or her shoulder set against it. Twelve years she has done it with her shoulder. A whole second life built side-on, a counter with its back to a pool, a town indexed feature by feature so that there would always be one more stranger's ear to measure and never a clear hour in which the one face she would not summon could come up the stairs of her on its own.

The face is up the stairs now. It has been up the stairs since the flip-flop, an hour ago, two, she has lost the time, which has not happened to her since she was a girl. Esme at eight on a Tuesday with her hair going up at the back, and it has not killed her, and it is still here, plain and warm and behind her eyes, waiting the way a child waits for you to find the keys you have already found, weight on the worn shoe, hip out, *come on, Mum.*

So.

She opens the book. She turns past the last clean entry — *CASE: CLOSED. Every face in this town, placed. Except one. And it was never this town's* — to the page that has been blank for twelve years, not for want of a face but because of the one face she has never let her hand go near. She takes the pen off the desk and uncaps it.

She lowers the pen.

* * *

She does not give it a number.

She nearly does — her hand starts the loop of a *0* the way it has started a thousand of them, FACE 0001, FACE 0447, the four-digit cold-case ones that surface out of the Met years like a body coming up in slack water, a number is a thing you can hold a face in, a place to put it that is not your chest — and she stops, the loop unfinished, an open mouth of ink, and lifts the nib clean.

No number.

Because a number is for the lost. A number is the dignity she gives the strangers — the man on the platform, the boy with the borrowed eyes, the brow on the churchwarden, the dead girl in the gala photo who is going to get her name back in stone — the filing that says *I have seen you, I have kept you, you are not nothing, you are FACE such-and-such in a book a woman keeps in the dark.* It is what you do for property that nobody claimed. It is mercy for the unclaimed.

And the one face on the page in front of her was never, for one second of one day, unclaimed.

She does not number her daughter. She writes her name.

*ESME.*

The pen moves the way it moved when Esme was alive and the name went on book bags and the inside of wellingtons and the dotted line at the top of a hundred infant-school worksheets, *Esme Webb, age 6, age 7, age 8,* a hand that has written this word more than any word it owns and has not been allowed to write it once in twelve years. Five letters. The pen knows them in its sleep. The pen is, it turns out, exactly as good at this as it has always been at the other thing, the swerving — only this time it does not swerve, it goes straight at the name and lays it down and does not flinch, and the not-flinching is the whole of what she came back here in the dark to learn she could do.

Under the name she does not put a build. She has the build. She could give it to the page the way she gives every face to the page, the set and the cant and the heaviness of a particular upper lid, *exact, to the millimetre, held before* — God, held before, held harder and longer and with more of her than any face that has ever crossed her counter or her court or her life. She could fill the page. She does not fill the page. The build is not for the book. The build is for the kitchen, for the stool, for the wet curl at the back of the neck and the ends of the sausage roll and the nine-minute story with no point — the build stays where the warm things stay, in the part of her that is not a ledger and was never meant to be one, and that she has spent twelve years pretending she had paved over.

She writes, instead, the only fact about this face that the ledger has ever been short of. Not what it looks like. Where it goes.

* * *

She has written *STATUS* ten thousand times.

It is the engine of the whole book and she knows the weight of every word that can follow it. *UNCLAIMED* — for the flip-flop, the goggle, the wedding ring with no inscription; the great soft tide of the world's small abandonments that wash up on a metal shelf and go quietly green. *UNPLACED* — for the faces, the early ache of a stranger she has seen before and cannot account for, the question hanging. *PLACED* — the cold satisfaction of the net closing, the where and the why locked at last to the who, a thing finished and filed and done with.

None of them is the word.

*UNCLAIMED* is a lie and she will not write a lie in this book, not on this page; the child was claimed, is claimed, will be claimed every day this woman has breath, there is no version of the world in which Esme Webb goes to the unclaimed shelf. *PLACED* is worse. *Placed* is what you do to a thing when you have understood it and are therefore allowed to set it down and stop carrying it; *placed* is the lie she has been trying to forge for twelve years, the lie that there is a drawer this fits in and a moment after which she can be done. There is no being done. There was a road, and there was rain, and there was a turn, and there is no *placed* on the far side of that, not ever, and she is not going to pretend on the one page where she has finally agreed to stop pretending.

She holds the pen above the line where the status goes and she does the thing she has not done in twelve years, which is sit inside the question of what is actually true — not what is bearable, not what closes the file, not what gets the line moving again. What is *true* of this face, the one face, now.

It is true that she will never put it down. It is true that she does not want to. It is true that the wanting-to-keep-it and the not-being-able-to-bear-it have been, all this time, the same hand closed too hard around the same small thing, a fist she mistook for love because it hurt — and that a hand can hold a thing without crushing it, that there is a way to carry a face that is not a guard-post, that the whole indexed town has spent a month teaching her this and she was too busy keeping to learn it until tonight.

Gareth kept Carys with his back to her, twenty-two years, until the keeping killed a woman. Nerys kept her by remaking her, a thousand brows, a face reproduced because the original could not be looked at. The town kept her by recognising her and never knowing her, a name on a poster walked past every morning, a girl three streets down in the ground the whole time. They all kept her. Not one of them held her. And she has been so frightened of her own daughter's face for twelve years that she has done to it the exact thing she came to this town to stop doing to strangers — kept it in the dark, filed, guarded, and called the guarding love.

She is not going to keep her any more.

She writes the word.

*STATUS: HELD.*

* * *

She looks at it.

*ESME. STATUS: HELD.*

That is all. There is no build, no number, no descriptor line, no parenthetical she tells herself, no *do not write the other thing down*, because the other thing is written now, the other thing is the whole entry, the other thing has a name and a status and a place in the book at last and the place is not the shelf and not the cemetery and not the drawer that slides shut behind her eyes — the place is *held*, which is to say carried, which is to say with her, on purpose, in the light, for as long as she goes on being the woman who cannot forget, which is for ever, which she has spent twelve years experiencing as a sentence and is going to try, starting now, on this page, in this dying ridiculous beloved building, to experience as the thing it actually is.

She caps the pen.

She does not, she notices, feel the floor tilt. She has been braced for it — the old sensation, the way the world goes uncertain just before you understand you are going to be unwell, the feeling that arrived the first morning when a boy leaned over her counter wearing somebody's dead eyes. It does not come. The floor is a floor. The lamp is a lamp. Dil's flask is on the hook keeping its terrible tea warm in a coat, and on the counter a dead girl's flip-flop sits beside a dead girl's photograph, and on a page a living woman's daughter has, finally, an entry, and the floor holds her weight the way the floor has been holding her weight all along, patiently, without comment, while she spent twelve years certain it would give.

* * *

It is, she sees when she lifts her head, getting light.

Not light — the rumour of it, the grey going from no colour to a colour off the sea, the window across the square where Carwyn's urn-light burned all night gone pale now and pointless against a real one coming up grudging behind the harbour wall. February dawn in Aberglas, the worst light in Wales doing the only generous thing it ever does, which is end. The gulls have started. Somewhere a van. The Pelican is loud the way a shut building is loud — the boiler ticking down, a tap she will never find dripping into a sink she will never locate, the Bishop knocking once under the deep end and going quiet, *you again*, and she finds she has been here all night, in the lamp's circle, with the book open, and that the building has kept her the whole time, in the dark, the way it keeps everything, sorted by date, going quietly green, waiting to be claimed.

She has been claimed, she thinks. That is the joke of it. The woman who came to the quietest counter in Wales precisely so that nobody would ever need her, nobody would ever know her, nobody would ever come up to the hatch and find the one drawer she keeps shut — has been, in four months, comprehensively, irreversibly *found*: by a gossip with a dead dog on her lanyard, by a corporate boy who weeps into tea towels, by a chorus of terrifying women in swim caps, by a dying town that handed her its ghost and made her place it and would not let her do it alone. She came here to not be seen. She is the most seen she has been since the kitchen, since the stool, since the last clear afternoon. The arrangement, she thinks, has comprehensively failed. *Lovely, that.*

She reaches for her phone.

* * *

It rings twice before he answers, which means he was awake, which means he is sitting in the kitchen he got in the divorce with a cup of something at this stupid hour, and she knows, the way she knows everything, that he has the radio on low and that there is a window in front of him going the same grey hers is going a hundred miles west, two people at two windows who have spent twelve years being decent and exhausted and fond and never once saying the name across the wreck of it.

"Goldie." Just that. Tom has never needed many words; it is one of the four hundred reasons she married him and one of the three she left. There is a long pause on the line in which neither of them does the thing they have always done, the *no need, only ringing, how are you, fine, you, fine.* The pause goes on. It is not an empty pause. It is the most they have said to each other in twelve years.

"I wrote her down," Goldie says.

She does not explain it. There is no one alive she would have to explain it to less. He carried the same face out of the same kitchen; he had the stool and the wet curl and the ends not the middle; he is the one other person on the earth who knows exactly what it has cost her to be the world's leading authority on not saying it.

"Did you, love," says Tom, and his voice does the thing, goes, the way Sai's went into a tea towel, the way the face stays still and the leaking happens on its own — she can hear it down the line, the wet still place in the middle of a steady man, a hundred miles off, at a window, in the same grey. "Where'd you put her."

"I didn't put her anywhere." She is looking at the page. *ESME. STATUS: HELD.* "That's the whole — that's the thing of it, Tom. I stopped putting her places. I just." She turns the lamp a half-inch with two fingers, the way she turned it last night, so the light falls square on the line. "I held on to her. With my eyes open. That's all it is. That's all it was ever supposed to be."

A long breath, the radio, the gulls his end too maybe, the sea finding both of them. "Took you long enough," says Tom, which is the kindest thing he has ever said to her, and she laughs, an ugly cracked thing that is half the other, and so does he, the two of them at their windows, leaking and laughing in the grey, and it is not closure and it is not reconciliation and it is not tidy, it is only a door that has been shut twelve years swinging a little on its hinge, the cold coming in, and the warmth, and she leaves it open.

* * *

By the time she has got the big lights on — and she does get the big lights on, hauls the bank of switches by the boiler room so the Pelican comes up out of its dark all at once, every tired tile and rusted railing and curl of peeling Deco render exposed to the flat morning, a beloved wreck blinking awake — the day has started without asking her.

A van does come; it is the bread for the Mermaid, and Carwyn meets it across the square in a coat over pyjamas, scratching, and lifts a hand to her through the glass, *all right*, and she lifts one back. The first of the Belles arrives at twenty past seven because Maureen has decided, with no authority anyone can locate, that the Tuesday aqua slot now starts at half seven on account of a thing about the boiler that is not true, and Maureen comes through the turnstile in a mackintosh over a costume with a swim cap already on like a woman expecting trouble, and Glenys behind her mishearing something, and Pam behind Glenys with the paper folded to a story she is going to be partly right about, and they fill the foyer with the particular weather of themselves, and Maureen stops dead at the counter and looks at Goldie, who has plainly been here all night, and looks at the flask on the hook, which is Dil's, and looks at the book open on the counter, which is shut to everyone in Aberglas but is, for once, simply open, and Maureen — who buried a husband and two sisters and has terrified this town into loving her for sixty years — says nothing at all. She reaches over the counter. She puts one cold hard hand flat on the back of Goldie's hand, once, the way you'd press a stitch, or check a thing was still there, and takes it back, and says, "You'll want a proper cup. That tar Dil leaves is a disgrace," and goes through to the pool, and that is the entire conversation, and it is enough, it is more than enough, it is the warmest thing that has happened to Goldie at this counter and not one true word was said.

Dil arrives at eight, on her rails, the lanyard swinging its little dead dog, and clocks in a single sweep the night-shift of it — the lights, the flask returned to nobody, the woman who has not been home — and does not ask, because Dil does not have the words and would not trust them, and instead puts the kettle on in the back the way she has put the kettle on for forty-one years against every disaster the building has thrown up, because Dil's theology is simple and total and correct: that the cure for the unbearable is a hot drink you do not have to taste, only decide has happened to you.

"You left your flask," says Goldie.

"Did I," says Dil, who left it on purpose, and they both let that be true and untrue at once, the way the kind things are, and Dil brings the tea, the colour of the building, undrinkable, and sets it down by the open book without looking at the open book, because Dil clocked something in the Hollis hallway weeks ago that Goldie did not clock in herself, and a flask on a hook and a cup of tar at dawn are the only sentences she has, and they are, it turns out, the right ones.

* * *

Goldie drinks the tea. She does not taste it. She decides it has happened to her.

Through the swing doors the pool is coming alive, the slap of feet on wet tile, Maureen counting the class in like a drill sergeant, Glenys getting the count wrong and being corrected at volume, the warm chlorinated heart of a dying building doing the one thing it has always done, which is hold the town in the water and not let it sink. Out the dark glass — light glass now, ordinary morning glass — the lido lies flat and full of leaves and, against every odd, against the council and the developer and the orange flags up on the higher ground, still here. Still standing. Saved, or saving, or saved-enough; the town is in it now, the way the town gets in things, and a building the town is in is a hard building to knock down.

She does not close the book.

She has closed it ten thousand times — A4, ruled, heavy, the only confession she ever managed and it never was one — slid it under the counter, set the shelf between herself and the pool, faced the safe direction. She does not, this morning, slide it under the counter. She leaves it open on the desk in the flat ordinary light, the way you leave a window open, the name on the page where the morning can get at it, and she turns, on her stool, all the way round — her back to the shelf for the first time in four months, her face to the water and the doors and the whole loud ridiculous living machinery of the place — and she watches the next person come through the turnstile, a stranger, a kit bag, a face she has never seen, and feels the old involuntary inventory start to run its hands over them, the gift firing the way the gift fires, against her will, the way weather happens to a field.

And for the first time she can remember she does not brace for it.

She lets the face come.

---

# CHAPTER 28 — Save the Pelican

The woman at the trestle table selling raffle strips is a 1987 wedding.

Goldie has her before she has the float box open — fifty-something, the dyed copper grown out two inches to grey at the part, a sun-spot on the left temple shaped like Anglesey, and a way of holding a biro that says *typing pool, then nothing, then this.* But it is the laugh that lands her: three notes, the little duck on the second, the head canting right at the end of it like a bird that has heard a door. Goldie has filed that laugh before. She filed it in a bingo hall in October out of the throat of Carol-Anne Beddoe, and before that, though she did not know it then, out of a girl in a gala photograph who has been dead for twenty-two years and whose laugh was taught, week after week, Friday half-ten, to half the women in this room by a hairdresser who could not stop.

So that is the difference now. In October the laugh would have stood the hairs up on Goldie's arms — a dead girl coming out of a stranger's mouth, the floor going uncertain. This morning the woman laughs, and Goldie thinks: *Bryn-y-Môr, the salon's Tuesday-and-Friday run, Nerys did her colour for thirty years and gave her Carys's laugh in the chair and neither of them ever knew,* and then she thinks, *raffle strips are a pound, four for three, and I have four pounds,* and she buys the strips, and the laugh is a laugh, learned and lovely and harming no one, a thing this town carries the way it carries the cadence and the chapel and the price of batter. You can know a thing all the way down and let it be ordinary. That, it turns out, was the whole trick, and she had to come to a dying lido and ruin her own quiet to learn it.

"There's lovely," says the woman, tearing the strips. "You're the one off the desk. The one who—" and stops, because there is no end to that sentence yet that the town has agreed on, and settles for "—you found her."

"The town found her," says Goldie, which is true and which she has decided to keep saying until it sticks.

* * *

The function room has not been this full since the meeting in October, and the meeting in October was a wake pretending to be a battle.

This is the opposite. Goldie stood at the back of that one — Gareth Hollis at the front with his good warm capable voice, the room adoring him, the closure notice from Ceredigion County Council going round hand to hand like a death in the family, *cease operation within the current financial year* — and she had watched a building full of people fight to keep a thing without knowing they were fighting to keep a secret in the ground beneath it. She remembers thinking the room had the held-breath quality of people who suspect, somewhere under the bunting, that they are going to lose.

They did not lose.

A girl came up out of the ground with her name on. The papers had it — *RUNAWAY WHO NEVER RAN: BODY OF MISSING ABERGLAS TEEN FOUND AT DOOMED LIDO* — and then the better papers had the better version, the slow sad true one, a fifteen-year-old hidden away and a baby placed and a heart that stopped and a building that kept the lot. You cannot put a Lidl on a girl. The developer's orange flags came down off the higher ground without a word said, the way a man takes his hat off when he finally clocks where he's standing.

And the town did the rest. The town is doing it now, around her, at volume.

* * *

"It is *not*," says Maureen, "a memorial garden. We are not doing a *garden.* Gardens are for people who've given up. We are doing a *trust.*"

She is chairing, which is to say she has appointed herself chair, taken the gavel that is actually the little brass bell off Goldie's lost-property desk because it was the nearest thing to authority in the building, and is using it to silence a room of two hundred with the ease of a woman who has terrified this town into loving her for sixty years. Maureen buried a husband and two sisters and has never once in Goldie's hearing said a soft word, and she stands at the front of the function room in a mackintosh she has not removed because removing it would suggest she might leave, and she runs the meeting the way she runs the aqua class, like a drill sergeant who suspects the recruits of dying on purpose to spite her.

"The Pelican Community Trust," says Sai, who is beside her with a laptop and the look of a man who has been corporate his whole short career and has finally, catastrophically, started to care about something. He is doomed no longer; he is the duty manager of a building that is not closing, which is a job that did not exist a fortnight ago, and he has the febrile energy of someone running on it. "Community asset transfer. The council leases the building to the trust for a peppercorn, the trust runs it, the lido reopens phase by phase, there's a grant stream for the heritage side because of—" he gestures, helplessly, at the whole story, at the chapel, at the ground "—because of all of it, frankly, and the café stays, and the pool stays, and—"

"And the bingo stays," says Maureen, "or there is no trust."

"The bingo is constitutionally protected," says Sai, who has put it in the constitution.

A hand goes up at the back. It is Pam, who has been waiting her whole life for a meeting that would vindicate her, and is going to enjoy it.

"I want it minuted," says Pam, "that I said. In October. I said there was something in the ground. I said it at this very meeting, you can ask anyone, I said the bunting was hiding something—"

"You said it was *radon*, Pam," says Glenys.

"I said it was *covered up.*"

"You said *radon.*"

"The principle," says Pam, with enormous dignity, "was sound."

And the room laughs — the whole room, two hundred people, the warm helpless laugh of a town that has been wrung out and is allowed, this morning, to be ridiculous again — and Pam sits, vindicated enough, and Goldie, at the back by the hatch where she stood in October, feels the thing she has spent her whole life not being any good at, which is being in a room with people rather than reading them across it. She is in it. She is not at the edge of it cataloguing the brows. She is holding a fan of raffle strips she will not win anything with, in a crowd, in the warm, and the gift is running anyway — *the Bevan sisters, third row, still not over the bins; Carwyn slid in late, coat over the apron, fetched up next to the woman from the wool shop who he is going to marry and doesn't know it yet* — the gift never stops, weather happens to a field — but it is not, this morning, a thing she is hiding behind. It is just how she sees. There is a difference between a window and a wall, and she is learning it at fifty-one in a leisure centre, late, the way she learns everything.

Dil arrives at her elbow on her rails, the lanyard swinging its little dead dog.

"Glenys thinks we're building a *grotto,*" Dil reports, with relish. "She's told three people there's going to be a grotto. Maureen'll have her shot."

"Let her have the grotto," says Goldie.

"That's what *I* said," says Dil, scandalised and delighted, and goes to spread it.

* * *

Carwyn has done the coffee for the meeting, because of course he has, and it is — Goldie tests it, against her own advice — exactly, faithfully terrible, bitter and thin at once, a coffee let down from a great height, and she finds she is glad of it the way you are glad of a clock that still keeps the wrong time it has always kept.

She has decided the coffee has happened to her when Sai's voice changes at the front, drops out of the bright administrative register into something younger and more careful, and she looks up, and the room has gone quiet on its own, no bell needed, because of who is being helped up the aisle to the front.

Nerys Pryce comes through the parting crowd on the arm of Eira, who is ninety-five and the only person in the building old enough to walk a grieving mother to the front of a room without it looking like an arrest.

Goldie has the whole of Nerys at once and wishes, for once, that she did not. The cardigan two sizes into a body that used to fill it. The right thumb-pad with its thirty-year callus from the comb, worn now to a softness, the hand that built a thousand brows gone quiet in her lap when she sits. And the face that carries, still, *none* of Carys — Goldie checked that the first day in the flat above the salon and checks it now, helplessly, the inverse tell that broke the whole case open: the brow is on every head in Aberglas and never on the woman who made it, because you cannot give yourself a daughter's face, you can only give it away.

But the difference, this morning, is in the eyes — not the build of them, the *behind* of them. In the flat in November, Nerys had a clean edge where her memory of Carys's last spring should have been, a managed blank, a thing taken out so carefully she did not know it was gone — *she went to my sister's, Carmarthen way, to get herself sorted* — the cover story laid down in her own head by a kind man twenty-two years ago and dusted ever since. The edge is gone. What is behind Nerys's eyes now is the whole thing, restored, unbearable: she knows where her daughter went, and how, and that she did not run, and that she lay nine days dead in the building they are standing in while a town searched the wrong horizon. She knows, too — and this is the part Goldie cannot look at straight — what her own hands did with the missing of her. A mother told, at sixty-nine, that the daughter she has been quietly resurrecting on a thousand strangers for three decades was not a runaway who broke her heart but a child who died eight rooms away, alone, of a heart no one knew to listen to.

You could break a person with that. The town has decided not to.

Nerys does not speak. Sai does not make her. What happens is that Maureen — terrifying Maureen, who does not do soft — comes down off her self-appointed chairmanship, crosses the front of the room, and sits down in the empty seat beside Nerys, not facing her, just beside her, shoulder to shoulder the way the Belles sit in the shallow end, and takes Nerys's quiet callused hand off her own lap and holds it on her own knee, and stays there, looking out at the room with a face that dares anyone present to make it a moment. Glenys sits the other side. Pam, who has been right about everything this morning and is insufferable with it, sits on the floor at Nerys's feet because there is no chair and will not be told to get up. And the town goes on with its meeting around the four of them, gently, the way you go on talking near someone who is asleep, because the kindest thing you can do for a woman who has just been handed her own grief whole is not to stare at her carrying it.

*They are holding her,* Goldie thinks, and has to look at the raffle strips for a second, because that is the word now, that is the only word, she has worn it smooth this month — they are not keeping Nerys, they are not managing her, they are not protecting her from a thing by deciding it for her the way a kind man once did. They are holding her, in the light, with her grief in her hands and theirs around it, and letting it weigh what it weighs.

It is the warmest thing she has seen in this building, and not one word of comfort is being said.

* * *

The name that is not said all morning, that everyone is not saying with the particular care of a town that has buried a man before he is in the ground, is Gareth's.

He is gone from the front of the room where he stood in October. He is gone from the building. He is, Sai had told her low and unhappy in the office two weeks back, *on remand* — the word strange in his young mouth — pending the things that will take a year and a coroner and a court to say properly: the death certificate he should have signed in 2003 and did not; the ground he chose; and the worst of it, the only one that is a killing and not a covering, Branwen Maddox gone soft in her hospice bed the one night between deciding to talk and talking, *went lovely,* the pillow not quite where a pillow ought to be. The law will have its slow turn at Gareth. Goldie has been a witness in enough courtrooms to know how that goes and how little of the truth survives it.

But the town's verdict is being passed this morning, in the room, in what is not said, and it is stranger and harder than the law's. They are not gloating. There is no relish anywhere in the building for the fall of the man who led the campaign, who buried his wife two summers back, who organised every funeral in this room for thirty years including, God help them all, the wake of the woman he smothered. Goldie watches an old man by the radiator — she places him, *Gareth's bowls partner, Thursdays, twenty years* — sit with his cap in his hands and his eyes wet, grieving openly, in public, for a friend who turns out to have done a monstrous thing for a reason that was almost the opposite of monstrous, and nobody tells him to stop. They let him grieve. You are allowed, in Aberglas, this morning, to be heartbroken about the man and unforgiving about the act at the same time, to hold both, because the town has just spent a month learning that a person can be a good man and a terrible one in the same body, that he hid a girl to spare her child the shame, and managed her mother's memory to spare her the worse grief, and steered a boy away from a salon and a photograph for twenty-two years so that no one would ever do what Goldie did — and then killed a dying woman in her bed to keep the kindness intact, and the kindness was a crime the whole time, and the love was real the whole time, and both are true, and the town is holding both, because that is the thing this whole terrible winter has taught it how to do.

He kept Carys with his back to her. Goldie knows that posture. She does not say so. She has had her own reckoning with it in the dark, on a different night, over a different face, and it is not a thing you announce in a function room over bad coffee. But she stands at the back and watches a town learn to look at a thing it would rather not see, and she thinks the gift she was so frightened of — the thing that makes her unable to *not* see — might be, if she's lucky, if she's careful, the smallest bit useful to people who are only now learning to keep their eyes open.

* * *

They unveil the stone at noon, on the higher ground, with the tide a long grey-gold strip out past the harbour wall and the wind doing its February work on two hundred people who have come out in coats.

There is no orange flag anywhere. Where the developer's pegs stood in October there is now a square of turned earth gone green at the edges, and a stone — local slate, Goldie's eye has it at once, the same slate as the chapel roof, cut by Idris Tomos's cousin who does the headstones, plain, no angels — and on it, in a churchwarden's careful lettering because Olwen Tudor insisted on doing the layout herself and would brook no argument, the thing the town has agreed, at last, to know.

CARYS PRYCE
2003 — aged sixteen
*Daughter. Held now.*

No *missing.* No *runaway.* No dates that lie. Just the year, and the age, and the three words underneath that the whole town argued over for a fortnight and that Nerys, when Maureen brought her the three drafts to choose from, chose without hesitating, the only words she has said in public all winter, *that one, the held one,* and would say no more. It was Eira — ninety-five, who was behind the function-room glass in 2003 and has been quiet about it ever since — who had said the other thing earlier, out loud on the higher ground to nobody and to everybody, *she did not run,* and nobody argued with Eira; but the words on the slate are Nerys's.

The chapel bell rings — Huw is ringing it, the great soft article, up the little tower with the rope in his vast hands, who has cried at every retirement do this building has ever held and is making no attempt this morning to do otherwise, the tears just running off the big kind slab of his face while he pulls the bell for a girl he never met who has turned out to be the great-grandmother of half the children he has hauled out of the deep end — and the sound goes out flat over the grass and the gorse and the long water, and Goldie stands at the back of the crowd, where she stands, and runs her eye over them.

The young ones are here. Of course they are here. She has trained herself this month to look at them without the floor tilting, and she looks now: Lowri with her toddler on her hip and the natural never-closed gap in her smile, the gap a salon could not make, the born thing; the twins with the cheekbones split between them like a face cut down the middle and handed out; Gethin shepherding his little cousins, the ear, the left-handed cowlick, the boy whose heart they caught in time. And the ones who are not here yet because they are in Aberystwyth being looked after — Rhodri, Seren, the two whose hearts went grey at the deep end and the gala, the two who carry the eyes and the killing thing the eyes came wrapped in — getting the test now, the genetic one, the one that names the silent gene at last and gives them the little box under the collarbone that will not let the heart forget itself. *Long QT,* the cardiologist in Aberystwyth called it, the first time anyone in twenty-two years had said the true name of the thing that stopped Carys, and the saying of it is what saves the children, which is the only mercy in the whole story that runs forward instead of back.

A dead girl reached across twenty-two years, through a stranger's eyes at a lost-property counter, and kept her own great-grandchildren breathing. You could not have told Goldie, in October, behind her quiet counter, where the seeing would end up. She would not have believed the seeing could end anywhere but harm.

The bell stops. The wind takes the last of it. And in the quiet after, Glenys — who is deaf in the left ear and has misheard her way comically and occasionally crucially through this entire winter — turns to Goldie with her face wet and her swim cap, inexplicably, already on under her headscarf, ready for the half-twelve aqua that nobody has cancelled because nobody cancels the aqua, and says, in the carrying voice of the hard-of-hearing, into the silence the whole crowd is keeping:

"Lovely service. Is it her we're saving the building for, then?"

"It's her," says Maureen, before anyone can hush it, fierce and final, decided, the way you decide the coffee has happened to you. "It's all of them. Now get in the water, you'll seize up."

And the town laughs — soft, wet-eyed, allowed — and turns from the stone, and goes back down the worn path toward the building it has decided, against the council and the developer and the long odds and its own buried shame, to keep standing, because a town that knows what it is keeping is a hard town to knock down. Goldie does not go straight in with them. She waits while the crowd thins down the path, the coats and the swim caps and the toddler grizzling on Lowri's hip, until it is only her on the higher ground with the new stone and the old chapel and the long grey water.

She does not have a thing to leave. She is not a flowers woman; she would not know what to do with a bunch of them. So she does the only thing she has, the thing she has done at a counter every day for four months and is, she is starting to suspect, the only prayer she has ever known how to say — she reads the stone, all of it, slowly, the slate and the lettering and the two chosen words, the way she would read a face, and she files it, on purpose, with her eyes open, where she keeps the things she has decided to hold instead of guard.

Then she turns, with her back to the water for once and her face to the building, and goes down the path after her town, the bell rope still swinging in the empty tower behind her, and somewhere below, faint through the rust and the render and the swing doors, Maureen counting the half-twelve class into the water like a woman calling the living home.

---

# CHAPTER 29 — Now Then

The woman at the counter is nobody.

This is the part Goldie is still getting used to, six weeks on — that a face can arrive and be *nobody*, a clean stranger, no drawer sliding open behind her eyes, no old platform, no riot footage, no twenty-two-year-old girl wearing a piece of herself out of a child's face. The woman is fifty-odd, off-season pale, a holidaymaker out of her element in February, here because the indoor pool is one of three things in Aberglas that are warm and open and the other two are the chapel and the Spar. Goldie has her in the ordinary way, the human way and the other way at once: a wedding ring spun round so the stones sit underneath, a habit of nurses and of women who do a job with their hands; a sunburn line on the left forearm that says *gardener*, or *recent caravan*; a way of holding her purse to her chest that says she's been told, by someone, that this is the kind of town where you hold your purse to your chest, and has been wrong about that for the whole of her stay and will go home still believing it.

"Hiya," says the woman. "Sorry — I don't suppose anyone's handed in a—"

"Goggle," says Goldie.

The woman blinks. "Towel, actually."

"Right," says Goldie. "Sorry. It's mostly goggles." She comes off the stool. "Colour?"

This is new, too — the *coming off the stool.* For four months she ran this desk the way you'd run a hide, a place to watch from and not be watched, the hatch a one-way mirror she sat behind in the dark of her own choosing. She has caught herself, lately, walking round the end of the counter to a stranger. Dil has noticed. Dil notices everything, files it the soft wrong-on-the-dates right-on-the-feelings way, and has said exactly nothing about it except, once, *you've come out from behind there, then,* the way you'd remark that the weather had turned, and gone to put the kettle on.

The towel is on the shelf. Goldie knows it is on the shelf because Goldie now knows the shelf the way she knows the town — not as evidence any more, just as the slow green sediment of a place that loses things and, sometimes, claims them back. One (1) bath towel, navy, a child's name tape sewn at the corner in the careful capitals of a parent who labelled everything one September and gave up by October. She slides it across.

"There's lovely," says the woman, genuinely, the way the bay says it, and Goldie thinks: she'll go home and tell them the people were nice. They were. That's the thing nobody tells you about a town that buried a girl in its own ground for twenty-two years. The people were nice the whole time. That was never the lie.

---

The pool is loud through the swing doors, which is the sound of a thing not dying.

In October it was loud the way a held breath is loud.
