Now it is just loud — the slap and shriek of a half-term that has the building running at a capacity the Pelican has not seen since before the millennium, because a leisure centre that was in every newspaper in the country for the wrong reason is, it turns out, in every newspaper in the country, and people drive from Aberystwyth and Cardigan and once, memorably, from Shropshire, to swim in the saved lido and have their picture taken by the stone. Sai has had to put a man on the door. The man on the door is Huw, who is constitutionally incapable of stopping anyone coming in and has instead spent a fortnight learning everyone's grandchildren by name, which is not what Sai meant by *managing footfall* and is, Goldie suspects, considerably better for the soul of the place.

"Now then," says Huw, materialising at the hatch the way he does, vast and damp and beaming, a man built like the building and going the same hopeful colour of weather. He has a float under each arm and the specific air of someone who has come to ask a favour and is going to take three sentences to get to it. "Goldie. You all right? Lovely. Listen—"

"No," says Goldie.

"You don't know what I'm going to—"

"You're going to ask me to do the raffle at the swim club thing."

Huw's face does a slow collapse of betrayal. "Who told you."

"Nobody told me. You've got the face of a man about to ask someone to do a raffle." She has, in fact, read it off him — the apologetic forward lean, the float-clutching, the eyebrows already doing the work of asking — but she has stopped saying so out loud, mostly, because the saying so is the old reflex, the one that put a wall between her and every face she ever clocked. She lets him have the small mystery of it. "Yes. Fine. I'll do the raffle."

"You're a—" He stops himself, because the word he wants is *legend,* and the boy who used to say it to her across this counter is not, this morning, anywhere she can see, and Huw is a man who feels the shape of an absence in a room even when he can't name it. "You're a good 'un," he says instead, softer, and pats the counter twice with one huge flat hand, *knock-knock,* the way the boy used to knock the broken goggle for luck, and Goldie's chest does a thing she does not name and lets pass, because that is also the new arrangement: you let the small ones pass through instead of locking the door on them.

"Get back on your door, Huw."

"I am on my door." He is forty feet from his door. "I can see it from here."

---

The Belles come at half eleven, because the Belles come at half eleven, because the half-eleven aqua has run on a Tuesday and a Thursday through three managers, one closure notice, a murder, the unearthing of a body, and the saving of a building, and will run, Maureen has made plain, through the heat death of the universe, at which point she expects the lifeguard to *still count them in.*

They process past the hatch in their caps. Maureen first, mackintosh over the costume because she has not yet decided to trust February. Glenys behind, headscarf and cap both, deaf side to the world as ever, mishearing it into something better. Pam, with a folded newspaper she is going to be right about. And Eira, ninety-five, last, slow, on Maureen's elbow now since the winter, saying nothing, missing nothing — Eira who was *here* in 2003, who knew the quiet work and kept the knowing folded so small and so long that even she half forgot the shape of it, and who, since the stone went up, has seemed to walk a half-inch taller, the way you do when you've finally set down a bag you'd carried so long you'd stopped feeling the weight.

"Marigold," says Maureen, which is a summons.

"Maureen."

"Glenys says you've taken up flowers."

"Glenys is wrong."

"I am not wrong," says Glenys serenely, who has misheard *raffle* as *gladioli* and is at peace with it. "It's lovely you've taken up flowers, bach. It gives a person an interest."

"She's got an interest," says Pam darkly, unfolding the newspaper. "Don't you start her on a new one. She's only just finished the last one." This is the closest the Belles will come to referring directly to the winter, to the thing Goldie *finished*, and they all let it lie there for a second, a hard true sentence at the bottom of a soft scene, and then Maureen claps once, the sound of a woman refusing a moment, and says:

"In the water. All of you. You'll seize."

And they go, the four of them, down the poolside in the slow comic dignity of the old in swimwear, and Goldie watches them go the way you watch your own people go, which is a thing she could not have done in October because in October she did not have any, and thinks — without the floor tilting, which is the whole point, which is the thing she came to a dying lido to be unable to do and learned, instead, how to do safely — that she would mind, very much, if any one of them stopped coming.

---

Sai brings her a coffee at noon, which is an act of either love or war, and they have agreed it is love.

It is exactly, faithfully terrible. Carwyn has refused, on principle, to improve it; the trust has voted twice; the coffee is now technically a heritage asset. Sai sets it down with the air of a man delivering a writ and leans on the counter, and he is, Goldie notes, wearing a lanyard that says PELICAN COMMUNITY TRUST — DUTY MANAGER, laminated, slightly too proud of itself, a young man who came here to switch off the lights and is now running the place with the haunted devotion of the recently converted.

"The cardiology letter came," he says. "About the open day. The Aberystwyth lot want to do a stand — heart screening, the young swimmers, the families. Free. They're calling it—" he winces "—*Know Your Heart.*"

"Course they are."

"It's good, though." He says it defensively, because he is twenty-four and has not yet learned that you can find a thing both excruciating and good. "It's actually good, Goldie. They want to screen the club kids. All of them. Because of—" He stops. The sentence has no agreed ending in this building either. He settles, as the town settles, on the true plain one: "Because of what you found."

"Because of what the town found," says Goldie, on the rails of it now, worn smooth.

"Because of what the town found." He drinks his own coffee, flinches, soldiers on. "Three of them are already done, mind. The three you—" Another stall. He is a minefield of unfinished sentences, this boy, the whole town is, picking its way across a winter nobody has words for yet. "They're fine. They're all fine."

"I know they're fine," says Goldie.

She knows because she sees them. She sees them the way she sees everything, against her will and for no money, only now the seeing does not cost what it cost.

---

The other new thing — the thing she has not got used to and suspects she never will — is that people have started to *bring her things.*

Not lost things. Found ones. A man came last Tuesday, Dai Post, who once lost a wife to a man from Aberaeron and never forgave the postcode, and he stood at the hatch for a while turning his cap and then said, not looking at her, that his brother in Llandysul had a face he couldn't place from a funeral and would she — and she'd said she didn't do that any more, which was a lie, and then she'd done it, because the brother's face was a 1990s rugby club dinner and the man at the funeral was the brother's own cousin gone bald, and Dai Post had gone away lighter by the weight of a small wrongness he'd carried for a month. Word gets round in a town like a tide round a wall. They have worked out — the way they worked out everything about her except, for a long blessed while, the thing that mattered — that the woman at the lost-property desk can put a name to a face nobody else can, and they have started, shyly, in twos and threes, to bring her the unplaceable ones. A photograph in a drawer. A stranger in a wedding video. The man who was kind to them once at a hospital and whose name they never got.

She does it. This is the part she cannot explain to Tom, who phones on Sundays now, fond and tired and pleased with her in a way that is its own kind of ache and its own kind of warmth — that the desk she chose because nobody at a lost-property counter would ever need her to be *sure* has become, very quietly, the one place in Aberglas where people come precisely *because* she is sure. She sat behind it to stop seeing. She sits behind it now, on purpose, to see — sideways, gently, for the man with the cap and the brother and the unplaceable cousin — and the gift that was a sentence for fifty-one years has turned, in a leisure centre that smells of chlorine and chips, into a thing she can lend.

"You'll want a sign," says Dil, arriving on her rails with two cups of the holy-terrible coffee and the lanyard swinging its little dead dog. "*Faces identified, fifty pence. No refunds.*"

"I'm not charging."

"You should charge." Dil sets the coffee down with the grave tenderness of a woman who has decided the coffee is an act of devotion and not, as it tastes, a punishment. "Maureen charges for the bingo and that's just shouting numbers." She leans on the counter, surveys the loud bright impossible building she has run a reception for since 1983 and will go out of feet first, and is quiet a second, which from Dil is an event. "You've changed, mind," she says, not unkindly, the great soft article. "You came in here in October like a woman pulling a blind down. Now you're—" she gestures with the cup at the hatch, the shelf, the whole arrangement "—open all hours."

"It's the coffee," says Goldie. "It's done something to me."

"It's done something to all of us," says Dil, and drinks hers, and they leave it there, the true thing under the joke, where Goldie keeps the things she has finally agreed to keep.

---

They come in at one, the three of them, the way teenagers come anywhere, which is loudly and as if the building owed them an apology.

Rhodri first, because Rhodri is staff now — properly, badge and all, RHODRI in the centre's font, the trainee word gone off it — and he comes through the swing doors with a whistle round his neck and a clipboard he holds like a man who has been told to hold a clipboard. He is filling out, the shoulders catching up to the rest of him at last, and under the red lifeguard polo, if you know to look, there is a small flat squareness high on the left of his chest, a thing the size of a matchbox, that was put there in Aberystwyth six weeks ago and that will not, now, let his heart forget itself. He does not press his hand to his chest any more. She watched for it the first day he came back. It is gone. The half-second flinch that used to cross him at the deep end, the *checking for something that forgot to be there* — gone, because the something is no longer allowed to forget, because a girl who died of exactly this nine days after a birth nobody admitted to reached across twenty-two years and a stranger's eyes and a lost-property desk and put a little box under her great-grandson's collarbone.

He does not know that last part. He knows the box. He knows the screening, the family, the strange autumn of cardiologists and the new word, the one that finally got said. He does not know the box is, in a way, a gift from a girl in a 2003 gala photograph, and Goldie has decided — this is one of the things she has decided, behind her counter, where she keeps the things she has chosen to hold rather than guard — that he does not have to. Some inheritances you can hand a person without handing them the grief that came in the same envelope.

"All right, Goldie," he says, easy, alive as Tuesday, and grins, and tips his head up to say it properly the way he did on the first morning — and looks at her — and they are still her eyes, the same eyes, the build of them, the patient downward cant, the heavy forgiving lid, the eyes she knew were dead the first time she saw them and that are, it turns out, the most alive thing in the building.

She has trained herself, this winter, to look at them and not fall through the floor.

She looks at them now and just sees Rhodri.

"Goggle," she says.

"I haven't lost a goggle in *weeks,*" he says, wounded, which is a lie, and they both know it is a lie, and there are two of his goggles on the shelf behind her this minute, and the not-saying-so is its own small warm thing, a joke worn down to a comfort.

---

Gethin comes through with the little ones he is always shepherding, sixteen and gangly and patient as a sheepdog, the ear and the cowlick exactly as they were and exactly as they'll be, and lifts a hand to her without breaking stride. Then Seren.

Seren, who Glenys saw go funny four seconds before the whistle in October. Seren, who was dusk-blue and face-up and gone, for a stretch of seconds Goldie will keep forever because Goldie keeps everything, until the green machine said *shock advised* and Huw's huge hands did what they did and the breath came back into her at fourteen years old with the whole gala screaming. Seren, who carries the eyes again — the *same* eyes, the second set, the impossibility that turned out to be the simplest thing in the world, descent — and who has, since the autumn, the same flat matchbox squareness high on her chest, and a cardiologist in Aberystwyth who calls her by her first name, and a future, which is the entire point, which is the only mercy in the whole long story that runs forwards instead of back.

She comes to the counter on purpose. Goldie sees her decide to. There is a girl-shaped deliberation in it, an approach with intent, and for one cold half-second the old machine in Goldie braces — *what's wrong, what does she want, what does she know* — before the new arrangement catches up and reminds her that a person walking toward you is not, always, a threat to be read across a room.

Seren puts a swimming goggle on the counter. One. Just the one. Black strap. The lens, when Goldie looks, is fine; the goggle is whole; there is nothing lost about it at all.

"Someone handed this in," says Seren, with the elaborate carelessness of fourteen. "I think. Probably. You should put it in your book."

And she looks at Goldie — the dead-alive eyes, level, knowing more than she's saying, the way teenagers do and the way that particular family always has — and there is the ghost of something in it, not grief, nothing so heavy, just a girl who has worked out, in the way the young work things out sideways and never ask, that the woman at the lost-property desk had something to do with the fact that she is standing here at all, breathing, bored, immortal in the way only the recently-nearly-dead can be; and who cannot say thank you, because there is no version of that sentence a fourteen-year-old could survive saying; and so has brought a perfectly good goggle to a lost-property counter to be *kept,* on purpose, by the one person in town who keeps things, as the only thank-you she has.

Goldie takes the goggle.

"I'll keep an eye," she says, which is what she said to a boy about a flip-flop on her first morning, and means, this time, every word of it.

Seren goes pink, mutters something, and is gone into the chlorine and the echo and the noise, alive as anything, alive as the building, and Goldie stands with a whole undamaged goggle in her hand — half a thing's opposite, a *kept* thing, a claimed one — and does not, for once, have to wait to see whether the floor is a floor.

It is a floor. It has been, for a few weeks now, reliably a floor.

She picks up the pen.

---

> **FACE 0001** — female, 14, club swimmer (SEREN). Counter, 13:05. Returns one (1) goggle — *whole, unlost, nothing wrong with it.* Brought it on purpose, to be kept.
> *The eyes: set, cant, lid — exact. The same eyes. Alive.*
> No longer "whose." Hers.
> **STATUS: KEPT.**
>
> *(New book. First page. Mind how you go.)*

---

# CHAPTER 30 — The Next Face

The bunting goes up on a Tuesday, which is the wrong day for bunting and exactly right for the Pelican.

Huw is on the stepladder, where Huw should never be, because Huw weighs what the building weighs and the building is the colour of a bad tooth for a reason. He is stringing triangles of laminated flag along the curved Deco frontage — actual flags, run off by Sai at the library printer, each one a small grainy photograph of the pool *in its day*, 1951, the open-air water packed with seals on a rock, so that the whole front of the place is now hung with the faces of the long-dead enjoying themselves. It is the most cheerful thing Goldie has ever seen and she cannot work out why it does not feel like a graveyard. Then she works it out. It is because the building is full of noise behind it, and the dead on the bunting are flapping in a wind off a sea that is still here, and downstairs in a box the same water is still warm, and a thing is not a graveyard if you are still using it.

"Don't," she says, to Huw, who is reaching.

"I've got it."

"You haven't got it. You're forty feet up a ladder that's older than Dil."

"Nothing's older than Dil," says Huw, with love, and reaches anyway, and the ladder makes a sound like a decision being reconsidered, and Goldie watches a sixty-year-old man who has saved this lido for thirty years nearly unsave himself for a paper picture of it, and does the maths she always does, the involuntary one — *if he goes, he goes left, the right knee's the bad one, he'll grab the rail, the rail's the original rail, the rail will not hold him* — and is halfway off her stool to be useful before Maureen, below, simply puts one hand flat on the ladder's base and says, "Huw," in the voice, and the matter is closed.

He comes down. They always come down for Maureen.

It is the morning of the open day. *KNOW YOUR HEART*, the banner says, over the main doors, in a font the Aberystwyth cardiology department chose itself and is proud of, and beneath it, smaller, run off by Sai with his tongue between his teeth: *SAVE OUR PELICAN — REBORN. ALL WELCOME.* Both true. Both, this morning, the same sentence.

***

The thing about a day everyone has been waiting for is that it arrives, and then it is just a day, with the boiler in it.

The boiler is making the noise. The boiler will make the noise at Goldie's own funeral; it is the building clearing its throat to speak and never speaking, *the Bishop*, Huw calls it, because it knocks at solemn moments, and it knocks now, twice, into the gathering hum of a foyer filling with strangers, and Goldie thinks: *go on then. Say it.* It doesn't. It never does. That is also the arrangement.

By half nine there are people she does not know in the Pelican, and there are a lot of them, and this is the part that would have closed her throat in October and merely interests her now. A man from the *Cambrian News* with a real camera. Two girls from the comp doing a thing on their phones, narrating the lido to an audience Goldie cannot see and the girls plainly adore. A family down from Machynlleth with a buggy and a list of questions about armbands. The cardiology stand, three tables and a privacy screen and a queue, the queue being the entire point, the queue being a row of the town's children waiting to be told a number about their own hearts that nobody thought to ask for twenty-two years.

She watches Rhodri walk a small girl to the front of that queue with a clipboard and the manner of a man who has been handed responsibility and intends to die of it. She watches Gethin shepherd the under-eights past the screen with a patience that should be studied by science. She watches Seren *not* be in the queue, because Seren is done, screened and fitted and immortal, and is instead standing by the privacy screen telling the smaller children it doesn't hurt, which is a lie, and is the kind of lie that is load-bearing, the kind a town runs on.

She does not, this morning, watch them go grey. She has stopped watching for it. The watching-for-it is gone the way the boy's hand-to-chest is gone, because the thing it was watching for is no longer allowed to happen unseen, has a little box now and a cardiologist who calls and a number written down, and a girl who died of exactly this — alone, in a back room of this building, nine days after a birth nobody would admit to — reached across more years than the children have been alive and made certain of it. They do not know that part. Goldie knows it for them. It is, she has decided, a reasonable division of labour.

***

"You'll want to look respectable," says Dil, arriving on her rails with two cups of the holy-terrible coffee and a comb she has no intention of letting Goldie use. "There's press."

"I'm at the back."

"You are not at the back. You're the—" Dil stops, because the word the whole town has settled on for what Goldie is has not been invented, and the nearest ones are all wrong. *Detective* is wrong; she didn't detect, she *recognised*. *Hero* is wrong, and Goldie would walk into the cold lido sooner than wear it. *The one who found her* is closest and too sad for a morning with bunting. Dil, who has run this reception since 1983 and will go out of it feet first, lands, as she always lands, on the true plain thing. "You're ours," she says. "Drink your coffee."

Goldie drinks her coffee. It is exactly, faithfully terrible. Carwyn has been interviewed about it twice now and has refused, on camera, to apologise, and the coffee is the only thing in Aberglas that has emerged from the winter entirely unchanged, which is its own kind of comfort. You cannot let *everything* turn out to mean something. Some things have to just be bad.

"Eira's coming down," says Dil, lower, the way she lowers it for the things that matter. "Maureen's bringing her in the car. She wants to see the stone."

"It's been up six weeks."

"She wants to see it on a *day*," says Dil, and there is no arguing with that, because Goldie understands it exactly: a stone on an ordinary Tuesday is just a stone, but a stone on a day when the whole town is loud and warm and *here*, with the children getting their hearts read forty feet away — that is a stone with a town around it, which is the entire difference between a grave and a person, and Eira, who is ninety-five and was *there*, in 2003, behind the function-room glass, and kept the knowing folded so small for so long that she half forgot the shape of it — Eira has not waited twenty-two years to look at a stone on a quiet day.

***

The stone is in the chapel ground, on the higher grass, where the developer's orange flags used to be and where there is now, instead, a low square of slate the colour of the sea in February.

It is not a grand stone. The committee fought about it — of course they fought about it; the Belles can turn a stone into a constitutional crisis — and Maureen won, which meant simple, which was right. It says her name. It says *CARYS PRYCE*, and under that *2003 — aged sixteen*, the one date and the one age, no second number to do the lie's arithmetic, and under that the three words Nerys chose without hesitating and would say no more about: *Daughter. Held now.* That is all. No verse. No lamb. It was Eira, behind the function-room glass twenty-two years ago and silent ever since, who said the other thing out loud at last, on the path, to nobody and to everybody — *she did not run* — and nobody argued with Eira; but the words on the slate are Nerys's, and Goldie, who has spent a career reading what faces would not say, looks at the least evasive three words she has ever met cut into stone by the most evasive town in Wales, and finds she has nothing to add.

Nerys is there when Goldie comes up the path, which Goldie did not expect and is glad of.

She is at the stone, in the cardigan that goes two sizes into a body that used to fill it, and she has her right thumb on the top edge of the slate the way she once put it on a hundred heads, the comb-callus reading the stone like hair, and she is not crying, she is *tidying* — there is a leaf on the slate and she removes it, and there is a smear of gull on the corner and she works at it with a thumb licked like a mother's thumb, and Goldie understands that this is the only language the woman has, that Nerys Pryce spent thirty years giving a thousand strangers a piece of her daughter because tending was the one verb grief left her, and that she is tending now, the right one at last, the actual girl, found, named, and under a clean stone she can keep clean.

"There's a mark," Nerys says, to Goldie, without turning, the way you'd say it to anyone. "It'll be the birds."

"I'll have Huw do it with the hose."

"You'll not. Huw's hose takes the moss and the moss is nice." She straightens, slow. She looks at the stone, and then she looks at Goldie, and there is, in the look, the thing the town cannot quite hold yet and Goldie can, because Goldie has held the same thing in her own chest for twelve years and only just set it down — that Nerys *knows*, now, what she did with the brow, has been told, gently, with the Belles in the room and a sherry she didn't drink, that she rebuilt Carys on half the heads in town without ever once meaning to; and that knowing it has not broken her, because the town did not let it. They held her for it. They are holding her now. A woman who made her daughter a thousand times by hand is allowed, it turns out, to have made her out of love and not be a monster for it, and that permission is the tenderest thing Aberglas has ever managed, and it managed it.

"She'd have liked the noise," Nerys says, of the pool, of the day, of the children getting their hearts read. "She liked a racket, Carys. Couldn't be doing with quiet." She picks the imaginary last leaf off the slate. "I kept it too quiet," she says, "all them years," and that is the whole of it, that is the only confession the woman will ever make and the only one she owes, and Goldie does not fill the silence after it, because she has learned, finally, at fifty-one, that the silence after a true thing is not yours to fill.

"Come and have a coffee," Goldie says instead. "It's appalling. You'll hate it."

"I know it's appalling," says Nerys, "I've drunk it since the flood," and lets herself be walked back down the path, and the stone keeps its moss, and behind them the gulls go back to the corner the second they leave it, which is the gulls' job, and the moss's, and February's, and not, this morning, Goldie's.

***

The cardiology man finds her at the counter at noon and shakes her hand too long.

He is forty, lanyard, a registrar's careful kindness, and he has the specific over-gratitude of a doctor who has done the sums on what this town nearly lost and cannot say so to a journalist. "Three confirmed," he tells her, low, the way you'd hand someone a stone to keep. "Long QT, three of the young ones, gene-positive, all on beta-blockers now, two for devices. Caught. All of them caught." He says *caught* the way Dil says *ours.* "Without the history—" He stops. He has met the history. The history is three words in slate on the higher grass. "They'd have gone the way she went," he says. "One by one. At a pool, at a gala, in their sleep. And nobody would ever have known why." He looks at Goldie as if she is a piece of equipment he doesn't have the budget for. "How did you—"

"Filing," says Goldie.

"Sorry?"

"I do filing," she says, and the man laughs, uncertain, and lets her hand go at last, and goes back to his screen and his queue to read more numbers off more children, and Goldie sits with the oldest lie she owns and finds that, this once, she half wishes it were true — that it had been filing, ordinary filing, and not a girl's eyes on a boy at a counter and twelve years of refusing to look at her own. But it wasn't filing. It was the gift, the whole unburiable sentence of it, every face cemented and ageless and never once allowed to soften, the thing that took the Met fifteen years and then took her marriage and then took the inside of her head and made it a cemetery she could not stop walking through — and it has, in the end, in a leisure centre that smells of chlorine and chips, kept three children breathing. She does the sum the doctor did. It comes out, for once, in her favour. She is not used to the gift coming out in her favour. She decides to allow it.

***

The raffle is at one, and the raffle is a disaster, and the disaster is the best part of the day.

Goldie does the raffle because she said she would do the raffle, six weeks ago, to Huw, who asked her with the face of a man asking for a kidney, and because Maureen has decided that Goldie doing the raffle is *good for her*, which is Maureen's term for anything that drags a person up out of themselves whether they consent or not. The drum is a tombola cage that has not been turned since before foot-and-mouth and turns now with a shriek that brings Huw at a run thinking the Bishop has finally spoken. The prizes are what prizes always are in a building like this: a hamper assembled by the Belles from the back of four cupboards, a voucher for the bacon at the Mermaid, a "spa experience" that is, on inspection, ninety minutes in the warm pool with a float Maureen has personally tied a ribbon to, and, at the top, a framed laminated photograph of the 1951 lido, the seals on the rock, donated by Sai, signed by no one, priceless to everyone in the room and worthless to a soul outside it.

Goldie reads the numbers. She reads them in her dry flat Met voice, the one that once cleared a pool deck of a screaming gala, and the room loves it, the room thinks it is a *bit*, and it is not a bit, it is just the only voice she has for reading numbers to a crowd, and Glenys — deaf side to the room, blissful — hears every number wrong and wins, somehow, three times, because Glenys always wins, because the universe has decided that a woman who mishears *raffle* as *gladioli* and *legs eleven* as *eggs eleven* should be compensated in hampers. Pam claims the draw is rigged and produces, from her newspaper, evidence. Maureen rules the evidence inadmissible. Eira, in the front, on Maureen's arm, holds the winning ticket for the framed seals and says nothing and will not give it up, and so the most beloved object in the room goes to the one person in the room who was *there*, who swam in that water before half the town's grandparents were born, and the photograph of the lido goes home with a woman who is, herself, a photograph of the lido, and nobody planned it, and everyone, looking at Eira's small triumphant face, decides quietly that somebody must have.

***

She is allowing it when the next face walks in.

It is half twelve, the foyer thinning toward dinnertime, and a woman comes through the main doors out of the cold and the noise and the press of the open day, and stops, and looks about her the way people do in a building that used to be loved and is being loved again, taking the measure of it — the curved frontage, the bunting of the dead being cheerful, the queue of the town's children at the heart screen, the smell. She is fifty, maybe. Off-season. A stranger. And Goldie's eyes do what Goldie's eyes do, the involuntary inventory running its hands over her before she has crossed the mat — a good coat worn at the cuff, a wedding ring still worn, a way of holding a folded piece of paper to her chest that is not fear this time but *purpose*, a woman who has come a distance and rehearsed a sentence — and then the woman's face turns full into the foyer light, and the drawer behind Goldie's eyes—

stays shut.

Nobody. A clean stranger. No platform, no riot footage, no 2009, no dead girl wearing a piece of herself. A face Goldie has never seen, attached to a person Goldie does not know, and the not-knowing is not a relief and not a threat, it is just a door, standing open, with a person on the other side of it she has not met yet.

The woman comes to the counter. She has the folded paper still against her chest. She is nervous in the particular way of someone who has been told this is a place where you ask the unaskable, and has driven here to ask it.

"Hiya," she says. "Sorry. I don't suppose— " and stops, and starts again, braver. "Someone said. In Aberaeron. They said there was a woman here who could—" She unfolds the paper. It is a photograph, old, a wedding crowd, fifty faces in their best in front of a chapel, and her thumb is on one of them, a man at the edge, half-turned, blurred, the way the ones that matter always are. "My dad's in this," she says. "I know my dad. But there's a man stood next to him, see, and he was kind to me, once, at the hospital, the worst day of my life, and I never— I never got his name. And it's twenty years and I still—" Her voice does the thing. "I'd just like to know who he was," she says. "So I can thank him. In my head, like. Even if it's only in my head."

And Goldie looks at the photograph, and then she looks up at the woman — the clean stranger, the nobody, the door standing open — and she does not reach for the joke. The joke is right there, *I do filing*, the tourniquet of fifty-one years, and for once she lets it go past, because the woman has driven from Aberaeron with the worst day of her life folded against her chest, and she is not asking Goldie to be sure, she is asking Goldie to be *kind*, and those used to be the same thing wearing two coats and Goldie has finally learned to take the second one off.

"Come round," she says, and comes off the stool, and walks round the end of her own counter to a stranger, which is the whole arc of her, the entire distance, rendered in three steps and a hinge. "Sit down. Let's have a look at him."

The woman sits. Goldie takes the photograph and holds it to the light, and the gift comes up under her hand the way it always comes up, against her will and for no money — the man at the edge resolving, the set of him, the build, *not the blur, the bones* — and somewhere a drawer she did not open and cannot close slides quietly toward the light instead of away from it. She does not have his name. Names go missing like everyone's go missing, like flip-flops. But she will find it the slow human way, through this woman, through the wedding, through Dil who knows everyone's business and most of their PINs, through the warm loud machinery of a saved building full of people who are, all of them, somebody's. She will sit in the confessional she finally agreed to sit in. She will keep an eye.

She reaches for the ledger.

It is open to a clean page, because she keeps it open to a clean page now, on purpose, the way you leave a light on. She uncaps the pen. The woman is watching her hands, hopeful, the photograph between them on the counter like a thing handed in at last to be claimed.

Goldie does not write the man's face down.

She writes the woman's.

---

> **FACE 0002** — female, ~50, from Aberaeron way. Walked in off the open day, the worst day of her life folded in her coat. Looking for a kind man's name, twenty years gone, so she can thank him. In her head, like. Even if it's only in her head.
> *Not a fragment. Not a ghost. Just a face I never saw before, that wants to find one it can't forget.*
> No drawer. No dread. A stranger, come to the right desk.
> **STATUS: WELCOME.**
>
> *(Lovely, that. Now then — let's get her her man.)*
